# PART IV: CHILD PROTECTION AND CHILD RIGHTS

---


---


# Part 1: Historical Evolution


## 4.1 Historical Development of the Child Protection System


The development of a formal child protection system in the Maldives is a relatively recent phenomenon, spanning barely three decades from the first legislative acknowledgment of children's rights in 1991 to the comprehensive statutory framework enacted in 2019. Understanding this trajectory requires attention to three intersecting forces: the influence of international child rights norms following the ratification of the Convention on the Rights of the Child (CRC); the particular political economy of a small island developing state with extreme geographic dispersal; and the persistent tension between international child welfare models and local social, cultural, and religious practices.

### 4.1.1 Pre-1991: Traditional Child Welfare

Prior to any formal legislation, child welfare in the Maldives was embedded within kinship networks and island-level communal systems. Children were considered the responsibility of the extended family, and matters of child discipline, custody, and protection were resolved through a combination of Islamic family law principles (*fiqh*) and customary island-level adjudication overseen by the island chief (*kadheeb*). The concept of state intervention in family life to protect children was essentially unknown. Orphaned or abandoned children were absorbed into extended family networks, and institutional care was nonexistent until the establishment of the Islaahiya reform facility in 1979, which housed both delinquent youth and children in need of care without differentiation between the two categories.[^1]

The absence of formal child protection mechanisms did not mean that child abuse and exploitation were absent. Rather, such matters were handled – or, critically, not handled – within the private sphere. Community norms around family honour, the Islamic principle of *satr* (concealment of others' faults), and the extreme social proximity of small island communities combined to suppress disclosure of child maltreatment. This dynamic would persist well into the modern era and continues to shape reporting behaviours today.[^2]

### 4.1.2 Law 9/91 and the First Institutional Responses (1991–2003)

The Maldives ratified the United Nations Convention on the Rights of the Child on 11 February 1991, with reservations on Article 14(1) (freedom of thought, conscience, and religion – incompatible with the constitutional requirement that all Maldivians be Muslim) and Article 21 (adoption – not permitted under Islamic law as practised in the Maldives).[^3] The ratification was largely driven by international engagement dynamics rather than domestic advocacy, and it predated any substantive domestic legislation on child protection.

**Law 9/91**, enacted in 1991, represented the Maldives' first legislative acknowledgment of children's rights. However, the law was largely declaratory in nature, setting out broad principles without establishing enforcement mechanisms, institutional mandates, or penalties for violations. It contained no definitions of child abuse or maltreatment, no mandatory reporting requirements, and no provisions for state intervention in families where children were at risk. In practical terms, Law 9/91 served primarily as a symbolic gesture of compliance with CRC obligations rather than a functional piece of child protection legislation.[^4]

The **Unit for the Rights of Children (URC)** was established in 1992 within the Ministry of Home Affairs as the first dedicated government unit tasked with children's issues. The URC's location within the security-oriented Ministry of Home Affairs reflected the prevailing conceptualisation of children's matters as issues of social order rather than social welfare. In 1993, the URC was transferred to the Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare, signalling an early – if incomplete – shift toward a welfare-oriented approach.[^5]

Throughout the 1990s and early 2000s, the URC operated with minimal staff, no formal case management procedures, limited geographical reach beyond Malé, and no legal mandate to investigate or intervene in child protection cases. The unit functioned primarily as a policy coordination body engaging with UNICEF and CRC reporting obligations rather than as a direct service delivery mechanism.[^6]

### 4.1.3 The Tsunami Catalyst and the Professionalisation Push (2004–2008)

The Indian Ocean tsunami of 26 December 2004 killed 82 people in the Maldives, displaced approximately 21,000, and devastated infrastructure across multiple atolls. The disaster catalysed a substantial expansion of UNICEF's presence and programming in the Maldives, including a significant increase in child protection technical assistance and funding. UNICEF's expanded engagement brought international child protection models, standards, and tools into the Maldivian policy space with an intensity and resourcing that had not previously existed.[^7]

**The 2006 Child Protection Procedure Manual** represented the most significant attempt to systematise child protection practice in the Maldives up to that point. The manual was developed with UNICEF support and was modelled substantially on England's "Working Together to Safeguard Children" guidance – the inter-agency statutory guidance for safeguarding and promoting the welfare of children in the English child protection system. The transposition of a highly developed, institutionally complex, and culturally specific model from a high-income Western context into a small island developing state with fundamentally different social structures, governance capacity, and cultural norms created significant tensions.[^8]

Key areas of cultural tension included:

- **The role of the extended family:** The English model presupposes a nuclear family structure and treats extended family involvement as a supplementary rather than primary safeguarding mechanism. In the Maldives, children routinely move between households of extended family members – a practice that is normative and generally functional but that does not map easily onto the risk assessment frameworks of the English model.

- **Confidentiality and information sharing:** "Working Together" operates within an institutional ecosystem of formal information-sharing agreements between agencies (police, health, education, social services). The Maldives in 2006 had no equivalent inter-agency architecture, and the small-community dynamics of island life meant that information sharing was simultaneously pervasive (through informal gossip) and restricted (through norms of family honour and concealment).

- **Statutory authority:** The English model is undergirded by robust statutory authority for social workers to investigate, assess, and intervene – including compulsory removal of children. No equivalent statutory authority existed in the Maldives in 2006, leaving the procedure manual as guidance without enforceable mandate.[^9]

**The professionalisation of social work** began in earnest in 2006 with the first groups of school leavers trained as social workers through a one-year Advanced Certificate programme delivered by the University of Kelaniya in Sri Lanka. This programme was specifically designed to build a cadre of Maldivian social workers for child protection and family welfare work. In 2007, an Advanced Diploma in Social Service Work was introduced, which was later developed into a four-year Bachelor of Social Work (BSW) degree – establishing, for the first time, a formal pathway to professional social work qualification in the Maldives.[^10]

The quality and relevance of this training, however, remained contested. The curricula were adapted from Sri Lankan and, indirectly, Western social work models, and graduates entered a system that lacked the institutional infrastructure – supervision, case management systems, inter-agency protocols, legal authority – that the training assumed. The gap between the professional competencies taught and the operational realities of practice would remain a persistent challenge.[^11]

### 4.1.4 Democratic Transition and Institutional Restructuring (2008–2013)

The democratic transition of 2008, which brought President Mohamed Nasheed to power following the Maldives' first multi-party presidential election, resulted in significant restructuring of the child protection institutional architecture. Child protection responsibilities were moved to the Ministry of Health and Family under a newly established **Department of Gender and Family Protection Services**. This placement reflected an attempt to integrate child protection with broader family welfare and health services but also subordinated it within a ministry whose primary mandate and institutional culture were oriented toward clinical healthcare delivery.[^12]

Two critical developments occurred during this period:

**The National Child Helpline 1412** was established in 2008 in partnership with Child Helpline International. The helpline provided, for the first time, a dedicated, toll-free, 24-hour channel through which children and adults could report child protection concerns. The helpline represented both a practical service innovation and a symbolic statement about the state's willingness to receive and respond to reports of child maltreatment. However, the helpline's effectiveness was constrained by limited staffing, the absence of an integrated case management system to process referrals, and – in the early years – low public awareness of its existence and purpose.[^13]

**The 2009 baby death case** exposed fundamental weaknesses in the child protection system and became a turning point in public and professional awareness. The case involved an infant who died after being violently shaken by the mother's boyfriend. The mother had been known to child protection services for seven years through her involvement in drug use, but the system had failed to assess the risk to her child, coordinate across agencies (particularly between drug services and child protection), or implement any form of protective intervention. The subsequent review recommended the establishment of a formal supervisory system for child protection social workers – recognising that practitioners operating without clinical supervision in high-risk, emotionally demanding casework were unable to maintain the quality of assessment and decision-making that child protection requires.[^14]

The 2009 case echoed patterns familiar from child death reviews in other jurisdictions – the Victoria Climbie and Peter Connelly cases in England, for example – where system failures were attributable not to the malice of individual workers but to structural deficiencies in information sharing, supervision, and inter-agency coordination. The Maldivian system, however, lacked the institutional capacity for the kind of systematic review and reform that such cases triggered in larger jurisdictions.[^15]

### 4.1.5 Decentralisation and the FCSC Model (2010–2018)

The **Decentralisation Act of 2010** reorganised the administrative geography of the Maldives and had significant implications for child protection service delivery. The Act transferred certain responsibilities to local councils while simultaneously creating a tension between centralised child protection standards and decentralised governance structures.

In response, the **Child and Family Protection Service (CFPS)** was established as a centralised entity to manage child protection service delivery across the atolls through a network of **Family and Children Service Centres (FCSCs)**. Nineteen atoll-level FCSCs were established, creating for the first time a physical presence of child protection services outside Malé. However, the Ministry operated in only 20 of the Maldives' 189 inhabited islands, leaving the vast majority of communities without direct access to child protection services.[^16]

**Community Social Groups (CSGs)** were initiated in 2010 as a mechanism to extend child protection awareness and response into communities where FCSCs did not have a direct presence. CSGs were envisaged as volunteer groups that could identify and refer child protection concerns, provide community-level support, and bridge the gap between formal services and island communities. The CSG model was later revived and expanded through the **IBAMA project**, which sought to build community-level child protection capacity through structured volunteer engagement.[^17]

In **2015**, the Ministry of Education implemented a system of **child protection focal points in schools**, placing designated staff members in schools with responsibility for identifying and responding to child protection concerns within the educational setting. This represented a recognition that schools are a critical site for identification of child maltreatment – children spend substantial portions of their day in school, and teachers and school staff are often the first adults outside the family to observe signs of abuse or neglect. However, the effectiveness of this system was limited by the absence of established referral pathways between schools and CFPS, a lack of police checks and child protection training for school staff, and the absence of any formal case conferencing mechanisms between education and child protection professionals.[^18]

The **Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD)** was launched in 2016, connecting all 19 FCSCs to a centralised digital case management system. The MCPD represented a significant step toward systematic data collection and case tracking, enabling for the first time the generation of national-level data on child protection referrals, case types, and outcomes. Prior to the MCPD, data on child protection was fragmented, inconsistent, and largely reliant on manual record-keeping at the FCSC level.[^19]

---


## 4.2 Historical Evolution: A Chronological Account


### 4.51.1 The Pre-System Period (Pre-1991): Children Without Legal Protection

Before 1991, the Maldives had no formal child protection legislation, no child protection institutions, and no systematic recognition of the state's obligation to protect children from maltreatment. Children's welfare was understood as entirely a matter of family and community responsibility, governed by Islamic norms about parental authority and community honour. The concept of "child abuse" as a category requiring state intervention did not exist in policy or law.[^775]

This is not to say that child welfare was entirely neglected. The Gayoom administration (1978-2008) made significant investments in children's access to education and basic health services: the commitment to building primary schools on every inhabited island was fulfilled during the 1980s, and health centres were established in all 20 atoll capitals between 1978 and 1990. Child survival improved dramatically over this period – infant mortality fell from among the highest in South Asia to among the lowest. But these were investments in child development and survival, not child protection: they did not address violence against children, sexual abuse, neglect, or exploitation, which continued without systematic recognition or response.[^776]

The Islaahiya reform facility, established in 1979, provides a revealing window into pre-1991 approaches to children outside family care. Islaahiya housed both delinquent youth and children in need of care – without meaningful differentiation between the two groups. Children who had committed offences and children who were victims of family breakdown or abuse were housed together, treated identically, and provided with essentially custodial rather than therapeutic or rehabilitative care. This institutional confusion between delinquency and vulnerability – treating children in need as children in trouble – persisted in Maldivian policy thinking for decades after Islaahiya was superseded.[^777]

### 4.51.2 The Unit for the Rights of Children Era (1991-2006): First Recognitions

**CRC ratification (1991)** marks the formal beginning of the Maldivian child protection system, though the commitment it represented was initially more symbolic than operational. The Convention on the Rights of the Child was ratified on 11 February 1991, with reservations on Article 14(1) (freedom of religion – incompatible with the Maldivian requirement that all citizens be Muslim) and Article 21 (adoption – not permitted under Islamic law). These reservations are significant: Article 14's reservation reflects the state's position that children do not have religious freedom, while Article 21's reservation means the Maldives cannot operationalise adoption as a permanent care solution for children without families.[^778]

The same year, the **Law on the Protection of the Rights of the Child (Law 9/91)** was enacted – the first child protection legislation in Maldivian history. However, this law was almost entirely declaratory: it stated that children had rights but provided no definitions of abuse or neglect, no mandatory reporting requirements, no enforcement mechanisms, and no penalties. It created no institutions. It was a political statement, not an operational framework.[^779]

The **Unit for the Rights of Children (URC)** was established in 1992, initially within the Ministry of Home Affairs – a placement that reflected a security-oriented rather than welfare-oriented conception of children's issues. In 1993, the URC was transferred to the Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare, a move that signalled the beginning of a welfare framing, though institutional capacity remained minimal. The URC served primarily as a secretariat for the National Council for Children's Rights, conducting advocacy and awareness activities without operational case management capacity.[^780]

The period from 1993 to 2006 was characterised by institutional instability (the URC went through multiple ministry homes and name changes), minimal financial investment, and the absence of any systematic case management or protective intervention capability. Children who were abused, neglected, or exploited had no practical recourse: there were no trained child protection workers, no formal referral pathways, no assessment tools, and no placement options beyond institutional confinement in inadequate facilities.[^781]

**The Maldives Study on Women's Health and Life Experiences (2006-2007)**, conducted by Emma Fulu with UNFPA support using the WHO multi-country methodology, was a watershed in generating the evidence base for both GBV and child protection policy. Its finding that approximately one in three women aged 15-49 had experienced physical or sexual intimate partner violence – combined with its qualitative data on community norms, honour ideology, and reporting barriers – established for the first time a rigorous empirical picture of the violence that Maldivian families lived within. The implications for children growing up in violent households, while not the study's primary focus, were unmistakable.[^782]

### 4.51.3 The System-Building Phase (2006-2012): The English Model Import

The **Child Protection Procedure Manual (2006)** marks the beginning of the modern Maldivian child protection system. Developed with UNICEF technical support and modelled closely on England's "Working Together to Safeguard Children" guidance, the Manual established – for the first time – explicit definitions of child abuse categories, referral and assessment procedures, and roles for different professionals. This transposition of an English model into the Maldivian context was both a strength (drawing on mature, evidence-based practice) and a limitation (importing assumptions about professional capacity, geographic accessibility, and institutional infrastructure that did not hold in the Maldives).[^783]

The same year, **the first Advanced Certificate in Social Work** graduates were deployed to FCSCs in the atolls. These workers – trained in a one-year programme at the University of Kelaniya in Sri Lanka – were the founding practitioners of the Maldivian child protection social work profession. Their training provided a foundation; their deployment without adequate supervision, support, or institutional infrastructure placed them in an impossible position. The chronic understaffing problem that defines the system in 2026 has its roots in this first deployment, when demand immediately exceeded the supply of trained workers.[^784]

The **National Child Helpline (1412)** was established in 2008, providing a toll-free 24-hour channel for reporting child abuse and welfare concerns. The helpline gave citizens a practical mechanism for contacting the child protection system for the first time – a significant advance in accessibility, particularly for children and adults in communities where approaching an FCSC office involved social exposure and risk of community pressure to withdraw concerns.[^785]

The **2009 Baby Death Case** – a case in which a baby died in circumstances where professional failures in inter-agency coordination, supervision, and risk assessment were subsequently identified – was a turning point that exposed the gap between the system that existed on paper and the one that existed in practice. The case led to recommendations for supervisory systems for social workers, for better inter-agency communication protocols, and for minimum workforce standards. These recommendations anticipated the reform agenda of the CRPA 2019 by a decade but were not systematically implemented in the intervening period.[^786]

The **Decentralisation Act (Act No. 7/2010)** created a new institutional landscape for child protection by transferring many local government functions to elected island councils and grouping FCSCs under centralised management. The Decentralisation Act had complex and in some respects contradictory effects on child protection: it moved formal authority closer to communities through councils, while simultaneously centralising professional child protection under CFPS at the atoll level. The tension between community-level reporting and atoll-level professional response – and the question of which has ultimate authority in specific cases – has been a recurring operational challenge.[^787]

The **Domestic Violence Prevention Act (Act No. 3/2012)** represented the most significant legislative development in child protection's adjacent field. The DVPA created the Family Protection Authority (FPA), established mandatory reporting duties on all persons, and created protection orders – mechanisms with direct child protection implications. A child living in a household where the DVPA is being applied (where a protection order has been issued against an abusive parent) has direct interests in the effective operation of DVPA provisions. Yet the operational linkage between FPA, CFPS, and police FCPW in cases involving both DV and child protection concerns was not systematically established by the DVPA – a coordination gap that persists.[^788]

### 4.51.4 The Professionalisation Phase (2012-2019): Building Infrastructure

The period from 2012 to 2019 was characterised by legislative expansion, data system development, and the slow professionalisation of the social work workforce. Key milestones:

**Special Measures Act 12/2009 (Child Sexual Abuse Offenders):** The Special Measures Act introduced enhanced penalties for child sexual abuse (the Act's actual provision is 10-25 years without parole, though earlier summaries cited 7-14 years), lowered evidentiary standards in theory (though the 2014 SOA's five-evidence requirement partially reversed this), and mandated the creation of a Child Sex Offenders Registry. The registry's operational status has not been consistently maintained in practice – a significant gap in child protection infrastructure.[^789]

**Maldives National University (2011)** and the development of Bachelor of Social Work degree programmes created a pathway to degree-level professional qualification that had not previously existed. The BSW programme at MNU represents the long-term foundation for a professionalised child protection workforce – but graduates are slow to enter a system that does not consistently offer competitive salaries, adequate supervision, or manageable caseloads.[^790]

**The Sexual Offences Act 2014** created the first standalone criminalisation of rape and sexual assault – including child sexual abuse – as distinct offences with specific penalties. Its Section 52 five-evidence requirement was, however, a major setback for child protection: the evidentiary threshold effectively made prosecution of most child sexual abuse cases almost impossible, since abuse of children rarely produces multiple independent categories of physical evidence.[^791]

**The Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD, 2016)** was the most operationally significant child protection infrastructure development of this period. For the first time, CFPS had a digital case management system connecting all 19 FCSCs (later 20), enabling national-level data on case volume, type, and distribution. The MCPD enabled the statistics that appear throughout this chapter and in the Statistics Compendium – data that previously simply did not exist.[^792]

**The IBAMA community model (2016, revived as "You and I Together")** extended the CFPS reach into island communities through structured volunteer engagement. The revival in 2016 – building on the original 1999 Addu Atoll pilot – represented UNICEF and MoSFD's acknowledgement that professional services alone could not reach the 189 inhabited islands, and that community capacity was a necessary complement to professional intervention.[^793]

**The 2016-17 Demographic and Health Survey** included – for the first time in a DHS – questions about FGM/C prevalence. The survey found an FGM/C prevalence of 13% of women aged 15-49 in the Maldives – a figure that, while contested by community members who argued the survey question was misunderstood, established at minimum that the practice warranted systematic attention.[^794]

### 4.51.5 The CRPA Era (2019-Present): Framework Consolidation

The **Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (CRPA, Act No. 19/2019)** represents the most comprehensive legislative consolidation of child protection in Maldivian history. Its significance lies not only in its substantive provisions but in its architecture: the Act brings together definitions, mandatory reporting obligations, judicial orders, the children's helpline, the Children's Ombudsperson, and the obligation to provide services – into a single, integrated framework that replaced the patchwork of earlier legislation.[^795]

The **Juvenile Justice Act 2019 (Act No. 18/2019)** completed the legislative reform of child protection by addressing the juvenile justice dimension that the CRPA did not cover. The JJ Act raised the minimum age of criminal responsibility, established the Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ), created a diversion programme, and prohibited the death penalty for juveniles – addressing the scandalous situation in which five children had been sentenced to death between 2013 and 2015.[^796]

The **Children's Ombudsperson's Office**, established in 2020 under the CRPA, provides – in principle – independent oversight and accountability for the child protection system. The Ombudsperson investigates complaints, monitors care facilities, advocates for systemic reform, and reports publicly on child rights. This represents a structural advance over the pre-2019 system, in which there was no independent oversight mechanism. However, the Ombudsperson's office requires adequate resourcing and independence to function as intended.[^797]

The **COVID-19 pandemic (2020-2022)** provided a stress test for the child protection system that exposed its structural vulnerabilities. School closures removed the most important early identification mechanism for child abuse – daily contact with teachers. Lockdowns trapped children in homes with abusive family members. The economic crisis increased household stress. The FPA-CFPS-Police coordination mechanisms were tested by surge demand. The 1412 helpline saw increased call volumes. UNICEF activated GBV sub-cluster coordination, but the operational response was constrained by the same workforce and geographic barriers that constrain everyday child protection.[^798]

The **Multi-Disciplinary Team (MDT) protocol (2022)** – piloted in Malé and three atolls – represents the most significant coordination reform of the post-CRPA period. MDT case conferences bring together all relevant professionals for complex cases, sharing information and agreeing coordinated plans. The pilot's positive evaluation has led to the NAPVAC 2024-2028 targeting national roll-out – a target whose achievement will depend on the same workforce capacity constraints that limit every other ambition in the system.[^799]

The **Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) Policy Press chapter** – the most detailed academic analysis of Maldivian child protection system development – documents the trajectory from the 1991 system beginnings to the 2024 present with a critical analytical lens. Their central observation: that the Maldivian system has consistently produced frameworks ahead of the operational capacity to implement them, and that this gap is not incidental but structural – reflecting political preferences for legislative action over investment in the workforce and infrastructure that legislation requires.[^800]

---


## 4.3 Historical Timeline: A Complete Chronology


### 4.91.1 Pre-Modern Period: Kinship and Islamic Welfare

Before the modern child protection system, child welfare in the Maldives was governed entirely by two interlocking systems: Islamic welfare obligations and kinship networks. The archipelago's geography – 200+ inhabited islands across 90,000 square kilometres – meant that each island community was largely self-sufficient in its social welfare arrangements.

**Islamic welfare foundations:** Zakat (obligatory almsgiving) provided support to orphans, the poor, and others in need. The kadheeb (island chief), appointed under the sultanate system, served as the local administrator responsible for ensuring community welfare. Child welfare questions – custody disputes, orphan care, support for children whose parents had died – were resolved through Islamic jurisprudence (*fiqh*) and customary practice.

**Kinship care as default:** When parents could not care for children, extended family – grandmothers, aunts, uncles – provided care without any formal legal or institutional involvement. This system was robust enough that institutional child care was essentially non-existent until the late 20th century. The resilience of kinship care networks remains a strength of the Maldivian alternative care system, though its adequacy cannot be taken for granted in an increasingly urbanised, mobile society with elevated rates of family breakdown.

**The Islaahiya exception:** In 1979, the establishment of Islaahiya – the large reformatory school for boys with behavioural issues in Male Atoll – marked the first institutional care facility in the Maldives, and it served a reformatory rather than welfare function. The institution existed for 34 years (1979–2013) and remained one of the largest alternative care providers throughout that period.[^924]

### 4.91.2 The CRC Era Begins (1991–2003)

**1991:** UNCRC ratification (11 February). Law on the Protection of the Rights of the Child (Law 9/91) enacted – the Maldives' first child rights legislation. Largely declaratory; no enforcement mechanisms, no abuse definitions, no mandatory reporting.

**1992:** Unit for the Rights of Children (URC) established within Ministry of Home Affairs. Volunteer counsellors provided 2-week training courses. National Council for Children's Rights established (chaired by Minister of Home Affairs).

**1993:** First CRC reporting cycle. Growing recognition of gender inequality; URC moved to Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare.

**1996:** Second CRC reporting cycle. Drafting of a comprehensive Child Rights Protection Act begins with support from Defence for Children International and UNICEF.

**1997:** Juvenile Court established – a specialist court for children in conflict with the law, though the broader juvenile justice framework remains inadequate until 2019.

**1998:** Maldives College of Higher Education (MCHE) established.

**2001:** Repeal of mandatory reporting provisions in the Penal Code Law 1/66, creating a gap in the legal framework for reporting child abuse that persists until CRPA 2019.

**Late 1990s–2003:** Drug use emerges as a significant social problem, particularly among youth in Malé. Government response initially punitive. In 2002–2003, the first National Drug Use Survey reveals scale of substance abuse.

### 4.91.3 Post-Tsunami Acceleration (2004–2008)

**2004 (December):** Indian Ocean Tsunami devastates the Maldives. 82 confirmed dead, 26 missing. 14 islands permanently evacuated. Economic damage equivalent to 62% of GDP. UNICEF significantly expands its Maldivian programme, creating a dedicated child protection component.

**2004:** Kudakudhinge Hiya established to provide state care for children (0–9 years) from families affected by drug abuse – the first purpose-built child welfare institution (as distinct from Islaahiya's reformatory function).

**2005–2006:** Post-tsunami reconstruction drives institutional development. UNICEF and VSO provide technical support for developing policies and procedures. Groups of school leavers receive training at Ministry and at University of Kelaniya (Sri Lanka) – 1-year Advanced Certificate courses.

**2006 (11 May):** Aman Hiya opened – originally for domestic violence victims, now accommodates girls aged 9–18.

**2006:** Child protection procedure manual developed, drawing on England's *Working Together* guidance. Cultural tensions arise around imported definitions of abuse conflicting with local religious discipline norms.

**2006:** Fulu's *Maldives Study on Women's Health and Life Experiences* published – first national DV survey, finding 12.2% childhood sexual abuse prevalence (16.3% in Malé).

**2007:** Introduction of 1-year Advanced Diploma in Social Service Work (later replaced by 4-year undergraduate Social Work degree).

**2008 (First democratic election):** Mohamed Nasheed/MDP wins. Child protection moved to Ministry of Health under Department of Gender and Family Protection Services – framing shifts toward rights-based health approach.

**2008:** UNICEF Maldives supports government in drafting Juvenile Justice Act (not enacted until 2019).

### 4.91.4 Democratic Transition and Policy Reform (2008–2019)

**2009:** Baby death case – baby shaken to death by mother's boyfriend; mother had been known to child protection for 7 years. Death inquiry recommends formal supervisory system, accountability mechanisms. Barnardos (English charity) engaged for advice on alternative care institutions.

**2009:** Correctional and Training Centre for Children (CTCC) established on Feydhoo Finolhu island under presidential decree, run by Maldives Police Service. Closed 2013 for violating Article 35 UNCRC (children held arbitrarily, deprived of liberty).

**2009:** National Childline 1412 established with Child Helpline International assistance.

**2009:** Special Provisions Act to Deal with Child Sex Abuse Offenders (Law 12/2009) enacted – 7–14 year sentences, public offender disclosure.

**2010:** Decentralisation Act 7/2010 – elected local councils; CFPS centralised; FCSCs across 20 atolls.

**2010:** Community Social Groups (CSGs) established – later revived as IBAMA.

**2012:** Foster care panel established.

**2012:** Domestic Violence Prevention Act (Act 3/2012) enacted – first DV-specific law; DV made a criminal offence; protection orders established.

**2013:** Islaahiya closed. The 1979–2013 reformatory school that housed boys for "behavioural issues" ends operations.

**2013–2015:** Five juveniles sentenced to death under the pre-reform legal framework. Sentences ultimately not carried out.

**2014:** Sexual Offences Act (Act 17/2014) – strengthened criminal law protections against CSA; child pornography, child prostitution, aggravated CSA as specific offences.

**2015:** Child protection focal points established in schools (Ministry of Education) – but without referral systems, training, or police checks.

**2016:** Family Law Regulation establishes 18 as minimum age of marriage (with judicial exception).

**2016:** Gender Equality Act (Act 18/2016) enacted.

**2016 (with Qatar Foundation support):** Fiyavathi established – cares for 80 children under 13.

### 4.91.5 The CRPA Era (2019–Present)

**2019:** Child Rights Protection Act (Law No. 18/2019) enacted – the most comprehensive child rights legislation enacted in the Maldives. Operational from 2020.

**2019:** Juvenile Justice Act (Law No. 19/2019) enacted – comprehensive juvenile justice reform.

**2019:** Alternative Care Regulation (R.No.2020-R-69) enacted.

**2020:** COVID-19 pandemic. Children in Kudakudhinge Hiya and Aman Hiya distributed to 9 smaller atoll-based institutions (18–24 children each). Regulation on Reintegration (R.No.2020-R-30) enacted – emphasis on long-term planning, kinship care, family settings.

**2020:** Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ) operationalised.

**2020:** National Strategic Action Plan for Child Protection (NSAP-CP 2020–2024) adopted.

**2021:** Children's Ombudsperson office becomes operational. Sexual Offences (Amendment) Act – marital rape criminalised.

**2021:** UNICEF drafts Standard Operating Procedures for collaborative safeguarding, presented to National Council for Protection of Children's Rights (January 2021).

**2021:** Education Act strengthens CP provisions in schools.

**2022:** MDT (Multi-Disciplinary Team) Protocol for complex child abuse cases established.

**2022:** Child Rights Symposium co-hosted by Children's Ombudsperson and UNICEF – first major post-CRPA public review.

**2023:** 187 children in alternative care (82 girls, 105 boys). 18 registered foster carers caring for 16 children.

**2024 (planned 2025–2026):** National Child Protection Strategy 2025–2030 under development. Digital case management system piloted in selected FCSCs; national rollout planned by 2026. Foster Care Regulation drafted (2023), expected finalisation 2025.[^925]

---


# Part 2: Legal Framework


## 4.4 Legal Framework: From Law 9/91 to CRPA 2019


### 4.2.1 The Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (Act 19/2019)

The **Child Rights Protection Act (CRPA)**, enacted in 2019, represents the most comprehensive piece of child protection legislation in Maldivian history. The Act superseded Law 9/91 and established for the first time a detailed statutory framework for child protection that included definitions of maltreatment, mandatory reporting obligations, judicial orders for child protection, and the creation of a Children's Ombudsperson. The CRPA was the product of years of legislative development, international technical assistance (particularly from UNICEF), and advocacy by civil society organisations and child protection professionals.[^20]

Key provisions of the CRPA include:

- **Comprehensive definitions of child maltreatment:** The CRPA defines multiple forms of abuse – physical, sexual, emotional, and neglect – providing for the first time a statutory framework against which child protection concerns can be assessed and adjudicated.

- **Mandatory reporting:** The Act establishes a duty on specified professionals and, more broadly, on all persons to report known or suspected child maltreatment. This represents a significant shift from the voluntary, informal reporting mechanisms that previously prevailed.

- **Judicial orders for child protection:** The CRPA introduces three categories of court orders:
  - **Interim Assessment Order (Article 80):** Authorises the temporary placement of a child while an assessment is conducted, providing legal authority for emergency intervention.
  - **Protection Order (Article 90):** A longer-term order placing conditions on the care of a child, potentially including removal from the family home.
  - **Supervision Order (Article 93):** An order requiring the child to remain under the supervision of child protection services while remaining in the family or community, enabling monitoring without removal.

- **Five-day statutory timeline:** Orders must be applied for within five days of taking a child into state care, providing a safeguard against prolonged informal removal without judicial oversight.

- **Children's Ombudsperson:** The Act creates the office of the Children's Ombudsperson as an independent oversight body with authority to investigate complaints, monitor the child protection system, and advocate for children's rights. The Children's Ombudsperson co-hosted, with UNICEF, the Child Rights Symposium 2022, providing a platform for review of implementation progress.[^21]

- **Prohibition of child labour:** The CRPA prohibits the employment of children below the age of 16, aligning with the Education Act 2020's requirement for compulsory education until age 16.

- **Raising the marriage age:** The CRPA establishes 18 as the minimum age for marriage, though the critical inconsistency with Section 4(b) of the Family Act remains unresolved (see Section 4.13 below).

### 4.2.2 The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 (Act 18/2019)

The **Juvenile Justice Act (JJ Act)**, also enacted in 2019, complements the CRPA by establishing a separate statutory framework for children in conflict with the law. The JJ Act is explicitly oriented toward rehabilitation over retribution, reflecting international juvenile justice standards and a recognition that the existing system – which had permitted the death penalty for juveniles and lacked separate detention facilities – was fundamentally inadequate.[^22]

Key provisions include:

- **Age of criminal responsibility raised to 15:** Prior to the JJ Act, the age of criminal responsibility was 10, meaning children as young as 10 could be prosecuted and sentenced through the criminal justice system. The JJ Act raised this threshold to 15, bringing the Maldives closer to international standards.
- **Prohibition of the death penalty for juveniles:** Between 2013 and 2015, five juveniles were sentenced to death in the Maldives. The JJ Act explicitly prohibits capital punishment for offences committed by persons under 18.
- **Rehabilitation emphasis:** The Act mandates that rehabilitation, education, and reintegration be the primary objectives of the juvenile justice system.
- **Flogging not abolished:** Notably, the JJ Act did not abolish judicial flogging for juveniles, leaving open the possibility that children convicted of certain offences – particularly *zina* (sexual intercourse outside marriage) – could be subjected to corporal punishment by court order.[^23]

### 4.2.3 Related Legislation

Several other laws form part of the broader child protection legal architecture:

- **Special Measures for Perpetrators of Child Sexual Abuse (Act 12/2009):** Established enhanced penalties and monitoring requirements for child sex offenders.
- **Education Act 2020 (Act 24/2020):** Introduced compulsory education until age 16 and banned corporal punishment in schools.
- **Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 (Act 3/2012):** Relevant to children who witness or are directly affected by domestic violence.
- **Sexual Offences Act 2014 (Act 17/2014):** Addresses sexual violence including offences against children.
- **Prevention of Human Trafficking Act 2013 (Act 12/2013):** Relevant to child trafficking and exploitation.
- **Alternative Care Regulation 2020-R-69:** Governs the placement of children in foster care, kinship care, and residential care.
- **Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30:** Addresses the reintegration of children from alternative care settings back into family and community life.[^24]

### 4.2.4 International Treaty Obligations and Reporting

The Maldives submitted its **Combined 6th and 7th Periodic Report** to the Committee on the Rights of the Child in October 2021, providing the first comprehensive government account of CRC implementation since the democratic transition. The report was supplemented by information from the UNICEF Situation Analysis of 2021 (updated 2025) and the Child-Equity-Census-2022 analysis, which provided disaggregated data on child wellbeing indicators across atolls and demographic groups.[^25]

An **NGO Shadow Report on CRC** was submitted in 2006 by the Hama Jamiyya (Maldives NGO) and Care Society, providing an independent assessment of the state of children's rights. This Shadow Report documented significant gaps between the government's CRC reporting and the lived realities of children, particularly in areas of child abuse, juvenile justice, and children's participation in decision-making.[^26]

The **"Children in Maldives" Census 2014 analysis** provided the first detailed demographic profile of the child population, disaggregated by age, sex, atoll, and household composition. Key findings included that 74% of children aged 0–17 lived with both parents, while 22% lived with one parent – a figure that carries significant implications for child vulnerability given the Maldives' high divorce rate and the documented correlation between single-parent households and economic stress.[^27]

---


## 4.5 Comprehensive Analysis of Child Protection Laws


### 4.19.1 Constitutional Foundations

The **Constitution of the Republic of Maldives (2008)** provides the foundational basis for children's rights and state obligations toward the child population. Two articles are of direct relevance to child protection practice.

**Article 35** sets out a three-part children's rights guarantee:
- (a) Every child has the right to be protected from abuse and maltreatment, including all forms of physical and mental violence, and from any action that adversely affects the child's physical, emotional or mental health or development.
- (b) Every child has the right to education, social security, and care and assistance from the state.
- (c) Every child has the right to a name and nationality from birth.

The article further obligates the state to provide special protection to children without families. This constitutional provision is significant because it establishes the state's protective obligation as a matter of fundamental rights – not merely policy preference – and it encompasses all children regardless of birth status, nationality of parents, or social circumstance.[^585]

**Article 10** creates the fundamental constitutional tension that runs through all child protection law: it declares Islam as the state religion and provides that "no law contrary to any tenet of Islam shall be enacted in the Maldives." This provision has direct practical consequences. It shapes the treatment of child victims of sexual violence who become pregnant; it informs the debate on child marriage (where Islamic jurisprudence identifies puberty rather than chronological age as the threshold of adulthood); it affects the legal position of children born outside marriage; and it creates the tension between Islamic corporal punishment principles (*hudud*) and child rights standards on corporal punishment. The CRPA 2019 operates within this constitutional framework, not above it, and practitioners must navigate both sets of obligations simultaneously.[^586]

### 4.19.2 The Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (Act No. 19/2019): Legislative History and Architecture

The CRPA is the primary statute of Maldivian child protection law, replacing the earlier Law on the Protection of the Rights of the Child (Law 9/91), which was widely criticised as declaratory and lacking enforcement mechanisms. Its passage on 14 November 2019 – alongside the Juvenile Justice Act on the same day – represented the most significant legislative development in Maldivian child protection.[^587]

**Legislative history:** The CRPA was more than a decade in development. Initial drafting was supported by UNICEF beginning in 2006, following the 2004 Indian Ocean tsunami, which catalysed expanded child protection programming and created political conditions for legislative reform. Multiple iterations were debated in the People's Majlis. International advocacy from UNICEF, combined with growing domestic concern about high-profile child abuse cases and advocacy from youth pressure groups, eventually created sufficient political will for passage. The drafting process involved collaboration with Defence for Children International, drawing on comparative child rights legislation from across the Asia-Pacific region, with significant reference to England's Children Act framework – a choice that has been both a strength (bringing international best practice) and a weakness (creating provisions that assume service capacity that does not exist in the Maldivian context). Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note that the system has "absorbed the system of England which is a needs-based system" through UNICEF-facilitated technical support, despite the UN's own rights-based approach.[^588]

**The Act's scope:** The CRPA is comprehensive in intent, covering the full range of child rights and child protection functions: the right to protection from violence in all forms; the right to family care and alternative care; the right to education, health, and social security; mandatory reporting obligations; case management procedures; judicial orders for intervention; the institutional framework (CFPS, FCSCs, Children's Ombudsperson, National Council for Child Rights Protection); and the framework for community-level child protection (IBAMA groups).[^589]

### 4.19.3 The CRPA's Key Provisions: Operational Analysis

**Age definition (Article 4):** The Act formally defines a child as any person who has not attained the age of 18 years, bringing Maldivian law into alignment with Article 1 of the CRC and superseding older provisions in earlier legislation that used varying age thresholds. This unambiguous definition is significant in a context where the Family Act's marriage provisions continue to use puberty as a threshold, and where Islamic jurisprudence traditionally defines childhood in relation to physical maturity rather than chronological age.[^590]

**Abuse definitions (Article 133):** The CRPA introduced specific operational definitions of child abuse aligned with the CRC:
- *Exploitation* (Article 133(g)): "all inhumane acts which will have a negative impact on the physical and mental well-being and growth of the Child, for any kind of benefit"
- *Negligence* (Article 133(h)): "all those acts or omissions of the parents or guardians or persons under whose care the Child is, which could or would cause physical or mental harm to the Child"
- *Physical Violence* (Article 133(n)): detailed definition including lacerations, bruises, cuts, scars, fractures or other damage to bones, dislocation of joints, twists, bleeding, rupture of blood vessels, burns, blisters, a stop or change in physiological functioning or consciousness, and damage to hair or teeth
- *Sexual Abuse* (Article 133(o)): "any sexual act towards, against or exhibited to a Child, whether by a person in a family relationship with the Child or otherwise"

These definitions represent a significant advance over pre-2019 practice, when child protection workers operated with no statutory definitional framework. However, the definitions have been critiqued on several grounds. The sexual abuse definition does not explicitly address power dynamics or the child's developmental incapacity to provide informed consent. This creates ambiguity in cases involving older adolescents who have reached puberty, where the distinction between consensual *zina* (extramarital sexual activity, a *hudud* offence under Islamic law) and child sexual abuse may not be clear to investigators, prosecutors, or courts applying Islamic legal principles. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) identify this as a "grey area" in which child victims risk re-victimisation within the system that should be protecting them.[^591]

**Best interests principle (Article 6):** The Act codifies the best interests of the child as a primary consideration in all decisions affecting children. Courts, government agencies, institutions, and private entities are all required to apply this standard. However, the operationalisation of best interests determinations in practice – particularly in outer island settings where decisions may be made by local officials without specialist training – remains a challenge. The government has developed best interests assessment tools for FCSCs, but consistent application across all 24 FCSCs is not yet achieved.[^592]

**Children's participation rights (Article 7):** The Act includes provisions requiring that the views of children be considered in all decisions affecting them, commensurate with their age and maturity. In judicial proceedings, the Juvenile Court has developed child-friendly hearing protocols. In child protection cases, CFPS protocols require social workers to consult children directly as part of assessment. Consistent implementation across all FCSCs remains a challenge.[^593]

**Children's responsibilities (Article 36):** In an unusual provision with significant cultural context, the CRPA includes "Responsibilities and duties of a child" (Article 36 a-h) covering obligations toward parents, community, and the state. This provision was included in direct response to community concern that a rights framework without corresponding duties would undermine parental authority. As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document, the historical expectation in Maldivian society that "children must be seen but not heard" created a cultural backdrop against which the introduction of children's rights was experienced by some communities as disruptive. The cultural position that parents were permitted – indeed expected – to physically discipline children, including hitting or slapping for misbehaviour and beating children aged 7 and older who refused to recite the Qur'an, created resistance to the English-derived procedure manual's characterisation of such practices as abuse. The responsibilities provision was the legislative compromise enabling the Act's passage without triggering conservative backlash.[^594]

**Institutional framework provisions:** The CRPA established: the National Council for the Protection of the Rights of the Child (NCPRC); the Children's Ombudsperson (office operational 2021); legal recognition for IBAMA community protection groups; the National Child Protection Database (MCPD); and statutory basis for the Child Helpline 1412 (Article 76).[^595]

**Judicial orders for child protection (Articles 80, 90, 93):** Three types of judicial orders:
- *Interim Assessment Order (Article 80):* Can be obtained where an offence has been committed and investigation is required without parental consent. Both CFPS and police can apply.
- *Protection Order (Article 90):* A temporary order removing a child from their current situation and placing them with a state-approved person. Notably, the articles do not use the language "at risk of significant harm" central to English child protection frameworks – reflecting the ongoing contextualisation of the English-model framework to the Maldivian legal context.
- *Supervision Order (Article 93):* Allows CFPS or police to monitor a child while they remain with their parents daily. Parents have the right to appeal.

Orders must be applied for within five days of taking a child into state care. When the court requires assessments before making an order, this task falls to CFPS workers, adding to already critical caseloads.[^596]

**A critical gap – corporal punishment:** Despite widespread assumption (including in some government documents), the CRPA does *not* explicitly prohibit corporal punishment of children in all settings. The UNFPA's 2023 comprehensive legal analysis confirmed that neither the CRPA, the Domestic Violence Prevention Act, the Family Act, nor the Education Act contains an explicit prohibition on corporal punishment in all settings. Corporal punishment in schools has been prohibited administratively, reinforced by the Education Act 2020. However, corporal punishment remains lawful in the home, in alternative care settings, and as a judicial sentence (*hadd*) for children who have reached puberty. This gap has been the subject of repeated CRC Concluding Observations recommendations that remain unimplemented.[^597]

**Marriage age inconsistency:** While the CRPA raised the legal marriage age to 18, the Family Act's Section 4(b) loophole allows the Family Court to authorise marriage below 18 if the child has "reached puberty." This inconsistency has not been formally resolved. Conservative religious authorities (*fatwas*) have endorsed unregistered marriages, identified by the Family Court Chief Judge as a "huge challenge." The ADB (2025) documents a 2.2% child marriage rate before age 18, reportedly increasing rather than decreasing.[^598]

**Mandatory reporting (Article 126):** Failure to report known instances of child abuse is a criminal offence punishable by two years' imprisonment. Failure to report for financial gain attracts five years' imprisonment. Good-faith reporters are protected from civil or criminal liability. This reintroduced mandatory reporting following a gap since the repeal of Chapter 3, Section 72 of the Penal Code Law 1/66 in 2001.[^599a]

### 4.19.4 Special Provisions Act 2009 (Act No. 12/2009): Child Sexual Abuse Offenders

The Special Provisions Act to Deal with Child Sex Abuse Offenders (Act 12/2009) predates the CRPA and addresses the legal treatment of persons convicted of child sexual abuse. Key provisions:

- Imprisonment terms of **10–25 years without parole** for child sexual abuse offences – significantly heavier than general Penal Code sentences
- Mandated **Child Sex Offenders Registry** with public disclosure of convicted offenders' identities
- **Victim compensation** provisions
- Applies to offences against children under 18

A critical tension: For children aged 13 and above who are victims of sexual exploitation, the intersection of this Act with *zina* provisions in the Penal Code creates the risk that a child victim – particularly one who has reached puberty – may be characterised as a *zina* participant rather than an abuse victim. The Act's definition of child victims of sexual exploitation aged 13 or above can overlap with *zina* provisions, creating the risk that child victims of sexual exploitation may be prosecuted for consensual sex rather than treated as victims. The influence of "honour ideology," as Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) term it, exacerbates these challenges and creates a risk of re-victimisation for child victims within the child protection system.[^599]

### 4.19.5 The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 (Act No. 18/2019): Structural Overview

Key provisions analysed in depth in §4.27. The Act:
- Sets **minimum age of criminal responsibility at 10 years** (with full criminal responsibility at 15); below the CRC Committee's General Comment No. 24 (2019) recommended minimum of at least 14
- Explicitly **prioritises rehabilitation** over retribution, establishing diversion programmes and community-based alternatives to detention
- **Prohibits the death penalty** and life imprisonment for child offenders – addressing the critical gap under which five children received death sentences between 2013 and 2015
- Establishes the **Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ)** and the **Juvenile Court** as dedicated institutions
- Creates a formal **diversion programme** for first-time and minor offenders
- Does **NOT abolish flogging** – *hadd* corporal punishment for children who have reached puberty is not abolished, remaining one of the Act's most significant gaps[^600]

### 4.19.6 The Family Act (Law No. 4/2000): Child Protection Dimensions

**Section 4(b) – child marriage loophole:** Allows the Family Court to authorise marriage below 18 upon "reaching puberty" – directly undercutting the CRPA's age-18 standard. Conservative religious actors have exploited this loophole for both registered (judicial exception) and unregistered (religious ceremony without court registration) child marriages.[^601]

**Section 42 – the custody/remarriage trap:** A mother loses priority custody if she marries a man who is not a *mahram* of the children, or relocates to a different island without the father's consent. This provision converts custody law into a mechanism for post-separation coercive control and is documented as a significant feature of post-divorce abuse. Analysis of 24 High Court custody cases found that in every instance where the mother was awarded custody, she had remained unmarried.[^602]

**Maintenance enforcement gap:** The Act requires maintenance orders but lacks effective enforcement mechanisms. Approximately MVR 2 million accumulated unclaimed at Family Court as of the vulnerability mapping research period. Children going without court-ordered maintenance for years is a documented child poverty driver.[^603]

### 4.19.7 The Education Act 2021

Child protection provisions: prohibition of corporal punishment in schools; school-based child protection committees; positive discipline requirements (40% of teachers reached by 2023); compulsory education ages 6–16. Critical gap: despite the Act's provisions, there are no established referral systems, case conferences, or consistent review meetings between schools and CFPS. Police checks and comprehensive CP training for all school staff remain absent.[^604]

### 4.19.8 Anti-Human Trafficking Act 2013 (Act No. 12/2013)

Child trafficking is the most serious tier of trafficking offence, carrying enhanced penalties. A National Referral Mechanism was established but operates primarily in Malé. Updated regulations (2022) strengthened victim identification protocols and inter-agency coordination, with UNODC technical assistance for investigation training.[^605]

### 4.19.9 The Penal Code 2014: Child Protection Intersections

**Section 411(d) – *zina* and flogging:** Provides for 100 lashes for extramarital sexual intercourse. Approximately 85% of flogging sentences are against women and girls. A girl raped and impregnated has produced, through the crime committed against her, the primary evidence of an offence for which she herself may be prosecuted – unless she can meet the extremely high evidentiary standards for proving rape under the SOA.[^606]

**Section 130(b) – marital presumption of consent:** Maintains a rebuttable presumption of consent within marriage. In cases of child marriage facilitated by the Family Act Section 4(b) loophole, this provision creates a framework in which a child bride's sexual exploitation exists in legal ambiguity.[^607]

---


## 4.6 Definitions of Child Maltreatment Under Maldivian Law


The CRPA 2019 provides the first statutory definitions of child maltreatment in Maldivian law. Prior to the CRPA, the absence of definitions meant that identification, reporting, and adjudication of child protection concerns operated without a consistent legal framework – leaving decisions about what constituted abuse or neglect to the informal judgment of individual practitioners and community members.

The CRPA recognises the following categories:

- **Physical abuse:** Acts causing physical harm to a child, including hitting, shaking, burning, or any other act of physical violence.
- **Sexual abuse:** Any sexual act involving a child, including penetrative and non-penetrative contact, exposure to sexual material, and sexual exploitation.
- **Emotional abuse:** Persistent emotional ill-treatment of a child causing severe and adverse effects on the child's emotional development, including rejection, humiliation, threats, and isolation.
- **Neglect:** The persistent failure to meet a child's basic physical and psychological needs, including failure to provide adequate food, shelter, clothing, supervision, medical care, or protection from harm.
- **Exploitation:** The use of a child for labour, commercial sexual purposes, or other exploitative activities.[^28]

The operational challenge lies in applying these definitions within the Maldivian social context. Practices such as sending children to live with relatives in Malé for educational purposes – a widespread and culturally normalised practice – can, in some cases, constitute a form of exploitation where the child is expected to perform domestic labour in exchange for accommodation. The line between normative kinship-based caregiving arrangements and exploitative child labour is culturally contested and operationally difficult to assess.[^29]

---


## 4.7 Child Maltreatment: Definitions, Cultural Tensions, and Operational Challenges


### 4.21.1 Pre-CRPA Definitional Vacuum

Prior to the CRPA 2019, Maldivian child protection practice operated without statutory definitions of child abuse or neglect. In 2006, a procedure manual based on England's "Working Together to Safeguard Children" guidance was developed for frontline workers, introducing operational definitions from English child protection practice.[^628]

The English-derived definitions created immediate cultural tensions in the Maldivian context. The prevailing cultural expectation was that "children must be seen but not heard," and parents were permitted – indeed expected – to physically discipline children, including hitting or slapping for misbehaviour and beating children aged 7 and older who refused to recite the Qur'an. These practices reflected both cultural norms and Islamic teaching as locally interpreted. The procedure manual's characterisation of these practices as physical abuse created confusion: social workers, particularly those from rural islands for whom these practices were entirely normal, often disregarded the guidance in the manual altogether.[^629]

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document the unintended consequence: the debate about parental authority versus child rights that the procedure manual catalysed eventually led to the inclusion of "Responsibilities and duties of a child" (Article 36 a-h) in the CRPA 2019. The children's responsibilities provision was not a philosophical statement about duties, but a politically necessary concession enabling the Act's passage without triggering religious and conservative backlash.[^630]

### 4.21.2 The CRPA's Definitional Contribution and Remaining Gaps

The CRPA Article 133 definitions represent genuine progress. The exploitation, negligence, and physical violence definitions are specific enough to guide frontline assessment. The detailed physical violence definition provides practitioners with clear thresholds distinguishing discipline from abuse in the most serious cases.

**Remaining gaps in the sexual abuse definition:**
1. **Power differential:** Article 133(o) covers "any sexual act towards, against or exhibited to a Child," but does not explicitly address situations where a child nominally "participates" in a sexual act in which fundamental power imbalance prevents genuine consent
2. **Developmental incapacity for informed consent:** Older adolescents who have reached puberty may be legally or culturally treated as capable of consenting to sexual activity in ways that obscure the child protection dimension

These gaps are not theoretical. As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) illustrate through case examples, the grey area between *zina* and CSA creates real cases in which the child protection system must determine whether a pregnant 14-year-old is a victim of abuse or a participant in *zina* – with materially different consequences for the child.[^631]

**Limited accessibility of definitions:** The Article 133 definitions are not systematically disseminated to professionals in education, health, and the judiciary outside the trained CFPS social work cadre. A school principal, nurse, or magistrate applying their individual interpretation may reach very different conclusions from a trained CFPS social worker.[^632]

### 4.21.3 Religious-Cultural Tensions in Definition and Application

**Corporal punishment in religious education:** Physical discipline during Qur'an recitation lessons remains culturally embedded in some communities, particularly in outer islands, and occurs in settings outside the Education Act's school prohibition.

**Reproductive rights tension:** Social workers and doctors in the Maldives are often reluctant to recommend termination of pregnancy within the legally permitted 120-day window in cases where a pregnant adolescent is an abuse victim. As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document explicitly, child protection professionals and doctors are "reluctant to proceed with the abortion that is allowed within 120 days... Like many Muslim states, abortion is considered a criminal offence unless the mother's health is threatened by the pregnancy." This reluctance produces situations in which child victims of sexual abuse are forced to carry pregnancies to term, compounding the original abuse with an ongoing denial of reproductive rights.[^633]

**Ministry of Islamic Affairs consultation:** In complex cases where religious values and the formal child protection system conflict, the Ministry of Islamic Affairs is consulted for interpretations of Islamic law, ensuring decisions align with both the CRPA 2019 and an Islamic framework. This creates a dual authority structure that can introduce delays and – in extreme cases – outcomes that prioritise religious legitimacy over child welfare.[^634]

---


## 4.8 Islamic Law and Child Protection: Tensions, Accommodations, and Practice Realities


### 4.61.1 The Dual Legal Architecture

The Maldivian child protection system operates within a dual legal architecture: English-influenced statutory child protection procedures on one hand, and Shari'ah law on the other. The legal system is based on Shari'ah and English common law; family law and criminal law are informed by Shari'ah, while child protection procedures are primarily derived from the English needs-based model. Article 10 of the Constitution establishes Islam as the state religion, and the child protection legislation (CRPA 2019) is drafted to be compliant with both the UNCRC and Islamic principles.

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) describe this dual architecture not as a stable synthesis but as a source of ongoing tension that must be navigated case by case by practitioners. The harmonisation of legislation is not enough for concepts to be applied in practice; practitioners must continuously balance the stipulations of the CRPA, Islamic law as they understand it, and the principles of professional social work. This is a complex balancing act that requires ongoing professional development – and which has historically been compromised by inadequate training and supervision.[^860]

### 4.61.2 The UNCRC Reservations

Maldives ratified the UNCRC on 11 February 1991, but did so with formal reservations to:
- **Article 14(1):** Freedom of thought, conscience and religion – reserved to the extent inconsistent with Islam, which is the state religion under the Constitution
- **Article 21:** Adoption – reserved because formal adoption is incompatible with Islamic law, which does not recognise adoption in the Western sense (the Islamic alternative is *kafala*)
- **Partial reservation to Article 9:** Separation from parents – applicable only to the extent consistent with Islamic law and national family law

In 2007, a white paper was prepared to remove the reservation on Article 21 (adoption). The attempt failed and the reservation remains in force. In 2023, media reports suggested 10 children had been "adopted" – but in reality, what was occurring was long-term fostering (*kafala*) which is practiced on the ground even if not legally formalised as adoption.

These reservations are not merely formal legal declarations; they shape daily practice. Adoption as a permanency option for children in care – widely available in many countries as a route to a stable, legally permanent family – is unavailable in the Maldives. Children cannot be adopted by Maldivian families or by foreigners. The consequence is that permanency for children who cannot be reunified with birth families must be achieved through *kafala* (informal long-term fostering), with all the legal uncertainty and continuity risks that involves.[^861]

### 4.61.3 Hudood Offences and Children

A significant legal tension exists in relation to *hudood* offences – crimes considered to be against God, including murder, adultery, apostasy and armed robbery – which carry mandatory *hadd* punishments including corporal punishment. Under the Juvenile Justice Act (2019), a child who has reached puberty can be charged with a *hudood* offence. The Act prioritises protection of children, emphasising rehabilitation options, and prohibiting the death penalty. However, the Act does **not** abolish flogging, raising protection concerns – particularly for child victims of sexual exploitation aged 13 or above.

A grey area exists in distinguishing between consensual sex (*zina*) and child sexual abuse (CSA). The Special Measures for Perpetrators of Child Sexual Abuse Law (12/2009) lacks a clear definition of CSA. Article 133(o) of the CRPA 2019 further complicates the matter by not addressing the power differential in cases involving child victims unable to give informed consent due to developmental stage. Where a child aged 13 or above is involved in sexual activity that is framed as consensual *zina* rather than CSA, the child rather than the perpetrator may face the consequences of *hudood* law.[^862]

### 4.61.4 The Zina-Pregnancy-Rape Trap

The intersection of *zina* law and sexual violence creates one of the most acute child protection dilemmas in the Maldivian legal system: pregnancy out of wedlock is criminalised, including for children who became pregnant through rape or abuse.

For a child who has reached puberty (as young as 12 or 13) and is raped and becomes pregnant:
- Her pregnancy constitutes evidence of extramarital intercourse (*zina*)
- Under Penal Code Section 411(d), the punishment for *zina* is 100 lashes
- She can be simultaneously a crime victim and a criminal defendant
- Prosecution for *zina* following reported rape does not appear common in practice, but the legal possibility deters reporting: a rape survivor who becomes pregnant may choose silence over the double risk of being believed (and having evidence of her victimisation used against her) or being disbelieved and prosecuted

For child protection professionals, the response to a pregnant child who has been raped involves:
- Assessment of whether to pursue prosecution of the rapist (with the evidential challenges this entails)
- Decision-making about abortion within the 120-day window – a legally available option that professional and religious norms make practically very difficult to access
- If the pregnancy continues: case conferencing for the unborn child
- Post-birth: placement decisions for a baby whose mother is a child, whose father is a perpetrator, and who is born outside marriage

This cluster of issues represents one of the most demanding practice scenarios in any child protection system, anywhere, and it arises specifically from the interaction of Islamic *zina* law with the reality of child sexual abuse.[^863]

### 4.61.5 The Ministry of Islamic Affairs as a Consultative Body

In cases where religious values and the formal child protection system clash, the Ministry of Islamic Affairs is consulted for interpretations of Islamic law to ensure that decisions are in line with the provisions of CRPA 2019 and an Islamic framework. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) describe this as a necessary element of the Maldivian practice context: in a society where religious leaders are highly respected and influential, their interpretations and opinions can significantly impact community attitudes and practices. At its best, this consultative relationship supports practitioners in navigating complex cases with appropriate cultural and religious authority. At its worst, it introduces additional delay and places child protection decisions in the hands of religious authorities whose primary frame of reference may not be child welfare.[^864]

### 4.61.6 Corporal Punishment in the Religious Context

The prohibition of corporal punishment of children – now enshrined in CRPA 2019 Article 38 – sits in tension with historical religious and cultural norms. Prior to the rights-based paradigm introduced through UNCRC ratification, parents were permitted to slap or hit a child who misbehaved, and to beat a child aged 7 or above who refused to recite the Qur'an (Naseem, 2011). The shift from these practices to the legal prohibition of all corporal punishment has been contested.

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document a specific unintended consequence: the 2006 procedure manual based on the English *Working Together* document introduced a definition of physical discipline that classified traditional religious practices as physical abuse. Without accompanying awareness-raising about the relationship between rights and responsibilities, parents and teachers were left without alternative methods of discipline. Many observers reported increased behavioural problems among subsequent generations, leading the drafters of CRPA 2019 to introduce a specific section on "Responsibilities and duties of a child" (Article 36 a to h) as a counterweight. The introduction of CRC principles, while positive in many ways, created a rights-without-responsibilities perception that has complicated the social norming of positive discipline.[^865]

---


# Part 3: Institutional Architecture and Coordination


## 4.9 Institutional Architecture: Who Does What for Children


### 4.20.1 Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD)

The ministry responsible for child protection has changed names with each change of government. Under the Muizzu administration (2023–present): **Ministry of Social and Family Development (MoSFD)**. Previous incarnations: Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services (MoGFSS, Solih 2018–2023); Ministry of Gender and Family (Yameen 2013–2018); Ministry of Health and Family (Waheed 2012–2013). These name changes reflect real shifts in portfolio composition, ministerial emphasis, and institutional culture that affect child protection resource allocation decisions.[^609]

**Core child protection mandate:** Four substantive functions:
1. Child protection and welfare (CFPS, FCSCs)
2. Gender equality and GBV protection (FPA oversight, GEAP coordination)
3. Social welfare for vulnerable groups (elderly, disability)
4. Community-level social service coordination (LGA liaison, IBAMA oversight)

**Budget:** MoSFD total budget grew from MVR 180 million (2015) to MVR 420 million (2024) – a 133% nominal increase. FCSC operational budget: MVR 38 million (2024). DJJ (juvenile rehabilitation): MVR 22 million (2024). Child-protection-specific expenditure is difficult to disaggregate due to integrated budget lines; the ministry is developing child-sensitive budgeting frameworks to enable more precise tracking.[^610]

### 4.20.2 Child and Family Protection Services (CFPS): The Operational Core

CFPS is the specialised operational unit within MoSFD responsible for all child protection casework nationally.
