It is the successor to the Unit for the Rights of the Children (URC), established by the Ministry of Home Affairs in 1992. The URC's initial work was counselling by volunteer counsellors given a two-week introductory course. Over time, the mandate expanded from counselling to statutory child protection intervention – but this expansion occurred without a corresponding growth in professional capacity, creating tensions between the counselling and social control functions of child protection work that persist to this day.[^611]

**Structure:** CFPS is centralised in Malé with specialised teams for assessment, investigation, legal liaison, and case coordination – contrasting with the generalist FCSCs. Complex decisions about child removal, court applications, and inter-agency coordination funnel through Malé even when the child concerned lives on a remote outer island hundreds of kilometres away.[^612]

**Legal powers under CRPA:** Application for Interim Assessment Orders (Article 80), Protection Orders (Article 90), and Supervision Orders (Article 93). Joint investigation with police where criminal conduct is suspected. The Family Court's Family and Child Service section coordinates with CFPS on all cases involving children.[^612a]

**Case management process (CFPS SOPs, 2021):** Referral and intake; initial assessment (24 hours urgent, 72 hours non-urgent); full assessment and investigation (joint with police where criminal); case planning; intervention and service provision; monitoring and review; case closure. The Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD), launched 2016, links all 24 FCSCs with unique case identifiers enabling continuity when families move between islands. A pilot digital case management system was deployed in selected FCSCs in 2023, with national rollout planned for 2026.[^613]

A **Family Assessment Form (FAF)** was introduced in 2020 to standardise initial assessment across all FCSCs – a procedural improvement, though form-filling discipline depends on time that overloaded workers often cannot spare.[^613a]

**The caseload crisis:** As of the most recent data: **61 child protection social workers** serve a child population of **128,208**, yielding an average potential caseload of approximately **160 cases per worker** – more than five times the internationally recommended caseload of 25–30. In practice, workers carry 70–80 active cases each, still more than double the recommended standard.[^614]

Consequences documented by UNICEF (2022) and Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025):
- **Reactive rather than preventive practice:** Workers prioritise immediate crisis response over family support and prevention
- **Shallow assessment:** Insufficient time per case means assessments are often cursory, risk factors may be missed, and the quality of professional judgment is compromised
- **Burnout and turnover:** High caseloads combined with emotional intensity, personal safety risks (including death threats from gang-connected families), limited supervision, and modest remuneration drive high rates of burnout and staff turnover – further depleting an already inadequate workforce
- **Inability to provide sustained follow-up:** Even when cases are identified and initial intervention occurs, the capacity for sustained monitoring and support is severely limited[^615]

The scrutiny of oversight bodies (HRCM and Children's Ombudsperson) compounds the pressure: these organisations impose ambitious monitoring objectives that are challenging to fulfil given staff capacity, leaving social workers in a permanent state of anxiety about accusations of negligence – potentially driving defensive documentation practice over genuine child welfare improvement.

### 4.20.3 Family and Children Service Centres (FCSCs): The Atoll-Level Interface

24 FCSCs are operational as of December 2024 – one in each of the 20 administrative atolls plus dedicated centres in Addu City, Fuvahmulah, and additional urban centres. Each FCSC:
- Receives referrals from all sources (community members, schools, health workers, police, and the 1412 helpline)
- Conducts initial assessments of child protection cases
- Manages cases across all social service functions (not only child protection – also DV, elderly welfare, disability, family disputes)
- Coordinates with local police, schools, health facilities, and atoll-level Child Protection Committees
- Feeds data into the MCPD

The generalist character of FCSCs – handling all social service functions with the same small team – means that specialisation is impossible at the atoll level. Workers who may be the only child protection professional for an entire atoll must simultaneously manage acute child abuse cases, family reconciliation processes, elderly welfare visits, and disability allowance applications.[^616]

**Geographic reality:** An FCSC in an atoll capital may be responsible for 10–15 inhabited islands, some accessible only by boat with journeys of several hours in favourable conditions. During rough weather seasons, travel between islands can be impossible for days or weeks, meaning urgent child protection cases on outer islands may go unattended. The ministry's presence covers only 20 of 189 inhabited islands. For children on the remaining 169 islands, child protection depends entirely on informal community mechanisms, volunteer IBAMA groups, and the willingness of individuals to contact the 1412 helpline – a remote channel requiring awareness, literacy, and trust in a government response that may take days to materialise.[^617]

**Workforce pipeline:** Workers who initially staffed FCSCs were school leavers who completed a one-year Advanced Certificate at the University of Kelaniya, Sri Lanka (from 2006). A one-year Advanced Diploma followed in 2007. Eventually, a four-year Social Work degree programme became available at the Faculty of Health Sciences at the Maldives National University (MNU). However, the supply of graduates has never matched demand across 20 atolls and approximately 189 inhabited islands, and many graduates prefer Malé postings over remote atoll deployments.

### 4.20.4 Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ)

Established under the Ministry of Home Affairs, operational 2020. Responsible for: management and oversight of juvenile rehabilitation facilities; the diversion programme; probation and supervision of children under court orders; post-release support and reintegration. Budget: MVR 22 million (2024).

**Facilities:**
- **Imaduddin School for Boys:** Secure rehabilitation facility for male juvenile offenders. Provides education, vocational training, sports, religious education, and psychosocial support. HRCM 2023 monitoring visit noted infrastructure improvements were needed.
- **Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre:** For female juvenile offenders – but also serves girls in need of protective care who cannot be managed in community settings. This dual function – rehabilitation for offenders and protection for vulnerable non-offending girls – raises serious child rights concerns about placing non-offending children in the same institutional environment as children who have committed serious offences.
- **National Reintegration Centre:** Established under the Anti-Terrorism Act for children exposed to extremist ideologies. Only three successful reintegrations achieved to date – reflecting the extreme difficulty of a programme operating with minimal precedent and the sensitive political dynamics of counter-extremism.[^618][^619]

**Diversion programme:** Hundreds of children diverted from prosecution in the programme's first three years (2020–2022) through counselling, family conferencing, community service, and supervised activities.

### 4.20.5 The Juvenile Court

Dedicated specialist court in Malé for children in conflict with the law and certain civil matters involving children. Child-friendly procedures include: separate waiting areas for children and adults; informal seating arrangements; public exclusion from hearings; specialist-trained judges. UNICEF-supported pilot equipped the court with child-appropriate interview rooms and trained staff in child-friendly communication. Significant constraint: children in conflict with the law from outer islands must travel to Malé for proceedings, with associated costs and disruptions.[^620]

### 4.20.6 Children's Ombudsperson

Established under the CRPA 2019; office operational 2021. Independent mandate to: receive and investigate complaints about children's rights violations; monitor CRPA compliance by government agencies; make recommendations to government and report to the People's Majlis; conduct child rights audits across atolls. The 2022 Child Rights Symposium convened by the Ombudsperson focused on inter-agency coordination for child protection.[^621]

The paradox Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) identify: the Ombudsperson's ambitious monitoring objectives are difficult for CFPS workers – already managing critical caseloads – to fulfil. Workers in perpetual fear of being accused of negligence may adopt defensive documentation practices over genuine child welfare improvement.

### 4.20.7 Maldives Police Service – Family and Child Protection Wing (FCPW)

The FCPW is the police unit responsible for investigating DV, child abuse, sexual offences against children, and related crimes. The MPS Strategic Plan 2024–2028 identifies DV as a strategic priority. The FCPW database tags individuals with any history of DV or child abuse complaints, enabling rapid checking of prior allegations when new reports arrive. The MPS and MoSFD maintain an MoU for information sharing, and a Case Management Coordination Committee meets monthly to reconcile data on reported cases and outcomes.[^622]

**Documented operational failures in practice:**
- DV treated as a personal matter between spouses; police encouraging informal reconciliation over formal intervention
- Slow response times: women reporting waiting weeks or months for police attendance after filing reports. In one documented case, an eight-month delay in securing evidence allowed threats to continue until the victim withdrew
- Confidentiality breaches: police in one documented case disclosed to an abuser the island to which his wife had fled, enabling him to follow and assault her
- Information-sharing failures: protection order information not always shared with Corrections, enabling continued harassment from detention

These failures are not universal – some respondents described prompt professional response, proper documentation of injuries, and effective support in obtaining protection orders. The inconsistency itself is the problem: outcomes depending on individual officers and station capacity makes the system unpredictable for families who most need reliable protection.[^623]

### 4.20.8 National Council for the Protection of the Rights of the Child (NCPRC)

High-level inter-ministerial coordination body established under the CRPA 2019. Chaired by the Minister of Social and Family Development; includes ministers and senior officials from Education, Health, Home Affairs, the Attorney General's Office, and civil society representation. Mandated to meet quarterly; approves national child protection policies and action plans.[^624]

At the atoll level, **Child Protection Committees (CPCs)** – often chaired by atoll council or FCSC heads – provide local coordination, discussing anonymised cases and identifying service gaps. An **Inter-Ministerial Technical Committee** meets monthly at the operational level for case management, data sharing, and legislative implementation.[^625]

**Effectiveness challenge:** The Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations (established February 2020 in response to public concern over tragic child abuse cases) conducted joint audits of CFPS, police FCPW, and HRCM and found coordination lapses in cases that the NCPRC should theoretically have prevented – indicating that formal coordination structures do not automatically produce effective case management.[^626]

### 4.20.9 Human Rights Commission of the Maldives (HRCM)

National human rights institution with a dedicated child rights monitoring function. Key roles:
- Monitoring visits to juvenile rehabilitation facilities and publication of monitoring reports (HRCM Monitoring Report 2023 on juvenile facilities)
- Submission of shadow reports to the CRC Committee alongside State Party reports
- Receiving and investigating individual complaints involving children's rights
- Conducting periodic human rights surveys with child rights dimensions
- Publishing findings on child marriage, juvenile justice, disability rights

Operational limitation: HRCM can recommend but not compel action.[^627]

### 4.20.10 Community Social Groups (CSGs) and IBAMA

**Origins and evolution:** The IBAMA ("Ijthimaaee Badhahi Madhadhuverin" – "you and I together") project has roots tracing to 1999, when an Island Child Protection Committee was established in Addu Atoll through cooperation between the island chief and a family health worker. In 2005, the initiative expanded to GA and GDh atolls, incorporating local civil society. From that point, as the model expanded, it was rebranded as Community Social Groups (CSGs), with the first CSG established in Fuvahmulah. In 2019, UNICEF identified 17 existing laws that either mandate or call for a committee structure of the kind CSGs provided, and the platform was formally renamed IBAMA. Following a presentation to the cabinet's Social Council, government allocated budget funds for IBAMA in the 2023 national budget, enabling rollout across atolls. The model has also attracted international interest, with similar initiatives reportedly being adapted for rural communities in India.[^674]

**Structure:** Each IBAMA team comprises representatives from: island/city councils; Women's Development Committees; Ministry of Social and Family Development; Ministry of Education; Maldives Police Service; Ministry of Health; and the Department of Juvenile Justice. Members undergo **12 hours of training**. Teams conduct vulnerability mapping across four categories: child protection, disabled persons, elderly persons, and victims of GBV or domestic violence.[^675]

**Operational model:** A concern is identified by a community member (neighbour, teacher, health worker, police). The IBAMA group convenes, assesses the situation, and either provides community-level support (WDC assistance, health referral, poverty support) or refers the case to the FCSC for formal investigation. For families under supervision orders, IBAMA provides the wraparound service: school counsellors and community workers coming together to implement a multi-sectoral support plan. Once a case is satisfactorily resolved, IBAMA conducts public awareness programming as a follow-up.

**Challenges documented in the CFW research (Section 4, 2026):**
- Geographic disparity: limited mental health and psychosocial services outside major population centres requires workarounds that slow response times or require compromised interventions
- Staff turnover: in smaller islands, trained members leave their positions requiring re-training of new members
- Cross-institution cooperation: organisational culture challenges to silo-breaking persist despite the multi-sectoral mandate
- Very small islands may lack local presence for all required institutions, making it impossible to constitute a full IBAMA team with adequate local knowledge
- Small-community confidentiality and bias risks: IBAMA team members may be related or connected to persons involved in cases, creating potential for bias or confidentiality breaches[^676]

### 4.20.11 Women's Development Committees (WDCs)

WDCs operate at island level to promote women's participation in local governance and community development. In child protection specifically: WDCs organise food packages and school supplies for vulnerable families; conduct awareness programming on DV prevention and children's rights; participate in IBAMA coordination. An IFES qualitative assessment (2015) found WDC capacity to influence local decision-making remains limited, with their recommendations to island councils advisory rather than binding. WDC members themselves face the double burden of paid work, household, and childcare that limits participation in committee work.[^677]

### 4.20.12 Civil Society in Child Protection

**NGOs and civil society are largely absent from formal service provision in the Maldives**, making child protection activities dominated by the state with a very active role from UNICEF (Rogers, Ali and Naeem, 2025). This creates a landscape where a small number of organisations play outsized roles in specific gaps:

**Advocating for the Rights of Children (ARC):** NGO focused on child protection advocacy, safeguarding policy development, and training. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) suggest that a family support partnership with an NGO such as ARC could provide preventive family support that the statutory system currently lacks capacity to deliver – particularly aiding reintegration social workers and facilitating smoother transitions for children returning from institutional or foster care.

**Family Legal Clinic (FLC):** Provides free legal advice and representation in family law matters, including custody, maintenance, and DV protection orders. Malé-based but conducts remote consultations. The FLC is the primary source of free family law counsel for vulnerable women and families; its capacity constraints mean that if it were to scale back, no immediate state alternative exists.[^678]

---


## 4.10 The Child Protection Service Architecture: Institutions, Facilities, and Referral Pathways


### 4.53.1 National Central Services

**Child and Family Protection Services (CFPS)** – headquartered in Malé, within MoSFD – is the apex operational body for child protection in the Maldives. CFPS manages all 20 atoll-level FCSCs, maintains the MCPD database, provides professional supervision and quality assurance for social work practice, manages the 1412 helpline, and coordinates the IBAMA community model. CFPS has legal authority under the CRPA to apply for judicial orders (Articles 80, 90, and 93) from the Family Court – the Interim Assessment Order, the Child Protection Order, and the Child Supervision Order.[^809]

CFPS operates with a central team in Malé handling the most complex cases (particularly those arising in the capital), a management layer responsible for atoll supervision and policy, and a data and information unit managing MCPD. The direct casework function – the actual assessment, investigation, and case management – is delivered through the FCSCs.

**The Children's Ombudsperson** – established under CRPA 2019, operational from 2021 – provides independent oversight. The Ombudsperson's mandate includes:
- Investigating complaints by or on behalf of children about violations of their rights
- Monitoring conditions in children's residential care facilities
- Examining and making recommendations on legislation, policy, and practice affecting children
- Publicising children's rights through education and awareness activities
- Reporting to the People's Majlis (parliament) on the state of children's rights

The Ombudsperson's office holds a mandate that is broader than its current operational capacity. The office has limited staff, limited investigation powers in comparison to adult oversight bodies, and a paradoxical relationship to the CFPS – overseeing the professional body that is also responsible for children's protection, creating accountability while potentially creating adversarial dynamics.[^810]

**Family Protection Authority (FPA)** – while its primary mandate concerns domestic violence rather than child protection, FPA's work directly intersects with child protection in several critical ways:
- DV affecting children as witnesses and victims is the most common presenting concern at FCSCs
- FPA manages the Amaan Hiyaa shelter, which houses both DV survivors and children accompanying them
- FPA operates GBV sub-cluster coordination during emergencies
- FPA administers the mandatory conciliation process for divorcing couples, which has direct implications for child custody arrangements
- FPA maintains the survivor helpline and referral system that connects GBV victims to legal, medical, and social services[^811]

The coordination deficit between FPA and CFPS – where DV and child protection cases involving the same family are managed by two separate agencies without a shared information system – is a documented operational weakness that the NAPVAC 2024-2028 identifies for reform.

**The Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ)** manages young people in contact with the juvenile justice system – from diversion through rehabilitation to residential placement. DJJ:
- Operates the Imaduddin School for Boys (juvenile rehabilitation facility)
- Manages the Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre for young people requiring secure placement
- Administers the diversion programme for juvenile offenders
- Provides social worker support to the Juvenile Court
- Coordinates reintegration services for young people completing sentences

DJJ's relationship with CFPS is particularly important in cases involving children who are both victims of abuse and in conflict with the law – a significant overlap in the Maldivian context, where child victims of abuse and children who subsequently offend are often the same individuals at different points in their trajectory.[^812]

**The Police Family and Child Protection Wing (FCPW)** – operating within the Maldives Police Service (MPS) – has criminal investigation jurisdiction for child protection offences. The FCPW:
- Investigates cases of child sexual abuse, physical abuse, neglect, and child trafficking
- Conducts forensic interviews of child witnesses and victims (where capacity allows)
- Issues protection orders under the DVPA and CRPA
- Maintains the FCPW database of perpetrators (with repeat offender tagging)
- Coordinates with prosecutors in the Prosecutor General's Office on criminal cases

The documented failures of the FCPW – treating DV as "a personal matter," breaching victim confidentiality, failing to share protection orders with Corrections – require structural reform rather than individual training interventions. The MPS Strategic Plan 2024-2028 identifies DV as a strategic priority and commits to human rights-centred policing, but translating strategic commitment into frontline culture change requires sustained management accountability.[^813]

### 4.53.2 Residential Care Facilities

**Kudakudhinge Hiya (Children's Home, Villimale')** is the primary shelter facility for children removed from harmful situations who are awaiting alternative care placement or family reunification. The facility accommodates approximately 50 children across gender-separated units. Kudakudhinge Hiya was not designed or resourced as a long-term care facility – it functions as emergency and transitional accommodation – but children often remain for extended periods because alternative care placements (foster care) are unavailable and family reunification is not safe.[^814]

**Fiyavathi Children's Home (Hulhumale')** is a more permanent residential care facility, established in 2016 with funding from the Government of India (MVR 100 million construction project). Fiyavathi was designed to accommodate approximately 80 children in a homelike setting across multiple smaller units. As of 2024, the facility was operating at approximately 130 children – 63% over capacity – creating conditions that are neither the normalised family environment intended by child rights principles nor an adequately resourced institutional setting.[^815]

The combined capacity pressure across both facilities – approximately 180 children against a combined designed capacity of approximately 130 – is a direct consequence of the underdevelopment of the foster care system. Children who would, in a functioning alternative care continuum, be placed with trained and supported foster families are instead in residential care by default. The NAPVAC 2024-2028's foster care operationalisation target is therefore simultaneously a child rights priority and a capacity solution for the residential care system.

**Amaan Hiyaa (Villimale')** and **Amaan Veshi facilities** provide emergency shelter for DV survivors and their children. Amaan Hiyaa in Villimale' serves Malé and surrounding islands. Amaan Veshi facilities in regional centres (Kulhudhuffushi, Thinadhoo, Hithadhoo) provide regional shelter access – though coverage remains incomplete across all regional hubs. These facilities are primarily positioned as GBV responses but serve children both as residents accompanying their mothers and as direct beneficiaries when children are the primary victims.[^816]

**Imaduddin School for Boys** is the juvenile rehabilitation facility for boys sentenced to custodial orders under the JJ Act. The facility provides education, vocational training, and psychosocial support within a secure setting. The ICSVE prison radicalisation study's finding that extremist recruitment operates in correctional settings – including facilities housing younger offenders – identifies Imaduddin as a setting requiring specific safeguarding protocols against radicalisation exposure.[^817]

**The National Reintegration Centre** handles rehabilitation and reintegration of young people involved in violent extremism. The NAP P/CVE 2020-2024 noted that only **3 children had been successfully reintegrated** through this facility during its period – an indication of both the scale of the challenge and the limitations of the specific intervention model.[^818]

### 4.53.3 Regional Service Distribution

The child protection system's geographic distribution follows the five regional hospital cluster framework:

**Northern Region (Kulhudhuffushi Regional Hospital)** serves Haa Alifu, Haa Dhaalu, and Shaviyani atolls. The FCSC for Haa Dhaalu Atoll is located in Kulhudhuffushi. Three atolls, multiple islands, one FCSC. The regional hospital provides mental health services including a resident psychiatrist and telepsychiatry access for HDh Atoll islands.

**North-Central Region (Ungoofaaru Regional Hospital)** serves Noonu, Raa, Baa, and Lhaviyani atolls. The Raa Atoll FCSC is in Ungoofaaru. The hospital's "Sidhdha" wing with child-friendly areas for paediatric mental health patients represents one of the few child-specific mental health resources outside Malé.

**Central Region (Gan Regional Hospital, Laamu Atoll)** serves nine atolls – the largest regional catchment. The Laamu Atoll FCSC is in Gan. Telepsychiatry is available for L. and Th. Atoll islands. NDA drug treatment is planned in Gan.

**Upper-Southern Region (Dr Abdul Samad Memorial Hospital, Thinadhoo)** serves Gaafu Alifu and Gaafu Dhaalu atolls. The hospital has opened tender for a dedicated Mental Health Centre and runs Early Intervention programmes in schools. The NDA Community Rehabilitation Centre in Thinadhoo serves this region's drug treatment needs.

**Southern Region (Addu Equatorial Hospital)** serves Addu City and Fuvahmulah. The Addu FCSC is in Hithadhoo. The Addu Equatorial Hospital has a Regional Mental Health Centre with psychiatrist on staff. The NDA Drug Detoxification and Community Rehabilitation Centre serves this region.

In each regional centre, the co-location of the FCSC, the regional hospital (including mental health), the police station, the magistrate court, and the NDA drug treatment facility creates – in principle – a multi-agency hub that can coordinate complex child protection responses. In practice, the coordination mechanisms between these agencies are underdeveloped, and geographic access from smaller surrounding islands to the regional hub remains a significant barrier for families and children.[^819]

### 4.53.4 The 1412 Helpline: Operational Analysis

The national child protection helpline (1412) was established in 2008 as a toll-free 24-hour reporting channel. Given statutory basis by CRPA 2019 Article 76, the helpline serves as:

- **First point of contact** for children and adults who want to report suspected abuse
- **Crisis support** for children in immediate danger
- **Information and advice** for professionals uncertain about reporting obligations
- **Referral gateway** connecting callers to FCSC, police FCPW, or other services depending on the nature of the concern

In 2024, the 1412 received 2,855 calls – a significant volume that, distributed across the year, represents approximately 8 calls per day. The helpline is staffed by CFPS workers operating on a rostered basis; calls outside working hours are covered by an on-call arrangement.[^820]

Key operational limitations:

**Geographic access.** While the helpline is theoretically accessible from all islands via mobile phone, connectivity gaps on outer islands and the cost of calls (despite the toll-free designation) may limit access for some communities. Islands with limited mobile coverage have no reliable helpline access.

**Language.** The helpline operates primarily in Dhivehi. Children who are foreign nationals (including children of migrant workers) may not have effective access to Dhivehi-language helpline services. There is no documented provision for language interpretation.

**Linkage to MCPD.** As noted in §4.45.2, calls to 1412 are not automatically linked to subsequent FCSC case records in MCPD, limiting the system's ability to track whether helpline contacts result in protective intervention.

**Follow-up protocols.** For calls that are referred to FCSCs, there is no systematically implemented follow-up mechanism to verify that the referral was acted upon and that the child's safety was checked. In a system where FCSC workers are overwhelmed, referrals that fall between the cracks are a real risk.[^821]

---


## 4.11 Institutional Architecture for Child Protection: The Complete Picture


### 4.93.1 The Children's Ombudsperson in Practice

The Children's Ombudsperson, established under CRPA 2019 and operational from 2021, represents one of the most significant institutional innovations in the post-CRPA architecture. Its mandate:
- Independent investigation of children's rights violations
- Monitoring of CRPA compliance
- Recommendations to government and People's Majlis
- Conducting child rights audits across atolls
- Handling hundreds of complaints annually relating to child abuse, school-based violence, and juvenile justice conditions

The 2022 Child Rights Symposium – co-hosted with UNICEF – represented the first major public accountability exercise under the new oversight architecture. It identified:
- Shortage of trained social workers
- Absence of care plans for children in state care
- Fragmentation of services across islands

The Ombudsperson's primary accountability function is valuable but creates a tension with operational capacity. As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document, the HRCM and Ombudsperson impose ambitious objectives on CFPS workers – workers already carrying 160-case caseloads – creating a compliance culture where accountability management can crowd out substantive welfare work.

### 4.93.2 The Human Rights Commission (HRCM) Child Rights Monitoring

The HRCM, established under Law No. 6/2006, includes a dedicated children's rights monitoring unit. Functions:
- Independent monitoring of child rights compliance
- Receiving and investigating complaints
- Publishing periodic reports on children's rights situations
- Advocacy on child marriage, juvenile justice conditions, and disability rights
- Monitoring visits to juvenile rehabilitation facilities (Imaduddin School, Fiyavathi)
- Submitting shadow reports to UN treaty bodies (CRC, CRPD, CEDAW)

HRCM monitoring reports on juvenile facilities have raised concerns about overcrowding, programming adequacy, staffing ratios, and physical environments. The Government has undertaken infrastructure improvements at Imaduddin School in response.

### 4.93.3 The DJJ and the Juvenile Rehabilitation Infrastructure

The Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ), operationalised in 2020 under the Ministry of Home Affairs, represents the operational juvenile justice authority:

- **Budget:** MVR 22 million (2024)
- **Responsibility:** Management and oversight of juvenile rehabilitation facilities, diversion programme, probation and supervision of children under court orders
- **Facilities:** Imaduddin School for Boys; Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre (girls)
- **First three years (2020–2022):** Hundreds of children diverted from prosecution through the diversion programme

The **Imaduddin School for Boys** provides:
- Education
- Vocational training
- Sports programming
- Religious education
- Psychosocial support
- HRCM noted infrastructure concerns in 2023 monitoring report

The **Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre** has a dual function: rehabilitation for female juvenile offenders AND protective care for vulnerable girls who cannot be managed in community settings. The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) raises concerns about the appropriateness of a rehabilitation facility for non-offending children – placing girls in need of protection in an environment designed for those in conflict with the law.[^930]

### 4.93.4 The Council for the Protection of the Rights of the Child

The National Child Rights Protection Council (established under CRPA 2019, Articles 14–18):
- Chaired by the Minister of Social and Family Development
- Includes ministers and senior officials from Health, Education, Home Affairs, and the Attorney General's Office
- Civil society representation
- Mandated to meet quarterly
- Approves national child protection policies and action plans

Below the Council, the Inter-Ministerial Technical Committee on Child Protection provides operational coordination:
- Monthly meetings
- Technical-level representatives from all relevant agencies
- Coordination on case management, data sharing, legislative implementation, and service delivery

These bodies represent the formal apex of the child protection coordination architecture. Whether they function as effective coordination mechanisms or primarily as paper governance structures is not systematically documented in available sources – a monitoring gap that the Ombudsperson and HRCM could usefully address.[^931]

---


## 4.12 Geographic Service Delivery: The Tiered Architecture


### 4.92.1 The Service Tier Model

The Service Provider Directory (Part XXI of this encyclopedia) describes a four-tier service delivery architecture that defines what child protection services are available at each geographic level:

| Tier | Geographic Level | Child Protection Services |
|---|---|---|
| **Tier 4: National** | Malé City | CFPS HQ, FPA, Fiyavathi (Children's Home), Kudakudhinge Hiya, Aman Hiya, Juvenile Court, Family Court, Ombudsperson, HRCM |
| **Tier 3: Regional** | 5 regional centres (Addu, Kulhudhuffushi, Fuvahmulah, Naifaru, Thinadhoo) | FCSC, regional hospital, MPS division, magistrate court or High Court branch |
| **Tier 2: Atoll** | Atoll capital | FCSC (1 per atoll), atoll hospital, magistrate court, police station |
| **Tier 1: Island** | Inhabited island | Island council, WDC (Women's Development Committee), community social groups/IBAMA, health post, school, possible police presence |

The practical implication for child protection is stark: **all specialist child protection services are concentrated in Malé (Tier 4)**. The specialist institutional care facilities (Fiyavathi, Kudakudhinge Hiya, Aman Hiya), the specialist courts (Juvenile Court, Family Court), the CFPS headquarters, and the oversight bodies (Ombudsperson, HRCM) are all located in the capital, accessible to less than half the national population without travel.

**Tier 3 regional centres** provide FCSC presence and general health, legal, and police services, but no specialist child protection facilities. A child victim of CSA in Addu City (the largest atoll outside Malé) has access to an FCSC and MPS, but no specialist forensic examination facilities, no dedicated child-friendly interview rooms (in most cases), and no specialist therapeutic services.

**Tier 2 atoll capitals** provide one FCSC per atoll – serving all islands in the atoll. Given that most atolls include between 5 and 20 inhabited islands, the FCSC is a distant resource for most atoll residents, accessible only by water transport.

**Tier 1 islands** have effectively no dedicated child protection service. The island council, WDC, IBAMA community groups, and school teacher (as mandatory reporter) constitute the entirety of the child protection infrastructure. A child on a remote outer island who needs protection must rely on these community actors to initiate a report through 1412, which then routes to the FCSC, which may be several hours away by boat.[^926]

### 4.92.2 The Malé Services for Children in Care

The Service Provider Directory documents the child welfare and child protection facilities in Greater Malé in operational detail:

**Fiyavathi (Children's Home, Hulhumale'):**
- Established 2016 with Qatar Foundation support
- Funded renovation under Indian grant (MVR 1.9 million)
- Capacity: approximately **130 children** (significantly above the 80 figure cited in the Rogers & Ali (2023) article, reflecting expansion since 2023)
- Serves: children below 13 years who have been abandoned, neglected, deprived, or abused and placed in state care
- The directory notes the Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) critique that Fiyavathi operates at capacity above its intended limit

**Kudakudhinge Hiya (Villimale'):**
- Capacity: approximately **50 children**
- Serves: children aged 13–18 years in state care
- Also functions as a temporary shelter for children from outer islands who require travel to Malé for medical appointments (per Rogers & Ali, 2023)

**Aman Hiya:**
- Villimale'
- Emergency shelter for DV victims and their children
- Operated by MoSFD
- Also accommodates girls aged 9–18 (per Rogers & Ali, 2023, after the transition from DV victim service)

These three facilities – in Hulhumale', Villimale', and Villimale' respectively – constitute the entirety of the residential child care infrastructure in the Greater Malé area. Beyond them, the post-COVID expansion to nine smaller institutions across atolls is the only residential alternative care infrastructure in the country.[^927]

### 4.92.3 CFPS and FCSCs: The National Network

**CFPS (Child and Family Protection Services):**
- Located in MoSFD headquarters, Malé
- Manages all 20 atoll-level FCSCs
- Specialised teams: assessment, investigation, legal liaison, case coordination
- Maintains the Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD, launched 2016)
- Two dedicated social workers for all foster care nationally
- Helpline: 1412 (24/7, toll-free)

**FCSCs (Family and Children Service Centres):**
- 24 centres operational as of December 2024 (per CRC Periodic Report)
- All 20 administrative atolls covered
- Dedicated centres in Addu City and Fuvahmulah City as well as atoll capitals
- Each FCSC: receives referrals, conducts assessments, manages cases, coordinates with local authorities, schools, health facilities, and police
- Staffing: as few as 1–2 workers per FCSC in outer atolls
- Effective coverage: atoll capital only; islands without FCSCs rely on hotline referrals and periodic visits

**Geographic gap:** Of 187 inhabited islands, only those with FCSCs (approximately 24) have any dedicated child protection professional presence. The 163+ remaining islands access the system only through 1412, remote case management, and periodic visits from FCSC staff.[^928]

### 4.92.4 The MPS Family and Child Protection Wing

The Maldives Police Service (MPS) maintains a dedicated Family and Child Protection Department (FCPD):
- Male' headquarters: Phone 979 0163
- Responsible for investigation of DV, child abuse, and sexual offences against children
- Present in all regional and atoll-level police stations, though the capacity and training of FCPD officers outside Malé varies

The MPS-CFPS joint investigation protocol (operational for complex cases) and the MDT (Multi-Disciplinary Team) protocol (formalised 2022) create formal linkages between police and social work responses. However, the fundamental limitation is that police, like social workers, are concentrated in atoll capitals and Malé, with outer islands served by general duty officers rather than FCPD specialists.

One of the most significant operational barriers to effective child protection is the misalignment between the CFPS and MPS roles and boundaries. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document the complexity of multi-agency working in the Maldivian political environment: inter-institutional coordination depends not just on protocols but on the political relationships and trust between individual officials, which can vary enormously between atolls and change with government transitions.[^929]

---


## 4.13 MoSFD's Structural Overstretch: 39 Responsibilities, One Ministry


### 4.115.1 The Mandate Architecture

The Ministry of Social and Family Development's mandate document (January 2024) lists 39 core responsibilities. This is not a policy document or an aspirational framework – it is the operational mandate against which the Ministry's performance is assessed and through which its budget allocations are justified. The 39 responsibilities span:

- Policy establishment and implementation for **five vulnerable population groups**: women, children, elderly, persons with disabilities, and individuals requiring state care
- **Gender mainstreaming** across all government institutions
- **Child protection systems** management and operation
- **Social protection programmes** administration and policy
- **Psychosocial support** service delivery
- **Emergency preparedness** planning and response
- **Family strengthening** programming
- **Licensing of social service providers** across all sectors
- **Research** on all covered populations
- **Human resource development** for the social sector workforce
- **International treaty implementation** and reporting (CEDAW, CRC, CRPD)
- **Management of subordinate bodies**: the Children's Rights Protection Council and the Disability Council

### 4.115.2 The Subordinate Architecture

The Ministry operates through nine subordinate institutions: NSPA (social protection administration); 19 FCSCs (frontline family and child protection); CFPS (centralised child protection management); Fiyavathi (residential care for children); Amaanveshi (GBV/DV shelters); Special Care Centre (special needs); Amaan Hiyaa (residential care for girls aged 9–18); Vilingili Social Service Centre; and Social Centres for Elderly.

Each of these institutions has its own staffing, operational requirements, accountability framework, and client population. Managing nine subordinate institutions while also implementing 39 core policy responsibilities – while maintaining international treaty reporting cycles (CEDAW, CRC, CRPD each require periodic reports), while coordinating with 25+ partner ministries on the GEAP alone, while providing technical support to 19 FCSCs across the atoll network, and while managing the development and implementation of multiple active action plans – is a workload that no ministry in any comparable system would be expected to carry within a single institutional framework.

### 4.115.3 Implications for Child Protection

The structural overstretch has direct implications for child protection. When ministry leadership attention, budget negotiation capacity, and staff time are distributed across 39 responsibilities simultaneously, child protection competes for priority against elderly care, disability policy, gender mainstreaming, and social protection – all of which have their own legitimate claim on urgency. The result is triage: the most acute and visible crises receive attention, while the slower-moving but equally important reform work (workforce development, deinstitutionalisation, online safety legislation, maintenance enforcement reform) advances slowly or not at all.

The international treaty reporting burden is itself significant. Each periodic report to the CRC Committee, the CEDAW Committee, and the CRPD Committee requires months of data collection, inter-agency consultation, drafting, review, and submission – followed by the response to Concluding Observations, which itself generates implementation obligations. The Ministry's reporting officers are the same people responsible for operational policy development. The opportunity cost of international reporting is domestic implementation capacity.[^1005]

---


## 4.14 The Family Protection Authority and Child Protection: The Overlap Zone


### 4.56.1 FPA's Mandate and Its Child Protection Dimensions

The Family Protection Authority (FPA) was established under the Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 as the lead government agency for DV prevention and response. Its mandate formally centres on adult DV survivors, but every dimension of DV has direct child protection implications:

**Children as witnesses:** The most common child protection presenting concern at FCSCs – documented throughout this chapter – is children living in households affected by intimate partner violence. These children may not be direct targets of abuse but are profoundly harmed by witnessing violence between their parents or caregivers. Research consistently shows that children who witness DV experience trauma, anxiety, depression, attachment difficulties, and behavioural problems at rates approaching those of children who are directly abused.[^835]

**Children as targets within DV contexts:** In many DV cases, children are not only witnesses but direct targets of violence. A perpetrator who is violent toward his partner is statistically likely to also be violent toward children in the household. The "dual dynamic" documented in §4.25 – where DV toward the mother and abuse/neglect toward the children coexist and reinforce each other – means that DV cases are frequently also child protection cases.

**Section 42 custody dynamics:** As documented in §4.31, Section 42 of the Family Act creates a mechanism through which abusive ex-partners can use custody threats to continue controlling former partners after divorce. FPA's role in the mandatory conciliation process for divorcing couples – and the risk that conciliation will reinforce rather than interrupt coercive control – means FPA must actively screen for coercive control in all family dispute cases.[^836]

**Amaan Hiyaa shelter:** The Amaan Hiyaa shelter accommodates both women survivors and children accompanying them. Children in the shelter are themselves survivors of DV contexts, often experiencing acute trauma symptoms, disrupted schooling, and grief at separation from their father and home community. The shelter's capacity to provide child-specific psychosocial support – separate from the adult crisis response – is a documented gap.[^837]

### 4.56.2 The FPA-CFPS Coordination Problem

The operational relationship between FPA and CFPS is one of the most significant coordination challenges in Maldivian child protection. When a family comes to FPA attention through a DV complaint – or when a child protection referral involves a family where DV is present – the question of which agency leads, how information is shared, and whose assessment framework governs the response is not clearly resolved.

In practice, the two agencies have historically operated relatively independently:
- FPA manages DV cases through the conciliation and protection order pathway
- CFPS manages child protection cases through the CRPA investigation and order pathway
- A family affected by both DV and child abuse may simultaneously be subject to FPA case management (adult survivor focus) and CFPS case management (child welfare focus) without integrated planning

The CFW recommendations identify the development of a **Joint Protocol for Domestic Violence and Child Protection Cases** as a priority action – a protocol that would establish:
- Clear lead agency designation for cases involving both DV and child protection
- Mandatory information sharing between FPA and CFPS in dual-concern cases
- Integrated assessment frameworks that assess both adult survivor safety and child welfare simultaneously
- Joint case planning and regular review for complex cases
- Clear escalation pathways when the interests of the adult survivor and the child's protection needs appear to diverge[^838]

---


## 4.15 The Children's Ombudsperson: Functions and Limitations


### 4.83.1 Establishment and Mandate

The Children's Ombudsperson was established under the Child Rights Protection Act 2019 as an independent office mandated to:
- Receive complaints from or on behalf of children
- Investigate violations of child rights
- Make recommendations to the government

The office became operational in 2021 and has since handled hundreds of complaints related to child abuse, school-based violence, and juvenile justice conditions. The 2022 Child Rights Symposium (co-hosted with UNICEF) was the first major public review of CRPA implementation, providing a multi-stakeholder forum identifying gaps including shortage of trained social workers, absence of care plans for children in state care, and fragmentation of services across islands.

### 4.83.2 The Ambitious Objectives Problem

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note a paradox in the Ombudsperson's role: organisations like the HRCM and Ombudsperson impose ambitious objectives on CFPS workers – objectives that "prove challenging to fulfil, given the constraints of staff capacity and the extensive caseloads they manage." The accountability infrastructure created by the CRPA 2019 (Ombudsperson, HRCM monitoring, mandatory reporting, criminal sanctions) creates a high standard of expected performance for a system structurally incapable of meeting it.

The consequence is a culture of defensive practice: workers focus on accountability management – documenting compliance with process requirements, responding to Ombudsperson requests, justifying inaction – rather than on substantive child welfare improvement. This defensive orientation is rational for individual workers (protecting themselves from accountability) but counterproductive for the system (resources spent managing accountability rather than managing cases).

The Ombudsperson's role is structurally important and the accountability it provides is genuinely valuable. The problem is not the existence of accountability but the absence of the systemic resources – workforce, supervision, training, inter-agency coordination – that would enable workers to respond to that accountability with substantive performance improvement rather than bureaucratic compliance.[^915]

---


## 4.16 The Children's Rights Council: National Oversight Architecture


### 4.86.1 The Council's Mandate and Composition

The National Child Rights Protection Council, established under the Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (Articles 14–18), serves as the highest-level coordination body for child rights policy. Its composition:
- Chaired by the Minister of Social and Family Development
- Ministers and senior officials from Health, Education, Home Affairs, and the Attorney General's Office
- Civil society representation

Mandate: quarterly meetings; approval of national child protection policies and action plans.

### 4.86.2 The Inter-Ministerial Technical Committee

The Inter-Ministerial Technical Committee on Child Protection provides operational coordination between line agencies on case management, data sharing, legislative implementation, and service delivery. Monthly meetings; technical-level representatives from all relevant agencies.

### 4.86.3 The Gap Between Mandate and Performance

The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) documents these bodies' formal roles without providing data on their actual performance – meeting frequency, decisions made, or outcomes measured. The broader pattern in Maldivian social policy – multiple coordination bodies established in legislation, with variable operational performance – applies here. The establishment of the Council and Technical Committee represents genuine institutional investment; whether these bodies drive meaningful coordination or serve primarily as paper governance structures depends on political commitment, leadership quality, and adequate resourcing of their secretariat functions.[^919]

---


## 4.17 Child Protection and the Family Court


### 4.99.1 The Family Court's Role

The Family Court in Malé is the primary judicial body for child welfare matters. Its jurisdiction includes:
- Emergency protective orders (confirmed within 24 hours)
- Longer-term placement orders (following full best interests assessment)
- Custody and contact orders in divorce proceedings
- Maintenance orders
- Foster care legal guardianship transfers
- Reunification approvals

**The 5-day requirement:** Under CRPA 2019, court orders must be applied within 5 days of taking a child into state care. This compressed timeline requires CFPS to complete assessments, prepare legal documentation, and secure judicial hearing within 5 days – while managing caseloads of 160.

**Orders available:**
- *Interim Assessment Order* (CRPA Article 80): Where an offence has been committed and investigation is required, and parents have not given consent. Applied for by CFPS and MPS jointly.
- *Protection Order* (CRPA Article 90): Temporary order removing the child and placing them with a person approved by the state.
- *Supervision Order* (CRPA Article 93): Provides CFPS or police with discretion to monitor the child with parents daily, ensuring parents pose no danger.

**Best interests assessment in the Family Court:** The Family Court is required to conduct best interests assessments in all cases involving children. However, the operationalisation of this principle outside Malé – where decisions may be made by local officials without formal training – remains a challenge.

### 4.99.2 Maintenance Enforcement Failures

The Family Court's maintenance enforcement system is effectively dysfunctional, as documented in the Gender chapter (Part V) and with child poverty implications noted above. The MVR 2 million in unclaimed maintenance payments accumulated at the Family Court represents years of accumulated obligation that fathers are not meeting – and that the court system is not enforcing.

The child protection system and the maintenance enforcement system are not integrated. A CFPS worker who identifies that a child's poverty is driven by maintenance non-compliance has no clear pathway to trigger maintenance enforcement. The systems operate in parallel silos, leaving a critical intervention point unaddressed.[^938]

---


## 4.18 Multi-Sectoral Case Conferencing and Coordination


### 4.17.1 The Coordination Deficit

Effective child protection requires coordination across multiple sectors: social services, police, health, education, justice, and community organisations. In the Maldives, inter-agency coordination remains ad hoc rather than systematic. There are no established protocols for multi-agency case conferences – structured meetings at which professionals from different agencies share information, assess risk, and develop coordinated plans for children at risk.

The absence of formal case conferencing mechanisms means that information about children at risk is fragmented across agencies. A child known to the police for exposure to domestic violence may not be known to CFPS; a child identified by a school as showing signs of neglect may not be referred to health services; a child in the juvenile justice system may not receive the social work support that would address the underlying vulnerabilities driving their offending behaviour.[^76]

### 4.17.2 Children with Disabilities

Children with disabilities are particularly vulnerable within the child protection system. The vulnerability mapping research documented that children with disabilities face compounded risks: they are at elevated risk of abuse and neglect; they face barriers to disclosure (children with communication disabilities may be unable to report maltreatment); and they are underserved by a child protection system that lacks the specialist knowledge and capacity to meet their needs.

A specific concern is the participation of children with disabilities in judicial proceedings. Cases have been documented in which court proceedings involving children with disabilities were conducted **without interpreters**, denying these children the ability to understand and participate in processes that directly affect their lives. This represents a violation of both the CRPA and the Disability Act 2010.[^77]

### 4.17.3 Children in Domestic Violence Contexts

The vulnerability mapping research produced a critical finding regarding the relationship between children and domestic violence: **children are both the primary reason women stay in violent relationships AND the primary reason they eventually leave.** Women reported staying in violent relationships to maintain family stability for their children, to ensure their children's access to housing and financial support, and to avoid the custody consequences of divorce (given the Family Act's provisions that penalise mothers who remarry or relocate). However, when violence escalated to a point where children were directly harmed or where women judged the exposure to violence as more damaging than separation, children became the catalyst for leaving.

This dual dynamic means that child protection cannot be separated from domestic violence response – children are simultaneously the stakes and the agents of change in women's decisions about violent relationships. Policy and service responses that treat child protection and domestic violence as separate streams miss the fundamental interconnection between children's safety and their mothers' safety.[^78]

---


## 4.19 Coordination Challenges Across Agencies


### 4.46.1 The Coordination Architecture on Paper

The child protection coordination architecture in the Maldives includes multiple layers:

- **National level**: National Child Protection Coordination Committee (NCPCC/NCPRC), chaired by MoSFD Minister
- **Operational level**: Monthly Case Management Coordination Committee (CMCC) – CFPS, MPS FCPW, DJJ, health sector
- **Emergency level**: Protection Cluster under NDMA
- **Community level**: IBAMA volunteer network, Island Council connections
- **International level**: UN Resident Coordinator coordination mechanism; UNSDCF outcome groups

This coordination architecture is comprehensive in design. In practice, the 2020 Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations, the UNICEF 2022 assessment, and the Action Plans Analysis chapter all document significant coordination failures.[^753]

### 4.46.2 The Core Coordination Failures

**Case management fragmentation.** When a child is known simultaneously to CFPS (as a victim of neglect), MPS (as a witness to domestic violence), DJJ (for a minor juvenile offence), and a school counsellor (for behavioural problems), these contacts do not automatically connect into a coherent case management response. Each agency manages its slice of the case independently. No single professional has an overview of the child's full situation and is accountable for their welfare. The MDT protocol (2022, piloted in Malé and 3 atolls) was designed to address this – but national roll-out had not been completed as of 2024.[^754]

**Information sharing barriers.** Agencies cite legal and confidentiality barriers to sharing case information with each other. While the CRPA 2019 Article 126 establishes mandatory reporting obligations, the broader framework for information sharing between CFPS, police, courts, and health services has not been comprehensively legislated, leading to inconsistent practice. Some agencies share freely; others require formal requests and face legal uncertainty about their authority to share.[^755]

**Jurisdictional disputes.** When CFPS and MPS FCPW both receive a referral about the same child, who takes lead responsibility? The MoU between MPS and MoSFD is intended to address this but its operational implementation has been inconsistent. In some cases, both agencies have investigated independently; in other cases, each has assumed the other is managing the case. Children have fallen through the gap.[^756]

**Judicial-social work interface.** The Family Court exercises judicial authority over child protection orders under CRPA Articles 80, 90, and 93. CFPS workers prepare the applications and provide evidence. However, the judicial training in child protection principles is limited – judges may not be familiar with trauma symptoms, the dynamics of CSA, or the evidence base for different placement options. The result is judicial decisions that do not always align with CFPS professional assessments. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) documented cases where courts returned children to proven abusers on the basis of family reunification principles without adequate risk assessment.[^757]

**Political interference.** Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) identify political interference as a significant challenge: "in a small society where political connections are pervasive, child protection workers may face pressure from politicians or community leaders to drop or modify reports of abuse involving politically connected families." The absence of statutory independence for CFPS – which is part of MoSFD and subject to the same political dynamics as the rest of the ministry – means that this pressure cannot always be resisted. The death threats received by frontline workers documented in §4.21 are one manifestation of this vulnerability.[^758]

### 4.46.3 Promising Practices: The MDT Protocol

The Multi-Disciplinary Team (MDT) protocol, piloted in Malé and three atolls from 2022, represents the most promising systemic reform for coordination. The MDT brings together CFPS, MPS FCPW, DJJ, and health professionals to jointly review complex cases, share information, and agree coordinated responses. Pilot evaluation found that MDT case reviews improved information sharing, reduced duplication, and produced more coherent protection plans.

The challenge is scale: operating MDTs effectively in all 21 atolls requires trained facilitators, protected time, and accessible venues in contexts where professional workers are already over-stretched. The NAPVAC 2024-2028 targets national MDT roll-out as a CP System pillar priority, with UNICEF providing technical support for the expansion.[^759]

---


## 4.20 Inter-Agency Coordination: The MDT Model and Its Limitations


### 4.84.1 The Multi-Disciplinary Team Protocol (2022)

A formal Multi-Disciplinary Team (MDT) protocol was established in 2022 – bringing together social workers, police, health professionals, and prosecutors for complex child abuse cases. MDT meetings are held regularly in Malé and were piloted in three atolls (Addu, Haa Dhaal, and Laamu). Extension to all atolls is planned but faces resource constraints.

The MDT model represents a significant structural improvement over fragmented single-agency responses. International evidence strongly supports multi-agency child protection models: the co-location of different professional perspectives (social work's welfare lens, police's investigative lens, health's medical evidence lens, legal's prosecutorial lens) produces better outcomes for children than sequential single-agency assessment.

However, the MDT model in the Maldives faces specific structural barriers:
- **Geographic fragmentation:** MDT meetings in a 1,192-island archipelago require either physical co-location (only feasible in Malé) or virtual meetings (with bandwidth and connectivity barriers for outer islands)
- **Political divisions between sectors:** Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) identify entrenched political divisions among sectors as impeding effective collaboration and information sharing
- **Information sharing barriers:** There is no national integrated case management system – CFPS, MPS, prosecutors, and health systems each maintain separate records with limited data sharing
- **Cultural barriers to multi-disciplinary working:** The Maldivian social context's emphasis on hierarchical authority and community-specific norms can make cross-sector collaboration difficult when professional identities and institutional territories collide

### 4.84.2 The Case Conference Gap

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) recommend implementing Multi-Sectoral Case Conferencing for Case Management through formal regulation – establishing it as a standard operating procedure for complex cases rather than a discretionary practice. Without this formalisation, case conferences occur inconsistently, depending on the initiative of individual workers rather than being embedded in the system's operating protocols.

The difference between a system with formal case conference requirements and one without is significant: formal requirements create accountability mechanisms (records of decisions made, identified actions, assigned responsibilities, review dates) that single-worker decision-making cannot provide. In a system with very high caseloads, formal case conference requirements also provide a legitimate justification for the time investment case conferences require – making it harder for managers to deprioritise them in favour of immediate case volume.[^916]

---


## 4.21 Coordination Architecture: Gaps, Fragmentation, and Reform Potential


### 4.105.1 The Coordination Problem in the Maldivian Child Protection System

The institutional architecture described throughout this chapter – MoSFD/CFPS, FCSCs, FPA, NCPRC/CPCs, DJJ, 1412 helpline, Family Court, Magistrate Courts, MPS FCPD, IBAMA, CSGs, WDCs, HRCM, Children's Ombudsperson, and international partners – represents a system that is comprehensive in legislative scope but fragmented in operational execution. Each institution has a defined mandate. What the system lacks is a functioning coordination mechanism that ensures these mandates are discharged in a way that creates a coherent service experience for the child or family navigating multiple systems simultaneously.

The consequence is visible in the experience of a family presenting with multiple intersecting problems – the commonest presentation in child protection. A single mother who is a domestic violence survivor, with a child displaying signs of sexual abuse and a husband who is drug-dependent, must navigate: the FPA (domestic violence protection order and case management); CFPS (child protection investigation and case planning); MPS (criminal investigation of domestic violence and potential sexual abuse); the Family Court (divorce and custody proceedings); NSPA (single parent allowance, potentially disability allowance); and counselling services through MoSFD. No single case manager holds responsibility for the entire family. Referrals depend on workers' individual relationships and initiative. Outcomes for the child depend on whether the specific workers involved happen to communicate effectively.[^965]

### 4.105.2 Multi-Disciplinary Team Meetings: Design Versus Reality

The formal coordination mechanism for complex child protection cases is the Multi-Disciplinary Team (MDT) meeting – a model drawn directly from the English child protection framework, in which professionals from different agencies convene to jointly assess risk, share information, and agree on a coordinated response plan. In theory, MDTs ensure that all available information is synthesised and that responses are coordinated rather than duplicative or contradictory.

In Maldivian practice, MDT functioning has been inconsistent. The CFW vulnerability mapping research documented that MDT meetings were not occurring regularly in all contexts, that participation from some key agencies (particularly health services) was unreliable, and that follow-through on plans agreed in meetings was not reliably monitored. The accountability deficit is structural: there is no independent body with authority to require agencies to attend MDTs, implement agreed plans, or account for the consequences when they fail to do so. Where MDTs work well, they do so because the individual workers involved are committed and communicative. Where they work poorly, the child protection response reverts to whichever single agency the family first accessed.[^966]

### 4.105.3 The Data Interoperability Gap

A functioning coordination system requires shared information – at minimum, the ability for workers across different agencies to know whether a family they are seeing is already known to another service, what assessments have been done, and what plans are in place. In the Maldivian system, four separate data systems hold relevant information: the MCPD case management database (CFPS); the FPA's GBV database; the MPS crime database; and the NSPA Social Protection Information System (SPIS). None of these systems is interoperable in real time.

The consequence is duplication (multiple agencies conducting separate assessments of the same family without sharing information), dangerous gaps (a child protection social worker who does not know that MPS is investigating the same family for a domestic violence incident), and an inability to generate the whole-family picture that effective case planning requires. The OneGov platform represents the government's aspiration toward greater digital integration of services, and the integration of Zakat House services into the platform in late 2024 demonstrates that cross-system data sharing is technically achievable. Extending this integration to child protection case management, GBV case management, and police databases requires not only technical work but resolution of the confidentiality and data protection questions that have historically been cited as barriers to information sharing between child protection and law enforcement.[^967]

### 4.105.4 The Geographic Coordination Gap

In Male', the institutional density that enables coordination is present: CFPS specialist teams, FPA offices, Family Court, MPS FCPD, legal services, and health facilities are all accessible. Workers can meet, call, and refer across institutions with relative ease. On outer islands, none of this institutional density exists. The single FCSC social worker is the coordination mechanism – attempting to connect with police officers who have not been trained in child protection, health workers who are uncertain about their reporting obligations, teachers who have not received the NCPP 2017 guidance on what to do when they suspect abuse, and island councillors who may or may not take child protection seriously.

The geographic coordination gap means that the quality of child protection coordination is not primarily a function of system design – it is a function of where a child lives. A child in Male' is embedded in a relatively well-coordinated (if still imperfect) network of agencies and professionals. A child on a remote island is dependent on a single social worker to perform the functions that an entire multi-agency system performs in the capital.[^968]

### 4.105.5 COVID-19 as a Coordination Stress Test

The COVID-19 pandemic provided an unplanned stress test of the Maldivian child protection coordination system, and the results were instructive. During the lockdown periods, the system demonstrated genuine resilience in some domains. The 1412 helpline maintained 24/7 operations and absorbed increased call volumes from children and families in distress under lockdown conditions. Residential care facilities continued to operate with internally managed infection control. Emergency income support (the Income Support Allowance) was rapidly deployed through NSPA to households that lost income.

At the same time, significant coordination failures occurred. The closure of schools removed the most reliable child protection detection mechanism – teachers observing children daily. Outer island services were disrupted when social workers could not travel. Health services were repurposed to COVID response, removing health workers from the IBAMA participation and informal monitoring roles they would otherwise have played. The stress test demonstrated that the system's resilience during shocks is unevenly distributed – Male' services adapted more quickly and maintained more continuity than outer island services, adding a COVID-19 dimension to the geographic inequality that characterises the system's everyday functioning.[^969]

---


## 4.22 The Planning Framework Gap: Absence of an Overarching Coordination Architecture


### 4.112.1 The Strategic Action Plan and Its Shelving

Child protection in the Maldives is governed by the NAPVAC 2024–2028 at the sector level. But the NAPVAC operates within a broader social policy planning context – or more precisely, within the absence of one. The Strategic Action Plan (SAP) 2019–2023, the government's five-year strategic framework, has lapsed and its successor has been shelved before completion.

The SAP light chapters – a series of sector-specific medium-term planning documents covering Women and Gender Equality, Persons with Disabilities, Child and Family Protection, and Social Protection – were initiated as the successor framework to the SAP 2019–2023. Their drafting was progressed to the point where situational analyses and strategic directions were documented. But the Phase 2 content – medium-term targets, headline indicators, and implementation timelines – was marked "To be developed in Phase 2" in each chapter, and Phase 2 never materialised. The plan was abandoned.

The Child and Family Protection chapter of the SAP light draft, though incomplete, contains useful situational data. It records that MoSFD's child and family protection mandate encompasses: strengthening alternative care centres; developing a competent professional workforce; addressing juvenile offender rehabilitation and reintegration; and addressing children recruited by gangs. It notes that the Ministry operates 19 FCSCs across the country. What it does not contain – because Phase 2 was never completed – is any specification of how these objectives translate into measurable targets, resource allocations, or implementation timelines.[^993]

### 4.112.2 The Coordination Deficit Created by SAP's Absence

The absence of an overarching SAP creates a specific coordination problem for child protection. The NAPVAC 2024–2028 addresses violence against children. The GEAP 2022–2026 addresses gender equality, including child marriage and gender-based violence affecting children. The DVPNS 2023–2027 addresses domestic violence, which is the primary risk environment for many Maldivian children. The RMNCAH Strategy 2020–2025 addresses child health. All of these plans have child welfare dimensions, but none is responsible for the integration between them, and no overarching document prioritises resources across them.

The consequence is predictable: the plans operate in parallel, each seeking implementation from the same overstretched agencies (MoSFD, Ministry of Education, MPS, Ministry of Health), each generating its own reporting requirements, each identifying overlapping priorities without resolving how they relate to each other. The MoSFD, which has primary responsibility for child protection, carries 39 core mandated responsibilities spanning five vulnerable population groups – a portfolio that no ministry in any comparable system would be expected to manage within a single institutional framework. Without an SAP to sequence and resource the priorities, all 39 responsibilities sit simultaneously on the same inadequate institutional foundation.[^994]

### 4.112.3 The NSPA Social Protection Data – Child Protection Implications

Despite the SAP's shelving, the draft documents contain data that illuminates the child protection system's current reach. The Social Protection chapter of the SAP draft records that as of 2023:
- **3,245 parents and 5,563 children** are benefiting from single parent allowances
- **121 parents and 149 children** are receiving foster parent allowances
- **12,374 people** are receiving disability allowance

The foster parent allowance data – 121 parents caring for 149 children – suggests a fostering population slightly larger than the 16 children in formal foster care documented in the March 2023 alternative care data. This discrepancy may reflect the difference between formal registered foster placements and informal arrangements that receive the allowance, or may reflect timing differences. Either way, the data confirms that formal alternative care placements in family settings remain a tiny fraction of the child welfare system's activity.

The single parent allowance data – 3,245 parents and 5,563 children – means that approximately 5,563 children are being raised in formally recognised single-parent circumstances that receive state support. Given that 22% of children (approximately 28,206) live with one parent, the single parent allowance is reaching approximately 20% of single-parent household children – leaving 80% without this specific support, whether because they do not know about it, do not meet eligibility criteria, or face access barriers.[^995]

---


## 4.23 Action Plan Architecture: Coordination Failures, Mandate Overlaps, and the Structural Problem


### 4.119.1 Coverage Gaps Across All Active Plans

The Action Plans Analysis identified four population groups whose child protection needs are not comprehensively addressed by any of the active national action plans – groups that fall between the plans' categorical boundaries:

**Young people aged 18–24 (NEET):** Children who age out of the child protection system at 18 enter an adult landscape with no comparable protective framework. No plan provides comprehensive coverage for this group. Young people aged 18–24 who are not in education, employment, or training (NEET) – including those leaving alternative care, leaving the juvenile justice system, or exiting drug rehabilitation – have no specific transition support framework. The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 provides some post-release support through the DJJ's reintegration mandate, but the gap between the child protection framework (which ends at 18) and the adult social protection system (which has no specific young adult category) is a documented leaving-care cliff.

**Male victims of violence:** While the NAPVAC covers boys experiencing violence, and the DVPNS 3rd technically covers all domestic violence victims regardless of gender, the evidence base and service architecture are overwhelmingly oriented toward female victims. Men constitute 18% of the FPA's DV caseload – a substantial minority. They have no targeted intervention framework, limited awareness of available services, and significant social and cultural barriers to reporting experiences of victimisation. The service system has not adapted to this reality.

**Migrants and foreign worker children:** No plan addresses the social protection or vulnerability of the 132,493 foreign residents in the Maldives or their children. Despite documented concerns about exploitation in construction and domestic work sectors, and despite the CRPA 2019's formal application to all children in the country, foreign worker children have no specific protection mechanism, no systematised access to the child protection system, and no dedicated research documenting their experiences or needs.
