**Families in intersecting crisis:** Individual plans target specific vulnerabilities within families – child abuse, DV, disability, elderly care – but no plan provides an integrated family support framework that addresses the documented reality of compounding, intersecting vulnerabilities within the same household. The most common child protection presentation is not a single isolated concern but a constellation of problems: domestic violence + parental substance misuse + poverty + child neglect. No single plan addresses this constellation comprehensively.[^1016]

### 4.119.2 Mandate Overlap: Child Protection and Domestic Violence

The most consequential mandate overlap in the child protection context is the relationship between the NAPVAC and the DVPNS 3rd. Both plans address violence within the home – the NAPVAC focusing on children as victims and the DVPNS focusing on intimate partner violence. Yet the evidence is unambiguous: these are not separate phenomena. Children who witness domestic violence between parents are victims of that violence in ways that the research consistently documents – elevated rates of psychological harm, developmental delay, academic problems, and, critically, elevated risk of becoming perpetrators or victims of violence in adulthood.

The separation of child protection and domestic violence response into distinct plans with distinct implementing structures – CFPS leading child protection, FPA leading DV response – risks fragmenting what is functionally a single household crisis into two parallel responses that do not communicate. A child protection social worker and a DV caseworker may be simultaneously engaged with the same family, following different case plans, with different goals (child safety vs. victim empowerment), and with no formal mechanism to reconcile these potentially competing priorities.

The evidence cross-reference in the Action Plans Analysis records the finding from CFW research that children are "both the primary reason women stay in violent relationships AND the primary reason they eventually leave." This insight – that the child protection and domestic violence response are inextricably linked at the family level – has not been translated into an integrated operational model at the system level. Creating a genuinely integrated family violence response, in which the safety of the child and the safety of the adult victim are pursued through a coordinated rather than parallel framework, is the most important structural reform in the child protection and DV system design.[^1017]

### 4.119.3 Agency Responsibility Concentration

The Action Plans Analysis documents the concentration of implementation responsibility across active plans onto a small number of agencies, creating structural overstretch that is most severe for the agencies closest to the frontline:

**MoSFD** is the lead implementing agency for every social policy action plan. It carries 39 mandated responsibilities spanning five vulnerable population groups, international treaty reporting, gender mainstreaming, research, human resource development, and management of nine subordinate institutions. This concentration of responsibility in a single ministry with limited staff is not a planning anomaly – it is a structural feature of the Maldivian social policy architecture. No comparable system in the world assigns this breadth of responsibility to a single ministry without substantially greater resourcing.

**FCSCs** are expected to deliver child protection, DV response, and disability services across the atolls through 19 centres staffed by small teams of social workers. They are the primary access point for multiple vulnerable populations but are resourced for none at the scale required. When a family in an outer atoll presents with child abuse, domestic violence, a disabled family member, and poverty, the FCSC is simultaneously the child protection agency, the DV response agency, the disability service navigator, and the social protection referral point.

**The Maldives Police Service** carries responsibilities across four plans – child protection (NAPVAC), DV response (DVPNS), gender-based crime (GEAP), and counter-extremism (NAP P/CVE) – without an integrated policing framework for vulnerable populations. The FCPD operates within the broader MPS structure where child protection is a specialist function and the majority of police interactions with vulnerable families occur through general duty officers who have not received specialist child protection training.[^1018]

### 4.119.4 The Absence of Cross-Plan Coordination

The formal coordination mechanisms specified across the active plans are: the NAPVAC's proposed (but unspecified) monitoring and evaluation mechanism; the GEAP's Gender Focal Point network (which remained incomplete as of 2024); the NAPDI's Disability Council (assessed as "not adequate or effective" in the HRCM CRPD Shadow Report); and the National Ageing Policy's National Committee on Healthy Ageing (which has not convened since the pandemic).

More significant than any individual plan's coordination gap is the absence of cross-plan coordination entirely. No inter-ministerial body is tasked with ensuring that the NAPDI, NAPVAC, GEAP, Ageing Policy, DVPNS, and other plans are implemented in a coordinated manner, resolve conflicts, eliminate duplication, or address the gaps identified across them. Each plan has its own (often non-functional) coordination mechanism, but no supra-plan governance structure exists to hold the whole together.

This absence is most consequential for children who sit at the intersection of multiple plans – children with disabilities facing violence (NAPDI + NAPVAC), children in domestically violent households (NAPVAC + DVPNS), children of women experiencing gender-based violence (NAPVAC + GEAP). For these children, the system offers multiple plans that each address part of their experience and no coordinated response that addresses the whole.[^1019]

### 4.119.5 The Timeline Misalignment

The active plans are operating on different timelines that create policy gaps at critical moments. The Health Master Plan and the RMNCAH Strategy are in their final year (2025), meaning the health policy framework within which disability, gender, and child health goals are pursued will expire before the NAPDI and NAPVAC reach their midpoint. The NAP P/CVE has already lapsed (its 2024 end date has passed), creating an unaddressed gap in the counter-extremism and child radicalisation prevention framework that it had begun to address.

The GEAP will expire in 2026, two years before the NAPDI and NAPVAC. This creates the risk of a gender equality policy vacuum during the final years of those plans, precisely when gender dimensions of disability and child protection policy most need to be maintained. Without the overarching SAP framework that was shelved, there is no mechanism to sequence successor plans, align their timelines, and ensure continuity of policy commitment across transition points.[^1020]

---


## 4.24 Political Interference and Structural Power Dynamics in Child Protection Casework


### 4.82.1 The Political Dimension

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document one of the most politically sensitive dimensions of Maldivian child protection practice: the impact of political power dynamics on case management outcomes. Their analysis reveals several patterns:

**Media leaking by political families:** If a child like Jakob belongs to the family of a political actor, the case might be leaked to the media before being reported to police or FCSCs through proper channels – highlighting the failure of MoGFSS to maintain confidentiality. Social media has become a popular platform for such reporting, which can prompt rapid but uncoordinated intervention.

**Political monitoring:** When cases involve politically prominent families, the Human Rights Commission and the Children's Ombudsperson's Office would monitor the case very closely and request updates from social workers already overloaded with casework and constantly afraid of being accused of negligence. The political visibility of a case can accelerate institutional attention – but the kind of attention that focuses on accountability management rather than child welfare.

**Perpetrator power:** If a perpetrator is a gang leader, drug trafficker, or member of a violent extremist group, the case may not even be reported due to fear of reprisal attacks. Social workers who have reported such cases have received death threats (Human Rights Watch, 2023). The state's duty to protect children from harm cannot be fulfilled by workers who fear for their own safety when attempting to intervene.

**Cultural nepotism:** Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note that the Maldivian social context leans heavily on community and familial bonds – a factor that fosters nepotism and favouritism and can lead to corruption (Maldives Financial Review, 2023). In a small-island community context, the social connections between a child protection worker, a reporting adult, and a suspected perpetrator may all intersect, creating conflicts of interest that are structurally endemic rather than exceptional.

### 4.82.2 The Implications for Case Outcomes

The political dimension documented by Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) suggests that child protection outcomes in the Maldives are not determined solely by the child's needs or the quality of the response, but significantly by:
- The socio-political status of the family involved
- The perceived power of the perpetrator
- The media attention the case receives
- The worker's judgment about the personal safety implications of intervention

A system in which case outcomes depend on these factors cannot provide equitable protection. Children in families with less social power, in communities with active gang networks, or in cases that do not attract media attention face a structurally inferior level of protection to those in politically prominent families. This is not a failure of individual workers but a system-level failure – the system has not been designed with the protections (anonymous reporting, worker safety protocols, confidential case management, independent oversight) that would insulate case outcomes from these political dynamics.[^914]

---


# Part 4: Case Management, Social Work, and Practice


## 4.25 Child Protection Case Management and Procedures


### 4.4.1 The Four Pathways

Current child protection case management in the Maldives operates through four primary pathways, reflecting the most common presenting circumstances in which children come to the attention of protective services:

1. **Children witnessing domestic violence:** Children living in households where intimate partner violence or other forms of domestic violence occur. The Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 recognises witnessing violence as a form of harm, and CFPS may become involved either through police notification following a DV callout or through direct referral from schools, health services, or community members.

2. **Children experiencing direct abuse:** Cases where a child is the direct victim of physical, sexual, or emotional abuse, whether within the family or from persons outside the family. These cases may be reported through the Child Helpline 1412, by schools, health professionals, or community members, or may be identified through contact with other services.

3. **Children in custody conflicts:** Cases where custody disputes between separated or divorced parents place children at risk of harm – including being used as leverage in parental conflicts, being separated from primary caregivers and dispersed across relatives, or being exposed to inter-parental violence or harassment in the context of custody proceedings. The vulnerability mapping research documented that children are routinely "used as leverage in custody disputes," with consequent harm to their emotional wellbeing and stability.[^30]

4. **Children of stressed single parents at risk of neglect:** Cases where single parents – predominantly mothers – are experiencing economic stress, mental health difficulties, substance use, or social isolation that compromises their capacity to meet children's basic needs. Given the Maldives' high divorce rate and the documented inadequacy of maintenance enforcement, this pathway captures a substantial volume of referrals.[^31]

### 4.4.2 Judicial Orders and Legal Process

The CRPA establishes three judicial orders that provide the legal framework for state intervention:

- **Interim Assessment Order (Article 80):** May be sought when a child is taken into state care on an emergency basis. The order authorises the placement of the child while a full assessment is conducted. The five-day statutory timeline requires that an application be made to the court within five days of removing a child, providing a safeguard against indefinite informal placement.

- **Protection Order (Article 90):** A substantive order made following assessment, providing for the ongoing protection of the child. The order may specify placement arrangements, contact arrangements, conditions on parental behaviour, and requirements for service engagement.

- **Supervision Order (Article 93):** An order that allows the child to remain in the family or community while subject to supervision by child protection services. This represents a less interventionist option than removal and is intended to support families in addressing risk factors while keeping the child in their home environment.[^32]

### 4.4.3 Systemic Weaknesses in Case Management

Despite the legislative framework, child protection case management in practice suffers from significant systemic weaknesses documented in multiple evaluations and research studies:

**Care plans are rare.** The development of formal, written care plans – a cornerstone of professional child protection practice in most jurisdictions – is not standard practice. Children who enter state care or are subject to child protection intervention frequently lack a documented plan setting out the objectives of intervention, the services to be provided, the expected timeline, and the arrangements for review. The absence of care plans means that interventions lack structure, accountability, and measurable outcomes.[^33]

**Regular review meetings are not common practice.** Child protection cases are rarely subject to the kind of periodic multi-agency review that is standard in more developed child protection systems. Without systematic review, cases may drift without progress, children may remain in temporary arrangements indefinitely, and changing circumstances – including new risks or resolved risks – may not be identified or acted upon.

**No established referral systems or case conferences between schools and CFPS.** Despite the introduction of child protection focal points in schools (2015) and the requirements of the Education Act 2020, there is no operational inter-agency referral system or case conferencing mechanism between the education sector and CFPS. Teachers and school staff who identify child protection concerns face an institutional gap: they have been designated as focal points but lack clear protocols for referring concerns, receiving feedback on referrals, or participating in multi-agency planning for children at risk.[^34]

**Police checks and child protection training for school staff are absent.** Persons working with children in educational settings are not subject to systematic background checking or vetting, and child protection training for school staff is not mandated or systematically delivered. This represents a significant gap in the preventive architecture of the child protection system.[^35]

**Complex politics permeates child protection activities.** The small size of Maldivian society and the intensity of political networks mean that child protection work operates in a deeply politicised environment. If a child belongs to the family of a political actor, the case may be "leaked to media before being reported" through proper channels – compromising the child's confidentiality and potentially subjecting the family to public exposure before any formal assessment has been conducted. Conversely, if the perpetrator occupies a position of political, economic, or criminal power – for example, if "the father is a gang leader or drug trafficker" – the case may not be reported at all, as social workers and community members fear reprisal. Human Rights Watch reported in 2023 that social workers have received death threats in the course of child protection work, highlighting the personal risks that practitioners face in pursuing cases involving powerful individuals.[^36]

---


## 4.26 The CFPS: Case Management in Practice


### 4.87.1 The Case Management Protocol

CFPS operates a structured case management system with the following stages (formalised through CFPS Standard Operating Procedures developed in 2021):
1. **Referral and intake**
2. **Initial assessment:** Within 24 hours for urgent cases, 72 hours for non-urgent
3. **Full assessment and investigation:** Jointly with police where criminal conduct is suspected
4. **Case planning**
5. **Intervention and service provision**
6. **Monitoring and review**
7. **Case closure**

### 4.87.2 The Reality of Case Management at Scale

The case management protocol reads as a competent, internationally-informed child protection process. The gap between the protocol and the operational reality is documented throughout this chapter. Key operational realities:

**Assessment timeline compliance:** The 24-hour urgency assessment target is achievable in Malé – a single city with a concentrated CFPS presence. In outer atolls, where social workers may need to travel by speedboat between islands, the 24-hour window may be structurally impossible to meet for cases on remote islands.

**Joint investigation:** The protocol requires joint CFPS-police investigation where criminal conduct is suspected. In practice, the MDT protocol (formalised only in 2022) is the mechanism for this joint working. Outside Malé and the three pilot atolls, joint investigation remains inconsistent.

**Caseload and monitoring:** With an average caseload of 160 cases per worker, the monitoring and review stage – which requires regular contact with families and children – is impossible to implement at anything close to the intensity that case complexity requires. Cases that are not in immediate crisis may go weeks without a social worker contact.

**The 5-day order requirement:** CRPA requires court orders within 5 days of a child entering state care. The court order process requires assessment, legal documentation, and judicial hearing – a complex chain of actions that must be completed under extreme time pressure by workers already at capacity.[^920]

### 4.87.3 The MCPD: Case Data Infrastructure

The Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD) connects all FCSCs and provides a centralised case management platform. The MCPD was identified in the government's combined CRC report (2021) as a significant institutional achievement. As of 2024, a pilot digital case management system was deployed in selected FCSCs, with planned national rollout by 2026.

The transition from paper-based to digital case management enables:
- Centralised oversight of case volume and case type
- Cross-FCSC case tracking for children who move between atolls
- National trend analysis for planning and resource allocation
- Performance monitoring against case management standards

However, the value of digital infrastructure depends on data quality – which in turn depends on worker time for data entry (scarce given caseloads), training in the system (inconsistent), and the quality of the underlying records (which the absence of supervision and care planning compromises). A digital case management system applied to a fragmented, under-resourced case management practice produces digitised fragmentation – not systematic improvement.[^921]

---


## 4.27 Watershed Moments: Cases and Events That Shaped the System


### 4.57.1 The Baby Death Case (2009)

The 2009 baby death case was the first documented systemic case failure to result in significant policy recommendations for the Maldivian child protection system. A baby died in circumstances where inter-agency coordination failures, inadequate risk assessment, and absent supervision were subsequently identified as contributing factors. The case exposed the gap between the emerging system – with its procedure manuals, trained workers, and helpline – and the operational reality of practitioners working without adequate backup.

The recommendations that followed anticipated much of the CRPA 2019's content: supervisory requirements for social workers, inter-agency coordination protocols, minimum standards for risk assessment, and clearer lines of accountability. That these recommendations required a decade and a further piece of legislation to be implemented reflects the persistent gap between identifying reform needs and finding the political will and institutional capacity to act on them.[^839]

### 4.57.2 The Five Children Sentenced to Death (2013-2015)

Between 2013 and 2015, five children were sentenced to death in the Maldives under Shari'ah provisions. This was possible because the then-applicable Regulation on Juvenile Justice (2014) explicitly permitted death penalty sentences for children aged 7 and above – a provision that UNICEF, the CRC Committee, and the HRCM identified as wholly incompatible with the CRC.

President Ibrahim Mohamed Solih committed at his inauguration in 2018 to abolishing the death penalty for minors. The subsequent JJ Act 2019 prohibited the death penalty for juvenile offenders – but the constitutional Shari'ah basis for the provision created legal complexity in how this prohibition would operate in practice. The five cases served as a catalyst for the JJ Act's enactment and demonstrated to international partners and domestic civil society the extent to which the pre-2019 framework was irreconcilable with CRC compliance.[^840]

### 4.57.3 The 2020 Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations

The Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations, established by executive order in February 2020, conducted a wide-ranging inquiry into systemic failures in child rights protection across government agencies. Its report – which identified coordination failures, underfunding, workforce deficits, and political interference as structural problems – provided political legitimacy for the reform agenda that became the NAPVAC 2024-2028.

The Commission's report is particularly significant because it came from within government – not from UNICEF, HRCM, or civil society – and therefore carried authority that external assessments sometimes lack in the Maldivian policy context. The Commission's findings aligned closely with the UNICEF 2022 assessment, suggesting consistent and well-documented problems rather than contested interpretations of the system's performance.[^841]

### 4.57.4 The CRPA 2019 Drafting Process

The drafting of the CRPA 2019 was itself a significant event in the history of Maldivian child protection – not because of the outcome legislation alone, but because the multi-year consultation process involved civil society, practitioners, children themselves, religious scholars, and international partners in developing a child rights framework for the first time. The process required resolving tensions between Islamic jurisprudence and CRC principles, between community privacy norms and mandatory reporting obligations, and between family preservation values and child protection imperatives.

The way these tensions were resolved – or, in some cases, deferred (the corporal punishment prohibition was not absolute; the FGM/C prohibition was not enacted; the marital rape provisions remained incomplete) – reflects the political constraints of the drafting context as much as the substantive rights arguments. Understanding the CRPA not just as a piece of legislation but as a political negotiation about the boundaries of child rights in a Muslim-majority small island state is essential for understanding both what it achieves and what it does not.[^842]

## 4.28 Child Protection in Practice: Case Studies from the Field


### 4.60.1 Introduction to Maldivian Case Practice

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) present two case studies that illuminate how the Maldivian child protection system operates in practice – bridging the gap between legal frameworks and lived reality. These cases, presented as practice scenarios grounded in the realities of the Maldivian system, reveal the interaction of legal frameworks, cultural norms, religious practice, political dynamics, and workforce capacity in ways that statistics alone cannot capture.

### 4.60.2 Case Study 1 – Jakob: Physical Abuse in a Family Context

**The scenario:** Jakob's father physically abuses him at home. A neighbour witnesses the abuse and reports it.

**The legal framework:** Under CRPA mandatory reporting (Article 126), any person who knows of child abuse has a duty to report, with failure to report carrying a 2-year imprisonment sentence. The police and CFPS are both responsible for investigating reported abuse.

**Best practice response:** The best practice response in the Maldivian context would be for all child protection professionals from various sectors to work together to keep Jakob at home with a protective Supervision Order (CRPA Article 93). The Supervision Order would allow close monitoring with a wraparound service provided by IBAMA – a multisectoral plan of support implemented by school counsellors, social workers, and community members working together.

**The reality:** As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) make clear, the reality of the response would depend heavily on who Jakob's family is. If his father belongs to a political family:
- The case might be leaked to the media before being reported to police or FCSCs, highlighting the failure of MoGFSS to maintain confidentiality
- Social media sites have become a popular platform for reporting, prompting rapid but uncoordinated intervention
- The Human Rights Commission and Children's Ombudsperson Office would monitor the case closely and demand updates from already-overloaded social workers, who are constantly afraid of being accused of negligence

If Jakob's father is a gang leader, drug trafficker, or violent extremist:
- The case might not be reported at all due to fear of reprisal attacks
- When such cases have been reported, social workers have received death threats (Human Rights Watch, 2023)

**Assessment and outcomes:** An unintended consequence of the over-cautious approach in the Maldivian system (also noted by Rogers and Ali in the alternative care context) is that children are often rapidly removed from their biological families into institutional care, even when a Supervision Order might be sufficient. This removal – *owing to lack of foster care rather than to the needs of the child* – means that children like Jakob may enter the institutional system unnecessarily, with all the risks (gang recruitment, drug exposure, placement breakdown, family separation) that institutional care entails.

**Care planning:** In practice, development of care plans is rare and regular review meetings to discuss Jakob's potential return home are not common practice. Once a child is in institutional care, the case tends to stall.[^858]

### 4.60.3 Case Study 2 – Aysha: Child Sexual Abuse by a Family Member

**The scenario:** Aysha (age 9) discloses to a teacher that her uncle comes into her room every night and hurts her, disclosing sexual abuse.

**The legal framework:** Because Aysha's teacher is a mandatory reporter under CRPA Article 126(a), failure to report carries 2 years' imprisonment. Because Aysha is 9 years old – below the age of 13 from which consent questions become relevant under the Special Measures Act – her case is clear-cut from an age-of-consent perspective. Applications can be made to keep her uncle in custody throughout the investigation until the end of the court process under the Special Measures Act (Law 12/2009). For perpetrators found guilty of CSA, the Special Measures Act introduced sentences of 10–25 years' imprisonment without parole.

**The medical process:** The first step of the child protection process will involve a medical investigation by doctors and a medico-legal report. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note a concerning practice gap: sometimes doctors can share with the social worker that it could be sexual abuse but will not state it in the medico-legal report – limiting the evidential foundation for prosecution.

**Best practice:** The best practice response would be a multisectoral case conference bringing together professionals from different sectors. A care and protection plan would be developed; IBAMA groups would provide practical and psychological support to the family.

**Key tensions:** Significant tensions emerge in Aysha's case that go beyond the immediate abuse:

*The pregnancy scenario:* If Aysha had reached puberty and became pregnant as a result of abuse or rape, child protection professionals and doctors would face extreme ethical and legal complexity. While abortion is legally permitted within 120 days (in cases where the mother's life is at risk or in other specified circumstances), professionals are often reluctant to proceed given that abortion is considered a criminal offence in most Muslim states unless the mother's health is threatened (Cohn-Sherbok et al., 2013). The result is that case conferences for the unborn baby become crucial – but may still fail to override professional reluctance rooted in religious values.

*The child born out of wedlock:* If the pregnancy is taken to term and Aysha gives birth, her baby will be registered as a child born out of wedlock. As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note, *de facto* and *de jure* discrimination is experienced throughout the lives of children born out of wedlock in Muslim states (Ali, 2013). If Aysha's grandparents are not fit or willing to care for the baby, the baby will be taken into institutional care while long-term fostering options are explored. In this scenario, a child born of rape enters the institutional system at birth.

*The honour ideology:* Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) identify the influence of honour ideology as exacerbating the challenges of the Aysha case type. Honour ideology can lead to re-victimisation: the child who discloses sexual abuse may face community responses that treat the disclosure as shameful rather than courageous, creating risk of secondary harm within the community and within families. This re-victimisation risk operates alongside the formal legal protections, sometimes more powerfully.[^859]

---


## 4.29 The Social Work Profession: Historical Development and Workforce Analysis


### 4.59.1 Origins of the Social Work Workforce

The social work profession in the Maldives has its origins not in formal university training but in administrative and counselling functions within the child welfare system. The earliest workers in the Unit for the Rights of Children (URC), established in 1992, were volunteer counsellors provided with a 2-week introductory course in counselling techniques. These workers constituted the entire professional response to child welfare for the first decade of the Maldivian child protection system. They operated without legislative grounds for intervention – there was no comprehensive child protection, domestic violence, or sexual offences legislation until the 2000s – and with minimal training in what professional social work entails.

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) trace the growth of tensions between the counselling role and the social control aspects of child protection work as the system expanded. As mandatory reporting, court orders, and removal of children from families became features of the system, the requirements of the role evolved beyond what counsellors trained in a 2-week course were equipped for. This created the need for clearer role definitions and more substantial training pathways.[^853]

### 4.59.2 The Training Pipeline: From 2006 to the Present

The trajectory of social work training in the Maldives reflects both progress and persistent inadequacy:

**2004 (Tsunami):** The 2004 Indian Ocean Tsunami led to UNICEF Maldives expanding its programmes and creating a focused child protection programme, which built on UNICEF's former combined education and child protection programme. Financial and technical support was provided by UNICEF and the UK's Voluntary Service Organisation (VSO) in developing policies and procedures.

**2006:** Following the expansion of the UNICEF programme, groups of newly graduated school leavers received training through programmes delivered by social workers at the Ministry of Gender and Family and through university training in Sri Lanka at the University of Kelaniya. After completing an intensive 1-year Advanced Certificate course, they began work on the islands as social work managers at the Family and Children Service Centres (FCSCs). At the same time, a procedure manual was developed containing operational definitions based on the *Working Together* document in England – the UK's primary child protection guidance – adapted for Maldivian frontline social workers.

**2007:** The government introduced the 1-year Advanced Diploma in Social Service Work (Plath, 2011), providing a slightly higher qualification than the certificate course. This diploma later gave way to the full qualification pathway.

**Current:** A 4-year undergraduate Social Work degree programme is currently offered by the Faculty of Health Sciences in the Maldives. This is the full professional qualification. However, the pipeline from degree completion to deployment as an experienced practitioner is slow, and the majority of the current workforce was trained at certificate or diploma level rather than degree level. The number of social workers with tertiary education who are eligible to provide professional supervision under state licensing guidelines remains small – a fact with direct implications for the quality of case management across the system.

**The English model:** Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note that the Maldivian child protection system absorbed the procedural system of England, which is a needs-based rather than rights-based system, despite the rights-based framework of the UNCRC. This import has had both benefits (structured assessment frameworks, clearer thresholds) and costs (over-cautious approaches, rapid removal of children to institutional care rather than family-based alternatives, emphasis on severe cases at the expense of preventive work). The adaptation of an English needs-based system to a Maldivian, Islamic, small-island context has been imperfect and has required ongoing adjustment.[^854]

### 4.59.3 Current Workforce: Numbers and Caseloads

The quantitative workforce picture is stark:

- **61 child protection social workers** to serve a child population of **128,208** = a ratio of 1 social worker per 2,101 children
- Average caseload: **160 active cases** per worker
- Recommended caseload in comparative systems: **25–30 cases** per worker
- UNICEF recommended ratio: approximately 1 social worker per 500 children

The gap between the actual ratio (1:2,101) and the recommended ratio (1:500) is a factor of more than four. The gap between the average caseload (160 cases) and the recommended caseload (25–30 cases) is a factor of five to six. These are not marginal gaps – they are structural deficits that make competent child protection practice effectively impossible for most workers most of the time.

In outer island FCSCs, the situation is even more extreme. Many atoll FCSCs have a complement of one or two workers serving multiple islands. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) and the CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026) both note that case loads per social worker in outer atolls frequently exceed 40–60 active cases – and even this figure, as a subset of the national average of 160, implies that workers in Malé carry even heavier absolute caseloads to balance the national average.

**Orders within 5 days:** Under the CRPA, orders must be applied within 5 days of taking a child into state care. In cases where the court requires assessments, this task is passed to CFPS workers on top of their already overwhelming caseload. The combination of time pressure and caseload weight creates conditions in which assessments are necessarily superficial and the risk of error – in both directions, missing abuse and misidentifying abuse – is high.[^855]

### 4.59.4 The Supervision Gap

The absence of meaningful professional supervision is one of the most serious structural weaknesses in the Maldivian child protection workforce. Professional supervision – structured, regular, one-to-one sessions between a practitioner and a qualified supervisor to review cases, manage professional development, and support wellbeing – is a recognised essential safeguard for child protection workers. In the Maldives, it is effectively absent.

The reasons are systemic:

1. **Statutory basis without enforcement:** The CRPA states that social workers must receive professional guidance in their role – but this is not mandatory. It is a recommendation without teeth.

2. **Manager incapacity:** Managers do not have time to provide supervision because they themselves carry large caseloads and administrative responsibilities.

3. **Qualification barrier:** State licensing guidelines require supervisors to hold tertiary education qualifications in social work. Given the small number of degree-qualified practitioners in the system, very few workers are eligible to provide supervision. This creates a system in which the right exists on paper but cannot be exercised in practice.

4. **Fitness to practice:** There are limited fitness-to-practice safeguards. Criminal record checks are required only when registering for a licence – not on an ongoing basis. There is no requirement for continuing professional development. Practitioners can work indefinitely without refreshing their skills or demonstrating ongoing competence.

5. **Secondary traumatisation:** Without supervision or psychosocial support, child protection workers are highly vulnerable to secondary traumatisation – the psychological cost of repeated exposure to accounts and evidence of child abuse, violence, and neglect. Worker burnout and turnover perpetuate the workforce crisis: experienced workers leave, taking accumulated practice wisdom with them, and are replaced by less experienced workers who themselves receive no supervision.[^856]

### 4.59.5 School-Based Child Protection Focal Points

In 2015, the Ministry of Education implemented child protection focal points in schools, following the Committee on the Rights of the Child's Concluding Observations (CRC/C/MDV/CO/4-5, para 5a). This represented an important step toward embedding child protection within the education system, which is the primary institution through which children are encountered regularly.

However, Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document significant gaps in implementation:
- No established referral systems linking focal points to CFPS or FCSCs
- No case conferences or consistent review meetings with CFPS
- Despite provisions in the Education Act 2020, crucial mechanisms such as police checks and comprehensive child protection training for school staff remain absent

The school focal point system thus exists as a formal designation without the infrastructure of referral pathways, cross-sector communication, or training that would make it operationally effective. A child who discloses to a teacher may encounter a focal point who has a designation but no clear pathway for what to do next.[^857]

---


# Part 5: Mandatory Reporting and the Child Helpline


## 4.30 Mandatory Reporting: Legal Architecture, Implementation Reality, and Cultural Barriers


### 4.22.1 Legal Framework

**CRPA Article 126:** Any person who knows or suspects child abuse must report to CFPS or police. Failure to report is a criminal offence punishable by **two years' imprisonment**. Failure to report for financial gain (e.g., being paid to conceal abuse) attracts **five years' imprisonment**. Good-faith reporters are protected from civil or criminal liability.[^635]

**Legislative history of mandatory reporting:** Mandatory reporting obligations originally existed in Chapter 3, Section 72 of the Maldives Penal Code Law 1/66 – but this provision was repealed on 20 September 2001, creating a gap during which there was no clear legal mandate to report crimes against children. For approximately 18 years, mandatory reporting existed in policy guidance but lacked statutory foundation. The CRPA restored mandatory reporting with a clear focus specifically on child abuse and with genuine criminal enforcement provisions.[^636]

### 4.22.2 Implementation Challenges

**Knowledge barriers:** Many professionals and community members remain unaware of reporting obligations, or uncertain about the threshold for reporting. Teachers, health workers, and community members who encounter signs of neglect or abuse may not know they have a legal duty to report. Professional training in mandatory reporting is available for social workers and is being extended to school staff, but coverage across all relevant professional groups is incomplete.

**Social and relational barriers in small island communities:** Reporting a neighbour or family member for child maltreatment carries significant social costs in communities where everyone knows everyone. Reporting may cause irreparable social relationship damage, lead to ostracism of the reporter's family, create ongoing conflict from which escape is physically impossible, and attract threats or retaliation – particularly if the perpetrator has social status through political connections, gang affiliations, or economic power.

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document specific dynamics: if a child belongs to the family of a political actor, the case may be "leaked to the media before being reported to the police or the FCSCs" – political interference in both directions undermining normal reporting pathways. Conversely, if a father is a gang leader or drug trafficker, a case "might not even be reported due to fear of reprisal attacks." Social workers themselves have received death threats when attempting to intervene in cases involving powerful families (Human Rights Watch, 2023).[^637]

**System capacity barriers:** Communities that report and receive no effective response lose confidence in the system, further reducing future reporting rates. The cycle of low-capacity response generating low reporting rates generating under-detected abuse prevalence is a structural challenge the CRPA's criminal reporting mandate alone cannot break.

### 4.22.3 Mandatory Reporting in Schools: The Focal Point System and Its Limits

In 2015, the Ministry of Education implemented child protection focal points in schools under the Child Protection Policy. These focal points are designated as the school's contact for child protection concerns and trained to identify indicators of abuse.

However, Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) document that there are no established referral systems, case conferences, or consistent review meetings between schools and CFPS despite the Education Act 2020's provisions. Police checks and comprehensive child protection training for all school staff remain absent. The focal point system creates an entry point for reporting, but the pathway from school-level concern to effective CFPS intervention is not systematised. A school principal who reports to an FCSC may never receive feedback on the case outcome, making it impossible to assess whether the system responded adequately.[^638]

---


## 4.31 The Child Helpline (1412) and Mandatory Reporting


### 4.11.1 The National Child Helpline

The **National Child Helpline 1412** was established in 2008 in partnership with Child Helpline International as a toll-free, 24-hour telephone service for reporting child protection concerns. The helpline is supplemented by the **Social Services Hotline 1421**, which handles broader social services referrals including domestic violence, disability services, and elderly care.

The helpline serves multiple functions: it is a reporting channel for professionals and community members who identify child protection concerns; it is a direct line for children and adolescents to seek help; and it provides information and referral to services. The helpline is operated by trained staff and connects callers to CFPS for case management follow-up.[^62]

### 4.11.2 Mandatory Reporting Under the CRPA

The CRPA 2019 establishes mandatory reporting obligations that require professionals – including teachers, health workers, police officers, and social workers – and, more broadly, all persons who know or suspect that a child is being maltreated, to report such concerns to CFPS or the police. Good-faith reporters are protected from civil or criminal liability.

The implementation of mandatory reporting faces several challenges:

- **Awareness:** Many professionals and community members remain unaware of their reporting obligations or uncertain about the threshold for reporting.
- **Fear of consequences:** In small island communities, reporting a neighbour or family member for child maltreatment carries significant social consequences, including ostracism, family conflict, and – in cases involving powerful individuals – threats to personal safety.
- **System capacity:** Even when reports are made, the capacity of CFPS to respond is constrained by the extreme imbalance between caseload and workforce (see Section 4.16 below).[^63]

---


## 4.32 The Child Helpline (1412): National Reporting Infrastructure


### 4.29.1 Establishment and Legal Basis

**National Child Helpline 1412** was established in 2008 in partnership with **Child Helpline International** as a toll-free, 24-hour telephone service for reporting child protection concerns. It received statutory basis in **CRPA 2019 Article 76**, which formalised its role in the national child protection architecture. The helpline operates alongside the broader **Social Services Hotline 1421** (for DV, disability, elderly, and other social service referrals).[^671]

### 4.29.2 Functions and Operations

The 1412 helpline serves multiple functions:
- **Professional reporting channel** for teachers, health workers, police, and other mandated reporters to notify CFPS of concerns
- **Community reporting channel** for neighbours, community members, and relatives
- **Direct access channel** for children and adolescents to seek help themselves
- **Information and referral service** providing guidance to callers about available services

Operated by trained staff who assess urgency and connect callers to CFPS for follow-up. In 2024, the helpline received approximately **2,855 calls**.[^672]

### 4.29.3 Operational Limitations

The helpline's effectiveness is fundamentally constrained by the system capacity behind it:
- 2,855 contacts per year, against a CFPS workforce of 61 social workers, means approximately 47 additional helpline contacts per worker per year – added to existing active caseloads of 70–80 cases
- Response delays from workforce limitations reduce effectiveness for children in immediate danger
- In outer islands, geographic distance means physical presence is impossible for hours or days even with a responsive call
- Awareness of the 1412 number is uneven, particularly among children and communities in outer atolls without active community education

The helpline is necessary but insufficient as a stand-alone mechanism. Its effectiveness is directly proportional to the system capacity behind it – capacity that is currently at critical levels.[^673]


---


## 4.33 The 1412 Childline: Operations and Analysis


### 4.88.1 Establishment and Legal Basis

The national 24-hour child helpline 1412 was established with the assistance of Child Helpline International and has its legal basis in CRPA Article 76(a–h). The helpline receives thousands of contacts annually, with a significant proportion resulting in formal referrals to CFPS or FCSCs.

The 1412 helpline serves multiple functions:
- **Emergency child protection referrals:** Immediate reports of abuse, neglect, or risk requiring urgent response
- **Information provision:** Guidance for children and adults on child protection issues and available services
- **Counselling:** Some emotional support for callers in distress
- **Data collection:** Call data provides an indicator of reporting rates and issue profiles

### 4.88.2 The Helpline as an Access Point for Remote Communities

For children and families on islands without FCSC presence, the 1412 helpline is the primary point of access to the child protection system. A child on an outer island who wants to report abuse, or an adult who suspects a child is being harmed, has no other accessible institutional point of contact. This makes the helpline's capacity, response quality, and referral capability critically important for outer island child protection.

However, the helpline is primarily a routing mechanism, not a service delivery mechanism. Its value depends on the quality and speed of the FCSC or CFPS response that follows a referral – and in outer islands, the response capacity is severely constrained by geography, workforce, and resources.

### 4.88.3 The 1412 Data Gap

Despite receiving thousands of contacts annually, the 1412 data has not been systematically used for trend analysis and resource allocation planning. The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) notes the data gap in reporting case numbers imprecisely ("thousands annually... significant proportion result in formal referrals"). The development of the NCPD digital platform should eventually enable integration of 1412 call data with CFPS case management data – allowing tracking of cases from initial contact through to case closure. This integration has not yet been achieved.[^922]

---


# Part 6: Child Abuse and Neglect: Prevalence, Patterns, and Prevention


## 4.34 Prevalence and Forms of Child Abuse -- Research Evidence


### 4.5.1 Administrative Data on Reported Cases

The Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services (as it was then titled) reported **349 child abuse cases in the period March–May 2018**, of which 48% involved sexual abuse or physical violence. This figure – an average of approximately 116 reported cases per month – provides only a partial picture, as it captures only cases reported to the Ministry and does not include cases reported to police, cases handled informally within communities, or cases that were never disclosed.[^37]

Administrative data on reported child abuse cases for the period 2017–2022 (Table 8.60 in the Rogers, Ali & Naeem analysis) shows the following categories of reported maltreatment: neglect, physical abuse, emotional abuse, sexual abuse, bullying, harassment, witnessing domestic violence, and cyberbullying. The data reveals that sexual abuse consistently constitutes the largest single category of reported cases, followed by physical abuse and neglect. The introduction of cyberbullying as a category in more recent years reflects the growing recognition of online harms to children in a society with high mobile phone and internet penetration.[^38]

### 4.5.2 Survey and Research Data

Population-based survey data paints a broader picture than administrative data alone:

- **15% of adolescents** report having experienced sexual abuse, with rates among girls approximately double those among boys. The gendered pattern of child sexual abuse prevalence is consistent with international evidence but is particularly pronounced in the Maldivian context.[^39]

- **Nearly one-third of secondary school girls** report having experienced sexual abuse – a figure that, if accurate, represents an epidemic-level prevalence of child sexual victimisation within the education system and the broader community.

- The **2007 Women's Health and Life Experiences (WHLE) study** found that childhood sexual abuse prevalence was 12.2% overall and 16.3% in Malé – indicating that the capital, despite its greater concentration of services and institutions, had a higher reported prevalence than the national average. This finding may reflect either genuinely higher rates of abuse in the urban environment (possibly related to overcrowding, the presence of transient populations, and the weakening of community-level informal surveillance) or greater willingness to disclose in an anonymous survey context.[^40]

- **Hospital data from Indira Gandhi Memorial Hospital (IGMH) for the period 2005–2009** recorded 43 rape cases presenting to the hospital, of which 22 involved minors. Almost half of the cases involving minors were gang-rapes, indicating a pattern of group sexual violence against children that intersects with the Maldives' well-documented gang violence problem.[^41]

### 4.5.3 Child Abuse in the Malé Context

The unique dynamics of Malé – the most densely populated city in the world by some measures – create specific child protection risks. A documented pattern involves children from outer island families being sent to relatives in Malé to access educational opportunities. In many cases, these arrangements function well. However, research by Adam (2017) documented that **relatives' children brought to Malé for education may be forced into domestic work** – performing cooking, cleaning, and childcare duties in exchange for accommodation rather than receiving the educational access that was the stated purpose of the arrangement. These children occupy a liminal status: they are family members rather than employees, making their exploitation difficult to identify and categorise within formal child labour or child abuse frameworks.[^42]

The overcrowded living conditions in Malé – with multiple families sharing small apartments – also create heightened risks of sexual abuse through the co-habitation of children with unrelated adult males, including stepfathers, maternal partners, and male tenants sharing accommodation. The privacy deficit inherent in overcrowded housing means that children lack physical spaces of safety within the home.[^43]

---


## 4.35 Child Abuse and Neglect: Prevalence, Patterns, and Case Volume Data


### 4.23.1 Administrative Case Data

The administrative data from MoSFD and CFPS provides the most current picture of officially reported child protection cases:

| Year/Period | Total Cases Received | VAC Cases | % VAC |
|---|---|---|---|
| March–May 2018 | – | 349 (avg. 116/month) | 48% sexual abuse/physical violence |
| 2023 | 2,936 | 1,278 | 43.5% |
| 2024 | – | 2,855 calls to 1412 helpline | – |

*Sources: MoSFD/MoGFSS administrative data; NAPVAC 2024-2028; CRC State Party Report (1412 data)*[^639]

The upward trend from 2018 to 2023 reflects both improved reporting systems and genuinely increased case identification as the FCSC network has matured. The COVID-19 pandemic period (2020–2021) showed a particular spike consistent with patterns observed internationally during lockdown periods – children trapped with abusive caregivers and school-based protective factors withdrawn.

The NCB Statistical Yearbook (Table 8.60) records child abuse cases by type from 2009 to 2020, showing a consistent upward trend throughout the decade. Case types include neglect, physical abuse, emotional abuse, sexual abuse, bullying, harassment, witnessing domestic violence, and cyberbullying.[^640]

The 2,936 new cases received in 2023, added to a CFPS workforce of 61 social workers, translates to approximately 48 new cases per worker per year – added to existing active caseloads. This is the operational reality of Maldivian child protection practice.[^641]

### 4.23.2 Prevalence Studies: True Scale

Administrative case data systematically underestimates true prevalence, capturing only reported and recorded cases. Survey-based studies provide a closer approximation:

**Global School-based Student Health Survey (GSHS) 2014:**
- **15%** of adolescents reported having experienced sexual abuse
- Girls reported sexual abuse at **twice the rate** of boys
- Approximately **33%** of secondary school girls reported having experienced sexual abuse

**Women's Health and Life Experiences Study (WHLE/Fulu, 2007):**
- **12.2%** of women aged 15–49 reported childhood sexual abuse overall
- **16.3%** of women in Malé reported childhood sexual abuse

**IGMH data (2005–2009):**
- 43 rape cases treated at Indira Gandhi Memorial Hospital
- 22 (51%) involved minors
- Almost half of IGMH rape cases involved gang rape – indicating child sexual violence extends beyond intimate or family contexts into organised forms

**Administrative proportions:**
- **30%** of all child protection cases managed by MoSFD involve sexual abuse (MoSFD administrative data)
- Sexual abuse and physical violence together constituted **48%** of cases in the March–May 2018 quarterly dataset[^642]

### 4.23.3 Under-Reporting Drivers

**Social stigma and community silence:** Sexual abuse – particularly intra-familial abuse – may be actively concealed by family members to protect social standing. The survivor's own family may discourage disclosure to prevent the perpetrator (who may be a respected community member) from facing consequences.

**Fear of *zina* prosecution:** For adolescent girls who have reached puberty, disclosure of sexual abuse may trigger scrutiny of their own conduct under *zina* provisions, particularly if pregnancy has occurred. The risk of being prosecuted for the crime committed against them is a documented deterrent to reporting.

**Lack of child-sensitive reporting channels:** While the 1412 helpline exists, children in outer islands may not know about it, may lack phone access, or may fear that calls will be identified in a small community.

**Child's relationship with perpetrator:** Sexual abuse by family members (fathers, uncles, older brothers) is among the least reported forms – because the child is dependent on the perpetrator and disclosure will disrupt the family, and because children often lack language to describe what is happening.

**Institutional distrust:** Communities that have reported abuse and received ineffective or no response lose confidence in the system, reducing future reporting.[^643]

---


## 4.36 Children in Domestic Violence Contexts: The Dual Dynamic


### 4.25.1 The Critical Research Finding

The CFW vulnerability mapping research produced one of the most analytically significant findings regarding the relationship between children and domestic violence:

**Children are simultaneously the primary reason women stay in violent relationships AND the primary reason they eventually leave.**

Women reported staying in violent relationships to: maintain family stability for children; ensure children's access to housing and financial support; avoid the custody consequences of divorce – particularly the risk under Family Act Section 42 of losing custody if they remarried or relocated; and protect children from the disruption of family dissolution.

Women eventually left when: violence escalated to the point where children were directly harmed; women judged that continued exposure to violence was more damaging to children than separation; a specific incident made the existential risk to children (or themselves) undeniable.

**Policy implication:** Child protection and domestic violence response cannot be treated as separate service streams. A child protection response that focuses on the child while ignoring the violence experienced by the child's mother will fail to address the root cause of the child's risk.[^650]

### 4.25.2 Children Witnessing DV as a Child Protection Concern

Under Maldivian law, **causing a minor to witness domestic violence is defined as domestic violence** – treating indirect exposure as a form of child abuse. CFPS protocols mandate that when a child is identified as witnessing DV, the child receives access to medical or psychological care, referral to counselling, and ongoing monitoring even after violence against the other parent ceases.

The CFW research (Section 4, 2026) confirms: "If a child is routinely witnessing one parent abuse the other (a form of psychological harm), Maldivian law treats it seriously – causing a minor to witness domestic violence is defined as domestic violence." CFPS monitors the family over time even after violence ceases, because children who have witnessed DV may present with behavioural or emotional difficulties requiring sustained support.[^651]

### 4.25.3 Children Used as Leverage in Post-Separation Coercive Control

The vulnerability mapping research documented specific patterns of custody weaponisation:
- Ex-partners threatening custody proceedings to prevent women from seeking protection or forming new relationships
- Deliberate prolongation of custody proceedings to maintain ongoing contact and harassment
- Children moved between relatives and across islands to complicate custody enforcement
- Children coached to make negative reports about their mother to social workers and courts
- Fathers awarded formal custody while delegating actual care entirely to relatives – denying mothers custody while providing children with neither stable care nor maternal contact

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) summarise: children are "used as leverage in custody disputes, separated from primary caregivers, dispersed across relatives, and witnessing violence, experiencing direct abuse."[^652]

### 4.25.4 The Jakob Case Study: Practice Reality in DV Context

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) present the composite case of Jakob (age 8), identified as having been beaten by his father who is also violent toward Jakob's mother.

**Best practice:** All child protection professionals from various sectors work together to keep Jakob at home under a protective supervision order, with IBAMA wraparound services – school counsellors and social workers implementing a multi-sectoral support plan.

**Practice reality depends critically on who Jakob's father is:**
- If the father is a **political actor:** the case may be leaked to media rather than properly reported to police or FCSCs – "highlighting the failure of MoGFSS" (Rogers, Ali & Naeem, 2025)
- If the father is a **gang leader or drug trafficker:** the case may not be reported at all due to fear of reprisals; social workers in such cases have received death threats (Human Rights Watch, 2023)
- **Normal family:** the system attempts to follow procedure, but follow-up monitoring is often inadequate due to caseload constraints[^653]

### 4.25.5 Section 42 Family Act: Custody as Post-Separation Control

A mother loses priority custody if she marries a man who is not a *mahram* of her children, or relocates to a different island without the father's consent. Effects on child protection outcomes:
- Women cannot rebuild their lives (through new relationships) while retaining custody of their children from previous marriages
- Women cannot relocate to safety, employment, or family support networks without risking custody loss
- Ex-husbands can use custody threats to prevent women from forming new relationships or moving – effectively perpetuating post-separation coercive control
- The vulnerability mapping literature review documented "custody weaponisation" as a significant feature of post-divorce coercive control, with Section 42 as the legal mechanism enabling it

Analysis of 24 High Court custody cases: in every instance where the mother was awarded custody, she had remained unmarried. Fathers awarded formal custody frequently delegated actual daily care to relatives while denying mothers meaningful contact – producing outcomes that served neither children's welfare nor justice for mothers.[^654]

---


## 4.37 Violence Against Children: The Criminal Justice Funnel


### 4.67.1 Reporting and Case Volume Trends

Child abuse and neglect cases reported to CFPS and FCSCs have increased significantly over the reporting period 2015–2024. The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) attributes this increase to both rising reporting rates (as awareness of reporting obligations and channels increases) and genuine improvements in case identification as the system matures – not necessarily to increases in underlying prevalence.

The National Bureau of Classification Statistical Yearbook (Table 8.60) records child abuse cases from 2009 to 2020, showing a consistent upward trend throughout the decade. The COVID-19 pandemic period (2020–2021) showed a particular spike in abuse and neglect reports, consistent with patterns observed internationally during lockdown periods – periods of increased parental stress, economic pressure, reduced monitoring, and limited children's contact with mandatory reporters in schools and childcare settings.

### 4.67.2 The Criminal Justice Funnel for Child Sexual Abuse

MPS data recorded **291 incidents of child commercial sexual exploitation** investigated in one reporting year during the 2015–2024 period. Child sexual abuse cases investigated by MPS number in the hundreds annually, though the true prevalence is believed to be significantly higher given documented barriers to reporting.

**Barriers to reporting CSA in the Maldivian context:**
- Social stigma and fear of disbelief
- Family pressure to conceal abuse (particularly where the perpetrator is a family member – the most common pattern)
- Lack of confidence in the justice system
- Geographic remoteness and small community sizes in outer islands (where anonymity is impossible and disclosure has severe social consequences)
- For older children who have reached puberty: the intersection with *zina* law

**Conviction rates:** Conviction rates for child sexual abuse remain critically low relative to reported cases. Cases entering the criminal justice system face challenges including: evidence being primarily testimonial; recantation by child witnesses under family or community pressure; lengthy court processes; inadequate training of prosecutors and investigators in child-sensitive techniques; and historically, judicial interpretation of corroboration requirements. The aggregate effect is a criminal justice funnel that loses cases at every stage.

### 4.67.3 Multi-Disciplinary Team Protocol

A formal Multi-Disciplinary Team (MDT) protocol was established in 2022, bringing together social workers, police, health professionals, and prosecutors for complex child abuse cases. MDT meetings are held regularly in Malé and were piloted in three atolls (Addu, Haa Dhaal, and Laamu). Extension to all atolls is planned but faces resource constraints.

The MDT model represents a significant structural advance over the previous fragmented response, in which police, CFPS, prosecutors, and health professionals each operated largely independently, with limited joint working and information sharing. However, the MDT model has not yet produced measurable improvement in conviction rates, partly because the evidentiary and judicial challenges it is designed to address are systemic rather than procedural.

### 4.67.4 Child Trafficking

The Anti-Human Trafficking Act (Law No. 12/2013) criminalises trafficking in persons including children, with child trafficking as an aggravated offence. MoGFSS data from the reporting period identified **21 suspected child trafficking cases** that were referred to or identified by the Ministry, of which a significant proportion did not result in formal investigation.

The trafficking landscape in Maldives is complex:
- As a small tourism-dependent economy with large migrant worker flows, Maldives faces both transit and destination trafficking risks
- The tourism industry creates demand for commercial sexual services, with some risk of child sexual exploitation in resort and tourism contexts
- Migrant children accompanying trafficked parents in situations of labour exploitation face compounded vulnerabilities
- Domestic workers (many migrants) face labour exploitation and trafficking risks

The National Referral Mechanism for trafficking victims operates primarily in Malé, with limited capacity to identify trafficking situations in outer islands or resort zones – a geographic gap with significant implications for child victims.[^876]

### 4.67.5 Online Violence and Digital Safety

Maldives has high internet penetration – among the highest in South Asia – meaning children are exposed to online risks at rates comparable to high-income countries but with a significantly less developed institutional response.

Documented online risks for Maldivian children:
- **Cyberbullying:** A growing phenomenon particularly affecting adolescents
- **Online grooming:** Sexual predation via social media and messaging platforms
- **Sextortion:** Coercion of children via sexual images obtained through deception
- **CSAM:** The MPS Computer Crime and Digital Forensics unit works with INTERPOL on child sexual abuse material investigations, with a number of prosecutions initiated during the reporting period
- **Violent/extremist content:** The intersection with violent extremism concerns – noted in the ICSVE research on prison radicalisation – means that online exposure to extremist content is a child protection risk as well as a security risk

The legal framework for online child safety is developing, with relevant provisions in the Sexual Offences Act, the Penal Code and cybercrime legislation. However, the Government acknowledges that institutional response does not yet match the scale of the risk.[^877]

---


## 4.38 The Social Norms and Prevention Dimension


### 4.74.1 Prevention as an Underdeveloped Priority

Rogers and Ali (2023) and Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) both note that the Maldives' child protection strategy, modelled on the UK system, prioritises severe cases. This emphasis leaves preventative efforts in the background, concentrating on immediate risks rather than underlying needs. The development of family support services, community-based prevention programmes, and awareness-raising initiatives that reduce the incidence of abuse has been systematically underfunded relative to the reactive, investigative functions of the child protection system.

The NAPVAC 2024–2028 (National Action Plan on Violence Against Children) represents the most recent effort to address prevention alongside response – its five strategic areas include Prevention and Behaviour Change as a dedicated domain. However, effective prevention requires sustained investment over many years to produce measurable changes in social norms and abuse incidence, and the Maldivian track record on sustaining such investments is mixed.

### 4.74.2 Positive Parenting and Discipline

The Government's Strategic Action Plan (SAP) – Caring State and Dignified Families (2019) – mandates the Ministry to develop and implement country-wide awareness programmes on parenting skills, including child maltreatment and abuse. The Ministry of Education's positive discipline training programme reached an estimated 40% of teachers nationally by 2023.

However, Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note the social context in which positive parenting messages are received: a cultural transition from a history in which physical discipline of children was normalised and religiously sanctioned, toward a legal prohibition on all corporal punishment, has not been accompanied by adequate community-level engagement with the reasons for this transition or with viable alternatives. The message that "you cannot hit your child" without the accompanying message of "here is how to manage your child's behaviour effectively without physical force" has created a disciplinary vacuum in some families.

### 4.74.3 Community-Level Prevention: IBAMA

The IBAMA programme (discussed in detail in §4.41 of this chapter) represents the primary community-level prevention infrastructure in the Maldivian child protection system. IBAMA's 12-category community structure – covering education, health, social work, justice, religious authority, civil society and others – provides a framework for community-based prevention that is grounded in local social structures.

The key limitation of IBAMA, as documented across multiple sources, is its variable implementation. Where IBAMA functions well – with trained community members, active coordination, clear referral pathways, and responsive institutional connections – it provides a genuine community protective layer. Where implementation is weak – which is common, particularly in smaller or more isolated islands – it provides the appearance of community protection without the substance.[^888]

---


## 4.39 CFW Recommendations: A Full Inventory for Child Protection


The Community and Family Wellbeing (CFW) vulnerability mapping process (2026) produced 49 cross-sectoral recommendations, of which a substantial portion directly address child protection. The following summarises all CFW recommendations with significant child protection implications, organised thematically.[^760]

### 4.47.1 Prevention and Early Intervention

**R1: Relationship and family life education.** Introduce comprehensive relationship education at Grade 10 (secondary school) and at university level, covering communication, boundaries, healthy relationships, conflict resolution, and consent. This education is a primary prevention tool for both intimate partner violence and child abuse – children who understand healthy relationships are better positioned to recognise and report abuse.

**R2: Positive parenting programmes.** Scale the Beleniveriyaa (Positive Parenting) programme to reach all islands, not just Malé and a few regional centres. Include specific content on child development, age-appropriate expectations, non-violent discipline, and recognising child distress signals.

**R3: Teacher training.** All teachers should receive training to recognise warning signs of child abuse, neglect, domestic violence exposure, and mental health difficulties in students, and should know how to make mandatory reports to CFPS and how to support a student through a disclosure.

**R4: Health worker training.** Private disclosure opportunities – when parents or caregivers interact with health workers without the potentially abusive partner present – should be systematically used to ask about violence and child protection concerns. Health workers, particularly nurses and midwives at outer island health centres, require specific training for this role.

**R5: *Imam* and religious leader engagement.** *Imams*, Quran teachers, and Islamic scholars are among the most trusted community figures in Maldivian society. Their engagement in child protection messaging – on positive discipline, the Islamic duty to protect children from harm, and the permissibility of reporting abuse – is essential for reaching communities where secular child protection messaging has limited resonance.

### 4.47.2 Legal and Policy Reform

**R6: Child marriage.** Amend Section 4(b) of the Family Act to remove the court approval exception for marriage before age 18. Establish an absolute minimum marriage age of 18 with no exceptions.

**R7: Corporal punishment.** Enact explicit legal prohibition of corporal punishment in all settings – home, school, alternative care, and judicial – through amendment of the CRPA or stand-alone legislation.

**R8: FGM/C.** Seek a formal ruling from Islamic scholars that *sunnath* is neither obligatory nor recommended in the Maldivian context, and enact explicit legal prohibition of FGM/C framed within the child rights framework.

**R9: Marital rape.** Address the Penal Code Section 130(b) marital presumption of consent, which effectively nullifies the 2021 SOA amendment for prosecuting marital rape – including marital rape involving wives who are children.

**R10: Section 52 SOA evidence burden.** Amend Section 52 of the Sexual Offences Act to reduce the evidentiary threshold for sexual offence prosecution, particularly for cases involving children who rarely have access to multiple categories of physical evidence.

**R11: Birth registration.** Develop a specific protocol for ensuring registration and *wali* (guardian) assignment for children born outside formal marriage, including administrative mechanisms for establishing maternal guardianship where paternal guardianship is unavailable.

**R12: Out-of-wedlock children.** Reform the legal status of out-of-wedlock children to remove discriminatory provisions that increase their vulnerability to placement in informal care and state systems, and that prevent their mothers from exercising full parental rights.

### 4.47.3 Judicial and Police Reform

**R13: Judicial training.** All Family Court judges who make child protection and custody decisions should complete specialised training in: trauma and child development; dynamics of child sexual abuse; coercive control and domestic violence; and evidence-based risk assessment in child protection cases.

**R14: Police sensitisation.** All police officers – not only FCPW specialists – should receive mandatory training on child rights, trauma-informed response, mandatory reporting obligations, and appropriate handling of child victims of crime. This is particularly important in outer atolls where the local officer may be the first responder to child protection emergencies.

**R15: FCPW capacity building.** The Police FCPW should receive dedicated funding for specialised training, including forensic interviewing of children, online CSEA investigation, and multi-agency coordination. The documented failures of the FCPW – treating DV as a personal matter, breaching confidentiality, failing to share protection orders with Corrections – require structured reform.

**R16: Mediation screening.** Before mediation is recommended as a response to family disputes (particularly custody and maintenance disputes), screening for domestic violence and coercive control should be mandatory. Mediation is inappropriate and potentially dangerous where one party is subject to coercive control.

**R17: Custody weaponisation protocols.** Develop specific protocols for cases where custody is used as a mechanism for post-divorce coercive control – including situations where a father uses the Section 42 custody threat to prevent a mother from leaving or seeking safety.

### 4.47.4 Service System Reform

**R18: Emergency accommodation.** Establish emergency safe accommodation in all regional centres – not only Malé – with a specific protocol for rapid access by women and children at immediate risk. The current shelter system provides Amaan Hiyaa (Malé) and Fiyavathi; these cannot serve the outer atoll population effectively.

**R19: Transition support.** The first months after family separation or child removal from parental care are the highest-risk period for re-victimisation and system failure. Transition support protocols – including regular contact, service connection, and practical help – should be mandatory for all cases at this stage.

**R20: Social housing reform.** Social housing allocation criteria should include child protection risk factors. Children in family situations with documented domestic violence or child abuse history should receive priority in social housing allocation, enabling family separation and protection without the families becoming homeless.
