**R21: Child support payment reform.** The current processing pathway for maintenance payments – requiring court orders and Ministry of Finance transfer – takes 30-45 days even when functioning well. Electronic transfer mechanisms and automatic enforcement systems should replace this process.

**R22: Maintenance enforcement.** The approximately MVR 2 million in unclaimed maintenance payments at the Family Court as of the vulnerability mapping research period represents a system failure for children. A dedicated maintenance enforcement unit with attachment of earnings powers should be established.

**R23: Foster care operationalisation.** The Foster Care Regulation (draft 2023, expected 2025) should be enacted and funded. A dedicated foster care programme with recruitment, training, support, and payment for foster carers should be established in each atoll, not only in Malé.

**R24: Alternative care standards.** Minimum standards for all residential care settings – Kudakudhinge Hiya, Fiyavathi, and any future children's homes – should be established by regulation and enforced by the Children's Ombudsperson through regular inspection.

### 4.47.5 Perpetrator Management

**R25: Perpetrator behaviour change programmes.** Evidence-based behaviour change programmes for perpetrators of domestic violence should be established, with mandatory participation for convicted perpetrators as a condition of any custody or unsupervised contact arrangement.

**R26: Enforceable perpetrator conditions.** Protection orders against abusers should include enforceable conditions – GPS monitoring, no-contact requirements, substance use screening – that are actively monitored rather than paper-based obligations with no enforcement follow-up.

**R27: Substance use screening.** All parents in child protection cases where substance use is a risk factor should be required to complete substance use screening and, where indicated, be connected to NDA treatment services with treatment compliance monitored as part of the child protection plan.

**R28: Sex offender management.** The Special Measures Act 12/2009 child sex offender registry should be operationalised and maintained as a tool for monitoring known offenders who may have ongoing contact with children. Current operational status of the registry requires clarification.

### 4.47.6 Economic Security for Families

**R29: Social protection expansion.** Expand the Single Parent Allowance and food aid coverage to all eligible families in outer atolls. Current coverage gaps – documented by the Hameed et al. (2022) disability allowance study and inferred from NSPA data – mean that the most economically vulnerable families, who are often the most at risk for child maltreatment from poverty-related stress, are not receiving available support.

**R30: Employment pathways for single mothers.** Single mothers who are primary carers for children – and who are therefore most constrained in their employment options – should receive targeted employment support including childcare access, flexible work facilitation, and vocational training options accessible from their home island.

**R31: Financial literacy and economic empowerment.** For women who have experienced domestic violence and who may have been denied access to family finances as part of coercive control, post-separation financial literacy and economic empowerment programmes should be available through FCSCs.

### 4.47.7 Digital Safety and Online Protection

**R32: Mandatory digital safety curriculum.** From Grade 4 onwards, all students should receive age-appropriate digital safety education covering online grooming, sextortion, CSAM, and reporting pathways. This curriculum should be developed by Ministry of Education in partnership with CFPS and police.

**R33: National CSEA reporting mechanism.** A dedicated, accessible reporting mechanism for online CSEA – including a dedicated email/web portal and integration with the 1412 helpline – should be established and publicised.

**R34: ISP reporting obligations.** Internet service providers operating in the Maldives should be required by law to report CSAM identified on their networks to the MPS Cyber Crime Unit, consistent with international practice in countries that have enacted online child safety legislation.

### 4.47.8 Workforce and System Capacity

**R35: Social worker recruitment.** A multi-year, funded social work recruitment and retention strategy should be developed, with competitive salaries, clear career pathways, professional registration requirements, and support for outer island posting (including family accommodation, connectivity, and regular supervision via video technology).

**R36: Mandatory reporting training.** All professionals designated as mandatory reporters under CRPA Article 126 should receive annual refresher training on their obligations, identification skills, and reporting procedures. Training records should be maintained by employers and auditable.

**R37: Burnout prevention.** CFPS workers, IBAMA volunteers, and police FCPW officers should have access to structured debriefing, peer support, and professional counselling to address secondary traumatic stress. The current absence of any occupational health support for frontline child protection workers is both a workforce retention and a child welfare risk – burned-out workers cannot provide effective protection.

**R38: Coordination protocol.** A formal, legally-grounded information sharing protocol – specifying what information can be shared between CFPS, MPS, DJJ, Family Court, Ministry of Health, and education institutions in child protection cases – should replace current ad hoc practice. This protocol should be developed with legal advice on its basis in CRPA Article 126 and the constitutional right to privacy.

**R39: Civil society engagement.** ARC (Advocates for Rights of Children), Family Legal Clinic, and other civil society organisations working on child protection should receive sustainable programme funding from MoSFD rather than relying entirely on project-based international donor funding. A civic society engagement strategy should identify and maintain relationships with qualified organisations in each atoll.[^761]

---


# Part 7: Child Sexual Abuse, Exploitation, and Trafficking


## 4.40 Child Sexual Abuse: Patterns, Legal Responses, and Challenges


### 4.6.1 Prevalence Patterns

Child sexual abuse (CSA) in the Maldives follows several documented patterns:

- **Intra-familial abuse** by fathers, stepfathers, uncles, and other male relatives constitutes a significant proportion of cases. The high divorce and remarriage rate in the Maldives means that children are frequently exposed to stepfathers and maternal partners who do not have biological bonds with the child – a risk factor identified in international CSA literature.

- **Peer and gang-related sexual violence** is a growing concern, particularly in Malé, where gang culture intersects with sexual exploitation. The IGMH data documenting that almost half of child rape cases presenting to hospital were gang-rapes points to a pattern of group sexual violence that is qualitatively different from the intra-familial abuse that dominates child sexual abuse caseloads in most jurisdictions.

- **Online sexual exploitation** is an emerging concern given high internet and smartphone penetration among Maldivian children and adolescents, though systematic data on the prevalence of online CSA remains limited.

### 4.6.2 Legal Framework and Gaps

The legal response to CSA is governed primarily by the **Special Measures for Perpetrators of Child Sexual Abuse (Act 12/2009)**, the **Sexual Offences Act 2014**, and the **CRPA 2019**. However, significant gaps persist:

- The intersection of CSA law with the *zina* provisions of Islamic criminal law creates a perverse dynamic in which child victims of sexual abuse may themselves face prosecution for *zina* if the sexual contact is deemed consensual or if evidentiary requirements for proving assault are not met. While the CRPA and Sexual Offences Act establish that children below a certain age cannot consent, the practical application of these provisions in a judicial system that also applies *hadd* punishments remains problematic.

- **Forensic capacity** for CSA investigation and prosecution is limited. The Maldives lacks a dedicated child sexual abuse investigation unit with specialised training, and forensic medical evidence collection is not systematically available outside IGMH in Malé.

- **Victim support services** – including therapeutic intervention, safe housing, and court preparation – are minimal. Children who report sexual abuse may face re-traumatisation through the investigative and judicial process without adequate support.[^44]

---


## 4.41 Child Sexual Abuse: Legal and Practice Dimensions


### 4.24.1 Legal Framework for CSA

Primary legal frameworks:
- **Sexual Offences Act 2014 and 2021 Amendment:** Core criminal offence definitions for rape, sexual assault, child sexual abuse material (CSAM), and exploitation; SOA Section 52's five-types-of-evidence requirement applies equally to child and adult cases
- **Special Provisions Act 2009 (Act 12/2009):** **10–25 years imprisonment without parole** for child sexual abuse offences; Child Sex Offenders Registry; victim compensation; applies to offences against children under 18
- **Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (Art. 133(o), Art. 126):** Definitional framework and mandatory reporting obligations
- **Penal Code 2014:** Adult perpetrators; *zina* provisions creating grey area for adolescent cases

The SOA Section 52 five-types-of-evidence requirement applies to child sexual abuse cases just as it applies to adult rape cases. Prosecution rate: approximately 14% of reported cases. Conviction rate among prosecuted cases: 16.7%. The overwhelming majority of child sexual abuse cases result in no criminal accountability.[^644][^645]

### 4.24.2 The Aysha Case Study: Practice Reality in CSA

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) present the case of Aysha (age 9) disclosing to her teacher that her uncle sexually abuses her nightly:

1. **Teacher reports immediately** under CRPA Art. 126 mandatory reporting – failure to report would attract a two-year imprisonment sentence
2. Aysha **remains with her parents**; the uncle can be kept in custody throughout investigation and trial under the Special Measures Act 12/2009
3. **Medical investigation:** doctors produce a medico-legal report. Notably, "sometimes doctors can share with the social worker that it could be sexual abuse, but not state it in the report" – reflecting professional reluctance to make definitive written findings in cases where community relationships may be affected
4. As Aysha is 9 years old, **questions of consent under Islamic law do not arise** (she is below puberty)
5. Uncle if convicted: **10–25 years** under Special Measures Act

Best practice response: a multi-disciplinary case conference bringing together professionals from different sectors to ensure rapid decision-making – particularly important in time-sensitive decisions such as termination of pregnancy within the 120-day legal window if Aysha were older and pregnant.[^646]

### 4.24.3 The Adolescent Dimension: Where the *Zina*/CSA Boundary Becomes Critical

The sharpest tensions arise when the victim is an adolescent who has reached puberty:

- *Zina* provisions technically apply to the sexual activity – both the perpetrator and the victim may potentially be characterised as parties to *zina*
- A pregnant adolescent who discloses abuse may simultaneously be a victim (of the abuse) and a potential *zina* defendant
- Child protection professionals and doctors are often reluctant to recommend termination of pregnancy within the 120-day legal window due to prevailing religious norms
- If pregnancy is carried to term: the child born is registered as born out of wedlock, with the social and legal consequences that status carries; if grandparents are unable or unwilling to provide care, the infant is placed in institutional care while the young mother faces social and potentially legal consequences
- These situations require multi-sectoral case conferencing bringing together CFPS, health, legal, and religious authorities to navigate decisions in the child's best interest

Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) explicitly cite this as a case type requiring "careful balancing... upholding traditional Islamic values while also ensuring that the fundamental rights and welfare of young people are protected and prioritised."[^647]

### 4.24.4 Online and Digital Child Sexual Abuse

CSAM production, distribution, and possession is criminalised under the SOA 2014 as amended. The MPS Computer Crime and Digital Forensics unit works with INTERPOL on CSAM investigations. NAPVAC 2024–2028 identifies online safety as one of five strategic areas, with outcome targets including reviewed cybercrime regulations and decreases in online violence against children.

The scale of institutional response does not yet match the scale of risk. Maldives has internet penetration rates among the highest in South Asia, high smartphone ownership among adolescents, and limited digital safety education in schools. A comprehensive national online child safety strategy was under development as of 2025. Victim support for children who have experienced digital abuse is primarily available in Malé.[^648][^649]

---


## 4.42 Child Trafficking and Commercial Sexual Exploitation


### 4.26.1 Scale and Patterns

**Documented case volume:**
- **21 suspected child trafficking cases** identified by or referred to MoGFSS during the CRC reporting period – likely significant under-detection given detection capacity limitations
- **291 child commercial sexual exploitation (CSEC) cases** investigated by MPS in one reporting year (CRC State Party Report data)
- The gap between identified cases and investigations, and between investigations and prosecutions, reflects systemic challenges in victim identification, evidence gathering, and inter-agency coordination

**Risk vectors in the Maldivian context:**
- **Domestic work exploitation:** Children from outer islands brought to Malé or resort communities ostensibly for education but subjected to exploitative domestic labour – documented by Adam (2017) as a pattern of children "forced into domestic work" by relatives
- **Tourism industry:** The resort economy creates demand for commercial sexual services, with documented risk of child sexual exploitation in tourist zones and urban entertainment areas
- **Migrant context:** Children accompanying trafficked parents in situations of labour exploitation; children of undocumented migrants with no documentation face barriers to education and services
- **Digital/online trafficking:** Grooming through social media platforms; Maldives' high internet penetration rates and mobile device ownership create accessibility for online traffickers[^655]

### 4.26.2 National Referral Mechanism Limitations

The National Anti-Trafficking Committee oversees a National Referral Mechanism (NRM) for trafficking victims. Victim identification protocols were revised in 2022. However:
- The NRM operates primarily in Malé; capacity to identify trafficking situations in outer islands and resort zones is limited
- Civil society (including Hope for Women) documents that the NRM "in practice operates primarily in Malé"
- Specialist trauma-informed services for child CSEC survivors are available primarily in Malé through CFPS counselling, IGMH psychiatric support, and NGO services; children in outer islands have very limited access to specialist support
- A **National Child Sexual Abuse Response Protocol** was in development by MoSFD with UNICEF support as of 2024, intended to establish clear multi-agency procedures for identification, immediate support, criminal investigation, and longer-term survivor services[^655a]

### 4.26.3 Out-of-Wedlock Children: A Trafficking-Adjacent Vulnerability

Children born through circumstances of abuse – where young mothers are unable to care for them and grandparents are unfit or unwilling – are placed in institutional care. This population of infants in institutional care represents a specific vulnerability: they have no family network, no legal guardian with capacity to advocate for them, and they exist within a system lacking resources for consistent attentive individual care. Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note that babies born in these circumstances are "taken into care and initially placed in an institution, while the possibility of long-term fostering is explored" – a process that can be protracted.[^656]

---


## 4.43 Cross-Cutting Vulnerability: Extremism, Gangs, and Trafficking as Child Protection Issues


### 4.48.1 Violent Extremism: Children at Risk and Children as Victims

The intersection of violent extremism with child protection in the Maldives operates at three levels. First, children and young adolescents are potential targets for radicalisation, particularly those who have been expelled or dropped out of school, who are involved in gang networks, or who are incarcerated in juvenile facilities. The ICSVE prison radicalisation study found a mean age at first arrest of 21.56 years among radicalised prisoners – suggesting that the radicalisation pathway begins in adolescence.[^762]

Second, children growing up in households where a parent or older sibling has been radicalised face a distinctive form of child protection risk. The extremist ideology – including the normalisation of violence as a religious duty, the demonisation of the state and its protective systems, and the isolation from mainstream community – creates a family environment that is harmful to children's development and safety. Children whose fathers are imprisoned for terrorism offences face the same parental incarceration disadvantages documented for drug offence prisoners, compounded by stigma.[^763]

Third, state care facilities – Imaduddin School, Fiyavathi, and the Maafushi prison environment that older juveniles may enter – have been identified in the ICSVE study as sites of radicalisation. Children placed in state care due to child protection concerns may encounter extremist networks in the very facilities designed to protect them. This creates a safeguarding obligation for state care facilities that extends beyond physical safety to include monitoring for and responding to extremist recruitment.[^764]

The NAP on P/CVE 2020-2024 identifies "state care facility staff expressed concern that staff was not equipped to deal with radicalized children and youth" as a specific implementation gap – confirming that this is a recognised but unresolved child protection challenge.[^765]

### 4.48.2 Gang Violence: Children as Victims, Witnesses, and Recruits

Gang violence in Male' creates multiple child protection risks simultaneously:

**Children as victims.** Children living in Male' neighbourhoods with active gang presence face risks of witnessing violence, being caught in violence, and being targeted for intimidation when their family members are involved with gangs on either side of a dispute. Children are not incidental bystanders to gang violence – they are often deliberate targets used to intimidate adults.

**Children as recruits.** The gang recruitment pipeline begins in early adolescence. Boys who drop out of school, who migrate to Male' without family support, and who are seeking belonging and economic opportunity are recruited into gangs through offers of money, status, protection, and social connection. The first step is often minor – running messages, acting as lookout – before escalating to drug distribution and violence.

**Children as witnesses.** Children growing up in households where gang-affiliated family members are present witness the normalisation of criminal networks, drug use, and violence as ordinary features of adult life. This exposure – documented in the child protection literature as "adverse childhood experience" – is a risk factor for future involvement in violence and a protective factor that child protection services are poorly positioned to address.[^766]

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 Community pillar identifies gang involvement as a specific strategic challenge, with outputs targeting safe recreation spaces, youth clubs, mentoring programmes, and diversionary activities as structural alternatives to gang recruitment. The cross-over between these preventive social activities and child protection is intentional – keeping children in structured, supervised activities reduces their exposure to gang recruitment environments.[^767]

### 4.48.3 Divorce and Family Breakdown as Child Protection Context

The Maldives' exceptional divorce rate – approximately 60% of marriages nationally, with women aged 26-40 disproportionately affected – creates a distinctive child protection context. As documented in §4.25 (Children in DV Contexts), and in Part XV (Cross-Cutting Issues §15.4), family breakdown in the Maldivian context is often violent, frequently involves custody weaponisation, and creates poverty for children in the households of divorcing mothers.

Children caught in high-conflict divorces – where Section 42 custody threats are used as coercive control mechanisms, where maintenance is not paid, and where fathers use legal systems to maintain post-separation control over mothers – experience complex, chronic harm that is difficult for the child protection system to recognise and respond to. These children are rarely presenting as victims of acute abuse – they are experiencing the compounding harms of economic deprivation, primary carer instability, coercive family dynamics, and legal uncertainty about their future care arrangements.[^768]

The Ahmed and Riyaz (2024) study on divorce documented that "communities with higher divorce rates experience elevated crime rates, particularly among youth from broken families, who exhibit significantly higher rates of delinquency compared to those from intact families." This creates a direct pipeline from high divorce rates to juvenile justice system contact – the very pathway that the NAPVAC 2024-2028 aims to interrupt through family-level prevention and early intervention.[^769]

---


# Part 8: Child Marriage and Female Genital Mutilation/Cutting


## 4.44 Child Marriage -- Prevalence, Law, and Prevention Efforts


### 4.13.1 Legal Framework and the Family Act Loophole

The CRPA 2019 raised the legal age of marriage to 18. However, a critical legal inconsistency undermines this protection: **Section 4(b) of the Family Act** continues to allow the Family Court to authorise marriage for children who have "reached puberty," effectively creating a judicial loophole through which child marriage can be legally sanctioned. The persistence of this loophole despite the CRPA's clear intent to establish 18 as the minimum marriage age reflects the difficulty of harmonising secular child protection legislation with Islamic family law provisions in a context where the latter carries significant cultural and religious authority.[^66]

### 4.13.2 Prevalence

- The **2014 Census** recorded 106 children aged 15–17 who were married, of whom **58.5% were girls** – reflecting the gendered pattern of child marriage globally and in the Maldives specifically.
- The **ADB (2025)** documented a child marriage rate of **2.2% before age 18**. While this is lower than rates in many South Asian countries, it represents a non-trivial number of children whose right to childhood, education, and development is compromised by early marriage.
- Most concerning is the evidence that **child marriage is reportedly increasing rather than decreasing**, contrary to the trend in most comparable countries. This increase has been attributed in part to the influence of conservative religious movements that endorse early marriage as a means of preventing *zina* (premarital sexual activity).[^67]

### 4.13.3 Unregistered Marriages

A significant dimension of the child marriage problem involves **unregistered marriages** – marriages that are conducted by a religious authority but not registered with the Family Court. Conservative *fatwas* have endorsed unregistered marriages, and the Chief Judge of the Family Court has described unregistered marriages as a "huge challenge" for the justice system. Unregistered marriages leave children – particularly girls – without the legal protections that registration provides, including rights to maintenance, inheritance, and divorce on specified grounds. Children of unregistered marriages may themselves face legal status complications.[^68]

---


## 4.45 Child Marriage: Legal Architecture, Prevalence, and Harm


### 4.31.1 The Legal Framework and the Section 4(b) Loophole

The minimum age of marriage in the Maldives is 18 years for both males and females under the Family Act (Law No. 4/2000) as amended. However, Section 4(b) of the Family Act creates a significant exception: marriage below age 18 may be permitted with **court approval**. The procedure requires an application to the Family Court, which must assess whether the marriage is in the interests of the child. In practice, this judicial oversight mechanism has functioned as a rubber stamp rather than a substantive protection – approvals appear to have been granted routinely, without robust child welfare assessment, legal representation for the child, or systematic consideration of alternatives.[^598]

The JJ Act 2019 and CRPA 2019 reinforced the 18-year minimum as a baseline, and the CRPA's definition of "child" as any person under 18 years creates a clearer framework. However, the Family Act's court approval mechanism remains in place, and its operation has not been systematically evaluated since the CRPA's enactment.[^599]

The CRC Periodic Report Draft acknowledged the issue, noting that "the Maldives Government is aware of the gap created by the court approval provision and has committed to reviewing the Family Act to close the loophole." As of 2024, this review had not been completed.

### 4.31.2 Prevalence Data

Available data confirms that child marriage, while not at crisis proportions by South Asian regional standards, remains a significant child rights concern:

- **2014 Housing and Population Census**: 106 children aged 15-17 were recorded as married, with girls (58.5% of cases) outnumbering boys. The actual number may be higher due to underreporting and non-registration of marriages.[^600]
- **ADB GESI Assessment 2025**: 2.2% of women aged 20-24 were married before the age of 18.
- **Statistics Compendium data**: Marriage below 18 is more common in outer atolls, where court oversight is more limited and community pressure on girls to marry is stronger.
- **Gender disparity**: Girls are consistently more affected than boys – boys' early marriages are relatively rare, while girls face disproportionate pressure to marry early, particularly if they have experienced sexual abuse or become pregnant out of wedlock.

The 2022 Census showed a significant reduction from 2014 figures – the proportion of very young married persons fell – but the structural conditions enabling early marriage, including the Family Act loophole and outer atoll community norms, remain in place.[^601]

### 4.31.3 Drivers and Vulnerabilities

Research on child marriage in the Maldives – including the Vulnerability Mapping Literature Review (2026), the Musawah research (Shafeega, 2018), and the CFW Section 2 analysis – identifies a distinctive Maldivian pattern of drivers:

**Poverty and economic vulnerability.** On outer islands with limited economic opportunities, families with limited resources may view early marriage as a way of reducing the family's economic burden or securing financial support from the groom's family. Girls from poor families are disproportionately at risk.[^602]

**Sexual abuse and pregnancy.** A critical driver that distinguishes the Maldivian context is the use of marriage to "manage" cases of sexual abuse or pregnancy outside wedlock. As documented in §4.24, girls who report sexual abuse or who become pregnant face the risk of *zina* prosecution if the abuse occurred without the five categories of evidence required by Section 52 of the SOA. In this context, marriage – including to the abuser – may be arranged by families as a way of avoiding criminal liability, community shame, or both. This dynamic is documented in the Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) chapter and the Vulnerability Mapping literature.[^603]

**Social norms and honour ideology.** Concepts of family honour, chastity, and respectability – described in §4.21 as inhibitors of abuse reporting – also drive child marriage. A girl who has been seen alone with a boy, who has been touched or abused, or who is perceived as sexually active may be married quickly to protect family honour regardless of whether she has reached 18.[^604]

**Out-of-wedlock birth vulnerability.** Children born to unmarried mothers, documented in §4.11 of the original chapter as facing heightened vulnerability to placement in informal care and state systems, are more likely to be in households where early marriage norms operate.[^605]

**Limited education and aspiration.** In communities where girls' secondary and tertiary education is undervalued, there is no strong competing incentive to delay marriage. The contrast with Male', where girls consistently outperform boys at secondary level and aspire to higher education and employment, is stark – but Male' is atypical. Outer atoll communities where formal employment opportunities for women are limited provide fewer structural incentives to delay marriage.[^606]

### 4.31.4 Consequences and Harm Pathways

The documented consequences of child marriage in the Maldives align with the global evidence base:

**Educational disruption.** Married girls typically leave school upon marriage or, at the latest, upon pregnancy. The statistical association between early marriage and educational attainment truncation is robust. Since the Maldivian economy increasingly rewards formal qualifications, early marriage directly limits girls' lifetime economic potential and is a predictor of poverty in adulthood.[^607]

**Early pregnancy and maternal health risks.** Early pregnancy – which typically accompanies or follows child marriage – carries elevated maternal and neonatal health risks. Adolescent pregnancies are associated with higher rates of anaemia, obstructed labour, obstetric fistula, and maternal mortality. In a health system where outer atoll health centres have limited obstetric capacity and transfer to regional hospitals is constrained by geography, these risks are amplified.[^608]

**Exposure to domestic violence.** The vulnerability mapping research documented that young women who married early – often in relationships with significant age disparities – faced higher rates of intimate partner violence. Young brides with limited education, economic independence, and family support have reduced agency within marriage and fewer practical options for exit.[^609]

**Divorce and serial vulnerability.** The Maldives' exceptionally high divorce rate (approximately 60% of marriages nationally; women aged 26-40 in Greater Malé disproportionately affected) means that many child brides will be divorced in early adulthood, entering the divorce and custody system with less education, fewer assets, and fewer resources than their peers who did not marry early.[^610]

### 4.31.5 Legal and Policy Reform Pathway

CEDAW, the CRC Committee, and the UN Working Group on Discrimination against Women and Girls have all called on the Maldives to remove the Family Act Section 4(b) court approval mechanism and establish an absolute minimum age of marriage at 18 with no exceptions. The ADB GESI Assessment 2025, the NAPVAC 2024-2028, and the CFW recommendations all include this reform as a priority action. The key reform elements identified are:

- **Amendment of Section 4(b)** of the Family Act to remove the court approval exception
- **Alignment of all relevant legislation** (Family Act, Penal Code, JJ Act, CRPA) to confirm the absolute minimum age of 18
- **Mandatory CFPS involvement** in all cases where marriage of a person under 18 is proposed
- **Public awareness programmes** addressing child marriage drivers, particularly in outer atolls
- **Economic empowerment programmes** for adolescent girls as a structural deterrent to early marriage
- **Multi-agency protocols** for cases of sexual abuse and pregnancy where marriage pressure is present[^611]

---


## 4.46 Female Genital Mutilation/Cutting: A Maldives-Specific Analysis


### 4.33.1 Overview and Cultural Context

Female Genital Mutilation/Cutting (FGM/C) in the Maldives takes a distinctive form that sets it apart from the FGM/C documented in sub-Saharan Africa and some parts of the Middle East. Maldivian FGM/C practice is characterised as **Type I (clitoridectomy – specifically Type Ia, removal of the clitoral hood)** and is typically described in local discourse as *sunnath* (a religiously sanctioned practice). The practice is performed on infant and young girls, typically within the first year of life, by female practitioners – often older women with traditional knowledge of the procedure.[^620]

The UNFPA/UNICEF Joint Programme on FGM/C and the WHO classification of FGM/C types both recognise that even the "milder" forms – including removal of the clitoral hood without excision of the clitoris – constitute FGM/C and are prohibited under international human rights frameworks. This classification is contested by some practitioners and religious scholars in the Maldives, who argue that the *sunnath* procedure is categorically different from "real" FGM/C and is a legitimate religious practice. This definitional dispute has impeded policy progress.[^621]

### 4.33.2 Prevalence Estimates

Prevalence data for FGM/C in the Maldives is extremely limited and historically underestimated because the practice occurs in infancy, is predominantly conducted in family and community settings rather than health facilities, and is not captured in national health statistics. The UNFPA country brief and the ADB GESI Assessment 2025 identify Maldivian FGM/C as "prevalent" but acknowledge the absence of nationally representative prevalence surveys.[^622]

The most detailed available evidence comes from qualitative and community research, including:

- Studies and community consultations conducted as part of the CFW vulnerability mapping process (2025-2026) found that the practice is common across all atolls, with higher reported prevalence in outer atolls than in Male'. In Male', urbanisation, exposure to global human rights discourse, and the influence of educated families appear to be reducing prevalence.
- Healthcare workers consulted by UNFPA reported that mothers bringing infants for vaccination at health centres sometimes disclosed that *sunnath* had been performed, but this was not systematically recorded.
- A key finding from the Gender chapter (§5.41) is that healthcare workers themselves – nurses and midwives in particular – sometimes believe the procedure is religiously required and do not actively discourage it.[^623]

### 4.33.3 Legal Status and the Policy Gap

FGM/C is not explicitly criminalised under Maldivian law. The CRPA 2019 provides the strongest available legal basis for characterising FGM/C as child abuse – through its definitions of harm, physical abuse, and exploitation of children – but no prosecution has been attempted under the CRPA or any other instrument.[^624]

The Ministry of Health has, on multiple occasions, issued circulars discouraging FGM/C, characterising it as medically unnecessary and harmful. However, these circulars lack legal force and are not accompanied by enforcement mechanisms. The most recent ministerial guidance (2023) was framed in public health rather than child rights terms, avoiding the religious dimensions of the issue.[^625]

This policy gap – where FGM/C is officially discouraged but not legally prohibited, and where the religious framing of the practice creates resistance to legal reform – has resulted in a situation where the practice continues with low visibility and without accountability. The CFW recommendations and the Gender chapter (§5.113) both identify explicit legal prohibition combined with religiously-literate community engagement as the necessary path forward.[^626]

### 4.33.4 Religious Debate and the Path to Policy Change

The Maldives Islands represents an important case study in the global FGM/C policy challenge: how to address a practice that is framed as religiously sanctioned within a predominantly Muslim community without triggering backlash that drives the practice further underground. The global scholarship on FGM/C in Muslim-majority contexts – including the work of scholars such as El-Damanhoury and the UNFPA-UNICEF research programme – has consistently found that Islamic scholars and *fatwas* vary significantly on whether *sunnath* is obligatory, recommended, or merely permitted, and that many Muslim-majority countries have successfully prohibited or significantly reduced FGM/C without conflict with religious authority.[^627]

In the Maldivian context, the most promising avenue for policy change identified in the vulnerability mapping process involves:

1. **Engagement with the Islamic Affairs Ministry** and the Supreme Council of Islamic Scholars to seek a formal ruling clarifying that *sunnath* is neither obligatory nor recommended under Islamic jurisprudence applicable to the Maldives
2. **Amendment of the CRPA or enactment of stand-alone legislation** explicitly prohibiting FGM/C, framed within the child rights framework rather than exclusively as a gender issue
3. **Training of health workers** – particularly nurses and midwives at health centres and regional hospitals – to identify FGM/C, provide accurate information to families about its health and rights implications, and document cases
4. **Community education** targeting mothers and grandmothers (the primary practitioners and decision-makers in infant FGM/C) through trusted community intermediaries including *imams*' wives, women leaders, and Women's Development Committee members[^628]

---


# Part 9: Juvenile Justice


## 4.47 Juvenile Justice: Rehabilitation vs. Retribution


### 4.9.1 Historical Context

The **Juvenile Court** was established in 1997 as a specialised court for hearing cases involving children in conflict with the law. However, for more than two decades, the Juvenile Court operated without a comprehensive juvenile justice statute, relying instead on general criminal law provisions and ad hoc judicial practice. The absence of a statutory framework meant that juvenile justice practice varied substantially depending on the presiding judge, the nature of the offence, and the political dynamics of the case.[^49]

The age of criminal responsibility was set at 10 prior to the Juvenile Justice Act 2019 – one of the lowest ages of criminal responsibility in the region. Children as young as 10 could be prosecuted, convicted, and sentenced, including to custodial sentences served in facilities that did not separate juveniles from adult offenders.

Between **2013 and 2015, five juveniles were sentenced to death** in the Maldives, drawing international condemnation and highlighting the punitive orientation of the pre-2019 system. These death sentences – which were not carried out but remained on the books – were imposed for murder convictions, though the circumstances of the offences typically involved gang violence in which the juveniles were themselves victims of exploitation and coercion by adult gang leaders.[^50]

### 4.9.2 The Juvenile Justice Act 2019

The JJ Act 2019 introduced several critical reforms:

- Raising the age of criminal responsibility from 10 to 15.
- Prohibiting the death penalty for offences committed by persons under 18.
- Establishing rehabilitation and reintegration as the primary objectives of the juvenile justice system.
- Requiring the use of diversion and alternative measures before resorting to formal prosecution.
- Mandating legal representation for juveniles in all proceedings.

However, the Act did **not abolish judicial flogging** for juveniles, meaning that children convicted of *zina* or other offences carrying *hadd* penalties can still be sentenced to flogging. This omission was criticised by international human rights bodies and children's rights organisations.[^51]

### 4.9.3 Rehabilitation Outcomes

Data on juvenile rehabilitation outcomes is concerning. Between **2010 and 2015, 89 juveniles were enrolled in rehabilitation programmes**, but only **11.2% successfully completed** their programmes. The low completion rate reflects multiple factors: the inadequacy of rehabilitation programme design and resourcing; the absence of community-based follow-up support; the pull of gang networks that actively recruit juveniles back into criminal activity upon release; and the limited educational and employment opportunities available to young people who have been through the justice system.[^52]

**Drug offences are the primary driver of juvenile contact with the justice system.** The Maldives' significant drug problem – particularly heroin and methamphetamine use among young people – means that a substantial proportion of juveniles entering the justice system do so for drug-related offences, including possession, trafficking, and offences committed under the influence of drugs. The intersection of the juvenile justice system with the drug policy framework creates a complex challenge: many juveniles in the justice system are themselves victims of exploitation by drug trafficking networks and are simultaneously in need of treatment, rehabilitation, and protection.[^53]

### 4.9.4 Detention Conditions

The Maldives lacks **separate juvenile detention facilities**. Juveniles who are remanded or sentenced to custody are held in facilities that do not adequately separate them from adult offenders, exposing them to risks of violence, exploitation, and further criminalisation. The absence of purpose-built juvenile facilities reflects both fiscal constraints and a historical lack of political priority for juvenile justice infrastructure.[^54]

The **National Reintegration Centre**, established under the Anti-Terrorism Act, has attempted to address the reintegration of children who have been exposed to extremist ideologies or who have been recruited into extremist activities. However, only three children have been successfully reintegrated through this facility, highlighting the challenges of a programme operating with minimal precedent, limited resources, and the sensitive political dynamics of counter-extremism in the Maldives.[^55]

---


## 4.48 Juvenile Justice: The Pre-2019 System and Its Failures


### 4.77.1 The Pre-2019 Legal Vacuum

Prior to the Juvenile Justice Act (2019), children in conflict with the law were processed through the adult criminal justice system with limited child-specific provisions. There was no clearly defined minimum age of criminal responsibility. Children above an undefined threshold could be tried in adult courts, detained in adult facilities, and sentenced under the general penal framework. No dedicated juvenile rehabilitation infrastructure existed – children who received custodial sentences entered the adult corrections system.

The conditions experienced by children in conflict with the law in this period were extensively documented by the HRCM. Between 2013 and 2015, **five juveniles were sentenced to death** in the Maldives – sentences that were ultimately not carried out but that illustrated the severity of the human rights gap in the pre-reform system. The corporal punishment briefing (Owen, 2015) documented that corporal punishment was lawful for juveniles who had reached puberty in *hudood* cases. The cumulative picture was of a system that treated children primarily as security threats rather than as persons with developmental needs and rights.[^895]

### 4.77.2 The Juvenile Court (Established 1997)

The Juvenile Court was established in 1997, predating the Juvenile Justice Act by over two decades. Aboobakuru (2017), writing from within the Juvenile Court system, documented the court's operational reality before the 2019 reforms:

- Drug offences were the primary driver of juvenile contact with the justice system
- Juvenile drug arrests increased from 16 in 2001 to 79 in 2006 and **164 in 2007** (representing 29% of total juvenile arrests in 2007)
- Between 2010 and 2015, **89 juveniles were enrolled in rehabilitation programmes** but only **11.2% successfully completed** them
- The low completion rate reflected inadequate programme design, absence of community-based follow-up, the pull of gang networks, and limited employment opportunities for justice-involved youth

The Juvenile Court's experience illustrated a paradox that would become central to the 2019 reform debate: children were being processed through a specialised court, but the court had almost no tools beyond custody and rehabilitation that were designed for children's developmental needs. Without family support services, mental health interventions, education pathways, drug treatment, or community supervision mechanisms, the Juvenile Court was sending children through a process that produced neither rehabilitation nor accountability.[^896]

### 4.77.3 The 2019 Reform: Juvenile Justice Act

The Juvenile Justice Act (Law No. 19/2019) represented a fundamental restructuring grounded in rehabilitation, reintegration, and the best interests of the child:

**Age thresholds:**
- Minimum age of criminal responsibility: 10 years (though the CRC Committee recommends at least 14)
- Full criminal responsibility: 15 years
- Juvenile justice framework applies: up to 18 years
- Children below 10: no criminal responsibility whatsoever

**The diversion programme:** For first-time and minor offenders, diversion from prosecution is available. Eligible children undergo structured programmes that may include counselling, family conferencing, community service, and supervised activities. In the first three years (2020–2022), hundreds of children were diverted from prosecution.

**Rehabilitation facilities:**
- *Imaduddin School for Boys*: secure rehabilitation facility for male juvenile offenders; provides education, vocational training, sports, religious education, and psychosocial support; HRCM noted infrastructure concerns in 2023
- *Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre*: facility for female juvenile offenders; also serves girls in protective care – a dual function raising concerns about appropriate environment for non-offending children

**Sentencing options:** Formal caution, conditional discharge, supervision orders, community service, probation, and as a last resort, placement in a rehabilitation facility. Imprisonment in adult facilities is prohibited for children under 18.

**Prohibitions:** Death penalty and life imprisonment for offences committed by persons below 18; physical punishment, degrading treatment or humiliation in rehabilitation facilities.

**Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ):** Established under the Ministry of Home Affairs, operationalised in 2020, budget MVR 22 million (2024).

**Juvenile Court protocol improvements:** Child-friendly procedures introduced – separate waiting areas, informal seating, public excluded from hearings, specialist judicial training. A pilot child-friendly justice initiative (UNICEF-supported) equipped the Juvenile Court with child-appropriate interview rooms and trained staff in child-friendly communication.[^897]

### 4.77.4 Ongoing Challenges: Legal Representation, Remand, Gangs, Recidivism

Despite the structural improvements of the 2019 Act, Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) and the CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) document persistent operational challenges:

**Legal representation:** Children in conflict with the law are entitled to legal representation under the Act, but legal aid for children is extremely limited. The public defender system is under-resourced. Many children appear in Juvenile Court without adequate legal counsel, particularly where family resources are limited.

**Remand detention:** While the Act limits pre-trial detention, practical pressures – including difficulty securing appropriate community supervision – can result in children spending extended periods in pre-trial detention. Pre-trial detention in a secure setting is particularly harmful for children whose involvement in conflict with the law may be linked to abuse, neglect, or trauma.

**Gang involvement:** Gang recruitment of children is a significant and growing concern in Malé and other urban centres. Gang leaders recruit young people – sometimes children – as members for drug distribution, extortion and violence. A comprehensive gang prevention strategy is under development by MoSFD, DJJ, and NDA. The use of minors for distribution is partly strategic: minors historically faced less severe penalties than adults, creating a rational criminal logic that exploits juvenile justice protections.

**The Hudood complication post-2019:** Despite the JJ Act's protections, a child who has reached puberty can still be charged with *hudood* offences. The Act prohibits the death penalty but does not abolish flogging for *hudood* cases. This means a post-pubescent child convicted of a *hudood* offence – including *zina* – may face corporal punishment, creating direct conflict between the child rights framework and the Shari'ah framework.

**Recidivism data gap:** Data on reoffending rates for children who complete diversion programmes or exit rehabilitation facilities is limited. Anecdotal evidence from DJJ and CFPS suggests reintegration is more successful for children who maintain family connections and access education and employment post-release.[^898]

---


## 4.49 Juvenile Justice: The Full System Analysis


### 4.27.1 Historical Context: Pre-2019 Failures

**Pre-1997:** No dedicated juvenile justice institution existed. Children in conflict with the law were processed through adult systems with limited specific provisions.

**1997:** The Juvenile Court was established in Malé – one of the older specialised child justice institutions in the country. However, the court operated without a comprehensive legislative framework.

**Pre-2019 systemic failures:**
- Age of criminal responsibility was undefined or set at 10 years
- No separate juvenile detention facilities – juveniles held alongside adults
- Rehabilitation completion rate: only **11.2%** (10 of 89 admissions) between 2010 and 2015
- **Five children sentenced to death** in separate cases between 2013 and 2015
- A **2014 Regulation** allowed children as young as 7 to be sentenced to death for intentional murder, with sentences executable upon turning 18
- Drug offences as primary entry point: juvenile drug arrests rose from 16 (2001) to 79 (2006) to 164 (2007, representing **29% of all drug arrests** that year)[^657]

### 4.27.2 The JJ Act 2019: Comprehensive Reform

**Minimum age of criminal responsibility:** Set at 10 years (with full criminal responsibility attaching at 15). The CRC Committee's General Comment No. 24 (2019) recommends a minimum of at least 14; the government has acknowledged this gap.

**Rehabilitation priority:** The Act explicitly prioritises rehabilitation over retribution, establishing a diversion programme and community-based alternatives to detention.

**Death penalty prohibition:** Prohibits both the death penalty and life imprisonment for offences committed by children – addressing the 2013–2015 death sentencing cases.

**Child-appropriate sentencing:** Formal caution, conditional discharge, supervision orders, community service, probation, rehabilitation facility placement. Imprisonment in adult facilities prohibited for children under 18.

**Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ):** Established under Ministry of Home Affairs, operational 2020.

**Diversion programme:** Hundreds of children diverted in the programme's first three years (2020–2022) through counselling, family conferencing, community service, and supervised activities.

**NON-abolition of flogging:** The Act does NOT abolish *hadd* corporal punishment (judicial flogging) for children who have reached puberty – one of the Act's most significant remaining gaps. The CRC Committee has repeatedly recommended complete abolition; this recommendation remains unimplemented.[^658][^659][^660]

### 4.27.3 Remaining Concerns

**Legal representation:** Children are entitled to legal representation under the JJ Act but legal aid is extremely limited in practice. The public defender system is under-resourced. Many children appear in Juvenile Court without adequate counsel. A specialist juvenile legal aid programme is in development in partnership with the Attorney General's Office.

**Remand detention:** Pre-trial detention of children remains a concern despite the Act's limits and preference for bail and supervision orders. Practical pressures can result in extended pre-trial detention.

**Children in gangs:** Gang involvement is a significant and growing concern in Malé and other urban centres. Gangs involved in drug distribution, extortion, and violence recruit young people – sometimes children – as drug runners, look-outs, and members. Gang membership intersects with drug use, school dropout, and exposure to violence. A comprehensive gang prevention strategy involving MPS, MoSFD, DJJ, and NDA was under development as of 2025.[^662]

**Recidivism and reintegration:** Data on reoffending rates after diversion or rehabilitation facility release is limited. DJJ and CFPS evidence suggests reintegration is more successful for children who maintain family connections and access education and employment pathways post-release. Strengthening post-release support is a DJJ priority.

### 4.27.4 Juvenile Drug Arrests: Rising Trends

| Year | Juvenile Drug Arrests | Context |
|---|---|---|
| 2001 | 16 | First recorded data point |
| 2006 | 79 | Nearly 5x increase from 2001 |
| 2007 | 164 | 29% of all drug arrests nationally |
| 2023 | 120 juvenile cases (MPS) | Post-JJ Act baseline |
| 2024 | 213 juvenile cases (MPS) | 77% increase year-on-year |

*Sources: DJJ records; MPS Bureau of Crime Statistics; UNODC Maldives*[^657]

The 77% year-on-year increase from 2023 to 2024 (120 to 213 cases) indicates either dramatically improved detection, a genuine surge in youth drug crime, or both. Drug offences remain the primary driver of juvenile justice contact – a pattern with roots in the 1970s drug crisis and unchanged by the 2019 legislative reforms.

### 4.27.5 Rehabilitation Facilities in Detail

**Imaduddin School for Boys:** Secure rehabilitation facility for male juvenile offenders. Provides education, vocational training, sports, religious education, and psychosocial support. HRCM 2023 monitoring visit noted infrastructure improvements were needed. Operational challenge: maintaining a therapeutic and educational environment for a population that includes young people with serious drug dependencies and gang affiliations.

**Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre:** For female juvenile offenders, but also serves as a protective care facility for girls who cannot be managed in community settings (severely abused girls, girls at risk of forced marriage, girls with nowhere safe to live). The dual function – rehabilitation for offenders AND protective care for non-offending girls – is inconsistent with the best interests principle and international alternative care standards. Placing a child in need of protection in the same institutional environment as children who have committed serious offences raises serious child rights concerns.[^663]

---


## 4.50 The Juvenile Justice System: Architecture, Operations, and Reform


### 4.71.1 Legislative Framework

The Juvenile Justice Act (Law No. 19/2019) established a comprehensive juvenile justice system replacing fragmented provisions in the Penal Code. Key provisions:

- **Minimum age of criminal responsibility:** 10 years (with children below 10 unable to be held criminally responsible). The CRC Periodic Report acknowledges that 10 is below the Committee's recommendation of at least 14 years in General Comment No. 24 (2019), and the Government is reviewing this threshold.
- **Full criminal responsibility:** Attaches at age 15, though children aged 15–18 remain subject to the juvenile justice framework
- **Diversion:** A diversion programme exists for first-time and minor offenders, with presumption toward non-criminal resolution
- **Prohibition of imprisonment for children under 15:** Except in exceptional circumstances
- **Child-appropriate sentencing:** Community service, probation, counselling and rehabilitation
- **Prohibition of death penalty and life imprisonment:** For offences committed by persons below 18
- **Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ):** Established as the responsible authority for juvenile rehabilitation facilities

### 4.71.2 The Hudood Complication

Despite the JJ Act's protections, a significant legal complexity arises from *hudood* offences. Under the JJ Act, a child who has reached puberty can be charged with committing a *hudood* offence. The Act prohibits the death penalty but does not abolish flogging. This means that a child aged 13 or above who has reached puberty and is charged with a *hudood* offence may face corporal punishment – creating a direct conflict between the child's rights under the JJ Act and the penalties available under Sharia-based law for *hudood* crimes.

El-Dakkak (2000) in *The Application of CRC in the Republic of Maldives from the Perspective of Islamic Law* advocated for the consideration of mental maturity rather than physical puberty to establish criminal responsibility – a position consistent with the evolving capacities principle in the CRC but not yet reflected in Maldivian law.

### 4.71.3 The DJJ and Rehabilitation Facilities

The Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ) was operationalised in 2020 with responsibility for rehabilitation facilities and juvenile justice coordination. Key DJJ facilities:

- **Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre for Girls:** Monitored by HRCM; concerns raised about overcrowding, programming adequacy, staffing ratios and physical environment
- **Imaduddin School for Boys:** Subject to infrastructure improvements following HRCM monitoring

DJJ's operational budget is MVR 22 million (2024) – a relatively small budget for a system serving the juvenile justice population of a national child population of 115,000.

The JJ Act prohibits any form of physical punishment, degrading treatment, or humiliation of children in conflict with the law or in rehabilitation facilities. DJJ has developed internal monitoring protocols with regular independent inspections. However, HRCM monitoring reports have raised concerns that reflect the wider workforce and resource constraints of the child protection system: understaffed facilities run by undertrained workers, managing children with complex needs and trauma histories, without adequate therapeutic programming.[^883]

### 4.71.4 The Distinction Between Child Protection and Juvenile Justice

A recurring theme in Maldivian child protection discourse is the blurring of the line between child welfare (children in need of protection) and juvenile justice (children in conflict with the law). Historically, the URC and CFPS were responsible for attending to children involved in the criminal justice system – a combined function that was only separated with the enactment of the JJ Act 2019. The establishment of the DJJ as a distinct institutional body represents structural recognition that these are different functions requiring different expertise.

However, in practice – particularly for children who are simultaneously victims and perpetrators, or whose involvement in criminality results directly from abuse and neglect – the distinction is less clear than it appears in legislation. A child recruited into a gang through the care system, or a child who has experienced years of abuse that contributes to violent behaviour, requires both child protection and juvenile justice responses simultaneously. The institutional separation between CFPS and DJJ, without adequate joint working protocols, risks leaving such children falling through the gap between both systems.[^884]

---


# Part 10: Alternative Care, Foster Care, and Leaving Care


## 4.51 Alternative Care: Foster Care, Institutional Care, and Kinship Care


### 4.10.1 Overview of the Alternative Care Population

As of **March 2023, 187 children were in alternative care** in the Maldives – 82 girls and 105 boys. This figure represents approximately **0.02% of all children** in the country (128,208 total), a rate that is very low by international standards. However, the low rate of formal alternative care should not be interpreted as indicating a low level of need; rather, it reflects the limited capacity of the formal care system, the preference for informal kinship arrangements (which are not captured in official data), and the high threshold for formal state intervention in family life.[^56]

The distribution of children in alternative care is overwhelmingly institutional: **91% of children in alternative care (approximately 170 children) are in residential (institutional) settings**, while only **18 registered foster carers care for 16 children (approximately 9%)**. This ratio – with institutional care vastly outnumbering family-based care – is contrary to international best practice, which strongly favours family-based placements (foster care and kinship care) over institutional care on the basis of extensive evidence that institutional settings are associated with poorer developmental outcomes, attachment difficulties, and elevated risks of abuse.[^57]

### 4.10.2 Institutional Care: History and Current Structure

The history of institutional care for children in the Maldives reflects an evolving understanding of children's needs, though reform has been slow and incomplete:

- **Islaahiya (1979–2013):** The first institutional facility for children, Islaahiya functioned as a reform school and residential care facility without clear differentiation between children in need of protection and children in conflict with the law. Children were placed in Islaahiya for reasons ranging from delinquency to parental abandonment to disability. The facility operated for 34 years before its closure in 2013.

- **Kudakudhinge Hiya (2004):** Established in Villingili for children aged 0–9, Kudakudhinge Hiya provided residential care for the youngest children in state care. The facility highlighted the particular challenges of institutional care for infants and very young children, for whom the absence of consistent primary caregiving relationships poses significant developmental risks.

- **CTCC – Children's Temporary Care Centre (2009–2013):** A transitional facility that provided short-term residential care.

- **Aman Hiya (2006):** A residential facility for girls aged 9–18, located in Kudakudhinge Hiya campus. Aman Hiya served girls who could not be maintained in family or community settings, including girls who had been sexually abused, girls in conflict with the law, and girls from families unable to provide care.

- **Fiyavethi (2016):** Established with support from the Qatar Foundation for children under 13. Fiyavethi represented a more modern approach to residential care, with smaller group sizes and a more child-centred physical environment.[^58]

### 4.10.3 Post-COVID Restructuring

Following the COVID-19 pandemic, which exposed the vulnerability of large institutional settings to infectious disease outbreaks, the government undertook a restructuring of the residential care system. The single large-facility model was replaced with **nine smaller residential units**, each housing **18–24 children**. This transition toward smaller, more home-like settings is consistent with international deinstitutionalisation trends and represents a positive reform, though it does not address the fundamental question of over-reliance on residential care relative to family-based alternatives.[^59]

### 4.10.4 Foster Care: Critical Underdevelopment

With only **18 registered foster carers** caring for 16 children, the formal foster care system in the Maldives is critically underdeveloped. The low number of registered foster carers reflects multiple factors:

- The absence of a developed foster care recruitment, assessment, training, and support infrastructure.
- Cultural norms that favour kinship-based care arrangements over the placement of children with unrelated families.
- The Islamic prohibition on formal adoption (*tabanni*), which means that foster care in the Maldives cannot provide the permanency and legal status that adoption offers in non-Islamic jurisdictions. Foster carers are providing care without the full legal parental relationship that adoption would confer.
- Limited financial support: the foster parent allowance, while available, is not adequate to fully cover the costs of caring for an additional child, particularly in the high-cost environment of Malé.[^60]

The **Alternative Care Regulation 2020-R-69** and the **Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30** provide the regulatory framework for alternative care placements and the return of children from care to family settings. However, the operationalisation of these regulations – including the development of assessment tools, placement matching procedures, and post-placement support mechanisms – remains a work in progress.[^61]

---


## 4.52 Alternative Care: Institutional and Family-Based Systems


### 4.28.1 Current Population

As of **March 2023**, 187 children are in formal alternative care: 82 girls, 105 boys. Approximately **0.02% of all children** – very low by international standards, reflecting limited formal system capacity rather than low need.

| Care Type | Number | Percentage |
|---|---|---|
| Residential/institutional care | ~170 | 91% |
| Foster care | 16 | 9% |
| Registered foster carers | 18 | – |

*Source: MoSFD administrative data; CRC State Party Report 2021*[^665]

The 91% institutional vs. 9% family-based distribution is the **inverse of international best practice**. The UN Guidelines for the Alternative Care of Children (2010) strongly favour family-based placements on the basis of extensive evidence that institutional settings are associated with poorer developmental outcomes, attachment difficulties, and elevated risks of abuse within the institution.[^666]

### 4.28.2 History of Institutional Care

**Islaahiya (1979–2013):** First institutional facility. No differentiation between children needing protection and children in conflict with the law – children were placed for reasons ranging from delinquency to parental abandonment to disability. Operated for 34 years before closure.

**Kudakudhinge Hiya (2004):** Children aged 0–9 in Villingili. Highlights developmental risks of institutional care for very young children, for whom consistent primary caregiving relationships are essential. Remains operational as one of two primary state children's shelters.

**CTCC – Children's Temporary Care Centre (2009–2013):** Transitional short-term residential care.

**Aman Hiya (2006):** Residential facility for girls aged 9–18, including girls who had been sexually abused, girls in conflict with the law, and girls from families unable to provide care.

**Fiyavethi/Fiyavathi (2016):** Qatar Foundation support; children under 13; smaller group sizes and more child-centred physical environment – representing a more modern approach.[^667]

**Post-COVID restructuring:** Following the pandemic, which exposed the vulnerability of large institutional settings to infectious disease outbreaks, the government restructured residential care into **nine smaller residential units**, each housing **18–24 children**. This transition toward smaller, more home-like settings is consistent with international deinstitutionalisation trends and represents a genuine improvement, though it does not address the fundamental over-reliance on residential relative to family-based care.[^668]

### 4.28.3 Foster Care: Critical Underdevelopment

With only 18 registered foster carers caring for 16 children, the formal foster care system is critically underdeveloped. Root causes:

**Absence of foster care infrastructure:** No developed system for recruiting, assessing, training, and supporting foster carers exists at scale. Building this requires sustained investment, professional capacity, and regulatory development.

**Cultural preference for kinship care:** The dominant cultural model for alternative care in Maldivian society is informal extended family arrangements – grandparents, aunts, uncles, or relatives taking in children when parents cannot care for them. These arrangements operate largely without formal legal oversight, assessment, or monitoring. While kinship care can be highly effective, the same family dysfunction that placed the child at risk in their immediate family may be present in the extended family network.

**Islamic prohibition on *tabanni*:** Formal adoption as practised in Western legal systems is not recognised in Islamic law. The *kafala* (guardianship) framework allows individuals to take responsibility for a child's care, education, and welfare without legally severing biological family ties or changing the child's name or inheritance rights. *Kafala* provides guardianship without full legal parental status – potentially less attractive to prospective long-term carers.

**Financial inadequacy:** The foster parent allowance under the Social Protection Act is available but insufficient to cover costs of caring for an additional child, particularly in Malé.

**Regulatory underdevelopment:** The Alternative Care Regulation 2020-R-69 and Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30 provide the framework, but operationalisation – assessment tools, placement matching, post-placement support – remains a work in progress. A Draft Foster Care Regulation (2023) is expected to be finalised and promulgated in 2025, establishing legal procedures for recruiting, assessing, and supporting foster families.[^669]

### 4.28.4 Informal Kinship Care: The Invisible System

Census 2022 data indicates approximately **9% of children aged 0–17 live in alternative care arrangements** – far larger than the 187 in formal state care. The vast majority are in informal kinship arrangements that:
- Receive no state financial support
- Are subject to no formal assessment of suitability or safety
- Have no legal framework governing carers' rights and obligations
- Are invisible to monitoring systems

Children in informal kinship care who experience abuse or neglect by their kinship carers have no automatic pathway to protection – their situation may be harder to detect precisely because the informal arrangement obscures their true living situation.[^670]

---


## 4.53 Children in Institutional Care: Detailed Analysis


### 4.54.1 The 91% Institutional Care Problem

The most significant failing of the Maldivian alternative care system – relative to international best practice – is the near-complete reliance on institutional care. Of 187 children in alternative care as of March 2023, 91% (approximately 170 children) are in residential institutions, and 9% (approximately 17 children) are with foster carers. International evidence consistently demonstrates that children under 3 in particular, and children more generally, experience significantly worse outcomes in institutional care than in family-based care: poorer cognitive development, poorer social and emotional development, poorer physical health, and higher rates of behavioural and mental health difficulties.[^822]

The 91% institutional figure reflects three structural realities:

1. **Foster care is essentially non-existent.** Eighteen registered foster carers – serving 17 children in a country with 128,208 children – represents a system that exists on paper but has not been operationalised. The Alternative Care Regulation (2020-R-69) provides the legal framework. The Foster Care Regulation (draft 2023) was expected to provide operational detail. Neither has been backed by the recruitment campaign, training programme, support system, and payment rates needed to build a viable foster care network.

2. **Informal kinship care is extensive but invisible.** Many Maldivian children live with extended family members – grandparents, aunts and uncles, older siblings – in arrangements that are informal, undocumented, and unsupported by the state. The 2022 Census found that 9% of children lived in "alternative care arrangements" – a figure that encompasses formal foster care (17 children) and the much larger informal kinship care population. These children and their informal carers receive no assessment, no support, and no monitoring from CFPS, yet they are among the most vulnerable children in the system.

3. **The CRC reservation on adoption.** Article 21 of the CRC covers adoption as a permanent alternative care option. The Maldives' reservation on this Article reflects the Islamic law prohibition on adoption in its traditional form (*kafala* – a guardianship arrangement rather than legal adoption – is permitted, but full legal adoption with new parentage is prohibited). This means that a child without parents who needs permanent family membership cannot access it through the Maldivian legal system, limiting permanence options to long-term foster care or institutional care.[^823]

### 4.54.2 Entry Routes into Alternative Care

Children enter alternative care through several pathways, each with different legal basis and different implications for case management:

**CRPA Article 80 – Interim Assessment Order:** Authorises temporary removal (up to 14 days, renewable) for initial assessment when there is reasonable concern for a child's immediate safety. Issued by the Family Court on CFPS application. Children placed under IAO may be held at Kudakudhinge Hiya during the assessment period.

**CRPA Article 90 – Child Protection Order:** A longer-term protective order that may include conditions on parental contact, mandatory services, and, in the most serious cases, removal from parental care. Children placed under CPOs may be placed in Kudakudhinge Hiya, Fiyavathi, or – if available – with foster carers.

**CRPA Article 93 – Child Supervision Order:** A community-based order that keeps the child with family while imposing conditions (mandatory CFPS monitoring, requirements for parents to complete programmes) and requiring regular review. Supervision Orders are intended as an alternative to removal for families where risk can be managed in the home.

**Voluntary placement:** Parents or caregivers may voluntarily request placement of a child in state care where they are unable to provide adequate care – due to illness, incarceration, economic crisis, or other circumstances. Voluntary placements should, in principle, have clear case plans and regular review with a view to reunification, but in practice may become indefinite in the absence of robust case management.[^824]

**Juvenile justice pathway:** Children convicted of offences under the JJ Act may be sentenced to residential placement in Imaduddin School or Fiyavathi. These placements overlap with, but are legally distinct from, child protection placements – yet the two populations may share facilities or encounter each other within the system.

### 4.54.3 Life in Institutional Care: Evidence from Research

The Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) chapter and the CFW Section 4 analysis provide qualitative evidence on conditions in Maldivian children's residential facilities. Key findings:

**Staffing and care quality.** Residential care facilities are staffed by care workers – not trained social workers – on shift rotations. This creates discontinuity of relationship (children encounter different adults across different shifts) and limits the therapeutic potential of residential placements. Children in long-term institutional care without consistent caring relationships develop what the developmental psychology literature identifies as "attachment difficulties" – challenges in forming and sustaining meaningful relationships that can persist into adulthood.[^825]

**Over-capacity strain.** Fiyavathi's operation at 130 children against a designed capacity of 80 creates physical overcrowding that limits children's access to private space, restricts programming, and increases social tensions among residents. The research evidence on institutional care consistently finds that quality deteriorates as occupancy rates increase.

**Educational continuity.** Children in residential care in Malé area attend local schools, which provides some continuity of education. However, children's educational progress may be disrupted by the circumstances that led to care placement, by the institutional environment, and by instability of placement if children are moved between facilities. No systematic educational outcome monitoring for children in care has been documented.

**Contact with family.** Best practice in residential care requires maintaining children's connections with family members where safe to do so – through regular contact visits, telephone communication, and supported family time. The extent to which Kudakudhinge Hiya and Fiyavathi systematically facilitate family contact in line with care plan provisions is not publicly documented.

**Preparation for leaving care.** Children who reach 18 in institutional care leave without the family support structures, housing, and economic foundation that most young adults can access. No "leaving care" protocol or "care leaver" support programme has been documented in the Maldivian system – meaning that young adults transitioning from institutional care face the double disadvantage of their original vulnerabilities and the lack of a family safety net.[^826]

---


## 4.54 The Seeds of Reform: Alternative Care in the Maldives -- Full Article Analysis


### 4.58.1 Overview and Significance

Rogers and Ali's 2023 article "The seeds of reform; the evolution of the alternative care system in the Maldives," published in *Institutionalised Children Explorations and Beyond* (Vol. 10(2), pp. 154–163, DOI: 10.1177/23493003231187974), provides the most comprehensive academic account of the Maldivian alternative care system available in the international literature. The article charts the evolution from informal kinship care as the dominant historical modality through the establishment of large residential institutions, the shift to smaller atoll-based facilities post-COVID, and the nascent development of formal foster care. It documents specific challenges and provides a reform roadmap grounded in Maldivian context.

The article's title is itself diagnostic: the seeds of reform *have been sown*, but have not yet germinated into a mature, family-based system. As of March 2023, 187 children were living in alternative care – 82 girls and 105 boys – representing just 0.02% of the child population. This is an exceptionally low number by international standards, but the conditions of those 187 children, and the system that cares for them, reveal structural weaknesses that determine the quality and outcomes of state care in the Maldives.[^843]

### 4.58.2 Scope and Definition of Alternative Care
