Alternative care in the Maldives is formally defined in Article 69(b) of the Regulation on Alternative Care Provision Guidelines (R.No.2020-R-69) (Maldives Government, 2019). The regulation characterises Alternative Care Services as "temporary sheltering arrangements by the state" for children in need of protection. Article 95 (1–8) of the Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (CRPA) grants the state powers to remove children assessed to be in unsafe home environments and to place them in alternative care. These powers are invoked in cases of abuse and neglect, domestic violence in the home, parental imprisonment, and situations where children with mental or physical disabilities are deemed unable to be cared for at home.

The alternative care system encompasses multiple modalities:
- **Residential institutional care** – the predominant form, provided in government-run facilities
- **Foster care** – a formalised family-based placement in which a registered foster carer takes legal guardianship of a child
- **Kinship care** – placement with extended family members (grandparents, aunts, uncles), which occurs both formally (through court orders) and informally (without legal sanction)
- **Kafalah** – the Islamic form of long-term guardianship, compliant with CRC Article 20
- **Private fostering arrangements** – informal placements with non-family members

As of 2023, 91% of children in alternative care in the Maldives are in residential childcare settings. Only 9% are in foster care. This ratio – overwhelmingly institutional – reflects the underdevelopment of family-based placement options and stands in stark contrast to the direction of international best practice, which has moved decisively toward family-based care since the UN Guidelines on the Alternative Care of Children (UNGA, 2009).[^844]

### 4.58.3 What the Regulation Lacks

The Alternative Care Regulations (R.No.2020-R-69) were drafted with reference to the UN Guidelines for Alternative Care of Children (UNGA, 2009), with Chapter 3, Article 5(e) requiring that siblings be kept together unless special circumstances prevent it, and Articles 6 and 7 emphasising promotion and monitoring of contact with parents. However, Rogers and Ali (2023) identify critical omissions from the regulatory framework that leave children in alternative care with inadequate formal protections. The regulations lack:

- A child's health plan requirement
- Family support and preventative services
- Anti-bullying policies
- Health and safety standards for residential facilities
- Protocols for missing children from care
- Recommended staff-to-child ratios
- Regulatory oversight and monitoring systems
- Services and support for young people leaving care (leaving care provisions)

The absence of leaving care support is particularly significant given that in Maldivian society, safety nets for young adults depend heavily on extended family networks. For children who have spent years in institutional care – with limited family contact, disrupted family relationships, and potential estrangement from their island community – exit from care without transition support means entry into a social environment for which they have been systematically underprepared.[^845]

### 4.58.4 Institutional History: From Islaahiya to Nine Atoll Facilities

The history of residential alternative care in the Maldives is a history of large institutions gradually being replaced by smaller, more dispersed facilities – a transition that is still incomplete.

**Pre-institutional period:** Before the establishment of formal residential care, informal kinship care was the predominant modality. Children who could not remain with their parents were looked after by grandmothers, aunts, and the wider family and kinship group. This tradition remains culturally significant and continues to operate in parallel with the formal system.

**Islaahiya (1979–2013):** The establishment of a large reformatory school – originally named Islaahiya and subsequently renamed the Educational and Training Centre for Children (ETCC) – in Male Atoll in 1979 marked the beginning of residential alternative care as a distinct institutional form. Islaahiya was created primarily for boys described as having behavioural issues, and represented a reformatory rather than welfare model. It remained a key alternative care provider for over three decades, until it was closed in 2013. At its peak it housed large numbers of children managed by relatively low numbers of care staff – a staffing model that predisposed the institution to the neglect and abuse that was later documented.

**Kudakudhinge Hiya (2004):** In response to the increasingly severe impact of drug misuse on families – particularly in Male – the state-run institution Kudakudhinge Hiya was established in 2004 to care for 45 boys and girls from birth to 9 years old. The establishment of this facility explicitly linked drug-related family breakdown to the need for out-of-home care. Today, Kudakudhinge Hiya functions primarily as a **temporary shelter** in Male, serving children from smaller islands who are placed in care and who require travel to the capital for medical appointments. It is no longer primarily a residential care placement for children from Male itself.

**Aman Hiya (opened 11 May 2006):** The building adjacent to Kudakudhinge Hiya is named Aman Hiya. It was originally built for domestic violence victims but now accommodates girls between the ages of 9 and 18.

**Correctional and Training Centre for Children (CTCC) (2009–2013):** In 2009, the government established a Correctional and Training Centre for Children (CTCC) under a presidential decree on the island of Feydhoo Finolhu, run by the Maldives Police Service. The CTCC was established as a reactive measure to what was perceived as a concerning number of juveniles engaged in drug use and gang violence. The Centre was closed in August 2013 after being found to be in conflict with Article 35 of the UNCRC: children were being held arbitrarily and deprived of liberty. Its establishment and closure illustrate the risks of security-driven responses to youth vulnerability: the CTCC treated juveniles as a law enforcement problem rather than a child welfare problem, with predictably rights-violating consequences.

**Fiyavithi (2016):** With support from the Qatar Foundation, the state built another institution – Fiyavithi – which cares for 80 children under 13 years of age.

**Post-COVID dispersal (2020):** A significant structural shift occurred during and after COVID-19: children previously in Kudakudhinge Hiya and Aman Hiya were redistributed into nine smaller institutions located across the atolls, each caring for no more than 18–24 children. This shift represented a deliberate policy decision to move away from large-scale institutional care concentrated in Male toward smaller, atoll-based facilities – consistent with both international best practice and the CRPA's orientation toward deinstitutionalisation. However, as Rogers and Ali (2023) note, this shift has been accompanied by neither a comprehensive care reform strategy nor adequate staffing or resources for the new institutions.[^846]

### 4.58.5 The National Reintegration Centre: A Problematic Hybrid

The National Reintegration Centre (NRC) is a facility established under the Anti-Terrorism Act (Law No. 17/2019) to temporarily detain children and mothers returning from war zones where concerns about radicalisation exist. The NRC occupies an ambiguous legal position in relation to the alternative care framework:

- The Child Rights Protection Act (2019), Article cited in Regulation R.No.2020-R-30, explicitly states that the NRC is **not** considered a state care facility.
- However, children in the NRC clearly require protection and case management responses that overlap with alternative care.
- Two reintegration committees exist: one under Chapter 9, Section 60–15 of Law No. 31/2021, and one under the Alternative Care regulation. The former committee has been dissolved.
- Children reintegrated into the NRC have no reintegration plan. Their monitoring is carried out by a social worker at the atoll level – problematic if the social worker is not resident on the same island as the child.

The practical consequences are severe: as of 2022, only three children who had been placed with their mothers in the NRC had been successfully reintegrated back to their respective islands (Abdulla, 2022). The NRC thus represents a category of highly vulnerable children – those returning from conflict zones and exposed to radical ideologies – who fall into a gap between the anti-terrorism framework and the child welfare framework, with neither system providing adequate support for their reintegration.[^847]

### 4.58.6 Key Stakeholders in Alternative Care

**Ministry of Gender and Social Services (MoGSS):** The government body with statutory duties for child welfare and protection. Within MoGSS is the Unit for Child and Family Protection Services (CFPS), formerly named the Unit for the Rights of Children (URC). CFPS maintains 21 social work teams across the 1,192 islands and has responsibility for child protection assessments and case management for all children in care.

**UNICEF:** Since 1992, UNICEF has played a key role in supporting child welfare and child protection activities in the Maldives. UNICEF has developed programmes with the Ministry to strengthen child protection systems, building capacity in the social services workforce through training and supervision, and advising on government policy relating to child welfare and protection.

**Barnardos:** In 2009, the Ministry sought assistance from the English charity Barnardos to advise on alternative care policy and practice, with particular reference to ETCC, Kudakudhinge Hiya and CTCC. Recommendations focused on improving the ways staff managed difficult behaviour, health and safety aspects of the buildings, and improving overall staff training.

**ARC (Advocating for the Rights of Children):** The primary NGO working in the alternative care space, ARC works to improve the quality of care through the provision of training to care workers and providing educational and leisure activities for children. It is worth noting that ARC's scope is limited and its capacity is small relative to the scale of need.

**IBAMA:** The community-level mechanism (translated as "You and I together") that provides a community-based system for preventing and responding to violence against children, the elderly, drug addiction and people with disabilities. IBAMA is a multisectoral mechanism managed at the island level with an official mandate.

**Care-experienced people:** Rogers and Ali (2023) identify the **complete absence** of organisations advocating for care-experienced people as a critical gap in the Maldivian alternative care reform landscape. In many countries worldwide, there is a growing movement of organisations led by care-experienced people who offer individual and systemic advocacy and who demand meaningful participation in the change processes that affect them. No such movement exists in the Maldives. The development of meaningful participation mechanisms for people with lived experience of Maldivian care would be of great benefit to policy and practice – but currently, care-experienced young people have no formal role in shaping the system they have lived in.[^848]

### 4.58.7 Foster Care: Development and Constraints

Foster care in the Maldives has been formalised only recently. A foster care panel was established in 2012, but the formal regulatory framework was established by the CRPA (2019) and the Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30 (which came into force in May 2020). The regulation places strong emphasis on long-term planning, kinship care, and allowing children to grow up in a family setting for optimal development.

**Current scale:** As of March 2023, there are 18 registered formal foster carers across the Maldives who are caring for 16 children. This represents 9% of children in alternative care – a tiny fraction of what would be needed if the system were to move toward family-based care as a default.

**The legal guardianship process:** Foster care in the Maldives resembles traditional adoption in some respects. When a child is placed with a foster carer, legal guardianship is transferred from the state to the foster family. This occurs through two steps:
1. The Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services takes guardianship of the child under state authority;
2. Legal guardianship is then transferred to the foster parent with approval from the reintegration committee.

Under the CRPA (enacted 2019, operational 2020), both steps must be completed **before** the child is placed with the foster parent – unlike the pre-CRPA practice in which placement occurred before the legal process was completed. An amendment was subsequently introduced to expedite the formal court process for guardianship transfer, as the requirement for court involvement was slowing placements significantly.

**Staffing for foster care:** Only two social workers within CFPS are solely responsible for conducting foster care assessments, making presentations to panels, and requesting court orders. For a country of 1,192 islands, the allocation of two social workers to all foster care operations nationally represents an extraordinary undersourcing of this critical function.

**Post-placement support:** Section 27 of the Reintegration Regulation 2020/R-30 explicitly states that post-placement support is a necessity. In practice, Rogers and Ali (2023) note that post-placement support is limited. This gap is particularly significant given the challenges faced by foster families – including managing children with trauma histories, behavioural difficulties, and complex needs – without professional support.

**Sibling care:** One area of positive practice is sibling placement. Rogers and Ali (2023) document that sibling separation in alternative care is very rare in Maldivian practice, reflecting the influence of Islamic cultural values that recognise the importance of family cohesion. The UN Guidelines on Alternative Care (Chapter 3, Article 5(e)) specifically require siblings to be kept together unless special circumstances prevent it, and this principle appears to be consistently observed in Maldivian placement decisions.[^849]

### 4.58.8 Specific Challenges in the Maldivian Alternative Care System

Beyond the quantitative workforce gap (61 child protection social workers for 128,208 children = 1:2,101 ratio), Rogers and Ali (2023) identify a set of qualitative challenges that compound the quantitative problem:

**No clear reform strategy:** Despite the development of CRPA and efforts to decentralise care settings, there is still no clear strategy or policy on care reform. The piecemeal nature of reforms – nine smaller institutions here, a foster care regulation there, a reintegration committee elsewhere – has not been accompanied by a coherent theory of change or a roadmap with measurable outcomes.

**Care plan quality:** Regulations provide forms for care plans for children, but these are often short-term plans. There is limited focus on permanency planning for children, despite the requirement under the Reintegration Regulations to take a long-term view. In the absence of permanency thinking, children drift through the system without clear plans for either family reunification or stable long-term placement.

**Training quality:** There has been an increase in training for social workers and residential staff, but this training is often ad hoc and conducted by various parties. The lack of standardised, sustained training on core competencies – trauma-informed care, behaviour management, case management – means that practice quality varies enormously across facilities and between workers.

**Children in institutions: documented harms:** Reports consistently highlight the complex needs and challenges faced by children in institutions, including subjection to abuse and neglect, drug abuse within facilities, and the risk of being recruited to gangs or being trafficked (Harr, 2013; Jernberg, 2021; Naseem, 2011). These findings indicate that institutional care in the Maldives is not a safe default: children placed in institutions face risks of further harm within the system intended to protect them.

**Therapeutic Crisis Intervention:** The absence of standardised Therapeutic Crisis Intervention (TCI) training is identified as an urgent gap. Without TCI or equivalent trauma-informed de-escalation frameworks, staff may respond to children's challenging behaviour in ways that violate rights and cause harm. This is particularly important for children with trauma histories, who are likely to present with behaviour that staff are unprepared to manage safely.

**Drug and alcohol context:** Growing concerns about drug and alcohol abuse in the community affect the care system in two ways: children enter care because of parental drug use, and children in care may encounter drug use within facilities or in the wider community. Social work and residential staff have limited training in supporting families with these issues.

**Placement breakdown and cross-atoll movement:** Managing behaviour and trauma-informed approaches to care are also missing, which often results in placement breakdowns and children moving from a care setting on one atoll to another. These breakdowns and moves across islands frequently disrupt children's relationships with family, impact the support networks that could serve as valuable resources – particularly at the point of leaving care.

**Family contact barriers:** Family contact is often limited to phone contact because there is no financial support for parents to travel to institutions located outside their home island. In a small island developing state where physical distance is a major structural barrier, the absence of travel support for family visits effectively cuts many children off from face-to-face contact with their families for extended periods.

**Inheritance risks:** Rogers and Ali (2023) flag an underappreciated dimension of institutional care in a Maldivian context: disrupting family ties impacts the vital role of inheritance under Islamic succession law, resulting in children in alternative care being at risk of missing out on family inheritance (Bargach, 2002). Islamic succession law confers inheritance rights based on family relationships. Long institutional stays, combined with limited family contact and potential family disengagement, may compromise children's ability to exercise inheritance rights that would otherwise support their transition to adult life.

**Professional supervision gap:** The CRPA states that social workers must receive professional guidance in their role – but it is **not mandatory**. As a result, supervision is not conducted regularly, because managers do not have time (given high caseloads), and because there are few experienced social workers with tertiary education who are eligible to provide supervision under state licensing guidelines. Furthermore, criminal record checks are required only when registering for a licence; there are limited fitness-to-practice safeguards; and there is no requirement for continuing professional development. This creates a system in which practitioners can work for years without the quality assurance mechanisms that are considered basic safeguards in child protection systems in higher-income countries.[^850]

### 4.58.9 Good Practice Elements Worth Preserving and Building On

Rogers and Ali (2023) do not present only a critical picture. They identify elements of good practice that should be recognised and built upon:

- **Child poverty improvements:** Over the last decade, widespread improvements for many children have been achieved, including reduction of child poverty and increased access to education.
- **Sibling unity:** Islamic cultural values around family cohesion have produced a genuine strength – sibling groups are kept together in placement, which international evidence identifies as a protective factor.
- **Formalisation of foster care panel:** The establishment of the foster care panel in 2012 and its subsequent formalisation under CRPA represents meaningful institutional investment, even though the scale remains tiny.
- **Shift to smaller facilities:** The post-COVID shift from large-scale institutions to nine smaller, atoll-based facilities is aligned with international best practice and represents genuine policy learning.
- **Family reunification work:** The ministry has embarked on processes of family reunification, establishing a reintegration committee and working to move children from institutions back to families.
- **Draft SOPs:** UNICEF developed draft Standard Operating Procedures in 2021 for collaborative working in safeguarding children and vulnerable adults, presented to the National Council for the Protection of Children's Rights in January 2021. These SOPs included processes for child protection conferences, pre-birth and review conferences.[^851]

### 4.58.10 The Reform Roadmap: Recommendations from Rogers and Ali (2023)

Rogers and Ali (2023) offer five principal recommendations for the way forward:

**1. Quality training and continuing professional development:** Training must improve in quality and must be sustained. Priority competencies are: case management and care planning; trauma-informed approaches; and cross-sector working with colleagues in education, health and criminal justice.

**2. Implementation of multi-agency SOPs:** The draft SOPs for collaborative safeguarding should be implemented. Child protection conferences, pre-birth conferences, and review conferences would significantly improve multi-agency functioning and produce better outcomes for children and families.

**3. Supported family contact:** For children in residential care, family contact must be promoted and supported. At present, geographical challenges and costs of travelling across the atoll restrict children's time with their families. The logistics and costs of travel need consideration when children are placed in a different atoll from their family.

**4. A coherent care reform roadmap:** The piecemeal nature of current reform initiatives must be replaced by a coherent roadmap with a strategic action plan, a theory of change, and measurable outcomes. Reform plans must be country-specific and culture-sensitive, reflecting the Maldivian context, but should also draw on lessons from countries that have successfully transitioned from institutional to family-based care. Key indicators should include: proportion of children in family-based versus institutional care; placement stability rates; family contact frequency; educational and health outcomes for children in care; and post-care support utilisation rates.

**5. Meaningful engagement of care-experienced people:** The development of mechanisms for meaningfully engaging care-experienced people – people with lived experience of Maldivian care – in the reform process is identified as a priority. Their voices should inform both policy design and service delivery in ways that cannot be replicated by professional or academic voices alone.[^852]

---


## 4.55 The Formal Foster Care System: Operational Detail


### 4.63.1 Legal Architecture of Foster Care

Foster care in the Maldives operates under the following legal architecture:

**Primary legislation:** Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (CRPA), which established the legal framework for formal family-based alternative care and the reintegration committee structure.

**Secondary regulation:** Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30, which came into force in May 2020. This regulation:
- Covers foster care placement
- Requires emphasis on long-term planning, kinship care, and family-setting upbringing
- Designates two social workers within CFPS as solely responsible for foster care assessments, presentations to panels, and court order applications
- Under Section 27, makes post-placement support a stated necessity

**Strategic framework:** The Government's Strategic Action Plan (SAP) – Caring State and Dignified Families (2019) – mandates the Ministry to develop and implement country-wide awareness programmes covering parenting skills, including child maltreatment and abuse. Strategy 4, Action 4.4(f) requires the development of minimum standards for the operation of each residential institution. The plan also requires family reunification processes.

### 4.63.2 The Foster Care Panel and Assessment Process

The foster care panel – first established in 2012 and subsequently formalised under CRPA – is the gateway through which prospective foster carers are assessed, approved, and matched with children. The panel draws on assessments conducted by the two dedicated CFPS social workers.

The process involves:
1. **Recruitment and expression of interest** by prospective foster carers
2. **Assessment** conducted by CFPS social workers – covering home environment, family stability, motivation, capacity to care for children with complex needs, and understanding of foster care responsibilities
3. **Presentation to panel** – the reintegration committee considers the assessment and makes a decision on approval
4. **Matching** – approved carers are matched with children whose needs the carer is assessed to be able to meet
5. **Legal guardianship transfer** – the Ministry takes guardianship of the child, then the Family Court transfers legal guardianship to the foster carer (post-CRPA: both steps must occur before placement)
6. **Placement and post-placement monitoring** – the child is placed; Section 27 requires post-placement support, though in practice this is limited

### 4.63.3 The Scale Problem

The scale of the foster care system – 18 registered carers, 16 children – is vanishingly small relative to the system's needs. A sustainable family-based care system for the Maldives would require:
- A much larger pool of assessed and approved foster carers across all atolls (not just Malé)
- Carers who are trained to manage children with trauma, behavioural difficulties, and complex needs
- Carers who are financially supported – currently, there is no systematic financial allowance for foster carers in outer atolls
- Post-placement professional support systems that can provide carers with access to advice, training, and respite
- A geographic distribution of carers that matches the geographic distribution of children – avoiding cross-atoll placements that sever family contact

All of these requirements represent either absent or embryonic elements of the current system. The two CFPS workers responsible for all foster care nationally cannot build this system without significant resourcing, leadership, and political commitment.[^867]

---


## 4.56 Kinship Care and Kafalah: Islamic Family Care in Practice


### 4.64.1 The Kinship Care Tradition

Historically, informal kinship care – placement of children who cannot remain with their parents with grandmothers, aunts, uncles, and the wider family/kinship group – was the predominant form of alternative care in the Maldives. This tradition predates the formal child protection system by centuries. It reflects deeply held Islamic values about the responsibility of the extended family for the welfare of children within the family network, and values about community responsibility for vulnerable individuals.

In the current system, kinship care operates in two modes:
- **Informal kinship care:** Children placed with extended family without any formal assessment, legal order, or state oversight. This is the majority mode and operates largely below the radar of the formal child protection system.
- **Formal kinship care:** Placement with extended family members arranged through CFPS, with the possibility of a court order (typically a Supervision Order under CRPA Article 93) providing legal oversight.

The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) acknowledges that many children remain in informal family arrangements with extended family members without formal legal status or oversight, representing a governance gap in the alternative care framework. The Government recognises this gap but lacks the workforce capacity to systematically formalize and monitor informal kinship placements at the scale at which they occur.[^868]

### 4.64.2 Kafalah: The Islamic Alternative to Adoption

*Kafalah* is the Islamic concept of guardianship and sponsorship that serves as the primary mechanism for providing permanent family care to children who cannot be raised by their birth parents. It is described in the CRC Periodic Report as compliant with CRC Article 20 (which requires states to ensure alternative family care for children deprived of their family environment).

**Legal definition:** *Kafalah* in the Maldivian context involves a guardian taking responsibility for a child's care, education and welfare without:
- Legally severing the child's ties to their birth family
- Changing the child's name
- Changing the child's inheritance rights
- Fully extinguishing the birth parents' legal status

*Kafalah* arrangements are registered with the Family Court.

**The distinction from adoption:** In Western legal systems, adoption is a legal act that creates a new parent-child relationship with full legal equivalence to biological parenthood, including rights of inheritance from the adoptive family. *Kafalah* provides care and responsibility without this legal permanence. A child in *kafala* retains inheritance rights from their birth family but typically does not gain inheritance rights from the *kafala* guardian family under Islamic succession law.

**In practice:** Rogers and Ali (2023) note that *kafalah* is complex and not widely understood in the Maldives. Media reports in 2023 suggested 10 children had been "adopted" – but what was actually occurring was long-term fostering. In reality, long-term fostering is practiced on the ground (Harr, 2013) even if not formally recognised as *kafalah*. The regulatory framework for *kafalah* arrangements and their oversight needs strengthening, as noted in the CRC Periodic Report.

**The permanency gap:** The unavailability of adoption as a legal instrument creates a permanency gap for children who cannot be reunified with birth families and for whom *kafalah* arrangements may not provide sufficient legal security. International evidence consistently shows that legally permanent family placement (adoption or equivalent) produces better long-term outcomes for children than care arrangements that are legally temporary or indeterminate.[^869]

---


## 4.57 Child Participation and Care-Experienced Voices


### 4.65.1 Child Participation in Policy

The Child Rights Protection Act (2019) includes provisions requiring that the views of children be considered in all decisions affecting them, commensurate with their age and maturity (Article 7). In judicial proceedings, the Juvenile Court has developed protocols for child-friendly hearings. A pilot child-friendly justice initiative supported by UNICEF equipped the Juvenile Court with child-appropriate interview rooms and trained staff in child-friendly communication.

In child protection cases managed by CFPS, social work protocols require social workers to consult children directly as part of the assessment process. However, consistent implementation across all 24 FCSCs remains a challenge.

At the policy level, child participation has been strengthened through the National Youth Parliament and periodic child consultation processes convened by MoSFD in partnership with UNICEF. The CRC Periodic Report (2026) notes that for this report itself, a dedicated child consultation was conducted in partnership with UNICEF, engaging children aged 12–17 from Malé, Addu City, and three outer atolls (Haa Alifu, Laamu and Thaa) through structured focus groups facilitated by trained social workers.

Formal mechanisms for ongoing child participation in policy development and monitoring remain nascent. The Government acknowledges that meaningful, sustained child participation requires institutional investment beyond one-off consultation events.[^870]

### 4.65.2 The Missing Care-Experienced Movement

Rogers and Ali (2023) identify the complete absence of organisations advocating for care-experienced people as one of the most significant gaps in the Maldivian alternative care reform landscape. In many countries worldwide:
- There are growing movements of organisations led by care-experienced people who offer both individual advocacy and systemic advocacy
- Care-experienced people participate formally in the design of services, the drafting of regulations, and the evaluation of system reform
- The voices of those with lived experience provide accountability that no external inspection or professional monitoring can replicate

In the Maldives, no such movement or organisation exists. The 187 children currently in care, and the many thousands who have passed through the care system over the decades since 1979, have no formal collective voice. This means that system reform is being designed entirely by professionals, officials and international development partners – without the knowledge that only those who have lived in Maldivian care can provide.

This is not merely a participation gap – it is an accountability gap. In the absence of care-experienced voices, the harms documented within institutions (abuse, gang recruitment, drug exposure) remain known primarily through professional reports rather than through the testimony of those who experienced them. Building a care-experienced people's movement in the Maldives is identified by Rogers and Ali as a priority for the next phase of reform.[^871]

### 4.65.3 Child Participation Through Naseem (2011)

The UNICEF-supported study on child participation in the Maldives (Naseem, 2011) documented the significant gap between the formal recognition of children's rights to participate and actual participation in practice. The Maldivian cultural context – in which children's deference to adult authority has historically been expected and enforced – creates social barriers to participation that formal legal provisions alone cannot overcome.

Youth pressure groups have, however, emerged as significant actors in the child protection space. Fahmy and Ibrahim (2022) document how advocacy by youth pressure groups has contributed to child sexual abuse being taken more seriously in the Maldives – with high-profile cases generating public pressure that has contributed to legislative and practice reform. The relationship between youth activism and formal policy change is an underexplored dimension of the Maldivian child protection system.[^872]

---


## 4.58 Leaving Care and Transition to Adulthood


### 4.85.1 Why Leaving Care Matters

The transition from alternative care to independent adulthood is one of the most vulnerable moments in a care-experienced person's life. Research across multiple contexts shows that care-leavers – people exiting alternative care on reaching adulthood (typically 18) – face dramatically elevated risks of:
- Homelessness (within 12 months of leaving care)
- Unemployment and poverty
- Mental health difficulties
- Involvement in criminal activity
- Substance abuse
- Social isolation

These risks are compounded in the Maldivian context by specific structural features:

**Islamic inheritance and family networks:** Young adults in the Maldives typically depend on extended family networks for housing, employment connections, and financial safety nets. These networks – and the inheritance rights that are part of them – depend on maintained family relationships. Children who have spent years in institutional care with limited family contact, disrupted relationships, and potential family disengagement may exit care without access to the family network safety net that is the primary support for non-care-experienced young adults.

**The absence of leaving care provision:** Rogers and Ali (2023) identify the absence of leaving care services as a critical gap in the Alternative Care Regulation (R.No.2020-R-69). The regulation contains no requirements for post-care support, transition planning, or follow-up of young people who leave care. Young people exit care without financial support, housing assistance, employment guidance, or ongoing professional contact.

**The housing gap:** Malé's extreme housing pressure (65,000 people per km²) makes self-provision of affordable housing effectively impossible for young people without family support. Care leavers without family networks enter a housing market in which affordable options do not exist independently.

**Continuity of identity:** Long periods in institutional care can create identity gaps – young people who are uncertain of their family relationships, their place in island community, their religious and cultural identity – that require therapeutic and social support to navigate. Without this support, the psychological challenges of leaving care compound the practical challenges.[^917]

### 4.85.2 The Void After 18

The CRPA 2019 provides care and protection for children – defined as persons below 18. There is currently no legislation or regulation specifically addressing the transition from child welfare services to adult services, or providing for any continuity of support across the 18-year threshold. On the day a care-experienced young person turns 18 in the Maldives, they exit the legal framework that has governed their care without entering any equivalent framework of support.

This is not a gap unique to the Maldives – it is a common failing across alternative care systems globally. But the specific Maldivian features (small island geography, family dependence, Islamic inheritance, housing pressure) make the gap particularly consequential. Addressing the leaving care gap should be a priority for the next phase of alternative care reform, including in the National Child Protection Strategy 2025–2030 currently under development.[^918]

---


# Part 11: Corporal Punishment


## 4.59 Corporal Punishment -- Legal Reform and Ongoing Challenges


### 4.12.1 Legal Status Across Settings

Corporal punishment of children in the Maldives is **not explicitly prohibited by law across all settings**. The legal position, as documented by the Global Initiative to End All Corporal Punishment of Children (2015), is as follows:

- **In schools:** The Education Act 2020 banned corporal punishment in educational settings, providing for the first time a clear legal prohibition in one institutional context.
- **In the home:** Corporal punishment remains lawful. The 2014 Penal Code draft explicitly authorised the use of "reasonable force" by parents for disciplinary purposes, reflecting a legislative tolerance for parental physical discipline that is inconsistent with CRC standards.
- **In alternative care settings:** No explicit prohibition exists. Children in residential care or foster care are not legally protected from corporal punishment by their carers.
- **In penal settings:** Judicial flogging of children remains legally possible for offences carrying *hadd* penalties, particularly *zina*. The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 did not abolish this punishment.[^64]

### 4.12.2 Cultural and Religious Context

Corporal punishment of children is widely accepted in the Maldives as a legitimate disciplinary practice, supported by cultural norms and by interpretations of Islamic jurisprudence that permit parents to discipline children physically. Reform efforts face resistance from those who view prohibition as an infringement on parental authority and Islamic parenting practices.

The CRC Committee has repeatedly recommended that the Maldives enact comprehensive legislation prohibiting corporal punishment of children in all settings, including the home. As of 2026, this recommendation has not been fully implemented.[^65]

---


## 4.60 Corporal Punishment: The Legal Gap and Practice Reality


### 4.30.1 The Constitutional and Legislative Position

The corporal punishment of children in the Maldives occupies a legally ambiguous and politically contested position. The Constitution of 2008 guarantees every citizen protection from "cruel, inhumane, degrading treatment or punishment" (Article 54) and enshrines special protection for children (Article 35). The CRPA 2019, in Article 4, prohibits all forms of discrimination, harm, and violence against children. Yet no Maldivian legislation explicitly and categorically prohibits corporal punishment in all settings – creating a structural gap that the UN Committee on the Rights of the Child has identified as inconsistent with Maldives' obligations under the CRC.[^585]

The CRPA 2019 represents the most recent and most comprehensive child rights framework, and its definitional provisions in Article 133 encompass a broad conception of harm and abuse that would appear to encompass physical punishment. However, the Act does not explicitly state that corporal punishment is prohibited in the home, alternative care settings, or schools. This omission is not incidental: it reflects ongoing political sensitivity around parental discipline, the widespread cultural and religious acceptance of physical correction, and the influence of interpretations of Islamic jurisprudence that permit parental physical chastisement within certain parameters.[^586]

### 4.30.2 The School Setting: The Most Advanced Legal Position

The clearest legal prohibition of corporal punishment applies in the school setting. The Ministry of Education's Safeguarding Policy for Children at Schools (2019) prohibits corporal punishment and provides explicit guidance to teachers. The Education Act 2020 reinforces child protection obligations for education professionals.[^587] However, the prohibition in school settings pre-dates these instruments: corporal punishment was officially removed from Maldivian school practice in the 1990s as part of education modernisation reforms.

In practice, the picture is more mixed. Qualitative evidence gathered through the Vulnerability Mapping process and child consultations conducted for the UNICEF Situation Analysis (2025) found that physical discipline – particularly slapping and hitting – continues to occur in school settings, particularly in some outer island schools where monitoring is weak and where individual teachers hold traditional views about discipline. The absence of a systematic reporting pathway, complaint mechanism, and accountability system for school corporal punishment means that enforcement of the prohibition depends almost entirely on individual school management culture rather than structural oversight.[^588]

### 4.30.3 The Home Setting: Parental Discipline and Islamic Law

In the home setting, no explicit legal prohibition of corporal punishment exists. The Penal Code (2014) and CRPA (2019) together establish that causing physical harm to a child constitutes abuse, but the practical interpretation of "reasonable parental discipline" as a defence has not been definitively settled in Maldivian law.[^589] The absence of a clear legal standard creates operational challenges for CFPS caseworkers who must distinguish between corporal punishment that is definitionally abusive and other forms.

The religious dimension is significant. Many Maldivians interpret Islamic jurisprudence as permitting moderate physical discipline of children by parents, particularly in the context of religious and behavioural correction. Religious education institutions (madrasas and Quran schools) have been particularly resistant to corporal punishment prohibitions. Children attending Quran classes – a common practice across the Maldives – have reported being struck on the hands or legs as correction, a practice that is normalised within the religious education context.[^590]

The Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) chapter on child protection in the Maldives identifies this cultural-religious dimension as one of the most challenging aspects of promoting child rights: "concepts of 'honour' and respect for elders are deeply embedded in Maldivian culture, and physical discipline is often seen as an appropriate expression of parental authority." The same chapter notes that "parents who hit their children are rarely seen as abusive – rather, as responsible."[^591]

### 4.30.4 Judicial Corporal Punishment and the Religious Law Intersection

A distinct dimension of corporal punishment concerns judicial punishment under Shari'ah law. The Maldives operates a dual legal system in which Shari'ah-derived criminal penalties apply alongside the secular Penal Code. Under the Penal Code (Section 411(d)), flogging is prescribed for the offence of *zina* (extramarital sexual intercourse), with a sentence of 100 lashes. The JJ Act (2019) did not explicitly abolish flogging as a permissible sentence for children aged 7-14 – the Act reforms the juvenile justice system but does not address corporal punishment under Shari'ah as a distinct legal category.[^592]

The HRCM's 2022 report on corporal punishment documented that 85% of flogging sentences were imposed on women and girls – reflecting the gendered operation of the *zina* law described in §4.24 above. The CRC Committee and HRCM have both called for the explicit abolition of judicial corporal punishment for all persons under 18, and the CRC Periodic Report Draft submitted by the Maldives acknowledges this as an "area requiring further reform."[^593]

The 2014 Regulation on Juvenile Justice explicitly permitted death penalty sentences for children aged 7 and above under Shari'ah – five children were sentenced to death between 2013 and 2015, a fact documented in the Statistics Compendium and confirmed by UNICEF and the CRC Committee. While President Ibrahim Mohamed Solih committed to abolishing the death penalty for minors in 2018, the legal framework for this abolition had not been fully consolidated as of 2024.[^594]

### 4.30.5 Alternative Care Settings and Institutional Contexts

Corporal punishment in alternative care settings – children's homes, Kudakudhinge Hiya shelter, Fiyavathi, and the Imaduddin rehabilitation facility – is nominally prohibited by institutional regulations. However, there is limited independent monitoring of these facilities. The Children's Ombudsperson's office has mandate over monitoring conditions in care facilities, but its capacity is limited relative to its mandate.[^595]

The UNICEF 2022 report on CFPS caseload noted that children who had experienced institutional care reported occasional use of physical restraint and isolation as disciplinary measures. CFPS workers conducting monitoring visits have a dual challenge: the same workers responsible for placing children in institutional care often conduct monitoring visits, creating a potential conflict of interest that limits critical oversight.[^596]

### 4.30.6 Policy Recommendations

Both the NAPVAC 2024-2028 and the CFW vulnerability mapping recommendations identify corporal punishment as requiring legislative action. Key recommendations include:

- **Explicit legislative prohibition** of corporal punishment in all settings – home, school, alternative care, and judicial – through amendment of the CRPA or enactment of stand-alone legislation
- **Public awareness campaigns** that address the cultural and religious dimensions of physical discipline, working with *imams*, religious scholars, and community leaders to promote positive discipline approaches
- **Training for parents, teachers, and care workers** in positive behaviour management strategies
- **Enforcement mechanisms** for the school-setting prohibition, including complaint pathways and accountability consequences for offending teachers
- **Clear policy guidance** for CFPS workers on the threshold between legally permissible parental discipline and child abuse[^597]

---


# Part 12: Children Born Outside Marriage


## 4.61 Children Born Out of Wedlock -- Legal Status and Social Stigma


Children born outside of marriage face particular legal and social vulnerabilities in the Maldives. Under Islamic family law as applied in the Maldives, children born out of wedlock (*walad al-zina*) lack a legal father and cannot be attributed to the biological father's lineage. This has consequences for inheritance rights, guardianship, and social status.

The social stigma attached to out-of-wedlock birth is intense in the Maldivian context, where Islamic norms around sexual morality are closely policed at the community level. Mothers of children born outside marriage may face criminal prosecution for *zina*, social ostracism, and loss of family support. The children themselves bear the consequences of their parents' transgression through a status that marks them as socially subordinate throughout their lives.

The CRPA 2019 establishes the rights of all children regardless of the circumstances of their birth, providing a legal basis for non-discrimination. However, the practical enforcement of this principle against deeply embedded social and religious norms remains challenging.[^48]

---


## 4.62 Birth Registration and Statelessness for Out-of-Wedlock Children


### 4.38.1 Legal Framework for Birth Registration

Birth registration in the Maldives is governed by the Civil Registration Act. All births must be registered within a specified period (typically 30 days) at the local island council or civil registration office. Registration is free. The Maldives has achieved near-universal birth registration for children born in formal marriages – the 2017 DHS found registration rates above 95% for children born to married couples.[^671]

The critical gap concerns children born outside formal marriage. As documented in §4.11 of the original chapter, Maldivian law and social convention do not recognise children born out of wedlock in the same framework as children born within marriage. A child whose parents are not married at the time of birth, or whose father is not identified or does not acknowledge paternity, faces a more complex registration pathway.[^672]

### 4.38.2 The Out-of-Wedlock Registration Pathway

Under the Civil Registration Act and Family Act, a child born to an unmarried mother is registered under the mother's household. The father's name may be added to the registration if paternity is legally acknowledged or established. The practical effect:

- Children born to unmarried mothers are registered under the mother's family name, without automatic paternal identification
- In a society where children are expected to carry their father's family name and where descent is traced patrilineally for inheritance and guardianship purposes, registration under the mother's name creates social stigma and practical complications
- Where the father is unknown, disputed, or has denied paternity, the child's registration may be incomplete, affecting their access to identity documents and services[^673]

Critically, the *wali* (legal guardian) of a child under Islamic law is always the father (or, in his absence, the paternal grandfather or other male agnatic relative). A child born out of wedlock whose father has not acknowledged paternity has no *wali* – creating significant legal complications for medical consent, school enrolment, passport applications, and other processes that require guardian authorisation.[^674]

### 4.38.3 Statelessness Risk

Children born in the Maldives to foreign nationals – including migrant workers from Bangladesh, India, Sri Lanka, and Nepal – may face statelessness risks if neither parent's nationality law grants citizenship by descent based on birth in the Maldives. The Maldives does not operate *jus soli* (citizenship by birth) – citizenship passes through the father's Maldivian citizenship under the Citizenship Act. A child born in the Maldives to foreign parents does not automatically acquire Maldivian citizenship.[^675]

Migrant worker communities in Male' and resort islands include a significant population of irregular workers whose employment and residence status is precarious. Children born to irregular migrants – including children whose mothers are Maldivian and fathers are foreign nationals – may face registration difficulties and potential statelessness. UNHCR has identified this as a concern in its regional statelessness work but has not published a specific Maldives assessment.[^676]

### 4.38.4 Policy and Data Gaps

The CRC Periodic Report Draft acknowledged birth registration completeness challenges and committed to ensuring universal registration, including for children in difficult circumstances. Key policy gaps include:

- **No systematic identification** of children who are unregistered or incompletely registered
- **No specific protocol** for ensuring registration of children born to out-of-wedlock parents, particularly where the father is absent, unknown, or foreign
- **No statutory reform** to address the *wali* gap for children without paternal guardianship, meaning that mother-headed households caring for out-of-wedlock children must navigate a legal system that structurally disadvantages them[^677]

---


## 4.63 Children Born Outside Marriage: A Specific Vulnerability


### 4.69.1 Legal and Social Status

Children born outside marriage face a specific cluster of legal, social, and developmental vulnerabilities in the Maldivian context. As Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) note, *de facto* and *de jure* discrimination is experienced throughout the lives of children born out of wedlock in Muslim states (Ali, 2013). The Maldives is no exception.

Under Islamic family law as practised in Maldives:
- Children born outside marriage may have their parentage contested or unrecognised, particularly in relation to the father
- Inheritance rights under Islamic succession law flow primarily through the recognised paternal line – children whose paternal parentage is legally uncertain face potential inheritance deprivation
- Social stigma attaches to the child as well as the mother, despite the child having no agency in the circumstances of their birth
- The child's *wali* (marriage guardian) for any subsequent marriage may be compromised if paternal lineage is unclear

### 4.69.2 The Birth Registration Gap

The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) notes near-universal birth registration for children born in health facilities. However, challenges remain for children born outside marriage: the registration process requires documentation of parentage, and where paternal parentage is denied or unclear, birth registration may be delayed or complicated.

Children born outside marriage to mothers who were victims of rape face the additional complexity noted in §4.61.4: the circumstances of their conception may have been criminalised, and documentation of those circumstances (through a rape report or prosecution) may create further legal complications for both mother and child.

### 4.69.3 Entry to Alternative Care

Children born outside marriage, or whose parents are unmarried, appear disproportionately in the alternative care system – not because out-of-wedlock birth is itself a ground for removal, but because of the social vulnerabilities that cluster around it: single motherhood (often young), economic vulnerability, family rejection, social isolation, and limited support networks. A mother rejected by her family due to pregnancy outside marriage may have no kinship care network to draw on if she cannot provide adequate care for her child, leading to institutional placement as the only available option.[^880]

---


# Part 13: Child Labour


## 4.64 Child Labour in the Maldives


### 4.7.1 Legal Framework

The CRPA 2019 prohibits the employment of children below the age of 16, and the Education Act 2020 establishes compulsory education until age 16, creating a complementary legal framework that should, in principle, prevent child labour. Prior to the CRPA, no explicit prohibition on child labour existed in domestic law – notably, in 2009, there was no compulsory education law at all, leaving children vulnerable to exploitation without legal recourse.[^45]

### 4.7.2 Patterns of Child Labour

Despite the legal prohibitions, the US Department of Labor has documented persistent patterns of child labour in the Maldives through its annual assessments:

- **2009 findings:** No compulsory education law existed; children were exploited as domestic servants and in forced prostitution.
- **2018 findings:** The Maldives made "minimal advancement" in eliminating child labour. Children continued to be engaged in forced domestic labor, commercial sexual exploitation, and drug trafficking.
- **2020 findings:** The Maldives made "moderate advancement," reflecting the enactment of the CRPA and the Education Act 2020, but significant gaps remained in enforcement and implementation.[^46]

The primary forms of child labour documented in the Maldives include:

- **Forced domestic labour:** As noted above, children – particularly girls – sent to relatives in Malé for education may be coerced into performing domestic work. The distinction between age-appropriate household chores and exploitative domestic labour is culturally contested.

- **Commercial sexual exploitation:** Children, predominantly girls, are exploited in commercial sexual activity. The intersection of child sexual exploitation with tourism – the Maldives' primary economic sector – has been raised as a concern in international reporting, though systematic evidence on the prevalence of tourism-related child sexual exploitation is limited.

- **Drug trafficking:** Children, predominantly boys, are used by drug trafficking networks to transport and distribute drugs. The involvement of children in drug trafficking is closely linked to the gang violence phenomenon, where gang leaders recruit minors for distribution activities in part because juveniles face less severe penalties than adults.[^47]

---


## 4.65 Child Labour: Intersections with Education, Fishing, and Family Enterprise


### 4.32.1 Legal Framework

The minimum working age in the Maldives is 16 years, established by the Employment Act (2008) in combination with the Education Act (2020), which establishes compulsory education through Grade 12 (approximately age 18). The CRPA 2019 prohibits child labour and economic exploitation of children under its general abuse and harm provisions. The Maldives has ratified ILO Convention 138 (Minimum Age for Admission to Employment) and Convention 182 (Worst Forms of Child Labour).[^612]

Light work is permitted for children aged 16-18 with limitations on hours (maximum 6 hours per day) and prohibitions on hazardous work, night work, and work that interferes with education. Hazardous work is defined to include work in fishing vessels, construction, and resort industry roles that involve alcohol service.[^613]

### 4.32.2 Patterns and Prevalence

Systematic data on child labour in the Maldives is limited. The most recent comprehensive assessment derives from the UNICEF Situation Analysis (2025), which drew on DHS data, administrative records, and community consultations. Key patterns include:

**Fisheries and family enterprise.** The most common form of child labour in the Maldives is involvement in family fisheries operations, particularly in outer atolls. Boys aged 14-17 may work alongside fathers and brothers on fishing vessels – including overnight fishing trips – in a practice that is deeply embedded in cultural and livelihood patterns. While this exposure to the family enterprise is framed as learning and socialisation, it frequently involves dangerous working conditions, long hours, and absence from school during fishing seasons.[^614]

**Tourism sector.** Young people aged 16-18 increasingly work in the guesthouse and tourism sector, particularly during school holidays. While technically legal above age 16, concerns have been raised about working conditions, exposure to alcohol in a religious prohibition context, and the pressure that part-time employment in tourism creates on educational engagement.[^615]

**Domestic work.** Girls from outer atolls who are sent to Male' to live with relatives while attending secondary school sometimes perform domestic work – cleaning, cooking, childcare – in exchange for accommodation. This arrangement, while not formally characterised as employment, constitutes a form of labour that occurs outside legal frameworks and without protections. Its prevalence is not systematically measured.[^616]

**Trafficking-related labour.** As documented in §4.26, children from economically marginalised backgrounds – including out-of-wedlock children and those from the poorest atoll communities – are vulnerable to trafficking into domestic servitude and commercial sexual exploitation. These represent the most severe forms of child labour and require the most intensive child protection responses.[^617]

### 4.32.3 The Education-Labour Tension

The Education Act 2020 establishes compulsory education but enforcement is limited. In outer atolls where secondary school attendance requires residential placement away from family, the combination of cost, separation anxiety, and available local work creates genuine tension between education continuation and early entry into the labour force. Boys are more likely than girls to leave school early for fisheries work; girls are more likely to leave for domestic roles or early marriage.

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 identifies early school leaving as a risk factor for both child labour and exposure to gang-related activity, particularly in the context of Male' migration. Boys from outer atolls who migrate to Male' for secondary education without adequate family support networks are at elevated risk of dropping out and being absorbed into the drug economy or construction labour market on unfavourable terms.[^618]

### 4.32.4 Monitoring and Enforcement

Labour inspection in the Maldives is conducted by the Ministry of Economic Development and Ministry of Labour, but inspection capacity is extremely limited in outer atolls. The fisheries sector – where the most common forms of child participation occur – is outside the main labour inspection framework. There is no dedicated child labour unit within the inspection system, and cross-referral mechanisms between labour inspectors and CFPS are underdeveloped.[^619]

---


## 4.66 Child Labour: Detailed Analysis


### 4.78.1 Legal Framework

The Employment Act (Law No. 2/2008) and its regulations establish the minimum working age at 16 years, with persons aged 16–18 restricted to non-hazardous work and limited hours. Below 16, all wage employment is prohibited. The Child Rights Protection Act (2019) reinforces this prohibition. Employment Act Regulations specify prohibited hazardous occupations for under-18s: fishing on the open sea, construction at height, dangerous chemicals, night work.

Prior to 2009, there was no compulsory education law – a fact documented by the U.S. Department of Labor (2009) as the primary enabling condition for child labour in the Maldives. The absence of compulsory education, combined with the absence of child labour prohibitions in statute, left children without legal protection from exploitation. The Education Act 2020 and CRPA 2019 together filled this legal vacuum, but the U.S. Department of Labor's 2020 assessment upgraded the Maldives only to "moderate advancement" – recognising the legislation while noting persistent gaps in enforcement.[^899]

### 4.78.2 The Worst Forms: USDOL Findings

The U.S. Department of Labor's triennial assessments of child labour in the Maldives document an evolving picture:

**2009 assessment (baseline):** Children exploited primarily as domestic servants and in forced prostitution. No compulsory education law. No government coordination mechanism for combating child labour. Girls sent to relatives in Malé for education were found to be vulnerable to coerced domestic work in arrangements where the line between household chores and exploitation was culturally contested.

**2018 assessment (pre-reform):** "Minimal advancement" – second-lowest rating. Children continued to be engaged in forced domestic labour, commercial sexual exploitation, and drug trafficking. Gang leaders recruited minors for drug distribution activities in part because juveniles faced less severe penalties than adults – a rational criminal calculation exploiting the juvenile justice protective framework.

**2020 assessment (post-reform):** Upgrade to "moderate advancement," reflecting enactment of CRPA 2019 and Education Act 2020. Persistent gaps identified: lack of hazardous work identification for children, continued forced domestic labour and sexual exploitation, insufficient enforcement capacity.

The trajectory from "minimal" (2018) to "moderate" (2020) advancement illustrates the direct but partial impact of legislative reform on compliance – progress in law was real, but enforcement remained inadequate.[^900]

### 4.78.3 Specific Child Labour Contexts

**The fishing sector:** Children from fishing families may assist with fishing from an early age. Participation in family fishing is culturally embedded and not considered labour in the traditional sense – it is part of the intergenerational transmission of fishing knowledge and skills. However, it can interfere with schooling and expose children to hazardous maritime conditions. MPS data recorded 24 potential child labour cases in 2022 and 23 in 2021 – statistics that likely represent a small fraction of the actual situation, given the cultural normalisation of children's participation in family fishing activities.

**The domestic work trap – Male' vulnerability:** Adam (2017), in the study "Child Abuse in Male' City of Maldives," documented a specific vulnerability pattern unique to the Maldivian urban context: relatives' children brought to Malé for education were often forced into domestic work – performing cooking, cleaning, and childcare duties in exchange for accommodation rather than receiving the educational access that was the stated purpose of the arrangement. These children occupy a liminal status – family members rather than employees – making their exploitation difficult to identify within formal child labour or child abuse frameworks. The child is part of the family, but the family relationship is being exploited.

Adam (2017) also found that the overcrowded living conditions of Malé – multiple families sharing small apartments – created heightened risks of sexual abuse through co-habitation of children with unrelated adult males (stepfathers, maternal partners, male tenants). The privacy deficit of overcrowded housing means children lacked physical spaces of safety within the home.

**Tourism support economy:** Children may be engaged in small-scale vending, tourism support activities, or informal service work in resort-adjacent communities. The formal resort sector has employment standards that preclude child labour, but the informal tourism economy is less well monitored.

**Migrant children at risk:** Children of migrant workers may be at risk of exploitative labour situations, particularly where parents are in irregular migration situations. Their documentation status complicates access to education and social services.

### 4.78.4 Enforcement Gaps

The Labour Relations Authority (LRA) is the primary enforcement authority for child labour provisions. Inspection coverage is low, and resource constraints limit proactive monitoring. A Joint Child Labour Monitoring and Response Protocol is under development between MoSFD and the LRA, expected in 2025.[^901]

---


# Part 14: Children with Disabilities


## 4.67 Children with Disabilities: Rights, Vulnerabilities, and the Protection Gap


### 4.34.1 Scale and Demographics

The Census 2022 identified 3,401 children aged 5-17 living with disabilities, representing approximately 14% of the total disability population of 24,401. The National Disability Registry (NDR) records 13,656 registered persons; disaggregating the 5-17 age group from registry data provides a partial picture only, as the NDR undercounts children with psychosocial and intellectual disabilities – precisely those categories where child protection risks are highest.[^629]

Registry data by disability type shows that children are disproportionately represented in categories of intellectual disability, autism, and multiple disabilities – conditions that require intensive support and place greatest demands on child protection and educational systems. Autism cases in the NDR total 584 (122 women, 462 men) – a gender ratio that is consistent with global patterns but may also reflect under-identification of autism in girls. Intellectual disability cases total 1,954 and multiple disability cases total 2,961 – categories in which children constitute a substantial share.[^630]

### 4.34.2 Elevated Child Protection Risk

Children with disabilities face significantly elevated risks of abuse, neglect, and exploitation across all settings:

**Violence and abuse prevalence.** The HRCM CRPD Shadow Report (2023) documented that the prevalence of **sexual violence against children with disabilities was 60%**, with the large majority of survivors being girls with disabilities. Persons with disabilities are four times more likely to experience violence than those without disabilities. For children with communication difficulties – deaf children, children with intellectual disabilities, non-verbal children – the barriers to disclosing abuse and being believed when disclosing are acute.[^631]

**Disability-specific communication barriers.** FCSC workers often lack training in working with deaf children or children with intellectual disabilities. The Sign Language crisis – the government's switch from Dhivehi sign language to American Sign Language in Male' schools – has disrupted communication for an entire generation of deaf children and their families. CFPS caseworkers seeking to assess abuse of deaf children face the additional challenge that there are only a handful of certified Dhivehi sign language interpreters in the country, and none are routinely available at FCSCs.[^632]

**Institutional care risk.** Children with disabilities who cannot be cared for at home due to family incapacity, stigma, or the absence of community-based support may be placed in institutional care. Kudakudhinge Hiya, the primary shelter for children in need of protection, was not designed or resourced to support children with complex disabilities. The gap between institutional capacity and the needs of children with severe intellectual or multiple disabilities places these children at particular risk within the care system itself.[^633]

**Neglect and poverty intersection.** The HRCM CRPD Shadow Report found that 55% of disabled households are female-headed. Female-headed households with a child who has disabilities are among the most economically vulnerable households in the Maldives. The Disability Allowance (MVR 3,000-7,000 per month) is a critical support, but coverage is incomplete (only 56% of Census-identified persons with disabilities are registered), and the allowance is often insufficient when medical costs, therapy, and care needs are factored in.[^634]

### 4.34.3 The Inclusive Education-Child Protection Nexus

The school environment presents both a child protection risk and a protective opportunity for children with disabilities. The risk: as documented in §6.5 of the Disability chapter, children with disabilities face systematic bullying and exclusion, including from extracurricular activities. Cases of bullying going unaddressed by school management normalise maltreatment and deprive children of the school as a protective environment. Teachers with limited disability awareness may inadvertently harm children – for example, by interpreting the communication or behavioural characteristics of autism as disobedience and responding with punitive measures.[^635]

The protective opportunity: schools that implement genuine inclusion – with trained SEN teachers, IEPs, peer awareness programmes, and effective anti-bullying policies – can provide disabled children with a community of relationships, regular monitoring by trained adults, and access to referral pathways. The shift from "SEN units" to pure mainstreaming without adequate support has, in practice, reduced the protective functions of specialist support for many children with disabilities.[^636]

### 4.34.4 Transition to Adulthood and Trafficking Vulnerability

Young people with intellectual disabilities and psychosocial disabilities are particularly vulnerable during the transition from child protection services to adult social protection. The lack of a formal transition protocol – equivalent to what welfare systems in some countries call "leaving care" procedures – means that when children with disabilities age out of child protection systems at 18, they often receive no continuity of support unless they happen to be registered in the NDR and receiving disability allowance. The trafficking vulnerability mapping documented that young women with intellectual disabilities are a particularly targeted group for trafficking into domestic servitude and sexual exploitation, precisely because their communication difficulties limit help-seeking and their social networks are often narrow.[^637]

### 4.34.5 Policy and Service Gaps

The intersection of child protection and disability systems is one of the most significant service gaps in the Maldivian social sector:

- **No dedicated protocol** for child protection assessment of children with disabilities at FCSCs
- **No systematic sign language interpretation** available at FCSC offices or Family Court
- **No disability-specific training** for CFPS caseworkers or Family Court judges
- **No community-based rehabilitation** or family support programme to enable children with disabilities to remain safely at home
- **No foster care matching** that accounts for the needs of children with complex disabilities[^638]

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 and CFW recommendations both identify disability-inclusive child protection as a cross-cutting priority, calling for joint protocols between MoSFD (child protection), MSFD (disability), and the Ministry of Health (therapy services) to ensure that children with disabilities receive integrated, appropriate protection responses.

---


## 4.68 Children with Disabilities and the Child Protection System


### 4.68.1 Heightened Vulnerability

Children with disabilities face heightened vulnerability to abuse and neglect compared to children without disabilities – a pattern documented consistently across international literature and specifically referenced in Maldivian data. The Disability chapter of this encyclopedia (Part VI) records that persons with disabilities are four times more likely to experience violence, and that 40–60% of visually impaired women report experiencing sexual abuse. For children, the disability-child protection intersection is acute.

Key vulnerability mechanisms include:
- **Communication barriers:** Children with speech, language or cognitive impairments may be unable to disclose abuse, or their disclosures may not be believed or understood by adults
- **Dependence on carers:** Children who depend on personal care from adults are exposed to those adults in ways that create opportunities for abuse
- **Isolation:** Children with disabilities in outer islands may be particularly isolated from protective adult relationships outside the family
- **Limited accessible reporting:** The Childline 1412 system and FCSC offices are not fully accessible for children with communication or mobility impairments

### 4.68.2 The Institutional Care Overlap

Children with mental or physical disabilities may be placed in alternative care under CRPA Article 95 where they are deemed unable to be cared for at home. Rogers and Ali (2023) note that the legal framework enables this placement; however, institutional care environments for children with disabilities are poorly equipped in most settings. There are no disability-specific residential facilities for children; children with disabilities who are placed in institutional care enter facilities designed for children without disabilities, with staff untrained in disability-specific care and communication.

The Disability and Special Needs Service Centre (DSNSC) in Malé provides specialist assessment, therapy, and early intervention services – but is located exclusively in Malé, requiring outer island children to travel to access specialist support. This geographic constraint means that children with disabilities in alternative care outside Malé have access to neither disability-specialist services nor the protective oversight of specialist professionals who might identify abuse.[^878]

### 4.68.3 Inclusive Education as Child Protection

The Ministry of Education has developed an inclusive education policy, with Special Education Needs (SEN) teachers posted to schools in all atolls. Inclusive education serves a child protection function as well as an educational one: children with disabilities who are educated in mainstream settings are in regular contact with mandatory reporters (teachers), have relationships with peers, and are visible to the community in ways that isolated children at home are not.

However, the ratio of SEN specialists to children with disabilities in mainstream schools remains low. Many schools lack accessible infrastructure, trained SEN staff, and adapted learning materials. The child protection benefits of inclusive education are available only to those children who can access it – and many cannot due to these implementation gaps.[^879]

---


## 4.69 Children with Disabilities: The Hidden Emergency in Child Protection


### 4.114.1 The Convergence of Vulnerability

Children with disabilities represent the group at highest risk of violence in the Maldivian child protection system – a finding documented across multiple source types with remarkable consistency. The UNPRPD Situational Analysis (Faisal, 2024) found a prevalence of sexual violence against children with disabilities of approximately 60%, with the majority of survivors being girls. WHO and UNICEF global data estimates that children with disabilities are four times more likely to experience violence than non-disabled children. The Maldivian census data shows that 4% of children have disabilities – meaning approximately 5,128 children are living with the intersection of disability and the elevated violence risk that accompanies it.

The 60% sexual violence prevalence finding, if it approximates the Maldivian reality, means that among the approximately 5,128 children with disabilities, roughly 3,077 have experienced sexual violence. Against the 2,936 total cases received by CFPS in 2023 (of which 1,278 were VAC cases), this suggests a catastrophic level of under-reporting for this specific population – a level of hidden suffering that the formal system has almost no visibility into.[^1001]

### 4.114.2 Why Children with Disabilities Are Particularly Vulnerable

The mechanisms through which disability intersects with child protection vulnerability are multiple and reinforcing:

**Communication barriers:** Children with intellectual disabilities, autism spectrum conditions, deafness, or communication impairments may not be able to report abuse through the verbal disclosure channels that the child protection system primarily relies upon.
