The MCPD case management database, the 1412 helpline, and the school-based detection pathways all assume that a child can articulate their experience verbally. For children who cannot, abuse may be entirely invisible to the system.

**Dependency relationships:** Children with significant physical or intellectual disabilities may be entirely dependent on carers for physical care – bathing, dressing, toileting, medical care – creating intimate physical relationships that create both opportunity for abuse and significant power imbalances that make disclosure difficult and dangerous.

**Institutionalisation risk:** The pattern documented in the Seeds of Reform review – that children with disabilities are disproportionately placed in residential institutions – means that this group is more likely than other children to experience the institutional abuse dynamics that institutional care settings can create. Historical abuse in Maldivian residential care institutions (documented in the Presidential Commission's mandate) affected children with disabilities disproportionately.

**Credibility assumptions:** Even when children with disabilities do disclose abuse, their disclosures may be disbelieved by adults who assume that disability affects the reliability of testimony. The Section 52 evidence requirements for prosecution – requiring five specified categories of evidence – create a threshold that is even harder to meet when the primary witness is a child whose disability affects communication or memory. The criminal justice funnel, already severely restricting for non-disabled children, effectively excludes most abuse of children with disabilities from prosecution.[^1002]

### 4.114.3 The Education Paradox for Children with Disabilities

The education system is simultaneously the most important detection pathway for child protection concerns and a risk environment for children with disabilities. The documented reality of inclusive education in the Maldives reveals a system that has adopted the aspiration of inclusion without building the infrastructure it requires:

- 87% of government schools had SEN (Special Education Needs) units as of 2017, but many were removed in the effort to practice "inclusion" – a reform that shifted children with disabilities into mainstream classes without providing the supports (accessible materials, sign language interpretation, specialist teaching assistants) that make mainstream education work for them
- Deaf students lack sign language interpreters and receive education in a language (Maldivian Sign Language or ASL) that changed midstream – the switch from Dhivehi Sign Language to ASL caused "major disruption" to students who had developed fluency in one system
- Students with intellectual disabilities are placed in higher grades based on age rather than ability, receiving instruction pitched at a level they cannot access
- Students with visual impairments lack accessible textbooks and learning materials
- Children with disabilities face bullying – from peers and in some cases from teachers – with no systematic school-level response

This picture means that children with disabilities who are in school are often experiencing educational deprivation (not receiving meaningful instruction) and heightened peer victimisation, while the protective function of school attendance (adult observation, routine, relationship with teachers who know the child) is partially compromised by the quality of those relationships.[^1003]

### 4.114.4 Child Protection Systems for Children with Disabilities – The Adaptation Gap

The child protection system – the CFPS, the 1412 helpline, the MCPD case management processes, the Family Court, the criminal justice response – has not been designed with children with disabilities in mind. The UNPRPD SitAn (2024) documented that court proceedings involving children with disabilities are conducted "without interpreters" – meaning that a deaf child in a child protection proceeding has no accessible communication channel, and that their experience, wishes, and testimony cannot be meaningfully incorporated into the process.

The 1412 helpline is a telephone-based reporting mechanism. It is inaccessible to deaf children. It requires verbal communication, which may exclude children with intellectual disabilities or communication impairments. There is no text-based, video relay, or augmentative communication alternative to the voice call. The most vulnerable children in the system face the most barriers to accessing its most visible entry point.

The reform agenda for children with disabilities in child protection needs to address: accessible reporting pathways that work across communication modalities; specialist assessment tools adapted for children with different types of disability; training for social workers and police in conducting child-appropriate interviews with children who have communication or cognitive disabilities; and courtroom accommodations (interpreters, intermediate support, accessible procedures) that make the justice system accessible for child witnesses with disabilities.[^1004]

---


# Part 15: Children, Drug Abuse, and Mental Health


## 4.70 Children, Drug Use, and the Narcotics Situation


### 4.35.1 The Scale of Youth Drug Involvement

As documented in Part VIII of this encyclopedia, the Maldives faces one of the most severe youth drug crises in South Asia, and children and adolescents are deeply implicated – as both users and as victims of family disruption caused by parental drug use. Several statistical markers define the scale of the problem:

- Arrests of juveniles for drug offences increased from 16 in 2001 to 79 in 2006 and 164 in 2007 – representing 29% of all juvenile arrests in 2007[^639]
- The 2021 NDA/WHO Situational Analysis tracked juvenile drug use from 2014-2020 and found persistent high levels across all atolls
- The mean age of substance users arrested by police increased from 27.7 years (2015) to 31.3 years (2020) – suggesting an ageing cohort of users alongside continued recruitment of new users from adolescent populations[^640]
- Drug use is the most significant single risk factor for entry into the juvenile justice system, as identified in the JJ Act and NAPVAC analysis
- The MPS data for 2023-2024 showed 120 juvenile cases (2023) rising to 213 juvenile cases (2024), with drug offences representing the largest single category[^641]

The 2003 Rapid Situation Assessment found a mean age of drug initiation of 21.4 years (range 15-42), with almost half of drug users under 20. The profile shifted dramatically with heroin introduction in 1993 – the primary user cohort shifted from 25-40 year olds to 16-25 year olds. Current synthetic drug trends are driving initiation even earlier, with some community workers reporting exposure in the 12-15 age range in Male' gang contexts.[^642]

### 4.35.2 Children Living in Drug-Affected Families

A dimension of the drug crisis that is frequently overlooked in the focus on users is the impact on children living in families where parents or older siblings are drug-dependent. The 2010 World Bank evaluation noted that among a cohort of drug users interviewed, 32% of their siblings also used drugs – suggesting powerful family-level transmission pathways. In communities where drug networks are embedded in social life, children grow up in environments where drug use is normalised.[^643]

The child protection implications of parental drug use are significant:

**Neglect.** Parents or caregivers who are actively using drugs are often unable to provide adequate supervision, nutrition, and emotional care. NDA treatment records and CFPS case files both identify parental drug use as a major factor in neglect cases brought to child protection attention.[^644]

**Domestic violence exposure.** The connection between drug use and intimate partner violence is well-documented globally and is consistent with Maldivian evidence. The Vulnerability Mapping research identified drug use – by husbands and partners in particular – as one of the most consistent risk factors for domestic violence, including child exposure to DV.[^645]

**Economic deprivation.** Drug-dependent parents typically divert household income to drug purchases, leaving children without adequate food, clothing, and educational resources. In severe cases, children's assets – including mobile devices and jewellery – are taken for drug money. Cases of children as young as 12 being sent to steal to support a parent's drug habit have been documented in CFPS files.[^646]

**Parental incarceration.** With 47% of prisoners in the Maldives serving sentences for drug offences, the incarcerated population disproportionately consists of the parents of young children. Parental incarceration is associated with psychological harm, economic hardship, and the need for alternative care arrangements – yet the child welfare implications of parental incarceration receive no systematic attention in the Maldives' criminal justice or social protection systems.[^647]

### 4.35.3 Drug Rehabilitation Services for Young People

The Drug Rehabilitation Centre (DRC) on Himafushi and the regional treatment facilities provide services to adult drug users. There are no dedicated youth drug treatment services for individuals under 18. The Drug Court can refer juvenile cases to NDA programmes, but the NDA's treatment architecture was not designed for adolescent populations and lacks the developmental, educational, and family-focused elements required for effective youth treatment.[^648]

The NDA and MoSFD have identified the development of youth-specific drug treatment services – involving family therapy, school re-integration support, and peer mentoring – as a priority, but no dedicated youth treatment facility had been established as of 2024.[^649]

### 4.35.4 Gang Networks and the Drug Recruitment Pipeline

The connection between juvenile gang involvement and drug supply networks in Male' creates a pipeline from gang membership to drug offending to incarceration. As documented in Part XV (Cross-Cutting Issues), gang violence is a significant social concern in Male', and gang networks are deeply integrated with drug distribution. Young men who join gangs for social belonging and protection often find themselves serving as drug runners and street-level distributors, bringing them into contact with the criminal justice system at an early age. The cycle of juvenile arrest, Imaduddin School placement, and post-release return to gang networks is a documented pattern in case files and practitioner evidence.[^650]

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 identifies gang involvement as a distinct strategic area within the Community pillar, with specific outcomes targeting the reduction of children's recruitment into gangs. The JJ Act's diversion provisions – which permit juvenile cases to be diverted from prosecution toward community service, counselling, and family support – represent the primary mechanism for interrupting this cycle before criminal records solidify.[^651]

---


## 4.71 Drug Abuse, Children, and the Child Protection Interface


### 4.80.1 The NDA-Child Protection Intersection

The Maldives' serious drug problem has direct implications for children across multiple intersecting pathways. The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) identifies drug use as associated with gang involvement, school dropout, criminal activity, and family breakdown – all of which have direct adverse impacts on child welfare.

The primary substances of concern are:
- **Heroin (brown sugar):** The foundational drug of the Maldivian drug crisis, with origins in the 1980s; associated with the establishment of the first state-run child care institution (Kudakudhinge Hiya in 2004) specifically in response to drug-related family breakdown
- **Methamphetamine:** A newer threat growing in prevalence, associated with more severe behavioural effects and more rapid addiction
- **Cannabis:** Widely available; often the first substance used by youth; gateway drug profile

The NDA 2021 survey estimated over **7,000 active drug users** in the Maldives, with the **15–29 age group** representing the largest user cohort. Children are affected both as users themselves (older adolescents) and as children in families where parental drug use is a primary driver of abuse and neglect referrals to CFPS and FCSCs.[^908]

### 4.80.2 Drug Use as an Entry Point to Alternative Care

Substance abuse within the family unit is a significant and documented pathway into alternative care in the Maldivian system. Rogers and Ali (2023) note that the reasons for children entering alternative care are "often due to concerns around parental drug use, maltreatment and domestic violence." The establishment of Kudakudhinge Hiya in 2004 was explicitly linked to the need to protect children whose parents were incapacitated by drug addiction.

The drug-to-care pathway creates a specific challenge for the alternative care system: children entering care due to parental drug use tend to have:
- Attachment disruptions from chaotic early caregiving
- Developmental delays associated with in-utero drug exposure (if the mother used during pregnancy)
- Trauma responses to witnessing parental drug use and associated domestic violence
- Complex behavioural presentations that require trauma-informed residential care

These children enter an alternative care system that is under-resourced in therapeutic capability, with staff who lack training in trauma-informed approaches and with no specialist drug-and-family service available. The consequence, as Rogers and Ali (2023) note, is placement breakdown and cross-atoll movement – children bouncing through a system that cannot meet their needs.[^909]

### 4.80.3 Youth Drug Use and the Juvenile Justice Interface

The intersection of youth drug use with the juvenile justice system is the primary driver of juvenile case volume. Aboobakuru (2017) documented the dramatic rise in juvenile drug arrests from 16 in 2001 to 164 in 2007, with drug offences constituting a growing proportion of juvenile court cases. The Substance Abuse chapter (Part VIII) of this encyclopedia records that 47% of adult prisoners are drug offenders – a proportion that reflects the upstream reality of youth drug involvement.

The Drug Act (Law No. 17/2011) provides for rehabilitation-oriented dispositions for first-time youth offenders. The NDA provides Youth Awareness and School-Based Prevention programmes reaching thousands of students annually. However, treatment services for young people with substance use problems are limited: the NDA's rehabilitation centre operates primarily for adults. Adolescents who develop drug dependence have very limited specialist treatment options available to them.[^910]

### 4.80.4 Parental Imprisonment and Children

A specific child protection consequence of the drug crisis is parental imprisonment. Approximately 47% of prisoners in the Maldives are drug offenders – meaning a substantial proportion of children with a parent in prison have that parent incarcerated due to drug offences. Parental imprisonment:
- Creates an immediate single-parent or no-parent household situation
- Removes financial support from the household
- Exposes the family to social stigma
- May trigger child protection involvement if the remaining parent cannot manage alone
- Creates the possibility of children entering alternative care specifically due to parental imprisonment

The CRPA 2019 explicitly includes parental imprisonment as a ground for alternative care provision (CRPA Article 95). The practical challenge is that a child whose parent is imprisoned due to drug offences – in a system where drug addiction is both a criminal and a health matter – may benefit more from family support services and parental rehabilitation than from removal into institutional care. The intersection of drug policy, criminal justice, and child welfare represents one of the most complex cross-cutting areas of Maldivian social policy.[^911]

---


## 4.72 Children and Mental Health


### 4.36.1 The Adolescent Mental Health Data

As documented comprehensively in Part IX of this encyclopedia, the Maldives faces a significant youth mental health challenge. The key data points most relevant to child protection are:

- **GSHS 2009**: 19.9% of students seriously considered attempting suicide during the past 12 months – nearly one in five school-age children[^652]
- **WHO STEP Survey 2021**: 18.8% of young people aged 15-29 were classified as depressed; for females, the figure rises to 33.5% – one in three young women
- **MSFD data 2024**: 211 self-harm/suicidal behaviour cases received by MoSFD, of which 74% were female – young women represent the most acute self-harm risk group
- **MPS data 2021-2024**: 37 attempted suicide cases involving child victims (persons under 18) reported to police
- **KAP Survey 2023**: 80% of parents worried about their children's mental health; 89% wanted more information on adolescent mental health[^653]

These figures – particularly the 19.9% suicidal ideation rate in the GSHS and the 74% female share of self-harm cases – place children's mental health in the category of child protection emergencies rather than merely clinical services concerns. Self-harm and suicidal behaviour among children require cross-sectoral responses that span health (clinical assessment and treatment), education (school-based early identification), and child protection (assessment of underlying abuse, neglect, or family dysfunction).[^654]

### 4.36.2 Mental Health as a Child Protection Risk Factor

The relationship between mental health and child protection runs in both directions:

**Mental health as a consequence of abuse.** Children who experience abuse, neglect, domestic violence, and other forms of maltreatment are at significantly elevated risk of developing depression, anxiety, PTSD, dissociation, and self-harm behaviour. The causal pathway from childhood maltreatment to adolescent mental health crisis is one of the strongest in developmental psychology. Given the documented levels of child abuse in the Maldives, a proportion of the mental health burden documented in the GSHS and STEP Survey is directly attributable to childhood maltreatment that went unaddressed.[^655]

**Mental health as a context for child protection risk.** Conversely, children growing up in households where a parent has untreated mental illness – or where the child's own mental health condition has not been addressed – face elevated risk of neglect and maltreatment. The connection between maternal depression and child neglect is particularly well-established; in contexts where women experience the high rates of depression documented in the STEP Survey (33.5% of females aged 15-29), the implications for infant and young child care are significant.[^656]

### 4.36.3 School-Based Mental Health: Counsellors and Early Identification

The school system provides the principal point of contact between a mental health response infrastructure and the adolescent population. The Maldives has begun deploying school counsellors – particularly in Male' and some regional schools – as a first-line response to student mental health concerns. School counsellors serve as a referral pathway to clinical services, a first point of disclosure for students experiencing abuse or family dysfunction, and a preventive resource through group-based social-emotional learning.[^657]

However, as noted in the Mental Health chapter (§9.4), school counsellor deployment is highly uneven:
- Most outer atoll schools have no counsellor
- Even where counsellors are nominally present, their caseload may be excessive and their training limited
- Male' schools with high student-to-counsellor ratios cannot provide meaningful individual support
- Counsellors are not systematically connected to CFPS referral pathways, meaning that disclosures of abuse made to school counsellors may not routinely trigger mandatory reporting procedures[^658]

The Mental Health Centre at Hulhumale' Hospital and Dr Abdul Samad Memorial Hospital (Thinadhoo) conduct **Early Intervention programmes in schools** – identifying early signs of mental health concerns and providing support. The Ungoofaaru Regional Hospital has established a dedicated "Sidhdha" wing with child-friendly areas for paediatric mental health patients. These are important advances, but they reach only a small fraction of the children in need.[^659]

### 4.36.4 Suicidal Behaviour: A Gendered Emergency

The 74% female share of MoSFD self-harm/suicidal behaviour cases in 2024, combined with the 33.5% female depression rate among 15-29 year olds, points to an acute and gendered mental health emergency among adolescent girls and young women. The vulnerability mapping research identified several structural factors driving girls' elevated distress:

- Domestic violence exposure (as victims or witnesses)
- Sexual abuse, often repeated and ongoing
- Child marriage and early pregnancy
- Limited educational and economic opportunity
- Social isolation, particularly for girls on outer islands
- Religious and social pressure around modesty, chastity, and compliance
- The inability to seek help without family knowledge due to confidentiality norms in small island communities[^660]

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 identifies the provision of psychosocial support for children in vulnerable families – with flexible, school-based options – as a key output, specifically citing the need to reach girls experiencing these compounding vulnerabilities. The CFW recommendations call for expanded and accessible mental health services linked to child protection and GBV response systems.[^661]

---


## 4.73 Online Safety and Digital Risks for Children


### 4.37.1 The Digital Context: Penetration and Risk

The Maldives has one of the highest rates of internet penetration in South Asia, with mobile connectivity reaching most inhabited islands. Among adolescents and young people, smartphone ownership and social media use are near-universal in Male' and increasingly common in outer atolls. This digital connectivity creates significant positive potential – access to education resources, social connection for isolated island communities, economic opportunity – alongside documented child safety risks.[^662]

The CRC Periodic Report Draft identified online safety as one of the key "new" child rights challenges requiring attention, noting that "the legal and institutional framework for child online protection is underdeveloped relative to the risks." The CSEC data presented in §4.26 confirms that online exploitation – including production and distribution of Child Sexual Abuse Material (CSAM) and online grooming – is a growing concern. CFPS case data shows a rising trend in online sexual exploitation referrals, though absolute numbers remain lower than reported offline cases.[^663]

### 4.37.2 Online Child Sexual Exploitation and Abuse

Online CSEA in the Maldives is characterised by several documented patterns:

**Grooming via social media.** Older individuals (adults or older adolescents) use platforms including Facebook, TikTok, Instagram, and messaging applications to build relationships with younger children, extracting sexual images or arranging in-person meetings under false pretexts. The small scale of Maldivian society – where many people are acquainted across digital networks – both facilitates predatory contact (exploiting social connections) and complicates anonymity for victims.

**CSAM production within peer relationships.** Cases involving adolescents sharing sexual images of each other – whether consensually or under pressure – have been referred to CFPS and MPS. The Maldivian legal framework does not clearly distinguish between CSAM production by peers (where the aggressor is also a minor) and CSAM production by adult abusers. The SOA 2014 and Penal Code provisions on child pornography were drafted before the current scale of peer-to-peer digital abuse was foreseeable.[^664]

**Sextortion.** Cases have been documented where abusers obtained sexual images and then threatened victims with their disclosure to family and community unless further images or money were provided. In the Maldivian context – where disclosure of sexual activity carries severe social consequences for girls – sextortion creates profound coercion that can be difficult for victims to escape and report.[^665]

**CSAM trafficking and international dimensions.** The NRM documentation of child CSEC cases includes evidence of Maldivian CSAM being distributed on international platforms. The MPS Cyber Crime Unit and CFPS have both received referrals from international bodies (including INTERPOL and NCMEC) regarding Maldivian-source CSAM. These cases require international cooperation frameworks that are still being developed.[^666]

### 4.37.3 Legal Framework for Online Child Safety

The Computer Crime Act (2015) criminalises several categories of online offending relevant to child protection, including unauthorised access, data interception, and computer-related fraud. The SOA 2014 covers offences involving digital means if they constitute sexual offences (grooming, production of CSAM).[^667]

However, the legal framework has significant gaps:
- No explicit legal definition of "grooming" as a standalone offence
- Ambiguous application of SOA provisions to peer-to-peer CSAM among adolescents
- No mandatory reporting requirement for internet service providers or social media platforms operating in the Maldives to report CSAM to authorities
- No child online safety legislation equivalent to the models being adopted in other countries[^668]

### 4.37.4 Digital Literacy and Awareness

The MoSFD and the ICT Authority have conducted periodic digital literacy and online safety campaigns, including school-based workshops and social media awareness campaigns. However, these have been ad hoc rather than programmatic, and they have not reached outer atoll schools consistently.[^669]

The CFW recommendations identify digital safety education as a component of the NAPVAC 2024-2028's Online pillar, calling for:
- Mandatory digital safety curriculum in schools from Grade 4 onwards
- Parent education programmes on monitoring and supporting children online
- Training for CFPS workers and police in handling online CSEA cases
- A national reporting mechanism for online CSEA
- Public awareness campaign on sextortion, grooming indicators, and reporting pathways[^670]

---


# Part 16: Child Poverty, Economic Vulnerability, and Climate


## 4.74 Child Poverty and Multidimensional Deprivation


Child poverty in the Maldives operates within a paradox: the country's upper-middle-income classification (by GNI per capita) masks significant internal inequality, particularly between Malé and the outer atolls. The concentration of economic activity in tourism and the Greater Malé region means that children in outer island communities may experience poverty rates substantially higher than national averages.

The **UNICEF Situation Analysis (2021, updated 2025)** and the **Child-Equity-Census-2022** analysis provide the most current evidence on multidimensional child deprivation in the Maldives, examining dimensions including education access, health, nutrition, water and sanitation, housing, and protection from violence. Key findings indicate that children in outer atolls face systematically poorer access to quality education, health services, and child protection services compared to their peers in the Greater Malé area.[^69]

The intersection of child poverty with other vulnerability factors – including single-parent household status, parental substance use, exposure to domestic violence, and disability – creates compounding disadvantage. The vulnerability mapping research documented that children who experience multiple intersecting vulnerabilities are least likely to be reached by the existing service delivery architecture.[^70]

---


## 4.75 Child Poverty and Economic Vulnerability: The Geographic Dimension


### 4.39.1 The Scale of Child Poverty

Child poverty in the Maldives cannot be understood without understanding the geographic dimension documented in Part XII of this encyclopedia. The headline 5.4% national poverty rate conceals a profound spatial divide: atoll poverty is 9.5% compared to 0.9% in Male', and atolls contain **92% of the country's poor**. Children are disproportionately represented in poor households because poor households in the atolls tend to have more children per adult.[^678]

The 2022 Child-Equity Census and the World Bank 2022 Poverty Assessment together provide the most detailed picture of child poverty:

- Households with more than one child per adult are more than **twice as likely** to be poor
- Such households face poverty rates of 12% nationally (13.9% in atolls, 12.5% in Male')
- Children living in households headed by someone without primary education face a national poverty rate of **12.6%** and a deprivation rate of 39.9% in Male'
- Having a household head with tertiary education is associated with a poverty rate approximately **30 times lower** than for households where the head has not completed primary school[^679]

The connection between child poverty and child protection risk is well-established in the Maldivian evidence base: CFPS case data shows that child protection cases are disproportionately concentrated in atoll communities and in low-income households in Male'. This is not simply because poor families are more abusive – it reflects both the additional stresses that poverty creates (housing overcrowding, food insecurity, parental mental health strain) and the differential exposure of poor families to state surveillance and reporting.[^680]

### 4.39.2 Children in the Atoll Economic Trap

The economic vulnerability of atoll children operates through several intersecting pathways:

**Education quality and access.** While primary school access is near-universal, secondary education quality is significantly lower in outer atolls than in Male', and access to secondary school often requires children to leave their home island and live with relatives or in boarding arrangements in a regional centre or Male'. The cost and disruption of this arrangement means that many atoll families do not invest in girls' secondary education, particularly where marriage is perceived as the principal life pathway. The result is a generational transmission of disadvantage that perpetuates atoll poverty.[^681]

**Health access and child survival.** Atoll health centres have significantly less capacity than Male' health facilities, including for maternal and child health. Neonatal mortality and under-5 mortality rates are higher in outer atolls. Children with serious health conditions requiring specialist intervention must travel to Male' – a journey that is costly, time-consuming, and emotionally stressful for families, and which may be deferred until conditions become acute.[^682]

**Social protection coverage gaps.** NSPA programmes – including the Single Parent Allowance, Foster Parent Allowance, and Food Aid programme – reach registered households but coverage in outer atolls is limited by awareness, registration barriers, and administrative capacity. The Hameed et al. (2022) study on disability allowance coverage found the same pattern for disability benefits: lowest coverage among outer atoll populations, precisely those who most need support.[^683]

**Fisheries vulnerability and climate change.** For children in fishing families – a significant proportion of atoll children – the climate-related decline in fisheries documented in Part XIV represents an existential livelihood threat. As tuna migration patterns shift, fishing effort must increase to maintain catch levels, drawing boys out of education and into work, increasing family economic precarity, and reducing household resources for children's welfare.[^684]

### 4.39.3 Child Poverty and Child Protection: The Nexus

The most important connection for child protection purposes is not simply that poor children are more likely to be maltreated – though the association is real and documented – but that the response capacity for child protection in poor communities is weakest precisely where protection needs are greatest:

- Only 20 of 189 inhabited islands have FCSCs
- Social workers in remote atolls carry caseloads of 70-80 active cases and often serve multiple islands
- Legal aid services for poor families navigating maintenance enforcement, custody disputes, and child protection proceedings are effectively unavailable outside Male'
- The 3.7% rate of women who experienced violence receiving any legal assistance (documented in the Legal Framework chapter) translates directly into a near-absence of legal support for poor mothers seeking protection for their children[^685]

The CFW recommendations and NAPVAC 2024-2028 both identify economic support for vulnerable families – including expanded social protection, employment pathways for single mothers, and maintenance enforcement reform – as components of the child protection system, not just poverty alleviation programmes. The rationale is that reducing family economic stress reduces child maltreatment risk and reduces the need for child removal.[^686]

---


## 4.76 Children in Poverty and Social Protection


### 4.96.1 The Poverty-Child Protection Nexus

The 18% child poverty estimate (HIES 2019–20) establishes poverty as the most prevalent structural risk factor for Maldivian children. Poverty-driven vulnerability intersects with child protection through multiple pathways: inadequate nutrition and healthcare, housing instability, parental stress and mental health challenges, reduced parental supervision due to long working hours, and the economic vulnerability of single-parent households following divorce.

The geographic concentration of poverty – 92% of the poor live in atolls, while 92% of services are concentrated in Malé – means that poor children are also geographically disadvantaged children. The compound disadvantage of poverty and geographic remoteness creates the most vulnerable group in the Maldivian child protection system: children in poor outer island households with limited access to either prevention services or protective response.

### 4.96.2 The Social Protection Response for Children

The National Social Protection Agency (NSPA) administers the key social protection instruments relevant to children:

**Single Parent Allowance (Madhana):** MVR 1,000 per month per child (maximum MVR 3,000 per household). This allowance targets the predominant female single-parent household created by the Maldivian divorce system. The Madhana was expanded in 2022 to increase coverage and benefit levels. Application available through OneGov portal.

**Disability Allowance:** MVR 2,000–7,000 per month (tiered by disability severity) for registered persons with disabilities. Children with disabilities can be registered and receive the allowance. Required documentation: registration in the National Disability Registry (ndr.nspa.gov.mv).

**Foster Parent Allowance:** Monthly payments to families providing foster care for children in state protection. This allowance is a critical financial support for the expansion of the foster care system – without financial support, prospective foster carers in outer islands may not be able to afford the costs of caring for an additional child.

**Aasandha:** Universal health insurance covering all Maldivian citizens including all children. Free at the point of use across the national health network. Has had a substantial impact on health outcomes for children, contributing to the significant decline in infant and child mortality.

**Zakat distribution:** The Zakat House administers the obligatory Islamic almsgiving, distributing support to the poor, orphans, and others in need. Integrated into the OneGov portal in late 2024.

### 4.96.3 The Maintenance Enforcement Gap as a Child Poverty Driver

The Family Court is required to determine maintenance arrangements for children before a divorce is granted. The minimum child maintenance payment is MVR 2,000 per month for one child. However, enforcement is effectively absent:

- Approximately MVR 2 million in maintenance payments had accumulated unclaimed at the Family Court
- Women reported maintenance going unpaid for years with no effective enforcement mechanism
- Court processing of maintenance orders was historically slow (30–45 days)
- The burden of collecting maintenance falls on the mothers to whom it is owed

Children in single-parent households whose maintenance obligations are being evaded are directly impoverished by this enforcement failure. The maintenance gap is not merely a women's rights issue – it is a child poverty issue with direct child protection implications. Children who are impoverished following their parents' divorce face the compounding vulnerabilities documented above: reduced nutrition, housing instability, parental stress, and reduced access to education and health services.[^935]

---


## 4.77 Child Protection and the Maldivian Economy: The Structural Links


### 4.121.1 Tourism Dependency and Child Vulnerability

The Maldives' near-total dependence on tourism as the primary driver of GDP – with tourism and its related services contributing approximately 30% of GDP and 60% of foreign exchange earnings – creates specific structural conditions for child protection that are rarely analysed in standard child protection literature.

Tourism dependency concentrates economic activity in resort islands that are legally and physically separate from inhabited islands where the majority of the local population lives. The separation of employment (in resorts) from family life (on inhabited islands) means that the typical male economic migration pattern involves fathers living in resort accommodation for extended periods, returning to their home islands infrequently. The impact on family structure – fathers absent from the home for extended periods, mothers functioning as de facto single parents with reduced income and support – creates conditions that elevate child protection risk through parental absence, reduced supervision, economic vulnerability when remittances are irregular, and the emotional strain on relationships that extended separation creates.

The asymmetric employment structure also means that the economic crisis of COVID-19 – when tourism revenue collapsed entirely – hit Maldivian households with unusual severity. The 44% average income reduction documented during COVID-19 was experienced most acutely by resort workers and their families, with direct consequences for children's material welfare, family stability, and parental stress that elevated child protection risk at precisely the moment when the professional response system was also disrupted.[^1025]

### 4.121.2 Housing Density and Child Protection

The housing conditions in Male' – the most overcrowded capital city in Asia by population density, with approximately 65,000 people per square kilometre – create child protection risk conditions that have not been systematically analysed in the Maldivian literature. The effects operate through multiple pathways:

**Lack of privacy:** Overcrowded housing means that children and adults share sleeping spaces, bathrooms, and living areas with limited privacy. The perpetration of sexual abuse within the home – already severely under-reported – is both facilitated (by the necessity of sharing intimate spaces) and complicated in its detection (by the normalisation of physical proximity that makes unusual behaviour harder to identify).

**Stress and conflict:** Overcrowding is a documented risk factor for domestic violence and physical child abuse globally. The physical and psychological stress of overcrowded living conditions, combined with the financial pressures of Male' housing costs, creates conditions in which parental capacity is compromised and the risk of child maltreatment elevated.

**No space for safe disclosure:** A child wishing to disclose abuse to a teacher, neighbour, or social worker needs a private space in which to speak. In overcrowded housing with limited room-by-room privacy, the absence of private space suppresses disclosure – there is no place where a child can be confident they will not be overheard.

The housing crisis also directly intersects with child protection outcomes through the Section 42 custody trap analysed in the gender chapter: a mother who needs to move islands to escape proximity to an abusive ex-partner loses custody of her children unless the father consents. In Male', where housing is both unaffordable and overcrowded, a woman who needs to move to affordable housing outside the capital faces this custody loss mechanism directly. Housing policy is child protection policy, but the two domains are never analysed together in Maldivian planning frameworks.[^1026]

### 4.121.3 Fisheries Sector and Remote Island Vulnerability

The traditional fisheries economy – characterised by male-dominated fishing crews spending extended periods at sea – creates family structures in outer atoll communities that share characteristics with the tourism economy's resort separation model. Fishing communities in which fathers are absent at sea for extended periods have similar child protection risk patterns to resort-employment families: de facto single-parent households, reduced supervision, economic volatility when catches are poor, and the emotional stress of extended partner separation.

The additional dimension in fishing communities is geographic isolation. A mother raising children alone in a remote fishing village has fewer institutional resources, less accessible emergency support, and more restricted mobility than an urban counterpart. The *gah'therikan* community solidarity that characterises island communities provides some protection, but it is unavailable for the most serious abuse cases – particularly those involving perpetrators who are themselves part of the tight-knit community network on which that solidarity depends.[^1027]

---


## 4.78 Children and Climate Vulnerability


As a low-lying island nation with a maximum natural ground elevation of approximately 2.4 metres, the Maldives faces existential climate vulnerability. Children are disproportionately affected by climate-related risks including:

- **Saltwater intrusion** affecting freshwater supplies on outer islands, with consequences for child health and nutrition.
- **Extreme weather events** including flooding, storm surges, and beach erosion that damage homes, schools, and health facilities.
- **Climate-related displacement** that disrupts education, separates children from support networks, and creates psychosocial stress.
- **Food security impacts** as changing ocean conditions affect fisheries – the primary livelihood and protein source for many island communities.

The intersection of climate vulnerability with child protection is an emerging area requiring integrated policy responses that connect environmental adaptation with child welfare planning.[^71]

---


## 4.79 Children and Climate Change: Vulnerability, Displacement, and Adaptation


### 4.40.1 Children as the Most Climate-Vulnerable Population

Children face disproportionate vulnerability to climate change impacts in every documented dimension. In the Maldivian context, where the entire population is existentially threatened by sea-level rise and climate disruption, children's specific vulnerabilities are amplified by developmental factors, dependency, and the long time horizons over which climate impacts will accumulate:

**Physical vulnerability.** Children are physiologically more vulnerable than adults to heat stress, vector-borne disease (dengue, diarrhoeal disease), and waterborne illness – the primary climate health risks in the Maldives. The under-5 population is most at risk from the diarrhoeal disease outbreaks that follow flooding events, freshwater contamination, and infrastructure damage on outer islands.[^687]

**Nutritional vulnerability.** As documented in §14.3, the Maldives' near-total food import dependency means that global climate-driven food price increases directly affect household food security. Children in poor households – particularly in atolls – are most exposed to nutritional shortfalls when food prices rise. The climate-fisheries nexus is particularly significant: tuna is the primary protein source for Maldivian children, and disruption to fisheries translates directly into reduced protein availability in atoll households.[^688]

**Educational disruption.** Extreme weather events – cyclones, flooding events, swell waves – disrupt schooling on outer islands. When island infrastructure is damaged, schools may be closed for extended periods. The increasing frequency of such events, documented in §14.6, translates into chronic educational disruption for outer island children that compounds the education quality gaps already documented. Children who miss extended periods of school due to climate events are at elevated risk of not returning – particularly girls, who face greater competing demands on their time and attention.[^689]

### 4.40.2 Disaster Displacement and Child Protection

The 2004 Indian Ocean Tsunami was the most significant climate-related disaster in Maldivian history, killing 82 people and causing damage equivalent to 62% of GDP. Fourteen islands were evacuated entirely, and several communities were permanently relocated. As documented in §14.5, the tsunami experience revealed critical gaps in disaster response for children:

- Child separation from families during evacuation
- Loss of birth and identity documentation in flooded homes, creating registration challenges
- Disruption of child protection referral pathways during the emergency
- Absence of child-friendly spaces in displacement camps
- Exploitation risk for displaced children, particularly adolescent girls and out-of-wedlock children without clear guardian status[^690]

The NDMA's National Emergency Operations Plan and the 7th National Disaster Management Plan include child protection provisions in emergencies, including the deployment of a Protection Cluster under NDMA for emergency situations. UNICEF has worked with MoSFD to develop child protection in emergency protocols. However, the 2024 NAPVAC analysis found that these protocols remain primarily on paper – the practical capacity for child protection response during emergencies is limited by the same workforce and geographic constraints that limit everyday child protection.[^691]

### 4.40.3 Forced Relocation and Psychosocial Impact

The government's population consolidation strategy – which envisages moving communities from the smallest and most vulnerable islands to regional hubs – has profound implications for children's wellbeing. Research on planned relocation in other Pacific Island contexts consistently finds that:

- Children who are relocated as part of community moves experience disrupted peer relationships, loss of familiar environment, and adjustment challenges
- Adolescents who have been relocated from small islands to Male' or regional centres report significantly higher rates of loneliness, social isolation, and mental health symptoms
- The loss of island identity – the deep attachment to specific places that characterises Maldivian cultural life – creates a form of grief (solastalgia) that is particularly acute for young people who have grown up in a specific place[^692]

The climate-mental health nexus documented in §14.4 has particular resonance for children growing up with awareness that their islands may be uninhabitable within their lifetimes. This existential climate anxiety – while less documented for children than adults – represents an emerging dimension of child mental health that is specific to SIDS contexts and requires specific acknowledgement in child mental health policy.[^693]

### 4.40.4 Gender-Responsive Climate Adaptation for Children

The Gender-Responsive Climate Financing Strategy (2023), developed by the UNFCCC Gender Programme, identifies women and girls as among the highest-risk groups for climate-related disasters. For girl children specifically:

- Girls bear disproportionate domestic and caregiving burdens during climate disruptions, reducing their availability for school and paid work
- Girls on outer islands face more limited mobility during emergencies, both due to cultural norms around female movement and due to practical care responsibilities for younger siblings and elderly relatives
- Adolescent girls face elevated trafficking and early marriage risks in displacement contexts, where family economic stress and disrupted community oversight create vulnerability[^694]

The GRFS 2023 and UNFCCC Gender Action Plan commitments documented in the Gender chapter (§5.72-5.73) establish a framework for gender-responsive climate adaptation that, when operationalised, should explicitly address children's gender-differentiated climate vulnerabilities.

---


## 4.80 Children and Climate Change: The Child Protection Dimension


### 4.122.1 Climate Vulnerability as Child Protection Risk

The Maldives' existential vulnerability to climate change – with 80% of land less than one metre above sea level and a growing risk of permanent inundation, storm surge, and freshwater contamination – has a child protection dimension that is rarely articulated explicitly in either climate policy or child protection policy.

Children are disproportionately affected by climate-related health risks. The WHO documents that children are particularly vulnerable to the health effects of climate change: waterborne disease from flooding, respiratory illness from dust and pollution during drought conditions, malnutrition from fisheries disruption, vector-borne disease expansion, and the mental health effects of climate anxiety, displacement, and community loss. In the Maldivian context, island flooding that contaminates freshwater supplies, disrupts schools, and forces temporary evacuation affects children's health, education, and stability in ways that create both acute and chronic child protection risk.

Displacement from inhabited islands due to climate risk creates child protection concerns analogous to urban migration: displaced families lose their *gah'therikan* safety net, enter unfamiliar communities without established social relationships, and face the additional stresses of permanent loss of home community and identity. The planned consolidation of vulnerable populations onto climate-resilient islands – a long-standing government policy implemented through the population consolidation programme – disrupts established community protective structures and creates the conditions in which child protection vulnerabilities are elevated during transition periods.[^1028]

### 4.122.2 Gender-Responsive Climate Frameworks and Children

The Gender-Responsive Fisheries Strategy (GRFS) 2023 and the UNFCCC Gender Action Plan that governs Maldives' climate response both recognise that gender shapes climate vulnerability and adaptation capacity. What is less developed in these frameworks is the child-specific dimension of climate-gender vulnerability.

Girls in climate-affected communities face compound vulnerabilities. When school attendance is disrupted by flooding or displacement, girls are more likely than boys to be withdrawn from school permanently – reverting to domestic and care roles that provide immediately needed household labour but foreclose their educational futures. When family income falls due to climate disruption of fisheries or tourism, girls may face increased pressure toward early marriage as a strategy for reducing family economic burden. When displaced communities experience the breakdown of social norms that accompanies disruption, girls are at elevated risk of gender-based violence and sexual exploitation.

Child protection planning has not systematically integrated climate change as a risk factor. The NAPVAC 2024–2028 does not include climate displacement as a risk context for VAC. The NAPDI does not address the specific vulnerability of children with disabilities to climate disasters, despite the documented reality that emergency evacuation and disaster response systems are inaccessible for wheelchair users and persons with mobility limitations. The integration of climate adaptation planning and child protection planning is a gap that will become increasingly critical as climate impacts intensify.[^1029]

---


# Part 17: The Child Protection Workforce and Service Infrastructure


## 4.81 Social Worker Workforce and Capacity Challenges


### 4.16.1 Caseload

The most fundamental challenge facing the child protection system in the Maldives is the extreme imbalance between the number of children requiring protection and the workforce available to provide it. As of the most recent data:

- **61 child protection social workers** serve a child population of **128,208**, yielding an average potential caseload of approximately **160 children per worker** – more than five times the internationally recommended caseload of 25–30 cases per worker.
- In practice, caseworkers handle **70–80 active cases each**, which – while lower than the theoretical maximum – still represents more than double the recommended caseload.[^72]

The consequences of chronic overload are well documented in the child protection literature and are evident in the Maldivian system:

- **Reactive rather than proactive practice:** Workers are forced to prioritise immediate crisis response over preventive work, family support, and planned intervention.
- **Shallow assessment:** Insufficient time per case means that assessments are often cursory, risk factors may be missed, and the quality of professional judgment is compromised.
- **Burnout and turnover:** The combination of high caseloads, emotional intensity, personal safety risks (including death threats), limited supervision, and modest remuneration drives high rates of burnout and staff turnover, further depleting an already inadequate workforce.
- **Inability to provide ongoing support:** Even when cases are identified and initial intervention occurs, the capacity to provide sustained follow-up, monitoring, and support is severely limited.[^73]

### 4.16.2 Geographic Distribution

The 61 child protection social workers are distributed across Malé and the 19 atoll-level FCSCs, with the majority concentrated in Malé and atoll capitals. The Ministry operates in only **20 of 189 inhabited islands**, meaning that children on the remaining 169 islands have no direct access to a professional child protection worker. For these communities, child protection depends entirely on informal community mechanisms, volunteer Community Social Groups (where they exist), and the willingness and ability of individuals to contact the Child Helpline 1412 or travel to the nearest FCSC.[^74]

### 4.16.3 Professional Development and Supervision

The professional development pathway for child protection social workers – from the initial one-year Advanced Certificate (2006) through the Advanced Diploma to the four-year BSW – has created a workforce with heterogeneous qualification levels. Some practitioners hold university-level social work qualifications; others entered the field with short-course training and have acquired expertise primarily through practice experience.

The recommendation for a **formal supervisory system**, first made following the 2009 baby death case, has been partially implemented but remains inconsistent. Clinical supervision – in which an experienced practitioner provides structured, regular oversight of a worker's caseload, professional judgment, and emotional wellbeing – is essential in child protection practice but is not systematically available across the system.[^75]

---


## 4.82 Workforce Capacity Gaps and Staffing Constraints


### 4.44.1 The Scale of the Understaffing Problem

The child protection workforce crisis in the Maldives is not a peripheral resource constraint – it is the defining operational reality of the system. Every other element of the child protection framework – the laws, the protocols, the databases, the referral pathways – depends for its functioning on the availability of adequate numbers of trained, supported, supervised professionals. When the workforce is insufficient, the framework operates in name only.

The core numbers, assembled from UNICEF 2022, the Statistics Compendium, and CFPS administrative data:

| Indicator | Figure |
|---|---|
| Total social workers (CFPS) | 61 |
| Children under 18 in the Maldives (Census 2022) | 128,208 |
| Social worker: children ratio | 1:2,101 |
| Recommended ratio (WHO, UNICEF) | 1:25-30 per active case; approximately 1:500 population |
| Average caseload (UNICEF 2022 estimate) | ~160 active cases per worker |
| Recommended maximum caseload | 25-30 cases per worker |
| Caseload excess factor | 5.3x-6.4x recommended maximum |
| DV caseworkers in Male' | 3 |
| Average DV caseload per Male' worker | 70-80 active cases |
| FCSCs covering 20 of 189 inhabited islands | 89% of islands with no dedicated office |
| Islands with any CFPS presence (IBAMA only) | Partial coverage, not systematically mapped |[^733]

These figures represent a system in structural crisis. When a social worker carries 160 active cases – each of which should receive regular contact, assessment, and documented review – the quality of every individual response is compromised. Workers must triage, choosing which children are most at immediate risk, and leaving lower-priority cases unvisited for weeks or months. In a system where child protection visits are the primary mechanism for monitoring safety, this is not a minor service quality issue: it is a direct risk factor for child harm.[^734]

### 4.44.2 Historical Context of Workforce Development

The workforce has not always been this small. The current figure of 61 social workers represents the result of decades of training, attrition, and constrained recruitment. The earliest CFPS workers, trained under the original 1997-2005 system building phase, received a one-year Advanced Certificate in Social Work – a qualification that, while limited, provided a foundation. Successive cohorts have received increasingly sophisticated training, including through the MALDIVS Social Work Certificate and Diploma programmes at the Maldives National University.

The NSAP-CP 2020-2024 set a target of increasing the social work workforce to 90+ professionals by 2024. This target was not met. Recruitment is constrained by:

- **Salary levels**: CFPS social workers are civil servants whose salaries are set by the civil service framework; the salary scale has not been competitive with private sector alternatives for education-level comparable graduates
- **Workload reputation**: The field's reputation for overwhelming caseloads and inadequate supervision discourages qualified graduates from entering
- **Geographic deployment**: Workers assigned to outer atoll FCSCs face isolation, separation from family, and limited career development opportunities
- **Professional burnout**: High attrition among experienced workers means that the effective institutional knowledge of the system is constantly being rebuilt[^735]

### 4.44.3 Supervision and Professional Support

One of the most significant structural deficiencies is the absence of an adequate professional supervision system for CFPS workers. Professional supervision in social work – a structured, regular, protected space for reflective practice, case consultation, and professional development – is a recognised prerequisite for effective and ethical practice. Without it, workers make complex judgements about child safety without appropriate collegial consultation, develop idiosyncratic practice patterns uncorrected by feedback, and carry unprocessed secondary trauma from exposure to child abuse cases.[^736]

The UNICEF 2022 assessment found that professional supervision ratios in the Maldivian CFPS were inadequate – supervisors themselves carried substantial caseloads, leaving insufficient time for the intensive supervision that complex cases require. The physical remoteness of outer atoll FCSCs means that even face-to-face supervision visits from Malé headquarters are logistically challenging, reducing supervision in the geographic areas of greatest need.[^737]

### 4.44.4 The NAPVAC 2024-2028 Workforce Targets

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 establishes revised and more ambitious workforce targets:

- Increase total social workers to **120** (a near-doubling of current workforce)
- Achieve a recommended maximum caseload of **25-30 active cases per worker**
- Establish a formal supervision system with a **supervisor:worker ratio** of no more than 1:6
- Pilot FCSC presence in **10 additional high-need islands** beyond the current 20
- Introduce **specialist caseworker roles** – separate caseloads for child sexual abuse cases, juvenile justice cases, and alternative care cases
- Develop a **professional registration system** for social workers, establishing minimum training standards and ongoing CPD requirements[^738]

These targets, if achieved, would represent a fundamental transformation of the system's operational capacity. However, achievement requires not only recruitment but sustained budget commitment – and the NAPVAC 2024-2028's implementation has not yet secured the multi-year budget envelope needed to guarantee workforce expansion independent of annual budget negotiations.[^739]

### 4.44.5 Training and Capacity Building Architecture

The training infrastructure for CFPS workers has improved substantially since 2010:

- The **Maldives National University** offers Certificate and Diploma programmes in Social Work, with an increasing emphasis on child protection practice
- **UNICEF** provides in-service training for CFPS workers on specific topics including case management, mandatory reporting, MDT participation, and trauma-informed practice
- **The Family Assessment Form (FAF) 2020**, a structured risk and needs assessment tool introduced with UNICEF support, has improved the consistency of initial assessments at point of referral
- **The MCPD database** provides a platform for case recording that, when used fully, creates a professional trail of decision-making that supports supervision and accountability[^740]

However, the baseline qualification for CFPS workers – the one-year Advanced Certificate introduced in the original system-building phase – has not been comprehensively upgraded. Many currently serving workers do not hold a degree-level qualification in social work or a related discipline. The professional registration system envisaged in the NAPVAC would, if implemented with appropriate transitional provisions, provide a pathway for current workers to achieve recognised qualifications while ensuring that new entrants meet a minimum qualification standard.[^741]

---


## 4.83 Service Infrastructure Directory: Child Protection Services Across the Country


### 4.120.1 Emergency Contacts for Child Protection Referral

The immediate emergency contact structure for child protection in the Maldives is:

| **Service** | **Number** | **Availability** |
|---|---|---|
| Police Emergency | 119 | 24/7 |
| Child Protection Helpline (1412) | 1412 | 24/7, toll-free |
| Family Issues Helpline | 1421 | Working hours |
| Police Family and Child Protection | 979 0163 | Working hours |
| Family Legal Clinic Hotline | 9977771 | 9am–4pm, working days |

The 24/7 availability of the 1412 helpline is the most important feature of this emergency architecture. For a child at risk outside working hours – when FCSCs are closed and when the formal system is at minimum capacity – the 1412 helpline is the primary accessible entry point. Its toll-free status removes the cost barrier that has historically deterred calls from outer-island communities.

The 1421 Family Issues Helpline, restricted to working hours, is less accessible for the households and children who most need help – those in crisis in the evening and on weekends, which is precisely when domestic violence peaks. The gap between the 24/7 1412 line (child-focused) and the working-hours 1421 line (family-focused) means that adult DV victims calling for help outside working hours have a more constrained pathway than children calling about the same crisis.[^1021]

### 4.120.2 Therapeutic Services for Child Protection: Male' Concentration

Child-specific therapeutic services in the Maldives are concentrated in Male' and Hulhumale'. The available services include:

**Autism and Developmental Services:**
- Maldives Autism Association (MAA) – intensive therapy for children aged 2–12 with ASD, with a team including paediatrician, psychologist, occupational therapist, speech therapists, and special education teachers
- Great Minds – autism therapy, occupational therapy, speech therapy
- Physio and Autism Clinic – branches in Male' and Hulhumale'
- Blyss Multi-Speciality Therapy Centre – Hulhumale'

**Counselling and Psychotherapy:**
- Institute for Mental Wellbeing (IMWB)
- Institute for Counselling and Psychotherapy (ICP)
- VCare Counselling and Psychotherapy Centre (Villa College)
- Family Relationship Services – family counselling and relationship services
- Multiple private practitioners offering individual and family therapy

**Physiotherapy and Rehabilitation:**
- Physio Solutions, Physio Clinic, Wellcare Physiotherapy (multiple Male'/Hulhumale' providers)
- NazaHealth and Bliss Care in Naifaru (Lhaviyani Atoll) – serving northern atolls from a single location

The concentration of therapeutic services in Greater Male' is absolute. A child in need of autism assessment, trauma therapy, or developmental intervention who lives outside the capital has no local access to any of these services. Telehealth provision has begun to address some of this gap – particularly for counselling and psychotherapy, which can be conducted via video – but specialist assessment (autism, developmental disability, trauma specialisation) remains effectively inaccessible for children on outer islands.

For child protection purposes, the unavailability of trauma-specific therapeutic services outside Male' means that a child who has experienced abuse in an outer island and receives CFPS intervention cannot access the therapeutic support that effective trauma recovery requires. The CFPS case plan may identify therapeutic needs; it has no mechanism to meet them in the child's community.[^1022]

### 4.120.3 Child Protection CSOs – Contact Details and Roles

The civil society organisations directly relevant to child protection:

| **Organisation** | **Focus** | **Contact** |
|---|---|---|
| Advocating Rights of Children (ARC) | Child protection advocacy, policy, training | 7799544, arc.org.mv |
| Family Legal Clinic (FLC) | Free family law legal aid, DV protection orders | 7798898, Hotline: 9977771 |
| Hope for Women (HFW) | GBV shelter, women's rights | 7456570, hopeforwomen.org.mv |
| Maldivian Red Crescent (MRC) | Humanitarian, MHPSS, 10 branches/20 units | redcrescent.org.mv |
| Care Society | Disability, community-based rehabilitation (5 atoll locations) | 3316072, caresociety.org.mv |
| Transparency Maldives | Research, governance, accountability | 3304017, transparency.mv |

The geographic reach of these organisations underscores the civil society gap. ARC, FLC, and Hope for Women are all Male'-based. The Maldivian Red Crescent has the broadest reach with 10 branches and 20 units, but its primary mandate is humanitarian rather than child protection. Care Society has 5 atoll locations, giving it the widest atoll-level presence among the child-adjacent CSOs.

For children and families on outer islands, none of these organisations is accessible in person. FLC can provide legal advice by phone (hotline 9977771, 9am–4pm working days). The Red Crescent's atoll branches can provide emergency humanitarian support. Beyond these, the civil society infrastructure does not reach the communities where professional social work presence is also thinnest – the precise locations where civil society could most usefully supplement the state system.[^1023]

### 4.120.4 Digital Services and the OneGov Platform

The digital services platform is becoming an increasingly important component of social protection access, with implications for child welfare:

- **OneGov** (one.gov.mv): Applications for disability allowance, single parent allowance, and foster parent allowance are all accessible through the OneGov platform. Zakat House services were integrated in late 2024. For families on outer islands with reliable internet access, OneGov removes the need to travel to Male' for allowance applications.
- **National Disability Registry** (ndr.nspa.gov.mv): Required for disability allowance and related benefits. The UNPRPD (2024) found the portal was "not in an accessible format" for persons with disabilities, requiring family assistance for navigation.
- **eFaas** (efaas.gov.mv): National digital identity system authenticating access to government services.

For child protection specifically, the digital infrastructure has not yet reached the case management domain. The MCPD (Maldives Child Protection Database) is an internal case management tool not accessible to the public. There is no digital case tracking mechanism through which a family with an open case can monitor their case status, access their case plan, or communicate with their worker. The asymmetry between the social protection digital infrastructure (increasingly accessible through OneGov) and the child protection infrastructure (paper-based, worker-mediated) reflects the priority given to benefit administration over case management modernisation.[^1024]

---


# Part 18: Community-Based Protection: IBAMA


## 4.84 IBAMA Programme: Community-Based Child Protection in Practice


### 4.41.1 Origins and Development

The IBAMA (Island-Based Awareness for Monitoring Abuse) programme represents the Maldives' most significant community-based child protection initiative and one of the most distinctive features of the national child protection architecture. It was designed as an adaptation of a child-protection community volunteer model – informed by international models but contextualised to the Maldivian island setting – and offers a partial solution to the otherwise intractable problem of providing child protection reach to 189 inhabited islands with 20 FCSCs and 61 social workers.[^695]

The programme has its origins in a 1999 initiative in Addu Atoll, where community members were trained to identify and report child abuse and to provide first-line support to families. The model demonstrated promising results in a context where the FCSC system was under-resourced and geographic barriers prevented professional social workers from providing timely responses. In 2005, following evaluation of the Addu pilot, IBAMA was expanded to additional atolls. In 2019, it underwent a rebranding and conceptual strengthening, aligning its framework with the CRPA's community protection obligations and the NAPVAC's socio-ecological model.[^696]

### 4.41.2 Operational Structure

IBAMA operates through a network of **trained community volunteers** – one or more per inhabited island – who are:

- **Recruited** from existing community structures, typically with links to Women's Development Committees, island councils, or community health networks
- **Trained** in child abuse recognition, basic interview techniques for first disclosures, mandatory reporting obligations under the CRPA, and referral pathways to FCSCs
- **Connected** to the FCSC network through a nominated contact person who coordinates follow-up and provides supervision
- **Monitored** through periodic contact visits and a reporting system to MoSFD[^697]

The training programme covers four categories:
1. Recognition of signs of physical abuse, sexual abuse, emotional abuse, and neglect
2. Understanding of the mandatory reporting framework and referral procedures
3. Basic supportive communication with children who disclose
4. Community awareness and prevention – promoting child rights and protective environments

Training is delivered through a **12-hour intensive curriculum** (typically two days), with follow-up refresher sessions. In 2023, IBAMA received dedicated budget allocation from MoSFD – a recognition of its institutionalisation as part of the formal child protection system.[^698]

The programme was inspired in part by models from India, particularly community-based child protection approaches piloted in Tamil Nadu and Maharashtra, adapted to the Maldivian island context by UNICEF and MoSFD technical teams. The India adaptation process involved careful consideration of cultural differences – particularly around the role of community volunteers relative to state authority, the position of women as reporters in conservative island communities, and the appropriate boundaries between community monitoring and professional assessment.[^699]

### 4.41.3 Strengths and Documented Contributions

IBAMA's core strength is that it exists. In a child protection system characterised by profound workforce constraints – 61 social workers for 128,208 children across 189 islands – community volunteers provide the only realistic mechanism for timely first response to child protection concerns on most inhabited islands. The programme has contributed:

- **Increased reporting** from outer atolls, particularly reports of neglect and physical abuse that would previously have gone unreported or been informally managed
- **First-line support** for child victims and families pending FCSC involvement
- **Community awareness** of child rights and reporting obligations
- **Community normalisation** of reporting – reducing the stigma of involving authorities in family matters
- **Identification of chronic cases** that might not trigger a single acute event but represent ongoing low-level maltreatment[^700]

The Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) case study of IBAMA described it as "a genuinely innovative adaptation to the geographic constraints of island child protection" and noted that in islands where IBAMA volunteers were active and well-connected to the FCSC system, referral rates and quality were significantly better than in islands where IBAMA had lapsed or was under-resourced.[^701]

### 4.41.4 Limitations and Challenges

IBAMA's limitations are significant and must be honestly acknowledged:

**Volunteer attrition and coverage gaps.** IBAMA volunteers are unpaid community members who manage child protection responsibilities alongside their primary livelihoods and family obligations. Attrition is high – some volunteers serve for years; others leave after months. Islands without active volunteers receive no IBAMA function, and there is no systematic tracking of coverage gaps across all 189 inhabited islands.[^702]

**Role ambiguity and mandate creep.** The appropriate scope of IBAMA volunteer activity has not been clearly codified. Volunteers sometimes attempt to conduct investigations or mediate situations that require professional assessment. The boundary between community-level awareness and support (IBAMA's mandate) and professional investigation and assessment (CFPS mandate) is not always clear to volunteers, to families, or to the communities they serve.[^703]

**Political and community dynamics.** On small islands where everyone knows everyone, the IBAMA volunteer occupies a socially complex position. Reporting suspected abuse of a family member, neighbour, or community leader carries social costs – social ostracism, family conflict, retaliation – that inhibit reporting even when the volunteer has identified clear indicators of abuse. The Rogers, Ali and Naeem case study documented cases where IBAMA volunteers faced threats and community pressure to withdraw reports. The CFPS death threat cases described in §4.21 affect community volunteers as well as professional staff.[^704]

**Training depth limitations.** Twelve hours of training provides a foundation but is insufficient for complex case situations – child sexual abuse involving close family members, cases involving traditional practices such as FGM/C, cases where disability creates communication barriers, cases involving perpetrators with authority or influence. Volunteers identify cases they recognise from training but may miss more subtle presentations.[^705]

**Supervision and support.** Volunteers who have made a referral report needing feedback about outcomes – both to validate their judgement and to improve their practice. Current systems provide limited systematic feedback to volunteers on the outcomes of cases they have referred. The psychological burden of witnessing child abuse without adequate professional support is significant and contributes to attrition.[^706]

**Gender dynamics.** Women are disproportionately represented among IBAMA volunteers, consistent with the gendered nature of community caregiving roles. Male volunteers are more effective in some contexts – particularly in engaging with male community members and in cases involving boys – but the recruitment of male volunteers to community child protection roles faces cultural resistance in conservative island communities.[^707]

### 4.41.5 Integration with the Broader CP System

IBAMA's effectiveness depends critically on its integration with the professional FCSC system. Where FCSC workers maintain regular contact with IBAMA volunteers – providing training, follow-up on referred cases, and consultation – the community-professional linkage functions as intended. Where FCSC workers are overloaded (caseloads of 70-80 active cases) and cannot maintain regular atoll outreach, IBAMA volunteers are effectively isolated and unsupported.[^708]

The NAPVAC 2024-2028 identifies strengthening the IBAMA system as a specific output under the Community strategic area, with recommended actions including:
- Full mapping of IBAMA coverage across all 189 inhabited islands
- Systematic recruitment to fill coverage gaps, with particular attention to islands with documented high vulnerability
- Enhanced training curriculum (extending to 24 hours, with supervised practice components)
- Improved feedback and supervision pathways connecting IBAMA volunteers to FCSCs
- Recognition mechanism for long-serving volunteers
- Budget allocation for volunteer transport and communication costs[^709]

---


## 4.85 IBAMA Community-Based Child Protection: Operational Model, Strengths, and Failures


### 4.101.1 Origins and Conceptual Framework

The IBAMA community group model – the acronym deriving from the Dhivehi phrase meaning "you and I together" – represents the Maldives' most ambitious attempt to embed child protection function within the community itself. Established from approximately 2010 under the Divehi Raajjeyge Gulhun (DRG) programme framework with UNICEF support, IBAMA groups are structured multi-sectoral community committees designed to operate at the island level as the first tier of the formal child protection response system.

The conceptual premise is straightforward and appropriate to the Maldivian context: in a country of 189 inhabited islands spread across 800 kilometres of ocean, no central government system can respond rapidly to every child protection concern.
