The Action Plans Analysis documents this explicitly: the Ministry operates in only 20 of 189 inhabited islands, meaning 169 islands receive at most irregular outreach from FCSCs rather than permanent professional presence. Social workers operating in those 169 islands – alone, without nearby professional support, in communities where they are known to everyone and where their safety depends on maintaining community relationships – face structural barriers to effective work that training and awareness campaigns cannot address.

The political interference dynamic takes multiple forms. Island councils can obstruct child protection investigations involving relatives or allies. Community leaders can pressure IBAMA members not to report concerns about connected families. Police officers on small islands may decline to pursue cases against individuals they know socially. Prosecutors may be reluctant to proceed against cases where community support for the accused is strong and where conviction would permanently damage their relationship with the local power structure. Recognising this dynamic explicitly – and designing accountability and support mechanisms for workers in vulnerable positions – is a system design requirement that neither the NAPVAC nor any other planning document has yet addressed with adequate specificity.[^998]

### 4.113.4 Online – An Emerging Threat Without a Legislative Foundation

Strategic Area 4's online child protection priorities address an area where the evidence base is acknowledged to be thin and the legislative framework is explicitly absent. The Action Plans Analysis records that online sexual exploitation is an "emerging concern" given high internet and smartphone penetration across the country. The NAPVAC itself acknowledges that cyber-crime regulations need review and implementation.

What the evidence adds to this picture is the specific dynamic of technology-facilitated abuse in the context of gender-based violence – a dynamic that affects children directly when they are witnesses to, or victims of, domestic violence that extends into digital spaces. Abusive partners use location-tracking applications to monitor survivors. Abusive ex-partners use social media to harass, stalk, and intimidate women who have left relationships. Children in these households are affected both directly (as witnesses to the abuse) and indirectly (as the tool through which contact is maintained by an abusive parent who would otherwise be excluded).

The specific child exploitation concerns – online grooming, child sexual abuse material production and distribution, commercial sexual exploitation facilitated through social media platforms – require legislative criminalisation that goes beyond the existing Computer Crime Act's general provisions. The NAPVAC's ambition to achieve legislative and institutional frameworks for online child protection by 2027 is achievable only if legislative drafting begins immediately and is prioritised within the Attorney General's legislative programme.[^999]

### 4.113.5 CP System – Workforce Specificity Gap

Strategic Area 5's system-strengthening outcome acknowledges the need to strengthen the integrated child protection system. What the evidence cross-reference reveals is that the plan's formulation of this need lacks the specificity that effective reform requires. The evidence shows:

- 61 child protection social workers serve 128,208 children – a ratio of 1:2,100
- The international standard caseload is 25–30 cases per worker
- The actual caseload is approximately 160 cases per worker (5.3–6.4× the recommended maximum)
- The Ministry's jurisdiction covers only 20 of 189 inhabited islands
- 3 DV/GBV caseworkers in Male' carry 70–80 cases each

The NAPVAC's strategic area commits to "strengthening the integrated child protection system" without specifying how many additional social workers are needed, what the training pipeline is, how the salary and career pathway problems that drive experienced workers out of the system will be addressed, or how service coverage will be extended to the 169 islands currently without any Ministry presence. Without these specifications, the strategic area is a direction without a route – pointing toward where the system needs to go without describing how to get there.

The evidence cross-reference concludes its analysis of this strategic area with a finding that is more damning than the workforce statistics alone: "No costed workforce expansion plan; no model for service delivery to the 169 islands without Ministry presence." These are not minor evidence gaps – they are the absence of the planning foundation on which implementation must be built.[^1000]

---


# Part 21: International Frameworks, CRC, and Comparisons


## 4.97 CRC Reporting Cycle and Child Protection Accountability


### 4.66.1 The CRC Reporting History

Maldives ratified the Convention on the Rights of the Child on 11 February 1991. The first reporting cycle to the UN Committee on the Rights of the Child occurred in 1993 (Maldives Government, 1996). Since that initial report, Maldives has submitted periodic reports to the Committee, with each cycle generating Concluding Observations that have shaped domestic child protection reform.

Key moments in the CRC cycle:
- **First report (1993):** A growing gender inequality agenda was identified. The URC was moved to the Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare.
- **Second cycle (1996 – OHCHR, 2007):** Efforts to enhance child protection laws developed. Drafting of the CRPA began with support from Defence for Children International and UNICEF.
- **Fourth periodic report and Concluding Observations (CRC/C/MDV/CO/4, 2016):** The Committee's principal concerns included minimum age of marriage, corporal punishment, juvenile justice reform, alternative care, and the implementation gap between legislation and practice in outer island communities. The 2019–2024 legislative reforms directly responded to many of these concerns.
- **Combined fifth and sixth periodic report (2015–2024, draft 2026):** The report acknowledges continued implementation gaps while documenting the major legislative changes of the 2019–2021 period.

The CRC reporting cycle has served as a critical external accountability mechanism for the Maldivian child protection system, creating periodic pressure for reform and providing international benchmarks against which domestic progress can be measured. The impact of this accountability is visible in the legislative output of the 2019 period, in which the CRPA and the Juvenile Justice Act were both passed – the most significant transformation in Maldives' child rights legislative history.[^873]

### 4.66.2 Concluding Observations: Outstanding Implementation Gaps

The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) acknowledges the following as key remaining challenges from the Committee's Concluding Observations:

**Child marriage:** Despite the Family Law Regulation (2016) establishing 18 as the minimum age of marriage, a judicial exception allows marriage below 18 with court approval. This exception persists under both the Family Law Regulation and the CRPA framework. Data from civil registration indicates that some marriages of persons under 18 continue to be registered annually, particularly in outer island communities. The Government acknowledges that closing this loophole requires legislative action.

**Minimum age of criminal responsibility:** The Juvenile Justice Act sets the MACR at 10 years – below the threshold of at least 14 years recommended by the Committee in General Comment No. 24 (2019). The Government is reviewing the MACR in the context of broader juvenile justice reform.

**Corporal punishment:** Despite legal prohibition in CRPA Article 38, corporal punishment in school settings continues to be reported through FCSC case data and HRCM monitoring. The transition from historically accepted practice to prohibited conduct requires sustained social change work.

**Alternative care:** The foster care system remains underdeveloped, with 91% of children in institutional care. The Government acknowledges the need to shift toward family-based care models.

**Outer island service gaps:** Children and families in outer islands continue to face significantly worse access to child protection services than those in Malé, constituting a de facto equity violation.[^874]

### 4.66.3 Budget Allocations for Child Protection

The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) documents budget trends that contextualize the implementation gaps:

- MoSFD's overall budget increased from MVR 180 million in 2015 to MVR 420 million in 2024, a 133% nominal increase
- The proportion allocated specifically to child protection services (as distinct from broader social protection) is difficult to disaggregate precisely
- The FCSC operational budget is MVR 38 million (2024)
- The DJJ (juvenile rehabilitation) budget is MVR 22 million (2024)
- Aasandha universal health insurance (covering all children): MVR 1.2 billion annually

The Government acknowledges that increases have not kept pace with the expanding mandate of the child protection system following the 2019 legislative reforms. A child-sensitive budgeting framework is being developed to allow more precise tracking of child-specific expenditure – currently not possible due to integrated budget lines across social protection functions.[^875]

---


## 4.98 The CRC Reporting Cycle as Accountability Framework


### 4.108.1 Maldives' CRC Compliance Architecture

The Maldives ratified the Convention on the Rights of the Child in 1991 – the same year that the CRC came into force and before most countries had ratified. This early ratification reflects the government's historic commitment to the CRC framework, but also creates a long accountability history that reveals the arc of progress and persistent gaps.

The CRC monitoring cycle requires state parties to submit periodic reports to the Committee on the Rights of the Child. The Maldives' most recent State Party Report (2021) represents the combined 5th and 6th periodic report – the combination reflecting the submission delays that have characterised the Maldives' CRC reporting. The Committee's Concluding Observations – recommendations issued after reviewing the State Party Report and input from civil society and other stakeholders – provide an independent, internationally authoritative assessment of the system's strengths and gaps.

The CRC report drafting process documented in Part XVIII-B of this encyclopedia provides the most recent government self-assessment. Across the nine CRC reporting clusters (General Measures of Implementation; Definition of the Child; General Principles; Civil Rights and Freedoms; Violence Against Children; Family Environment and Alternative Care; Disability, Basic Health and Welfare; Education, Leisure and Cultural Activities; Special Protection Measures), the pattern is consistent: significant legislative and institutional progress since the previous report, persistent gaps in implementation capacity, geographic inequalities in service access, and data limitations that constrain the government's own ability to assess its progress.[^980]

### 4.108.2 Concluding Observations Pattern: What CRC Repeatedly Flags

Successive CRC Concluding Observations on the Maldives have repeatedly identified the same priority concerns, which taken together constitute the long-standing structural gaps that have not been resolved across multiple reporting cycles:

**Corporal punishment:** The Committee has consistently called for an explicit legislative prohibition on all forms of corporal punishment in all settings – home, school, and institutional care – noting that while the CRPA 2019 introduced protections, the absence of a comprehensive prohibition leaves children vulnerable. The documented use of corporal punishment in schools (caning, physical punishment by teachers) and the application of judicial corporal punishment to juvenile offenders have been recurring concerns.

**Minimum age of criminal responsibility:** The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 sets the minimum age of criminal responsibility at 15 – below the Committee's recommended minimum of 14, and significantly below the 18 years that the Committee considers the appropriate upper boundary for the juvenile justice framework. Children aged 15–17 who offend are subject to the full criminal justice process, an approach that the Committee has repeatedly flagged as inconsistent with CRC standards.

**Child marriage:** The Committee has called for a clear legislative minimum marriage age of 18 without exception, noting that the Supreme Court regulation permitting marriage of minors under specific conditions creates a legal loophole inconsistent with the CRC. The intersection of this loophole with Islamic family law frameworks means that the political path to legislative change is not straightforward.

**Alternative care deinstitutionalisation:** The Committee has consistently urged the Maldives to reduce its dependence on residential care and develop the family-based alternative care options (foster care, kinship care support) that the international evidence base strongly supports. The 91% institutional care rate documented in 2023 represents no progress on this recommendation since it was first made.[^981]

### 4.108.3 The Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations

A significant institutional development of the post-2019 period was the establishment of the Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations in February 2020. The Commission – established by presidential mandate in the early months of the Ibrahim Mohamed Solih administration – represented an acknowledgement at the highest political level that past failures in child protection required not just administrative remediation but a formal accountability process.

The Commission's mandate was to investigate past violations of children's rights – including in institutional care settings, the juvenile justice system, and the child protection response – and to make recommendations for preventing future failures. Its work contributed to the reform energy of the 2019–2024 period that also produced the CRPA 2019, the Juvenile Justice Act 2019, and the professionalisation of the social work workforce.

The Commission's existence and mandate – a formal presidential accountability mechanism for child rights violations – is itself significant in the regional context. It reflects a political recognition that children's rights violations in state-operated or state-regulated systems are not simply operational failures but accountability failures that require formal investigation and remedy at the highest level of government.[^982]

---


## 4.99 International Obligation Mapping: Child Protection Under CRC and Optional Protocols


### 4.116.1 CRC Articles Directly Governing Child Protection

The Convention on the Rights of the Child provides the international legal framework within which Maldivian child protection law must be situated. The following CRC articles carry direct operational relevance:

**Article 19 – Protection from Violence:** States parties must take all appropriate legislative, administrative, social, and educational measures to protect children from all forms of physical or mental violence, injury or abuse, neglect or negligent treatment, maltreatment or exploitation including sexual abuse. This article provides the primary legal basis for the entire child protection system – the CRPA 2019, the CFPS, the FCSC network, the IBAMA model, and the 1412 helpline are all instruments through which Article 19 obligations are met. The gap between the obligation's comprehensive scope and the system's limited reach (20 of 189 islands) means that Article 19 is imperfectly implemented for the majority of Maldivian children.

**Article 20 – Alternative Care:** Children deprived of their family environment are entitled to special protection and assistance from the state. Forms of care include foster family placement, *kafalah* of Islamic law, adoption, or if necessary placement in suitable institutions. The explicit inclusion of *kafalah* in Article 20 means that the Maldivian practice of informal kinship care under Islamic principles is recognised as a legitimate alternative care form within the CRC framework – an important alignment between Islamic family law and international standards that the Maldives' child protection system has not fully operationalised in terms of formal recognition and support for kinship carers.

**Article 25 – Periodic Review:** Children placed in alternative care for the purposes of protection are entitled to periodic review of the placement. The 91% institutional care rate and the limited social work capacity to conduct regular reviews means that periodic review is formally required but practically limited in its depth and frequency.

**Article 34 – Sexual Exploitation:** States parties must protect children from sexual exploitation and sexual abuse. The interaction of this obligation with the SOA 2014's Section 52 evidentiary requirements – which make prosecution of sexual offences against children practically very difficult – creates a situation where the formal commitment is strong but the enforcement mechanism is deficient.

**Articles 37 and 39 – Treatment and Recovery:** Children deprived of liberty must be treated with humanity and respect, and children who have experienced violence, exploitation, or neglect are entitled to physical and psychological recovery and social reintegration. These articles provide the legal basis for the trauma-informed care mandate in the CRPA 2019 and for the therapeutic and reintegration services that the system must provide to survivors of abuse.[^1006]

### 4.116.2 CRC Optional Protocols

The Maldives has not ratified the Optional Protocol on the Sale of Children, Child Prostitution and Child Pornography (OPSC) or the Optional Protocol on a Communications Procedure (OPIC) – two of the three CRC Optional Protocols. The OPSC specifically requires states parties to criminalise the sale of children, child prostitution, and child pornography with appropriate criminal penalties. While Maldivian law addresses some of these through the SOA 2014 and the Anti-Trafficking Act, the specific comprehensive obligations of the OPSC have not been formally taken on. The NAPVAC 2024–2028 references the CRC Optional Protocols as relevant frameworks but the Maldives' non-ratification means these are voluntary aspirations rather than binding obligations.

Ratification of the OPSC would be particularly relevant given the growing concern about online child sexual exploitation and child sexual abuse material – the precise domain that the NAPVAC's Strategic Area 4 is attempting to address. The specific obligations the OPSC would impose (criminalisation, extraterritorial jurisdiction, extradition, international cooperation) would strengthen the legislative framework that the NAPVAC envisions and provide an international accountability mechanism for progress.[^1007]

### 4.116.3 The Action Plan → International Obligation Alignment

The Action Plans Analysis mapping of NAPVAC against international obligations reveals a specific gap: the NAPVAC directly addresses CRC Articles 19 (protection from violence), 34 (sexual exploitation), 37 (treatment), and 39 (rehabilitation). It does not explicitly address Article 20 (alternative care) – the section of the CRC most directly concerned with the 91% institutional care rate that successive CRC Concluding Observations have flagged. The NAPVAC is primarily a violence prevention and response plan; the deinstitutionalisation agenda it implicitly supports (through the system-strengthening strategic area) is not operationalised within the plan's framework.

The obligation-plan mapping also reveals that no current active plan explicitly addresses CRC Articles 21 (adoption), 22 (refugee children), or 33 (drug abuse) – dimensions of child protection that are present in the Maldivian context but are not covered by any of the active action plans' formal frameworks.[^1008]

---


## 4.100 International Comparative Framework: Maldives in Regional and Global Context


### 4.72.1 The UN Guidelines on Alternative Care

The UN Guidelines on the Alternative Care of Children (UNGA Resolution A/RES/64/142, adopted 2009) provide the international normative framework for alternative care systems. The Guidelines:
- Establish the primacy of family-based care over institutional care
- Require that institutional care be used only as a last resort and for the shortest possible time
- Set standards for the quality of care in all alternative care settings
- Require governments to develop policies for moving away from institutional care
- Establish specific standards for leaving care support for young people transitioning out of care

Rogers and Ali (2023) use these Guidelines as the benchmark against which Maldivian alternative care is assessed. The current Maldivian system fails against most of the Guidelines' requirements: 91% of children in institutional care; no leaving care policy; no systematic post-care support; no care-experienced people movement. The seeds of reform have been sown – the CRPA, the Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30, the shift to smaller institutions – but the system is far from the Guidelines' vision of a family-first alternative care model.

### 4.72.2 Learning from Country Comparisons

Rogers and Ali (2023) note that there is value in drawing on lessons from countries that have transitioned their systems from institutional to family-based care, while ensuring that reform plans are country-specific and reflect Maldivian culture and context. Countries that have achieved significant deinstitutionalisation – including various European countries following EU Structural Funds incentives for care reform – offer models of how the transition can be managed, including: the development of foster and adoptive carer recruitment campaigns; kinship carer assessment and support systems; family support services that prevent entry to care; and leaving care programmes.

However, the Maldivian context presents specific challenges not faced by those comparison countries:
- The geographic archipelago structure, which makes any national system inherently fragmented
- The Islamic legal framework, which precludes adoption and limits permanency options
- The absence of a significant NGO or voluntary sector in the child welfare space
- The very small population of children in care (187 nationally), which may not provide the scale needed for specialist services to be viable

These contextual factors require that international models be adapted rather than replicated – and that adaptation be led by people who understand the Maldivian context deeply, including those with lived experience of Maldivian care.[^885]

### 4.72.3 SAARC Regional Context

Within the South Asian Association for Regional Cooperation (SAARC) context, the Maldives' alternative care challenges are not unique but are particularly acute due to the archipelago geography. Neighbouring South Asian countries share: limited foster care development; over-reliance on institutional care; workforce shortages; and the interaction of Islamic or Hindu legal frameworks with international child rights standards. The SAARC Social Charter includes child welfare provisions, and SAARC cooperation mechanisms offer potential for regional learning and technical exchange – an opportunity that has been only partially exploited by the Maldivian child protection system to date.[^886]

---


## 4.101 International Comparative Framework: Maldives in Regional and Global Context


### 4.109.1 South Asian Comparisons

Placing the Maldives' child protection system in South Asian context reveals a system that is significantly ahead of regional peers in legislative framework and institutional architecture, while sharing many of the same implementation challenges.

The CRPA 2019 is more comprehensive than comparable legislation in several larger South Asian states. The mandatory reporting obligation, the specialist child protection court structure, the Children's Ombudsperson, and the explicit trauma-informed care mandate represent standards that exceed what most South Asian national legislation currently provides. The 1412 helpline is operational and accessible – a functioning child-specific reporting mechanism that several larger regional states lack.

At the same time, the implementation gaps that characterise the Maldivian system – workforce shortfalls, geographic access inequality, data system fragmentation, maintenance enforcement failure – are structural problems shared across the region. South Asian child protection systems universally struggle with the gap between ambitious legislation and limited implementation capacity, with urban-rural inequality in service access, and with the persistence of cultural norms (corporal punishment, child marriage, gender-based violence as private family matter) that undermine statutory frameworks.[^983]

### 4.109.2 Small Island State Comparisons

The small island state context provides a more precise comparative framework for the Maldives than regional comparisons with large continental states. The Pacific Island States (Samoa, Vanuatu, Fiji), the Caribbean (Barbados, Trinidad and Tobago, Jamaica), and other small island developing states face variations of the same structural challenges: geographic dispersal making service delivery costly and complex; small populations insufficient to support the institutional complexity that modern child protection requires; limited domestic civil society capacity; high dependence on international technical assistance; and the vulnerability of professional workforces where a small number of individuals carry disproportionate responsibility.

The Maldives' 61 social workers serving 128,208 children across 189 islands is comparable to, though at the more challenged end of, the range among small island developing states with active child protection systems. The dependence on UNICEF as a quasi-civil society actor is shared across Pacific Island systems, where UNICEF and regional bodies (SPC, PIFS) play similarly foundational roles in system development and technical support.

The most significant contextual difference is the Islamic law dimension – the interaction between the CRPA 2019's rights-based framework and Shari'ah provisions on family law, corporal punishment, and sexual offences that creates legal complexity not present in non-Muslim small island states. This dimension makes the Maldivian case distinctive within the small island state comparison group and requires analysis in its own terms rather than simple mapping from models developed in other contexts.[^984]

### 4.109.3 English Child Protection Framework – The Model and the Mismatch

As documented in §4.102.4, the Maldivian child protection system has substantially absorbed the English child protection model – a needs-based, process-intensive, multi-agency framework developed over decades in a country of 67 million people with an extensive social work profession and significant institutional infrastructure. The mismatch between model and operating context is a structural feature of the system that deserves explicit analytical attention.

The English framework's key features – child protection conferencing, child protection registers, multi-agency public protection arrangements, looked-after children review processes, independent reviewing officers, corporate parenting obligations – assume a density of professional capacity and institutional infrastructure that simply does not exist in the Maldives. The children's social care workforce in England numbers approximately 35,000 qualified social workers (a ratio of approximately 1 per 1,900 children); the Maldives' ratio is 1 per 2,100 children but is severely complicated by geographic dispersal that makes English workforce ratios essentially irrelevant as a comparative benchmark.

The appropriate model for the Maldives is not the English framework – a point acknowledged by Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) and by the NAPVAC 2024's own implicit recognition that community-based approaches must complement statutory systems that cannot reach the whole country. The relevant models are those developed in contexts of geographic dispersal, limited professional workforce, and strong community social structures – the Pacific Island experiences, the rural African community-based protection models, and the adaptations of statutory frameworks to small-island realities that have been developed in Caribbean child protection systems. A Maldives-contextualised framework would build on the CRPA 2019's legislative foundation while designing operational systems around the IBAMA community model, mobile outreach from regional hubs, digital case management connecting outer-island workers to specialist support, and a civil society layer that provides the preventive family support the statutory system cannot deliver.[^985]

---


## 4.102 The National Reintegration Centre and Children Returning from Conflict Zones


### 4.62.1 The Radicalisation Context

Maldives has the highest per-capita rate of fighters departing for foreign conflict zones (specifically Syria and Iraq) of any country in the world by some estimates. The phenomenon of Maldivians departing for conflict zones – particularly during the period of Islamic State activity 2013–2018 – created a specific category of child welfare need: children born in conflict zones to Maldivian mothers, or children accompanying parents who departed and then sought to return, carrying complex trauma from their experience in war-affected environments and potential exposure to radical ideological indoctrination.

### 4.62.2 The NRC Framework and Its Flaws

The National Reintegration Centre (NRC) was established under the Anti-Terrorism Act (Law No. 17/2019) to temporarily detain children and mothers returning from war zones where concerns about radicalisation exist. The NRC is housed as a facility that operates under anti-terrorism rather than child welfare legislation – a categorisation that creates fundamental problems for child-centred practice.

Key legal tensions identified by Rogers and Ali (2023) and the Child Rights Protection Act:
- The CRPA states that the NRC is **not** a state care facility. Therefore, CFPS case management protocols – including best interests assessments, care planning, regular review, and monitoring – do not automatically apply.
- Two reintegration committees exist with overlapping mandates: one under Chapter 9, Section 60–15 of Law No. 31/2021, and one under the Alternative Care regulation. The former has been dissolved, leaving the reintegration framework effectively non-operational.
- Children in the NRC have no formal reintegration plan. Their monitoring is carried out by a social worker at the atoll level – which is problematic if the social worker is not present on the same island as the child.
- Only three children placed with their mothers in the NRC had been successfully reintegrated back to their respective islands as of 2022 (Abdulla, 2022).

The NRC thus represents a case in which the child welfare framework and the anti-terrorism framework pull in opposite directions. Anti-terrorism logic treats the returning family as a security threat requiring detention and monitoring. Child welfare logic treats the child as a victim of circumstances requiring therapeutic support, stable care, and reconnection with family and community. The lack of a child-centred reintegration plan – required by the Reintegration Regulation 2020/R-30 – means children in the NRC experience neither security nor welfare outcomes.[^866]

---


## 4.103 The Child Protection System and Violent Extremism


### 4.97.1 The Radicalisation-Child Protection Interface

The Maldives has the highest per-capita rate of foreign terrorist fighters of any country in the world by some estimates. The phenomenon of Maldivians departing to conflict zones, particularly during the Islamic State period (2013–2018), created specific child welfare and child protection challenges:

**Children of departed parents:** Children left behind when one or both parents travel to conflict zones face immediate family disruption. The remaining parent (often a mother) may face social isolation, financial hardship, and the specific challenges of explaining a parent's absence to children.

**Children born in conflict zones:** Maldivian women who traveled to conflict zones with partners may have had children in Syria or Iraq. Children born in conflict zones may have witnessed violence, experienced trauma, and may have no birth registration. Their return to Maldives involves both security screening (NRC process) and child welfare assessment.

**Adolescents at risk of radicalisation:** The ICSVE (International Centre for the Study of Violent Extremism) 2022 research on prison radicalisation documented how young men and adolescents in Maldivian prisons were recruited into extremist networks. The intersection of juvenile justice and violent extremism creates a child protection challenge: adolescents in juvenile facilities are exposed to radicalisation risks, and the DJJ's response capacity for counter-extremism programming is limited.

### 4.97.2 The "Prevent" Function and Children

The Ministry of Home Affairs' Preventing Violent Extremism National Action Plan (2022) addresses radicalisation risk in educational and community settings. The P/CVE (Preventing/Countering Violent Extremism) dimension of child protection involves:
- School-based awareness programmes identifying at-risk youth
- Community-level IBAMA mechanisms with a P/CVE function
- DJJ programming for detained adolescents with extremism-related risk
- CFPS assessment protocols for children returning from conflict zones

The intersection of P/CVE work and child protection highlights the complexity of the Maldivian social welfare context: a child protection system operating in a country where violent extremism, drug use, gang violence, and family breakdown all intersect with child vulnerability creates demands that no single-domain response can adequately address.[^936]

---


# Part 22: Cross-Cutting Issues: Gender, Demographics, and Religion


## 4.104 Gender and Child Protection: The Missing Analysis


### 4.89.1 The Gender Dimension of Child Protection

Child protection in the Maldives has a significant gender dimension that is underanalysed in most child protection discussions:

**Sexual abuse:** The disproportionate burden of sexual abuse falls on girls. The Special Measures Act data from 2005–2009 shows 43 rape cases at IGMH, of which 22 involved minors and "almost half of cases involving minors were gang-rapes." Survey data shows childhood sexual abuse at 16.3% in Malé with rates approximately double for girls compared to boys.

**Custody as child protection:** The Family Act's Section 42 provisions – discussed in depth in the Gender chapter (Part V, §5.115.3) – use custody as a mechanism of post-divorce control that functions as a child protection issue as well as a women's rights issue. When custody is weaponised by an abusive ex-partner to control a divorced mother, the children are directly affected: they may be caught between parents in a conflict that is fundamentally about adult power rather than child welfare, may be used to gather information about the mother, or may be placed with fathers who then delegate actual care to relatives while denying the mother contact.

**Boys in alternative care:** The 2023 data shows 105 boys and 82 girls in alternative care – boys constitute 56% of those in care. This disproportionate representation of boys may reflect referral patterns, the historic role of Islaahiya as a boys' reformatory institution, and the greater visibility of boys' behavioural presentations relative to girls' internalised responses to abuse.

**Girls' vulnerability in boarding settings:** As noted above, girls who leave home islands to board for secondary education face specific exploitation and sexual abuse risks in atoll capital settings.

### 4.89.2 The FPA-CFPS Operational Gap

The Family Protection Authority (FPA) and the Child and Family Protection Service (CFPS) share a critical operational zone: families where domestic violence is occurring are also families where children are at risk. A woman experiencing domestic violence is likely to have children who witness that violence – and witnessing domestic violence is itself a form of harm under the CRPA 2019.

Despite this overlap, the FPA and CFPS operate largely in parallel rather than in integrated mode. The Gender chapter (Part V, §5.56) documents the FPA-child protection nexus in detail. The key practical gap is the absence of a joint FPA-CFPS protocol for cases where domestic violence and child protection concerns co-exist – a situation that should be the rule rather than the exception in the most serious DV cases.[^923]

---


## 4.105 Demographic Trends and Child Protection: The Implications of Demographic Transition


### 4.107.1 Population Dynamics and the Child Cohort

The Maldives' demographic transition has profound implications for child protection system planning. The total fertility rate has fallen from 6.40 in 1990 to 1.58 in 2022 – a decline of extraordinary speed that has fundamentally reshaped the age structure of the population. The absolute number of children aged 0–4 actually fell between 2014 and 2022 (from 31,834 to 30,621), while the number aged 5–9 increased significantly (from 28,903 to 33,115) and older age groups expanded substantially. The dependency ratio fell marginally, from 48 to 46 dependents per 100 working-age persons.

These demographic shifts have ambiguous implications for child protection. On the one hand, a falling birth rate means fewer children entering the system in future years – potentially reducing absolute workload if the system capacity is held constant. On the other hand, population concentration in Greater Male' (which grew from approximately 35% to 41% of the Maldivian population between 2014 and 2022, with Hulhumale' alone growing from 14,000 to 54,879) means increasing child protection demand in an area where housing overcrowding, family disruption, and urban poverty create distinct risk profiles.

The rural-to-urban migration pattern – families moving from outer atolls to Male', Hulhumale', and Addu City in search of employment, education, and services – generates specific child protection risks. Children who move with migrating families lose the *gah'therikan* informal safety net of their island of origin, enter an urban environment where anonymity reduces community-level monitoring, and often find themselves in overcrowded housing where domestic privacy is limited but professional support is also more accessible than in remote islands.[^977]

### 4.107.2 The Marriage and Divorce Ecosystem and Child Risk

The marriage and divorce statistics provide essential context for understanding the child protection system's demand. The Maldives' high divorce rate – approximately 62% of marriages ending in divorce in 2016, with a divorce-to-marriage ratio consistently above 50% throughout the 2010s – means that a substantial proportion of Maldivian children experience family dissolution. The 22% of children living with one parent in the 2022 Census reflects this reality.

The impact on children's welfare is not simply the disruption of the divorce itself but the financial, social, and protective consequences that follow. The Family Act's maintenance enforcement failures – approximately MVR 2 million in accumulated unpaid maintenance at the Family Court – mean that many children in single-parent households are being raised in poverty not because poverty is inevitable but because the maintenance system has failed. The Section 42 custody provisions – which penalise mothers for remarrying – mean that some children are growing up in technically parent-headed households where the de facto daily carer is a grandparent, aunt, or neighbour, with the social worker and the system entirely unaware of the practical care arrangements.[^978]

### 4.107.3 Migration, Foreign Workers, and Children's Intersecting Vulnerabilities

The presence of 132,493 foreign residents (Census 2022) – predominantly Bangladeshi and Indian male workers in construction, hospitality, and domestic service – creates child protection vulnerabilities that the Maldivian system has not systematically addressed. Foreign domestic workers working in Maldivian households have direct, unsupervised access to children – creating a risk vector for abuse that the standard child protection monitoring mechanisms (teacher observation, health worker contact, IBAMA community monitoring) are unlikely to detect.

Children of foreign workers who are living in the Maldives represent a further gap. The CRPA 2019's child protection framework applies to all children in the Maldives, regardless of nationality. However, foreign worker children are less likely to be enrolled in Maldivian schools (where child protection detection most commonly occurs), less likely to have community connections that activate *gah'therikan* protective responses, and more likely to be living in overcrowded worker accommodation without the domestic privacy that enables abuse to be identified.

The statistical invisibility of these children – they do not appear in the Census data on Maldivian children, they are unlikely to be in CFPS case management systems, and their parents may be deterred from reporting by fears about visa and employment consequences – means that the child protection system does not have a reliable picture of this population's needs or vulnerabilities.[^979]

---


## 4.106 Census Data and Child Welfare: The Equity Lens


### 4.81.1 Children in Maldives – Leave No One Behind (Census 2022)

The Leave No One Behind analysis (Maldives Bureau of Statistics / UNICEF, 2022) using Census 2022 microdata provides the most current equity-focused picture of child welfare in the Maldives:

**Core finding:** 128,208 children live in the Maldives. But their circumstances are highly unequal.

**Poverty and education of household head:**
- Households with more than one child per adult: **more than twice as likely to be poor** (12% vs. 4.9%)
- Households headed by individuals without primary education: poverty rate **12.6%**, deprivation rate **39.9% in Malé**
- Households with a head with tertiary education: poverty rates approximately **30 times lower** than when the head had not completed primary school

**Geographic disparities:**
- Children in atoll communities face particular risks from limited secondary education facilities, health service gaps, and water and sanitation deficits
- Poverty rate national: ~5.4%; atolls: ~9.5%; Malé: ~0.9%
- 92% of the poor live in atolls

**Family structure:**
- The 2014 Census analysis (National Bureau of Statistics / UNICEF, 2015) found that **74% of children aged 0–17 lived with both parents**, while **22% lived with one parent**
- The one-parent household rate of 22% intersects with documented inadequacy of maintenance enforcement and post-divorce economic vulnerability, representing a structural child poverty and child protection risk

**Deprivation concentration:** The education-poverty gradient – where having a household head without primary education is associated with a 39.9% deprivation rate in Malé – illustrates how child welfare outcomes are determined not primarily by the child's own characteristics but by the household's structural position. Children in low-education, low-income households face compounding disadvantages in health, education, and safety that are invisible in aggregate national statistics.[^912]

### 4.81.2 Child Poverty as a Child Protection Risk Factor

The 18% child poverty estimate (HIES 2019–20) – compared to the 5.4% national poverty figure – suggests that poverty is disproportionately concentrated among children, reflecting:
- Higher fertility in lower-income households
- Inadequate maintenance enforcement after divorce (most single-parent households are headed by mothers)
- Limited social protection for children, despite improvements in the Madhana allowance

Child poverty is a direct child protection risk factor. Research across multiple contexts shows that:
- Financial stress increases parental conflict and domestic violence
- Poverty-driven overcrowding increases sexual abuse risks
- Economic inability to access basic needs can trigger neglect even in families without intentional maltreatment
- The correlation between poverty and child protection involvement reflects both genuine risk and possible bias in case identification (lower-income families may be more visible to services)

The CFPS and FCSCs operate without systematic anti-poverty case management tools. The relationship between poverty-driven vulnerability and child protection intervention is managed primarily through referral to the National Social Protection Agency – a referral pathway that is inconsistently used and that does not address the deep structural poverty facing many families with child protection involvement.[^913]

---


## 4.107 Prevention and Family Support: The Underdeveloped Infrastructure


### 4.94.1 The Prevention Investment Gap

Rogers and Ali (2023) and Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) both identify the Maldivian child protection system's orientation toward severe cases at the expense of prevention and family support as a fundamental structural weakness. The UK-derived case management model that was imported into the Maldivian system from 2006 prioritises identification and response to cases of significant harm – leaving preventive services in the background.

This model creates several problems in the Maldivian context:

**Late identification:** Families experiencing stress, poverty, drug use, or deteriorating parenting capacity are not identified or supported until abuse or neglect is already occurring and reportable. Earlier intervention – through parenting support, substance abuse treatment, income assistance, or domestic violence services – could prevent harm from occurring.

**Over-representation of severe cases:** The system becomes concentrated on the most severe cases, leaving moderate-risk families without support. Workers with caseloads of 160 have no capacity for anything other than crisis management.

**Family breakdown before intervention:** By the time a family enters the formal child protection system, family breakdown may be well advanced. Intensive family preservation services – which aim to support families at the point of crisis to prevent unnecessary removal of children – do not exist in the Maldivian system.

**No specialist family support NGO:** Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) recommend the introduction of a family support mechanism, whether integrated within CFPS or through a partnership with an NGO such as ARC. Effective family support could be pivotal during a family crisis, potentially preventing abuse and family breakdown, and aiding reintegration social workers while a child is in foster or institutional care.

### 4.94.2 The Parenting Programme Landscape

Government parenting support programmes are provided through FCSCs, including family counselling, parenting skills training, and home visiting programmes. The SAP (Caring State and Dignified Families, 2019) mandates country-wide parenting skills awareness.

The Ministry of Education's positive discipline training programme reached approximately 40% of teachers nationally by 2023 – a significant but not yet complete roll-out. The programme translates the legal prohibition on corporal punishment into practical skills for managing children's behaviour without physical force.

The IBAMA community mechanism provides a community-level family support function in communities where it is effectively implemented. IBAMA's 12-category community structure – including health workers, teachers, social workers, religious leaders, and community members – creates a multisectoral community protective layer.

However, the scale and consistency of prevention investment remains limited relative to the reactive, investigative side of the system. The NSAP-CP 2020–2024 and NAPVAC 2024–2028 both include Prevention and Behaviour Change as strategic priorities – but the resourcing of prevention activities compared to response activities remains skewed toward the latter.[^932]

### 4.94.3 Family Reunification as Prevention of Unnecessary Care

Family reunification – returning children from alternative care to their birth families – serves a prevention function as well as a welfare function: children who return successfully to families do not require ongoing costly institutional care, and families that are successfully supported to resume care avoid the long-term damage that institutional care can cause to family relationships and children's development.

The Regulation on Reintegration (2020/R-30) establishes family reunification as a priority. The reintegration committee process provides a formal mechanism for planning and monitoring reunification. The Government's SAP has also embarked on family reunification processes and has established a reintegration committee specifically to move children from institutions back to families.

Rogers and Ali (2023) note positive progress on reunification – "some reintegration to the family has taken place" – while noting that the process remains piecemeal in the absence of a coherent care reform roadmap. The transition from institutional to family-based care requires not just returning children to families, but ensuring that the conditions that led to placement are addressed: parental drug use treated, domestic violence safety measures in place, poverty alleviated, parenting skills supported.

Without these accompanying supports, reunification can fail – children return to families that have not changed, abuse recurs, and children may re-enter care. A family reunification process without accompanying family support services risks creating a revolving door rather than a genuine resolution of the underlying risk.[^933]

---


# Part 23: Statistics, Data Systems, and Evidence


## 4.108 Data Systems and Evidence Gaps


### 4.45.1 The Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD)

The **Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD)**, launched in 2016 with UNICEF support, represents the primary data system for child protection in the Maldives. The MCPD connects all 19 FCSCs (and now all 20) to a centralised digital case management platform that records referrals, assessments, plans, and outcomes at the national level.

Prior to the MCPD, case recording was entirely paper-based, held in individual offices, and impossible to aggregate across the system. The MCPD's introduction enabled, for the first time, national-level statistics on child protection case volume, case type, and geographic distribution – the data that underpins the Statistics Compendium and informs NAPVAC planning.[^742]

**Operational strengths:**
- Real-time data entry across all FCSC offices
- National case registry enabling identification of children known to multiple services
- Data disaggregation by case type, age, sex, atoll, and referral source
- Integration with the 1412 helpline call logging system
- Basis for annual child protection statistics reported in the Statistics Compendium

**Documented limitations:**
- **Under-recording**: Workers with excessive caseloads prioritise direct client contact over case recording; a proportion of cases received and managed are not entered into MCPD, particularly on outer islands with connectivity challenges
- **Quality of data entry**: Where cases are recorded, the depth and quality of recording – particularly of assessments, plans, and outcomes – is uneven
- **Outcome tracking**: The MCPD captures case open and closure data but does not systematically track child safety outcomes after case closure, limiting the system's ability to measure whether interventions actually improved children's safety
- **Inter-agency data linkage**: MCPD is not technically integrated with MPS FCPW case records, DJJ records, Family Court records, or hospital admission data – limiting the ability to track cases across the full child protection and justice system[^743]

### 4.45.2 The 1412 Helpline Data System

The 1412 helpline generates its own data, separately from MCPD. In 2024, 2,855 calls to 1412 were recorded. The call data captures:
- Call volume by month and atoll
- Referral source (self, professional, community member)
- Nature of concern reported
- Outcome of call (referred to FCSC; referred to police; referred to other service; information only; no action required)

The helpline data provides a partial indicator of community awareness and reporting patterns but is not automatically linked to MCPD case records, meaning that a call to 1412 and the subsequent FCSC case may not be systematically connected in the data system.[^744]

### 4.45.3 Police FCPW Data

The Maldives Police Service Family and Child Protection Wing maintains its own database of child protection cases received by police. In 2024, the MPS data showed:
- 120 juvenile cases received (2023)
- 213 juvenile cases received (2024)
- Child trafficking: 21 suspected cases investigated (year unspecified)
- CSEC: 291 cases investigated
- Attempted suicide cases involving children: 37 (2021-2024 aggregate)

MPS FCPW data is the primary source for information on the criminal dimensions of child protection – CSA cases with criminal referrals, juvenile offences, and child trafficking. However, this data is not routinely shared with CFPS in a systematic format, and cases that are managed by police without CFPS involvement may never appear in the MCPD.[^745]

### 4.45.4 DJJ and Family Court Data

The Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ) maintains records of young people passing through the juvenile justice system – including those diverted to community programmes and those sentenced to the Imaduddin School or Fiyavathi Rehabilitation Centre. The Family Court records child custody cases, maintenance applications, and protection orders. Neither DJJ nor Family Court data is systematically linked to CFPS or MCPD, meaning that children known to the child protection system and the justice system simultaneously may not be systematically identified as requiring integrated case management.[^746]

### 4.45.5 Critical Evidence Gaps

Despite progress in data systems, critical evidence gaps remain that limit the Maldives' ability to plan, evaluate, and improve child protection outcomes:

**No national prevalence study of child abuse and neglect.** The most recent comprehensive data on child abuse prevalence comes from the GSHS 2014 (CSA in 15% of adolescents) and the Fulu 2007 study. A nationally representative child maltreatment prevalence survey – using validated instruments such as the ISPCAN Child Abuse Screening Tool (ICAST) – has never been conducted in the Maldives. Without such a survey, the relationship between case system data (which captures only reported cases) and actual prevalence remains unknown.[^747]

**No outcome evaluation of child protection interventions.** The MCPD captures case input data (referrals, case types) but does not systematically measure whether children are safer after CFPS intervention than they were before. The absence of outcome measurement means that the child protection system cannot demonstrate its effectiveness, cannot identify which interventions work, and cannot be accountable to the children it serves.[^748]

**Limited disaggregation by disability status.** MCPD does not routinely record disability status of children referred for child protection. Given the 60% sexual violence rate among children with disabilities documented in the HRCM CRPD Shadow Report, this gap is significant – it means that children with disabilities are invisible in child protection data even though they face the highest abuse risks.[^749]

**No systematic child trafficking data.** The National Referral Mechanism for trafficking victims is nascent. The 21 suspected child trafficking cases and 291 CSEC cases documented represent cases that reached formal investigation – an unknown proportion of actual trafficking. No systematic data collection on children at risk of trafficking (including out-of-wedlock children, children in domestic service, and children in contact with known traffickers) has been established.[^750]

**Absent FGM/C surveillance.** Despite FGM/C being a widespread practice with child protection implications, no health information system captures FGM/C prevalence or incidence. The practice occurs in family and community settings without health facility contact, and healthcare workers who encounter FGM/C (e.g., when examining girls) are not required to record it systematically.[^751]

### 4.45.6 The Research Agenda

The CFW vulnerability mapping recommendations and NAPVAC 2024-2028 both identify a priority research agenda for child protection:

1. **National child maltreatment prevalence survey** using ICAST or equivalent validated instrument, with population-representative sampling including outer atolls and oversampling of high-risk groups
2. **Child protection outcome evaluation study** examining safety outcomes for children 12 and 24 months after CFPS case closure
3. **FGM/C prevalence rapid assessment** using community-based methodology with ethical safeguards
4. **Child trafficking rapid assessment** including mapping of at-risk populations and recruitment pathways
5. **Cost-effectiveness analysis** of child protection interventions (IBAMA, Beleniveriyaa, foster care) to support resource allocation decisions[^752]

---


## 4.109 The Child Protection Database and Data Architecture


### 4.70.1 The National Child Protection Database

The National Child Protection Database (NCPD), mandated under the Child Rights Protection Act 2019, is in active development as of 2024. A pilot digital case management system was deployed in selected FCSCs in 2023, with planned national rollout by 2026. Currently, case data is collected through a combination of paper-based and digital systems, with CFPS maintaining centralised records from FCSC reports.

This means that for much of the decade since CRPA's enactment, the primary evidence base for child protection decision-making has been paper records held at individual FCSCs, with limited centralised analysis capacity. National trends are difficult to track; case outcomes are rarely systematically recorded; and the identification of systemic patterns across cases requires manual aggregation that in practice does not occur.

### 4.70.2 Key Data Sources and Their Limitations

The CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) identifies the following key data sources for child rights monitoring:
- Maldives Bureau of Statistics Population Census (most recent: 2022)
- MoSFD/CFPS annual case statistics
- Maldives Police Service crime statistics (disaggregated by age of victim and offender)
- Ministry of Education school enrollment and attendance data
- Ministry of Health vital statistics and health surveys
- National Drug Agency surveys
- HRCM periodic reports
- National Bureau of Classification yearbook (Table 8.60: child abuse cases 2009–2020)

Significant data gaps persist:
- No national prevalence survey for child abuse and neglect (prevalence data relies on reported cases, which dramatically undercount actual prevalence)
- No systematic data on child labour in the informal economy
- No data on children in vulnerable migration situations
- No systematic data on outcomes for children who leave the formal child protection system
- Limited data on child marriage rates by atoll
- No data on violence rates for children in alternative care versus in family settings[^881]

### 4.70.3 The 1412 Childline as a Data Source

Childline 1412 receives thousands of contacts annually, with a significant proportion resulting in formal referrals to CFPS or FCSCs. The helpline data constitutes one of the most sensitive real-time indicators of child protection need, but its use as a systematic data source for trend analysis and resource allocation planning remains limited. The CRC Periodic Report notes that FCSCs collectively manage several thousand active cases at any given time, with physical abuse, emotional abuse, neglect, and sexual abuse all featuring significantly in the case mix – but precise case numbers are not publicly reported in the draft periodic report, reflecting the ongoing data infrastructure limitations.[^882]

---
