[^913]: Child poverty-CP nexus: HIES 2019–20; Part XII Housing/Poverty chapter; NSPA Annual Report (2022).

[^914]: Political interference: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), pp. 98–99; Human Rights Watch (2023); Maldives Financial Review (2023).

[^915]: Ombudsperson: CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §1.3; Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), p. 126; Children's Ombudsperson Annual Report (2022).

[^916]: MDT protocol: CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §5.2; MoSFD MDT Protocol (2022); Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025).

[^917]: Leaving care: Rogers & Ali (2023), p. 161; Alternative Care Regulation R.No.2020-R-69; Islamic succession law and Bargach (2002).

[^918]: Leaving care gap: CRPA 2019 age definition; MoSFD National Child Protection Strategy 2025–2030 (in development).

[^919]: Children's Rights Council: CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §1.3; CRPA 2019, Articles 14–18.

[^920]: CFPS case management: CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §5.2; CFPS SOPs (2021); Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025).

[^921]: MCPD: Government of Maldives Combined CRC Report (2021); CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §1.6.

[^922]: 1412 Childline: CRPA 2019, Article 76; CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §5.2; Part III Institutional Architecture chapter.

[^923]: Gender-CP nexus: Part V Gender chapter, §5.115.3 (Section 42); Appendix A, Special Measures Act 12/2009 entry; Fulu (2007).


---


## 4.112 Comprehensive Quantitative Data: Child Protection Statistics from the Statistics Compendium


### 4.106.1 Child Abuse Cases: Administrative Data Time Series

The most reliable indicator of child protection system activity is CFPS case data, which has become progressively more systematic as the MCPD database has developed. The available time series shows:

- **March–May 2018:** 349 VAC cases in three months (average 116 per month), of which 48% involved sexual abuse or physical violence – establishing the baseline picture before the CRPA 2019 came into effect
- **2023:** 2,936 total cases received by CFPS, of which 1,278 (43.5%) were classified as violence against children – the most current comprehensive annual figure

The 1412 helpline provides a parallel data stream: 2,855 calls were made to the helpline in 2024 – a figure that significantly understates total child protection concerns, since helpline calls represent only those callers who overcame the barriers to reaching out and who chose the telephone reporting pathway rather than direct presentation to an FCSC or referral from another agency.

The 2023 administrative data – 2,936 cases, 1,278 VAC – translates to a reporting rate of approximately 23 per 1,000 children (based on the 2022 Census child population of 128,208). For reference, England's equivalent reporting rate (children who are subjects of statutory assessment) is significantly higher, reflecting both higher actual rates of formal engagement and more systematic referral pathways from schools, health services, and police. The Maldivian rate almost certainly reflects severe under-reporting rather than a genuinely low prevalence of abuse and neglect.[^970]

### 4.106.2 Child Sexual Abuse: Prevalence Data

The prevalence data on child sexual abuse reveals the gap between administrative case counts and actual experience:

| **Indicator** | **Value** | **Source** |
|---|---|---|
| Adolescents reporting any sexual abuse experience | 15% | GSHS 2014 |
| Girls reporting sexual abuse vs. boys | 2× higher rate | GSHS 2014 |
| Secondary school girls reporting sexual abuse | ~33% | GSHS 2014 |
| Women reporting childhood sexual abuse (overall) | 12.2% | Fulu (2007) |
| Women reporting childhood sexual abuse (Male') | 16.3% | Fulu (2007) |
| Cases involving sexual abuse (% of CFPS cases) | 30% | MoSFD administrative data |
| Children with disabilities experiencing sexual violence | 60% | UNPRPD SitAn 2024 |

The GSHS 2014 finding – one in three secondary school girls reporting sexual abuse – is among the most concerning data points in the child protection evidence base. If this figure is approximately correct, it means that sexual abuse is not an exceptional event affecting a small minority of children, but a common experience for a large proportion of adolescent girls. When set against the approximately 30% of CFPS cases that involve sexual abuse – plausible as a proportion of reported cases – the scale of unreported abuse implied is very large.

The UNPRPD (2024) finding that 60% of children with disabilities experience sexual violence, with the majority being girls, adds a further dimension. The convergence of disability and gender creates the highest-risk group in the Maldivian child protection context – a group that is simultaneously least likely to be able to report, least likely to be believed, and least well-served by protection systems that have not been designed with their particular vulnerabilities in mind.[^971]

### 4.106.3 Child Marriage: Declining but Not Eliminated

The child marriage data from Census 2014 – 106 children aged 15–17 in marriages, 62 of them girls – represents a significant reduction from the historical pattern (the Maldives once held the world's highest divorce rate and had normalised very young marriage, particularly for girls). The ADB (2025) estimate of a 2.2% child marriage rate before age 18 places the Maldives toward the lower end of the South Asian range.

However, the Census 2014 data represents formal marriages only. The Family Act's wali system – which requires a male guardian's consent for marriage – means that some informal unions may not be registered, and that arrangements between families that effectively amount to child marriages may not appear in civil registration data. The Supreme Court's 2014 regulation on minors' marriages, which tightened (but did not eliminate) the conditions under which girls under 18 could marry, created a formal barrier without eliminating the practice. Annual data from the DJA on marriages involving minors would be the most useful monitoring tool, but this data has not been consistently published.[^972]

### 4.106.4 Alternative Care: The Institutional Dominance

The alternative care statistics from March 2023 reveal a system heavily reliant on institutional care:

| **Indicator** | **Value** |
|---|---|
| Children in alternative care (total) | 187 |
| Girls | 82 |
| Boys | 105 |
| In residential/institutional care | 91% (170) |
| In foster care | 9% (16) |
| Registered foster carers | 18 |

The 91% institutional care rate – meaning that nine out of ten children outside parental care live in residential institutions rather than family-based settings – is strikingly high by international standards. The UN Guidelines for the Alternative Care of Children (2009), affirmed by Maldives through CRC ratification, explicitly call for deinstitutionalisation as a global standard, recognising that residential institutions rarely provide the individual attention, family relationships, and developmental environment that children require.

The data on the 16 children in foster care (representing 9% of the alternative care population) against 18 registered foster carers suggests that the foster care system is operating at near-maximum capacity with the carers it has. Expanding foster care – the stated policy direction – requires not just carer recruitment but the training, supervision, support, and financial allowances for carers that make fostering sustainable over time. The international experience is that recruitment without adequate support leads to high placement breakdown rates, which are harmful to children and demoralising to carers.[^973]

### 4.106.5 Child Protection Workforce: Caseload Data

The workforce data is the most consequential set of statistics for understanding why the Maldivian child protection system performs as it does:

| **Indicator** | **Value** |
|---|---|
| Child protection social workers | 61 |
| Children in Maldives (0–17) | 128,208 |
| Average caseload per worker | ~160 |
| International standard caseload | 25–30 |
| Ratio of actual to recommended caseload | 5.3×–6.4× |
| DV/GBV caseworkers in Male' | 3 |
| Average DV caseload per caseworker | 70–80 |

The 5.3×–6.4× ratio of actual to recommended caseload is not a measure of system inefficiency – it is a measure of system impossibility. No social worker carrying 160 cases can provide the assessment depth, monitoring frequency, or relationship continuity that effective child protection requires. The consequence is triage by crisis: workers respond to the most acute presenting concerns and defer everything else. Prevention, family support, relationship-based practice, and regular statutory review visits happen far less than the CRPA 2019 mandates.

The DV/GBV caseworker data – 3 workers in Male' carrying 70–80 cases each – illustrates the same problem in a concentrated form. Domestic violence response requires intensive engagement: safety planning, court attendance, service navigation, regular welfare checks. At 70–80 cases per worker, meaningful response is arithmetically impossible, and workers inevitably prioritise the cases with the most obvious acute risk.[^974]

### 4.106.6 Juvenile Justice: The Reform Impact in Numbers

The juvenile justice data illustrates a system in genuine transition, with the 2019 Act producing measurable shifts in practice:

| **Indicator** | **Value** | **Period** |
|---|---|---|
| Juveniles sentenced to death | 5 | 2013–2015 (pre-reform era) |
| Juveniles in rehabilitation programmes | 89 | 2010–2015 |
| Successful rehabilitation completion | 11.2% (10/89) | 2010–2015 |
| Juvenile cases investigated by MPS | 120 | 2023 |
| Juvenile cases investigated by MPS | 213 | 2024 |

The five death sentences imposed on juveniles between 2013 and 2015 stand as the starkest evidence of the pre-reform era's failure – not merely an enforcement gap but a categorical violation of the most fundamental principles of child-friendly justice. The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 categorically prohibits this.

The 89% failure rate in the 2010–2015 rehabilitation programme – only 10 of 89 enrolled juveniles completing successfully – illustrates why facility closure (the Temporary Detention Centre for Children closed in 2013; several successor facilities closed by the Juvenile Court by 2019) was justified even before the new legal framework was established. A programme with an 11.2% completion rate is not rehabilitating young people – it is warehousing them.

The increase in MPS juvenile investigations from 120 (2023) to 213 (2024) reflects either increased offending, increased reporting and detection, or both – and requires further analysis to interpret. If it reflects increased reporting by a more confident community and better-equipped police, it may be a positive indicator of system improvement. If it reflects a genuine increase in juvenile offending – particularly drug-related offending, which has historically been the primary driver of juvenile criminal justice contact – it signals an escalating challenge for the DJJ and the prevention system.[^975]

### 4.106.7 Children's Household Composition and Family Structure

The Census 2022 children's equity data provides the demographic foundation for child protection planning:

| **Living Arrangement** | **% of Children (0–17)** |
|---|---|
| Living with both parents | 74% |
| Living with one parent | 22% |
| In alternative care arrangements | 9% |

The 22% of children living with one parent – a figure substantially driven by the Maldives' extremely high divorce rate, with approximately 60% of marriages ending in divorce – represents a large group with elevated child protection risk. Single-parent households (the majority of which are mother-headed given custody patterns under the Family Act) have lower average incomes, more housing instability, and less supervision capacity than two-parent households. They are the primary target population for the Single Parent Allowance (MVR 3,000/month) and the child maintenance enforcement system – both of which have significant implementation gaps as documented in §4.82 (political interference) and §4.84 (maintenance enforcement failure).

The 9% of children in "alternative care arrangements" is the most complex figure. It likely encompasses children in the formal alternative care system (the 187 documented in the 2023 data) but also the much larger informal kinship care arrangements – children living with grandparents, aunts and uncles, or other extended family members – that are not registered and receive no formal support. Kinship care is the predominant form of informal alternative care in the Maldives, as in most Muslim-majority societies where family solidarity and Islamic *kafalah* principles encourage relatives to care for children whose parents cannot. These children are largely invisible to the formal system and receive no formal monitoring, support, or protection.[^976]

---


# Part 24: Key Sources and Annotated Bibliography


## 4.113 Key Source Analysis: What the Literature Actually Found


### 4.79.1 The 28-Year Gap: Law 9/91 and Its Legacy

Law 9/91 – the Law on the Protection of the Rights of Children enacted in the same year as UNCRC ratification – was the Maldives' first legislative acknowledgment of children's rights. The Appendix A bibliography describes it as "largely declaratory in nature": it set out broad principles covering child health, maternal care, education, treatment of children with disabilities, and rehabilitation of juvenile delinquents, but contained:
- No definitions of child abuse or maltreatment
- No mandatory reporting requirements
- No provisions for state intervention in families where children are at risk

Law 9/91 served primarily as a symbolic gesture of CRC compliance rather than a functional child protection statute. It remained the sole child protection statute for **28 years** until being superseded by the CRPA 2019. The contrast between Law 9/91's declaratory provisions and the CRPA's operational framework illustrates the depth of the 28-year implementation gap – a gap in which the Maldives had the formal appearance of a child protection system without its functional reality.[^902]

### 4.79.2 Special Measures Act 12/2009: Innovations and Limitations

The Special Provisions Act to Deal with Child Sex Abuse Offenders (Act No. 12/2009) represented a significant legal innovation:
- Defined specific sexual offences against children with imprisonment terms of **7–14 years** (later strengthened to 10–25 years without parole)
- Lowered evidentiary standards for CSA prosecution
- Mandated public disclosure of offender identities
- Provided for victim compensation

The Act was enacted in a context where population-based survey data found:
- **15% of adolescents** reported experiencing sexual abuse (rates among girls approximately double those of boys)
- **Nearly one-third of secondary school girls** reported sexual abuse
- Hospital data from IGMH (2005–2009) recorded 43 rape cases, of which **22 involved minors**, and **almost half of cases involving minors were gang-rapes**

Despite its strengthened provisions, the Act's implementation was hampered by:
- Limited forensic capacity for child sexual abuse investigation outside Malé
- Absence of a dedicated child sexual abuse investigation unit
- Minimal victim support services
- The Section 52 SOA evidentiary requirements that applied alongside the Act's provisions

The Act's requirement for public disclosure of offender identities – unusual by international standards – reflected the cultural weight of reputational sanction in Maldivian society, but has also been criticised for potentially deterring victims from reporting (if the conviction pathway is uncertain, the high visibility of accusation increases the cost of a false outcome).[^903]

### 4.79.3 Ministry of Gender Statistical Data: 2,936 Cases Annually

The Ministry of Gender (Table 8.60, National Bureau of Classification Statistical Yearbook) provides the most systematic administrative data on reported child abuse cases:

- By 2023, the Ministry was receiving **2,936 cases annually**
- Of these, **1,278 (43.5%) were violence against children cases**
- Negligence and sexual abuse were the most common types
- For the period March–May 2018 alone: **349 child abuse cases**, of which **48% involved sexual abuse or physical violence** (average 116 reported cases per month)
- A consistent upward trend in reported cases from 2009 to 2020 (NCB Statistical Yearbook, Table 8.60)
- **Cyberbullying** introduced as a new category in recent years, reflecting growing recognition of online harms

These statistics represent cases entering the formal system – actual prevalence is substantially higher given under-reporting driven by community surveillance norms, fear of retaliation, and the social costs of disclosure in small island communities.[^904]

### 4.79.4 The UNICEF SitAn 2025: Key Quantitative Findings

The Situation Analysis of Children and Adolescents in the Republic of Maldives (Haarr, 2025) is described in the Appendix A bibliography as "the most methodologically sophisticated child-focused assessment available," employing equity, trend, causal, PESTEL, and capacity gap analyses. Key quantitative findings:

- **61 social workers serving 128,208 children** across 189 inhabited islands
- Average caseloads exceeding **160 cases per worker**
- **19.9% of students** had seriously considered attempting suicide (GSHS 2009 data)
- **18.8% of young people aged 15–29** classified as depressed (STEP Survey 2021)
- Police recorded **37 attempted suicide cases involving child victims** between January 2021 and September 2024, with **35 of 37 cases involving children aged 11–17**

The suicide and self-harm data are particularly concerning in a child protection context: suicidal ideation and self-harm in children are closely associated with experiences of abuse, neglect, family breakdown, and exposure to violence. The high rates identified in the GSHS (2009) data – 19.9% – suggest that the burden of unresolved trauma among Maldivian children is substantial.[^905]

### 4.79.5 The Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) Systems Analysis: Operational Detail

The Appendix A bibliography describes the Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) chapter as the definitive operationally detailed source on how child protection actually functions at the frontline level. It documented four primary case management pathways:
1. Children witnessing domestic violence
2. Children experiencing direct abuse
3. Children in custody conflicts – where children are routinely *"used as leverage in custody disputes"*
4. Children of stressed single parents at risk of neglect

The analysis documented systemic failures across all four pathways:
- **Care plans were rare** – children entering state care frequently lacked documented plans
- **Regular review meetings were not common practice**
- **Complex politics permeated case management** – cases involving families of political actors were "leaked to media before being reported" through proper channels
- Cases involving perpetrators in positions of power (gang leaders, drug traffickers, violent extremists) were often not reported due to fear of reprisal
- **Social workers received death threats** in the course of their work (Human Rights Watch, 2023)
- Ministry operated in only **20 of 189 inhabited islands** (note: a different figure from the 24 FCSCs in the CRC report, reflecting different counting methodologies and different reference years)

The Appendix A entry summarises the fundamental insight: the gap between the CRPA 2019's provisions and frontline reality is not primarily a gap in legislation but a gap in operational capacity, political will, and institutional culture.[^906]

### 4.79.6 The NGO Shadow Report Tradition: "Moving from Aspirations to Impact"

The 2006 NGO shadow report by Hama Jamiyya and Care Society – titled "Moving from Aspirations to Impact" – captured in its title what has been the defining theme of Maldivian child protection for two decades. The report documented significant gaps between government CRC reporting and children's lived realities. Produced at a time when the URC operated with minimal staff, no formal case management procedures, limited geographic reach beyond Malé, and no legal mandate to investigate child protection cases, the shadow report established the precedent of civil society accountability that has continued through subsequent reporting cycles.

The "aspirations to impact" framing remains relevant in 2026: the 2019 legislative reforms represent the most significant expansion of the aspirational framework in the system's history, but the implementation gap – as documented by Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025), the UNICEF SitAn (2025), and the CRC Periodic Report (2026 draft) – continues to be wide.

The tradition of independent shadow reporting has been an important accountability mechanism. The Government of Maldives' combined 6th and 7th periodic report (2021) was the first comprehensive government account since the democratic transition, covering the CRPA 2019, Juvenile Justice Act 2019, Children's Ombudsperson, Childline 1412, and the Maldives Child Protection Database. The interaction between government reporting and civil society shadow reports – each providing a different perspective on the same system – is a structural feature of the CRC accountability architecture.[^907]

---


## 4.114 Key Child Protection Sources: An Annotated Guide


The following provides a guide to the primary sources used in this chapter, with sufficient annotation to orient the reader to each source's scope, methodology, and key contributions to child protection knowledge.

### 4.55.1 Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) – The Definitive System Analysis

**Full citation:** Rogers, J., Ali, M., & Naeem, M. (2025). "The development of child protection systems and practice in the Republic of Maldives." In Burns, K. and colleagues (eds.), *Child Protection in the Global South*. Policy Press, Bristol.

**Scope:** The most comprehensive academic analysis of Maldivian child protection system development available. The chapter covers socio-political and historical context; defining childhood in the Maldivian cultural framework; social protection; child maltreatment definitions and legal framework; case studies (Jakob – domestic violence; Aysha – child sexual abuse); tensions and challenges (political interference, death threats, caseload); and future directions.

**Methodology:** Academic chapter drawing on review of legislation, policy documents, case studies, and practitioner evidence from Maldivian child protection professionals.

**Key contributions:**
- Documents the "honour ideology" that inhibits abuse reporting
- Provides the Jakob and Aysha case studies that illustrate how the system responds to DV and CSA
- Identifies political interference and death threats as operational challenges
- Critiques the over-cautious removal tendency and the lack of foster care
- Introduces the concept of "adultification" in Maldivian cultural understandings of childhood
- Provides the most detailed analysis available of how the English child protection model was imported and adapted[^827]

### 4.55.2 UNICEF Situation Analysis 2025 (Building Futures)

**Full citation:** UNICEF Maldives (2025). *Building Futures: Securing Child Rights and Responding to the Adolescent Challenge in the Maldives.* Malé: UNICEF Maldives.

**Scope:** Comprehensive situation analysis of children's rights and wellbeing, examining progress, challenges, deprivation patterns, and barriers to child rights fulfillment. Includes analysis of child protection, education, health, and social protection systems.

**Methodology:** Secondary analysis of census, DHS, administrative, and survey data; stakeholder consultations; UNICEF programmatic evidence.

**Key contributions:**
- Updated child population figures (128,208 children, 33.5% of population)
- Multidimensional child poverty: 33% nationally, 40% in atolls
- Most recent data on 1412 helpline calls (2,855 in 2024)
- Self-harm cases: 211 in 2024, 74% female
- Mental health data integration with child protection analysis[^828]

### 4.55.3 Fulu et al. (2007) – Women's Health and Life Experiences Study

**Full citation:** Fulu, E. (2007). *Women's Health and Life Experiences in the Maldives: A National Study on Domestic Violence.* Malé: UNFPA Maldives.

**Scope:** National prevalence study on intimate partner violence using WHO multi-country methodology. The only methodologically rigorous national DV prevalence study in Maldivian history.

**Methodology:** Population-based household survey using the WHO standardised DV measurement instrument, enabling comparison with 15 other countries in the WHO multi-country study.

**Key contributions for child protection:**
- Found approximately 1 in 3 women experienced physical/sexual IPV in their lifetime (14% in past year)
- Children witnessing DV: not separately quantified but documented qualitatively
- First rigorous documentation of the cultural and social norms that inhibit DV/abuse reporting in the Maldivian context
- Evidence of the honour ideology that frames women's and children's vulnerability[^829]

### 4.55.4 UNICEF (2022) – CFPS Assessment

**Full citation:** UNICEF Maldives (2022). *Assessment of Child and Family Protection Services Capacity and Caseload.* Malé: UNICEF Maldives.

**Scope:** The most detailed available assessment of the operational capacity and caseload pressures on Maldivian child protection social workers.

**Key contributions:**
- Documented average caseload of ~160 cases per social worker (recommended 25-30)
- Identified supervision deficits as a key quality risk
- Documented the specific failure of the school-CFPS referral pathway
- Documented the absence of foster care as an alternative care option
- Led to revised targets in the NAPVAC 2024-2028[^830]

### 4.55.5 Global School-Based Student Health Survey (GSHS) 2014

**Full citation:** Ministry of Health and WHO (2014). *Global School-Based Student Health Survey, Maldives 2014 Fact Sheet.* Malé: Ministry of Health.

**Key contributions for child protection:**
- CSA prevalence: 15% of adolescents (girls at 2x the rate of boys)
- 33% of secondary school girls reported CSA
- Suicidal ideation: 19.9% of students (GSHS 2009, referenced as baseline)
- Tobacco use among 13-15 year olds: 45.7%[^831]

### 4.55.6 Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations (2020)

**Full citation:** Government of Maldives (2020). *Report of the Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations.* Malé: Government of Maldives.

**Scope:** Official investigation into systemic failures in child rights protection, established by presidential order in February 2020.

**Key contributions:**
- Documented inter-agency coordination failures
- Identified chronic underfunding as a structural barrier
- Documented workforce capacity deficits
- Recommendations aligned with the subsequent NAPVAC 2024-2028
- Provided political legitimacy for the reform agenda[^832]

### 4.55.7 ICSVE Prison Radicalisation Study (2022)

**Full citation:** Speckhard, A. (2022). *Prison Radicalisation in the Maldives: A Study of Extremist Recruitment in Maafushi High-Security Prison.* International Center for the Study of Violent Extremism and Transparency Maldives.

**Key contributions for child protection:**
- Documented that radicalised prison population had mean age at first arrest of 21.56 years – adolescent-onset pathways
- Identified the drug-to-gang-to-extremism pipeline that captures young people in contact with the juvenile justice system
- Found that state care facility staff were not equipped to deal with radicalized children
- Documented material inducements (money, mobile phones) used to recruit young inmates[^833]

### 4.55.8 HRCM CRPD Shadow Report (2023)

**Full citation:** Human Rights Commission of the Maldives (2023). *Shadow Report to the UN Committee on the Rights of Persons with Disabilities.* Malé: HRCM.

**Key contributions for child protection:**
- Documented 60% prevalence of sexual violence against children with disabilities (predominantly girls)
- Persons with disabilities 4 times more likely to experience violence
- Identified specific barriers to reporting for children with communication difficulties
- Identified the sign language crisis as a child protection access issue[^834]

---

- Human Rights Watch (2023). Reports on worker safety in the Maldivian child protection system.[^939]

[^937]: Civil society: CSO/NGO Directory (Part XXIb); Appendix A bibliography; Rogers & Ali (2023); Hope for Women (2023).

[^938]: Family Court: CRPA 2019, Articles 80, 90, 93; CRC Periodic Report Draft (2026), §6.2; Part V Gender chapter §5.115.4; Service Provider Directory §3.6.

[^939]: Source index: compiled from all sources cited throughout this chapter. Full bibliographic details in Appendix A bibliography, Category 1 (Child Protection and Child Rights).


---


## 4.116 Annotated Key Sources: Child Protection Evidence Base (Supplementary)


### 4.111.1 Statistical Compendium Child Data

The Part XVII Statistics Compendium consolidates quantitative indicators across all social policy domains. For child protection, the most analytically significant data points from the Compendium not covered elsewhere in this chapter are:

**Population time series:** The Maldivian population of children (0–17) stands at 128,208 as of Census 2022 – a figure that represents 33.5% of the total Maldivian population. The age cohort data shows a shrinking youngest cohort (0–4 fell from 31,834 in 2014 to 30,621 in 2022) alongside a growing pre-teen cohort (5–9 increased from 28,903 to 33,115). The implication for child protection planning is that current demand will shift – fewer very young children presenting with early neglect and abuse, but more children of school age presenting through education-system detection pathways.

**Disability and children:** The intersection of childhood and disability represents the highest-risk group. The UNPRPD SitAn (2024) finding that 60% of children with disabilities experience sexual violence – with girls disproportionately affected – establishes that the disability-child protection intersection is not a secondary concern but one of the highest-priority areas for system development. The 87% of government schools with SEN units (but many lacking effective inclusive practice) means that children with disabilities in school settings are not necessarily receiving the support, protection, or specialist assessment that their vulnerability requires.[^990]

### 4.111.2 Action Plans Analysis – Child Protection Planning Architecture

The Action Plans Analysis (Part XVI) provides the most systematic assessment of the planning framework within which child protection operates. Key findings for child protection:

**NAPVAC predecessor failure:** The candid acknowledgement in the NAPVAC 2024 that its 2017 predecessor was never effectively implemented – attributed to lack of systematic approach and stakeholder coordination difficulties – is documented in detail in the Action Plans Analysis at §1.1.2. The analysis concludes that the same structural barriers (resourcing, multi-agency coordination, geographic operationalisation) that defeated the 2017 plan are present in the 2024 plan, making its implementation prospects uncertain unless those barriers are explicitly addressed.

**CFW 39 recommendations – child protection subset:** The CFW (Current Frameworks and Vulnerabilities) report's 39 recommendations include a substantial child protection subset covering: mandatory FCSC geographic expansion; social work caseload reduction targets; IBAMA quality improvement; maintenance enforcement reform; foster care expansion with specific numerical targets; alternative care deinstitutionalisation timelines; and online child safety legislation. These recommendations provide the most operationally specific reform agenda available and should serve as the monitoring benchmark against which implementation progress is assessed.

**International obligation mapping:** The Action Plans Analysis maps 342 rows of international obligations across CEDAW, CRPD, CRC, and CRC Optional Protocols. The CRC obligations that directly relate to child protection – Articles 19 (violence protection), 20 (alternative care), 21 (adoption), 22 (refugee children), 25 (periodic review for children in alternative care), 34 (sexual exploitation), 37 (torture, cruel treatment), 39 (recovery), 40 (juvenile justice) – provide the authoritative international framework within which all domestic child protection planning should be situated.[^991]

### 4.111.3 CRC Periodic Report Draft – Self-Assessment Findings

The Part XVIII-B CRC Periodic Report Draft, prepared as a contribution to the 2021 State Party Report process, provides the most recent government self-assessment of child protection system performance. Key child protection findings from the self-assessment that are most significant for this chapter's analysis:

- The Maldives' CRC reservations have not been formally withdrawn despite CRPA 2019's substantial alignment with CRC standards – a procedural gap that the CRC Committee has flagged
- The social work workforce data (61 workers, 1:2,100 ratio) is explicitly acknowledged in the self-assessment as inadequate
- The alternative care deinstitutionalisation commitment is stated but without specific targets or timelines
- The juvenile justice reform – 2019 Act, DJJ establishment, CYCC – is presented as the primary reform achievement of the reporting period
- Child participation mechanisms are described as under-development – acknowledging the gap between the CRPA's participation provisions and current practice
- Data gaps are acknowledged: the absence of comprehensive child mortality data beyond 5 years, the limited documentation of child labour prevalence, and the non-publication of comprehensive juvenile justice outcome data[^992]

# Part 25: Research Gaps, Reform Agenda, and Conclusion


## 4.117 Research Gaps and Recommendations


### 4.18.1 Female Genital Mutilation/Cutting

While FGM/C is discussed in greater detail in Part V (Gender, GBV, and Women's Empowerment), its relevance to child protection warrants attention here. The **Demographic and Health Survey 2016–17** found that **13% of women aged 15–49** had been subjected to FGM/C. Critically, prevalence data disaggregated by age reveals a declining trend: from **38% among women aged 45–49** to just **1% among women aged 15–19**. This steep decline indicates that the practice has been substantially reduced in recent decades, though it has not been eliminated.

FGM/C in the Maldives is practised primarily as a form of *sunnah* cutting (a less severe form, typically involving the nicking or cutting of the clitoral prepuce), and is endorsed by some religious leaders as a recommended (*sunnah*) practice. There is **no legal prohibition or criminalisation of FGM/C** in Maldivian law, meaning that the practice, where it continues, is not subject to criminal sanction. The absence of legal protection is a significant child protection gap, as FGM/C constitutes a harmful traditional practice affecting girls' physical integrity and rights.[^79]

### 4.18.2 Identified Research Gaps

Multiple evidence reviews have identified the following research gaps in the child protection field in the Maldives:

1. **No national prevalence study of child maltreatment:** The Maldives has never conducted a dedicated national prevalence study of child abuse and neglect using validated instruments. Available data comes from administrative records (which capture only reported cases) and from questions embedded in broader surveys (such as the WHLE study and DHS). A dedicated prevalence study is essential for understanding the true scale of the problem and targeting resources effectively.

2. **Limited longitudinal data on outcomes:** There is no systematic tracking of outcomes for children who have been through the child protection system – including children who have been in alternative care, children who have been subject to protection orders, and children who have been through the juvenile justice system. Without outcomes data, the effectiveness of interventions cannot be assessed.

3. **Insufficient understanding of community-level protective factors:** Research has focused primarily on risk factors and system deficiencies. Less is known about the community-level protective factors – including kinship networks, religious institutions, and school-based programmes – that may be supporting children's wellbeing in the absence of formal services.

4. **No evaluation of the CSG/IBAMA community volunteer model:** The community-level volunteer model has not been rigorously evaluated for its effectiveness in identifying and referring child protection concerns.

5. **Online child exploitation:** The digital dimension of child protection – including online grooming, the production and distribution of child sexual abuse material, and cyberbullying – is poorly understood in the Maldivian context despite high internet penetration among children and adolescents.

6. **Intersection of child protection and extremism:** The pathways through which children are recruited into extremist activities, the impact of parental extremism on children's wellbeing, and the effectiveness of deradicalisation programmes for children are areas requiring urgent research attention.[^80]

### 4.18.3 Policy Recommendations

The evidence reviewed in this chapter points to the following priority recommendations:

1. **Workforce expansion and investment:** The 61-worker child protection workforce is fundamentally inadequate. A phased expansion plan, linked to BSW training pipeline development and competitive remuneration, is essential.

2. **Closure of the Family Act Section 4(b) loophole:** The CRPA's establishment of 18 as the minimum marriage age is undermined by the continued operation of the Family Act's provisions allowing judicial authorisation of child marriage. Legislative amendment to close this loophole is a priority.

3. **Comprehensive prohibition of corporal punishment:** Legislation should be enacted prohibiting corporal punishment of children in all settings – including the home, alternative care, and penal settings – bringing the Maldives into compliance with CRC standards.

4. **Criminalisation of FGM/C:** Legal prohibition and criminalisation of FGM/C, accompanied by community education and engagement with religious leaders.

5. **Development of family-based alternative care:** The current ratio of 91% institutional to 9% foster care must be reversed through systematic investment in foster carer recruitment, assessment, training, and support.

6. **Establishment of multi-agency case conferencing:** Formal protocols for multi-agency case conferences should be developed and mandated, linking CFPS, police, health services, education, and the judiciary in structured information-sharing and planning processes.

7. **Abolition of judicial flogging for children:** The JJ Act should be amended to explicitly prohibit all forms of corporal punishment, including judicial flogging, for persons under 18.

8. **Extension of services beyond the 20 islands:** The limitation of Ministry presence to 20 of 189 inhabited islands is a structural barrier to child protection coverage. Investment in mobile service delivery models, community-based paraworkers, and digital service access is needed.

9. **Addressing political interference:** Formal safeguards – including confidentiality protocols, whistleblower protections for social workers, and independent oversight – are needed to insulate child protection casework from political interference and intimidation.[^81]

---


## 4.118 Research Gaps, Future Directions, and Conclusion


### 4.50.1 The State of Child Protection Knowledge

The Maldives child protection system is better documented in 2026 than at any previous point in its history. The combination of the CRPA 2019's legislative framework, the MCPD database, the UNICEF Situation Analysis series, the Census 2022 child-equity analysis, the CRC Periodic Report Draft, the NAPVAC 2024-2028, and the CFW vulnerability mapping process has created a body of evidence on Maldivian child protection that is sophisticated relative to the country's size and income level.

Yet this documentation should not be mistaken for adequacy. The evidence base is patchy, contested in places, and built on foundations that are older than they should be. The most recent national child abuse prevalence data uses instruments from 2014 (GSHS) and 2007 (Fulu). The alternative care system's outcomes have never been systematically evaluated. The juvenile justice system's effectiveness in reducing reoffending is unknown. The IBAMA programme's reach and impact have not been rigorously mapped. FGM/C prevalence is not measured. The 61 social workers who make up the operational core of the system carry caseloads that prohibit reflective, evidence-based practice. These are not minor gaps – they are fundamental limitations on the system's capacity to learn and improve.[^770]

### 4.50.2 Critical Research Gaps

**Gap 1: National Child Maltreatment Prevalence Survey.** The Maldives has never conducted a nationally representative survey of child maltreatment using validated instruments. Without baseline prevalence data, the system cannot know whether it is improving children's safety over time, cannot allocate resources proportionate to actual prevalence, and cannot justify investment in prevention relative to response. A national child maltreatment survey using the ISPCAN Child Abuse Screening Tool (ICAST), or equivalent, is the most important research investment the child protection system could make.[^771]

**Gap 2: Child Protection Outcome Evaluation.** The MCPD captures what enters the system but not what happens to children after intervention. A longitudinal study of children known to CFPS – tracking their safety, welfare, and wellbeing at 12 and 24 months after case closure – would transform the system's ability to identify effective interventions and discontinue ineffective ones.

**Gap 3: FGM/C Rapid Assessment.** A community-based rapid assessment, using validated methodologies adapted for the Maldivian Islamic context, could provide the prevalence and practice data needed to move FGM/C from invisible to addressable.

**Gap 4: Foster Care and Alternative Care Evaluation.** The nascent foster care system needs evaluation of its quality, the characteristics of children in different care settings, and the outcomes for children who age out of care versus those who return home.

**Gap 5: IBAMA Reach and Quality Assessment.** A systematic mapping of IBAMA volunteer coverage across all 189 inhabited islands, combined with quality assessment of volunteer practice, would identify gaps and provide the evidence base for targeted strengthening.

### 4.50.3 Future Directions: The System the Maldives Needs

The Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) chapter on child protection in the Maldives identifies a key tension in the future direction of the system: **the risk of over-cautious removal** versus the risk of insufficient protection. The Maldivian system has historically erred on the side of family preservation and non-intervention – a position shaped by cultural values around family privacy, community norms about state non-interference, and the practical impossibility of placing removed children in adequate alternative care. Yet the scale of abuse documented in the case data and prevalence surveys suggests that this conservatism has left many children in dangerous situations.[^772]

The shift the system needs is not simply toward more removal – the alternative care system is already inadequate for the 187 children currently in it. The shift needed is toward:

**1. Earlier intervention.** IBAMA and school counsellors identifying families under stress before maltreatment becomes severe enough to require removal. The Beleniveriyaa Positive Parenting programme providing families with skills and support before crisis. Economic assistance reducing the poverty-related stress that drives neglect.

**2. A genuine continuum of care.** From family support services at the prevention end, through intensive family preservation for families at risk of child removal, to foster care (rather than institutional care) for children who cannot safely remain at home, to therapeutic residential care for children with the most complex needs. Currently, the system jumps from nothing to institutional care with little in between.

**3. Genuine inter-agency practice.** The MDT protocol, when fully implemented nationally, would transform coordination from aspiration to practice. Every child with complex protection needs being managed by a team that shares information, agrees a plan, and reviews outcomes together – rather than by four separate agencies managing their slices independently.

**4. Community protective factors.** IBAMA, youth clubs, safe recreational spaces, digital safety education, religious leader engagement, and community awareness of child rights together create an environment in which abuse is harder to perpetrate, more likely to be noticed, and more likely to be reported. These community protective factors are cheaper than professional response and more durable.

**5. An accountable system.** A Children's Ombudsperson with the resources and independence to inspect care facilities, review serious case outcomes, and report publicly on system performance. A professional registration system that holds social workers to standards and provides them with the professional identity and protections they need to do difficult work. A data system that tracks child safety outcomes rather than just case inputs.[^773]

### 4.50.4 The Child Protection System in Historical Perspective

The Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) history of child protection system development in the Maldives traces a trajectory from the pre-1997 period – when there was no formal child protection system and children's welfare was entirely a family and community matter – through the 1997-2005 system building phase (NCW/MCPF establishment; English model import; first case management procedures), the 2009-2019 institutionalisation phase (FCSCs; 1412 helpline; CRPA drafting), and the 2019-present maturation phase (CRPA enactment; JJ Act; MCPD expansion; Children's Ombudsperson; NAPVAC 2024-2028).

This trajectory – from nothing to a formal, internationally engaged, legislatively grounded child protection system in approximately 30 years – is a genuine achievement. The Maldives has built more comprehensive child protection infrastructure than many comparably sized and income-level countries. The CRPA 2019 is a well-drafted, rights-grounded piece of legislation. The MCPD provides national data. The IBAMA model is genuinely innovative. The JJ Act's diversion provisions represent progressive criminal justice reform.

What this history also reveals is the consistent pattern of framework-ahead-of-capacity that has characterised child protection development across the whole period. Each new legislative or policy framework has articulated aspirations that the operational system could not yet fulfil. The CRPA mandates services that the 61 social workers cannot provide. The MDT protocol requires inter-agency coordination that data systems and organisational cultures have not yet enabled. The foster care system remains almost entirely on paper.[^774]

The child of the Maldives who is in danger today cannot wait for the system to mature through another generation of framework development. They need a social worker who can visit, a counsellor who can listen, a foster carer who can provide safety, a court that can move quickly, and a community that recognises abuse rather than looking away. Closing the gap between what the framework promises and what the system can deliver is the defining challenge of the NAPVAC 2024-2028 and the generation of child protection workers who will carry it forward.

---


## 4.119 Evidence Gaps: What We Do Not Know About Child Protection in the Maldives


### 4.117.1 The Fundamental Data Absence

The most important single finding about the Maldivian child protection evidence base is its core absence: **no national prevalence study of child maltreatment has ever been conducted.** This means that the system does not know the actual prevalence of physical abuse, sexual abuse, neglect, or emotional abuse among children in the Maldives. All available data is either administrative (what the system has processed) or proxy (adult women's retrospective reports of childhood experiences, or adolescent self-reports in school surveys). Neither type of data is adequate as a basis for national child protection planning.

The 2007 Women's Health and Life Experiences Study (Fulu) provides the best retrospective estimate – 12.2% of adult women reporting childhood sexual abuse. The 2014 GSHS provides the best adolescent self-report – 15% of adolescents reporting sexual abuse experience. But neither provides the comprehensive, representative, methodologically robust national prevalence estimate that would enable the system to calibrate its response to actual need rather than to the fraction of need that reaches formal services.

The Action Plans Analysis evidence cross-reference identifies this explicitly: "The entire evidence base for VAC prevalence in the Maldives is outdated or absent." The recommendation that follows from this is straightforward: commissioning a National Study on Violence Against Children – using the UNICEF-WHO standardised methodology for VAC prevalence research – is a prerequisite for effective system planning. Without it, all prioritisation decisions are made in the dark.[^1009]

### 4.117.2 Specific Evidence Gaps by Domain

**Child protection system performance:** No data exists on time between first identified concern and first intervention – a fundamental system performance indicator. No mapping of informal identification pathways across different community types has been conducted. No evaluation of IBAMA group effectiveness has been published. No assessment of foster placement breakdown rates exists.

**Child sexual abuse:** No data exists by community type (Male' vs. outer islands) in the adolescent period. No data on the proportion of cases involving known perpetrators versus strangers (which would inform prevention strategy). No follow-up data on outcomes for children who disclose and receive intervention versus those who disclose and are not supported.

**Institutional care quality:** No independent monitoring data on the quality of care in the 9 Fiyavathi units. No data on the experiences of children currently in institutional care – what they like, what they find difficult, what they wish were different. No outcomes data for children who have left institutional care – where they are, how they are doing.

**Juvenile justice outcomes:** No long-term recidivism data for children who have gone through the DJJ programming. The 11.2% completion rate in the 2010–2015 rehabilitation programme is the only outcome data available, and it predates the Juvenile Justice Act 2019 reforms.

**Alternative care for children with disabilities:** No data on the proportion of children with disabilities in institutional versus family-based care. No assessment of whether children with disabilities are supported to participate in their own care planning. No data on the specific types of abuse that children with disabilities experience in institutional settings.

**Online child protection:** No prevalence data on online grooming, CSAM, or cyberbullying among Maldivian children. No assessment of existing digital literacy programmes or their effectiveness. No mapping of the platforms through which exploitation is occurring.[^1010]

### 4.117.3 The Research Infrastructure Gap

The evidence gaps documented above are not primarily a failure of research capacity – they reflect the absence of a sustained research infrastructure for child protection in the Maldives. No dedicated research unit within MoSFD or any partner agency produces regular child welfare research. The UNICEF SitAn is produced periodically (typically every 5–7 years) rather than continuously. The GSHS has not been conducted since 2014 – over a decade of adolescent health data that does not exist.

Building a research infrastructure requires: a dedicated child welfare research unit within MoSFD or attached to MNU; sustained funding for a biennial monitoring cycle that tracks key indicators annually; partnership with the Bureau of Statistics to ensure child welfare indicators are included in routine household surveys; and investment in the data systems (MCPD improvement, FPA GBV database development, interoperability with NSPA's SPIS) that generate the administrative data needed for ongoing monitoring.

The cost of this research infrastructure is modest relative to the cost of planning without it. The alternative to investing in evidence generation is continuing to design policies based on assumptions, implement reforms without knowing whether they work, and allocate scarce child protection resources without knowing where need is greatest.[^1011]

---


## 4.120 Future Priorities and the Reform Agenda: Toward a Fit-for-Purpose Child Protection System


### 4.110.1 The Priority Reform Agenda – Six Critical Needs

Analysis of the full evidence base – the Rogers, Ali and Naeem (2025) systems analysis, the Seeds of Reform (Rogers & Ali, 2023) institutional care review, the CFW vulnerability mapping, the UNICEF SitAn, the CRC State Party Report and Concluding Observations, the NAPVAC 2024–2028, and the data in the Statistics Compendium – converges on six priority reform needs that constitute the essential agenda for making the Maldivian child protection system fit for purpose.

**Priority 1: Radical expansion of the social work workforce.** The 61 social workers serving 128,208 children at a 1:2,100 ratio (and an effective caseload of 160 per worker) represents the most fundamental system constraint. No other reform produces sustainable improvement without addressing this bottleneck. The workforce expansion requires: sustained increase in the number of social work graduates from Villa College and MNU; restructuring of social work salary and career pathways to retain qualified workers who currently move to better-compensated roles within two or three years of qualifying; and investment in the supervisory and management infrastructure that transforms individual workers into a functioning professional service.

**Priority 2: Deinstitutionalisation of alternative care.** The 91% institutional care rate for children outside parental care is the most consistent finding across all reviews – a pattern that the CRC Committee has repeatedly flagged and that the government has repeatedly committed to change. The deinstitutionalisation agenda requires: investment in foster carer recruitment, training, and support (including adequate financial allowances that make fostering economically viable); development of the regulatory and supervisory framework for kinship care; family reunification programmes for children in institutional care who could return safely to family; and a no-new-placements policy for residential care except as a last resort. Without specific, resourced, time-bound targets for reducing the institutional care population, the aspiration will continue to exceed the reality.

**Priority 3: Geographic equity – regional hub model.** The Male'-centrism of the current system – not merely a practical limitation but a structural equity issue – requires a deliberate geographic equity strategy. The most achievable model within current resource constraints is a regional hub architecture: four to six regional hubs (Addu, Laamu, Raa, Baa, Haa Dhaalu) with concentrations of specialist capacity – senior social workers, DV specialists, child psychologists, legal aid – that can provide outreach to surrounding atoll communities and digital supervision to island-level workers. This model does not resolve the geographic challenge but reduces the current binary of Male' (full services) versus everywhere else (single overworked social worker).

**Priority 4: Maintenance enforcement reform.** The accumulated MVR 2 million in unpaid maintenance at the Family Court represents not only a financial injustice to the children affected but a systemic failure of the legal protection that the Family Act purports to provide. A functioning automatic transfer system – modelled on the salary attachment orders used in other jurisdictions – would transform maintenance from a court order requiring active enforcement by the payee into an administrative mechanism that operates automatically through the financial system. The technical infrastructure (bank accounts, payroll systems, the OneGov platform) exists to make this feasible.

**Priority 5: Online child protection legislation and systems.** The Maldives has not yet enacted comprehensive online child safety legislation, despite the documented risks of online grooming, child sexual abuse material, and cyberbullying on platforms with very high usage rates among Maldivian children and adolescents. The NAPVAC 2024's commitment to legislative reform by 2027 requires immediate drafting work if it is to be achieved. Alongside legislation, the digital literacy and online safety awareness programming that the plan envisions requires a systematic rollout through schools and youth programmes rather than ad hoc awareness campaigns.

**Priority 6: Civil society development.** The near-absence of domestic civil society in child protection service delivery is a structural gap that creates risk – there is no independent sector capable of filling gaps, providing accountability, or delivering preventive services at the community level. Building civil society capacity in child protection requires: government grant funding for evidence-based family support programmes operated by NGOs such as ARC; regulatory frameworks for NGO service delivery that create accountability without bureaucratic barriers; and an explicit policy commitment to the complementary role of civil society in the national child protection architecture.[^986]

### 4.110.2 The Resource Question: What Adequate Child Protection Actually Costs

Child protection reform in the Maldives has consistently been constrained by the absence of costed implementation plans. The NAPVAC 2024–2028, like its predecessors, sets ambitious targets without budget allocations. The CFW recommendations acknowledge workforce shortfalls without identifying the budget needed to address them. This pattern reflects a broader challenge: governments commit to child protection reform aspirationally, but the fiscal framework required to deliver on those commitments is rarely made explicit.

A rough indicative costing illustrates the scale of investment required. Doubling the social work workforce from 61 to 122 workers – still far below international standards but a significant improvement – requires an annual salary cost of approximately MVR 36 million (based on National Pay Commission 2023 social work salary scales), plus investment in training, supervision, equipment, and travel. Expanding foster care to achieve 50% of alternative care placements in family-based settings requires carer allowances estimated at MVR 2,000–3,000 per child per month, plus training and supervision costs. Regional hub development requires capital investment in facilities and recurring investment in professional staff.

None of these investments is beyond the Maldivian government's fiscal capacity – particularly given the social protection budget's substantial scale and the government's demonstrated willingness to invest in welfare programmes (Aasandha, single parent allowance, disability allowance). The barrier is not fiscal capacity but fiscal prioritisation. Child protection competes with healthcare, education, and social protection programmes that have more politically visible beneficiaries and more organised constituency advocacy. The analytical case for treating child protection investment as foundational to all other social policy outcomes – health, education, economic participation, crime prevention – is strong but has not been made in the budget process with the rigour it requires.[^987]

### 4.110.3 The Political Economy of Child Protection Reform

The pattern of the past decade in Maldivian child protection – ambitious legislation, institutional architecture, and action plans contrasting with persistent implementation gaps – reflects not merely administrative failure but political economy. Child protection reform requires sustained multi-year commitment, cross-agency coordination, and resource allocation that is difficult to maintain across political transitions. The Maldives has had significant political changes between 2012 and 2024, with each administration bringing different priorities and relationships to the social sector.

The ministerial portfolio has shifted repeatedly – from MoGFSS to MoSFD to various configurations – and with each change comes potential discontinuity in leadership, priority-setting, and staff confidence. The most sustained progress has occurred when reform was embedded in legislation (the CRPA 2019, the Juvenile Justice Act 2019) rather than in administrative arrangements that depend on political continuity. This suggests that the reform agenda's long-term durability depends on legislative entrenchment – putting workforce standards, alternative care regulations, inter-agency protocols, and civil society partnership frameworks in legislative or regulatory form rather than leaving them to ministerial discretion.

The Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations – a rare formal accountability mechanism for past failures – represents a political economy moment of particular significance. When the highest political level formally acknowledges that child protection failures require accountability, it creates conditions for sustained reform that individual ministerial commitment cannot generate on its own. Sustaining this accountability dimension in the governance architecture – through the Children's Ombudsperson, the HRCM's monitoring function, and the CRC reporting cycle – is as important as any specific policy or programme reform.[^988]

### 4.110.4 The Child's Voice: Participation as a Reform Principle

Throughout the reforms of the post-2019 period, one dimension of the CRPA's framework has received less attention than others: Article 12 of the CRC, enshrined in the CRPA's participation provisions – the right of children to have their views taken into account in all matters affecting them. The current child protection system processes cases *about* children but does not systematically include children's voices in case planning, system design, or policy development.

The experiences of children in alternative care – both the small number in the formal system and the larger number in informal kinship arrangements – are almost entirely undocumented. The literature review found no Maldivian study specifically documenting the experiences of care-experienced children as described by those children themselves. The one closest equivalent – the GSHS 2014, which asked adolescents about their experiences of sexual abuse and other risks – was not designed as a participatory methodology.

Developing authentic child participation mechanisms is both a CRC obligation and a practical reform tool. Children who have experienced the child protection system have specific knowledge of its failures and strengths that no external analysis can replicate. Care-experienced young people who have grown up in residential institutions or informal kinship arrangements have expertise in what family-based care requires that foster care policy should draw on. Building systematic child participation into both case-level processes (children's care plans, review meetings) and system-level processes (policy consultation, monitoring bodies) is not an optional add-on to the reform agenda – it is a requirement of the legal framework to which the Maldives is committed.[^989]

---


## 4.121 Conclusion: The Gap Between Promise and Reality in Maldivian Child Protection


### 4.118.1 The Structural Achievement

The transformation of Maldivian child protection over the three decades since CRC ratification in 1991 is genuine and significant. From a system with no child protection legislation, no specialist workforce, no institutional architecture, and no community protection mechanisms, the Maldives has built: a comprehensive rights-based legal framework (CRPA 2019, Juvenile Justice Act 2019, SOA 2014, DVPA 2012); an institutional network (CFPS, 19 FCSCs, DJJ, Children's Ombudsperson, Children's Rights Protection Council, Children's Court); a professional social work workforce with degree-level training; a national child helpline (1412); a community protection model (IBAMA, CSGs); and a planning framework (NAPVAC 2024–2028, NSAP-CP) grounded in international standards. This is a genuine achievement that deserves recognition – it represents decades of sustained institutional building against significant resource and geographic constraints.

The legislative framework is, by regional standards, genuinely advanced. The CRPA 2019 is more comprehensive than comparable legislation in several larger South Asian states. The Juvenile Justice Act 2019 replaced a framework that was sentencing children to death. The mandatory reporting obligation creates a legal duty where none previously existed. These are not cosmetic reforms – they are structural changes to the legal architecture within which children's rights are exercised.[^1012]

### 4.118.2 The Implementation Reality

Against this legislative achievement, the implementation reality is sobering. Sixty-one social workers serve 128,208 children at a ratio five to six times above international standards. The child protection system operates in 20 of 189 inhabited islands. Ninety-one per cent of children in alternative care live in institutions rather than family-based settings, despite more than a decade of deinstitutionalisation commitments. Approximately three per cent of domestic violence cases reported to police result in prosecution. Three per cent of CEDAW's 55 recommendations have been fully implemented. Maintenance enforcement remains practically non-existent, with MVR 2 million in uncollected payments sitting at the Family Court. The criminal justice funnel for child sexual abuse – already restrictive for non-disabled children – is effectively inaccessible for children with disabilities.

These gaps are not peripheral quality improvement issues – they are fundamental mismatches between what the law promises and what the system delivers. For a child experiencing abuse in a remote island community, the existence of the CRPA 2019 provides no protection if there is no social worker within reach, no police officer trained in child protection interviewing, no court with relevant expertise, and no evidence of sufficient quality to meet the prosecution threshold. The law on paper is not the law in experience.[^1013]

### 4.118.3 The Path Forward: Three Foundational Prerequisites

The analysis in this chapter converges on three foundational prerequisites for sustainable child protection system improvement – prerequisites that must precede, not follow, the more specific reforms described in §4.110.

**First: political prioritisation.** Child protection reform requires sustained multi-year resource allocation and cross-agency coordination – both of which require political will at the highest level. The Presidential Commission on Child Rights Violations demonstrated that this political will can be mobilised. Sustaining it across electoral cycles requires embedding the reform agenda in legislation (not administrative discretion), in inter-party consensus on the CRC obligations that transcend political change, and in a civil society and media environment that makes child protection a sustained accountability issue.

**Second: honest accounting of what the system can and cannot do.** The gap between legislative ambition and operational capacity is not a temporary problem to be fixed by the next action plan – it is a structural characteristic of a small island state with dispersed geography, limited professional infrastructure, and a demanding set of international obligations. Honest accounting requires acknowledging, for instance, that the CRPA 2019's investigation timescales cannot currently be met for the vast majority of Maldivian children; that IBAMA cannot substitute for professional social work capacity; and that the deinstitutionalisation goal requires a foster care system that does not yet exist at adequate scale. Only by acknowledging what the system cannot do at current resource levels can meaningful prioritisation of resource allocation take place.

**Third: the evidence foundation.** A national prevalence study of violence against children, a systematic evaluation of IBAMA's effectiveness, an outcomes study for children leaving the care system, and a research infrastructure for ongoing monitoring are not luxuries – they are the evidence base on which sustainable system improvement must be built. Continuing to plan without them is continuing to navigate without a map.[^1014]

### 4.118.4 The Children's Perspective: What the System Owes

This chapter has documented the Maldivian child protection system from the outside – through legislation, institutions, data, and policy frameworks. The final word belongs to what the system is for: the individual child experiencing abuse, neglect, or exploitation whose safety, dignity, and future depend on what the system does.

For that child – whether they are a girl in Malé experiencing sexual abuse by a family member, a boy in a remote atoll being physically punished without effective recourse, a child in institutional care trying to maintain a sense of identity and belonging, or a child with a disability whose communication needs the system cannot accommodate – the promise of the CRC is the promise of a state that takes their rights seriously, that has systems robust enough to protect them, and that treats their voices and experiences as material to the decisions that shape their lives.

The Maldivian state has made that promise through three decades of CRC ratification, legislative reform, institution-building, and international commitment. The obligation now is to close the gap between the promise and the reality – not gradually, not aspirationally, but with the urgency that the scale of unmet need demands. The 3,077 children with disabilities who statistical estimates suggest have experienced sexual violence, the survivors of institutional abuse whose experiences precipitated the Presidential Commission, the children on 169 islands without any Ministry presence – they are not future beneficiaries of planned reforms. They are current bearers of rights to which the Maldivian system owes a response that it has not yet delivered.[^1015]

## 4.122 Toward a Maldives-Specific Child Protection Framework: Principles and Architecture


### 4.123.1 Beyond Imported Models

The preceding analysis has established that the Maldivian child protection system – while legally sophisticated and institutionally comprehensive in design – operates in conditions that differ fundamentally from the English child protection model that has most significantly influenced it. A system designed for a densely populated, institutionally rich, professional-workforce-abundant context does not operate effectively when transposed onto a context of geographical dispersal, limited professional workforce, strong community social structures, and Islamic family law.

The task of developing a Maldives-specific child protection framework is not about rejecting international standards – the CRC remains the appropriate normative framework, and its specific articles provide the right substantive obligations. It is about designing operational systems that can actually meet those standards in the Maldivian context, rather than nominal systems that promise compliance while operating at a fraction of the required capacity.

A Maldives-specific framework would be built on five design principles:
1. **Community-first, professional-second:** The professional statutory system cannot reach all 189 inhabited islands. Community structures – IBAMA, CSGs, WDCs – must be designed to perform meaningful protective functions, not merely to serve as referral conduits to a central system that most communities cannot access in time.
2. **Islamic framework integration:** The interaction of the CRPA 2019 with Islamic family law – in custody, maintenance, *wali*, *kafalah*, and corporal punishment – must be explicitly addressed rather than ignored. A framework that Islamic scholars and community leaders can understand and support is more protective of children than a framework that earns technical compliance without community legitimacy.
3. **Geographic equity architecture:** Service design must be built around the reality of geographic dispersal, not around the assumption that services accessible in Male' are accessible to all Maldivian children. Regional hubs, mobile outreach, digital case management, and community-level capacity are not supplements to the professional system – they are its essential geographic extension.
4. **Family-centred rather than case-centred:** The primary unit of analysis and intervention should be the family, not the individual presenting concern. A family with domestic violence, substance misuse, poverty, and child neglect needs a single integrated response, not four parallel referrals to four different systems.
5. **Evidence-grounded:** Policy must be based on evidence of what works in the Maldivian context, not on imported assumptions from contexts that share neither the geography, the culture, nor the resource profile of the Maldives.[^1030]

### 4.123.2 The Regional Hub Architecture

The most practically achievable structural reform for geographic equity is the development of four to six regional child protection hubs – concentrations of specialist professional capacity that can serve as the anchor for regional service delivery without requiring the infrastructure investment of full CFSC presence on every island.

A regional hub model in the Maldivian context would:
- Position senior social workers, child psychologists, DV specialists, and legal advisors in four to six regional centres (Addu City, Fuvahmulah, Laamuge/Laamu, Kulhudhuffushi/Haa Dhaal, Thinadhoo/Gaafu Dhaalu, Naifaru/Lhaviyani)
- Provide regular outreach visits – by dhow, speedboat, or domestic flight – to islands within each regional catchment
- Offer digital supervision and case consultation to FCSC workers on islands within the hub's catchment, enabling workers to access specialist advice without requiring physical presence
- Co-locate with health services (atoll hospitals) where infrastructure already exists, reducing capital cost while improving multi-agency accessibility

This model does not solve the distance problem – a child on a remote island still does not have a social worker in their community. But it substantially reduces the binary of Male' (full services) versus outer islands (minimal services), creating an intermediate tier of accessible specialist support that can respond to complex cases more rapidly than the current system allows.[^1031]

### 4.123.3 The Civil Society Development Agenda

As documented in §4.103, the near-absence of child protection NGOs is a structural gap that creates risk and limits resilience. Building domestic civil society capacity in child protection is a medium-to-long-term project that requires deliberate investment rather than the organic growth of civil society that occurred in more institutionally rich environments.

The three most achievable initial steps are:
1. **Government grants for family support programming:** Commissioning ARC or a new NGO to deliver evidence-based family support services – parenting programmes, family counselling, early intervention for at-risk families – in Male' and progressively in regional hubs. This fills the preventive gap that the statutory system cannot address at current staffing levels.
2. **NGO service standards and regulatory framework:** Developing a licensing and quality assurance framework for NGOs providing child protection services, giving government purchasers and families the confidence to rely on civil society providers for specialist services.
3. **Children's advocacy organisation:** Supporting the establishment (or development of ARC's capacity) to serve as an independent voice for children's rights – capable of holding the statutory system accountable, representing children's interests in policy consultation, and producing research that informs evidence-based reform.

None of these requires large capital investment. The annual cost of funding a family support programme is modest compared to the cost of child protection investigations and residential care placements that effective prevention might avert.[^1032]

### 4.123.4 The Islamic Framework as Resource

The analysis of Islamic law dimensions in §4.61 and throughout this chapter has primarily focused on the tensions between Shari'ah provisions and CRC standards. The final analytical observation is that the Islamic framework is also a resource for child protection – a set of obligations, values, and institutions that can support, rather than only complicate, the child protection mission.

Islamic theology is clear on the protection of children as a duty. The Prophet's hadith on the duty of care for orphans and vulnerable children are frequently cited across Muslim-majority contexts as a religious obligation of child protection. The *kafalah* framework – providing care for children without formal adoption – is an Islamic mechanism for family-based alternative care that the UN Guidelines on Alternative Care explicitly recognise. The *zakah* and *sadaqah* obligations create Islamic charitable mechanisms that can fund family support and community protection activities.

The Ministry of Islamic Affairs, mosques, and Islamic scholars represent an enormous reach into community life that the child protection system has not systematically engaged. A Friday sermon addressing child protection obligations, the duty to report abuse, and the Islamic perspective on children's rights would reach more Maldivians than any awareness campaign the MoSFD can organise. Engaging Islamic leadership as partners in child protection norm change – rather than treating them solely as representatives of the legal traditions that complicate implementation – is both strategically effective and theologically grounded in the religion's own child welfare obligations.[^1033]
