[^1022]: Therapeutic services geography: Service Provider Directory §3.13; Maldives Autism Association programme documentation; northern atoll coverage (NazaHealth, Naifaru); telehealth provision assessment.

[^1023]: Child protection CSOs – contact and geography: Service Provider Directory §3.9; CSO/NGO Directory (21b); geographic coverage analysis. FLC phone access (9am-4pm) as outer-island access mechanism.

[^1024]: Digital services and child protection: Service Provider Directory §3.12; OneGov platform development; UNPRPD 2024 on NDR accessibility; MCPD internal system vs. public digital infrastructure.

[^1025]: Tourism dependency and child vulnerability: Part XII Housing, Poverty chapter; COVID-19 income impact data (44% average income reduction); resort separation model; Part XIV Climate Vulnerability.

[^1026]: Housing density and child protection: Part XII Housing, Poverty chapter (Male' 65,000/km²); overcrowding and domestic violence risk; Section 42 custody trap and housing mobility constraint; privacy and disclosure dynamics.

[^1027]: Fisheries sector vulnerability: Part XII; Part XIV Climate chapter; fishing community family structure analysis; *gah'therikan* and its limits in tight-knit communities.

[^1028]: Climate change and child protection: Part XIV Climate Vulnerability chapter; WHO documentation of children's climate health vulnerability; population consolidation policy; displacement and community protective structure loss.

[^1029]: Gender-responsive climate frameworks and children: GRFS 2023; Part V gender chapter climate-gender nexus section; NAPVAC climate gap; NAPDI disaster response accessibility gap; girls and school withdrawal in climate disruption.

[^1030]: Maldives-specific framework principles: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) model analysis; Islamic family law integration challenge; geographic equity architecture requirement; family-centred approach; evidence-grounding need. Five principles synthesised from the analytical findings of this chapter.

[^1031]: Regional hub architecture: Service Provider Directory geographic distribution; Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) geographic analysis; existing infrastructure (atoll hospitals) as co-location opportunity. Specific hub locations based on administrative atoll distribution and population concentration.

[^1032]: Civil society development agenda: §4.103 civil society analysis; ARC recommendation from Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025); international NGO commissioning models for family support; regulatory framework requirements.

[^1033]: Islamic framework as resource: §4.61 Islamic law dimensions; CRC Article 20(3) explicit *kafalah* recognition; Ministry of Islamic Affairs reach; mosque network as awareness channel; theological child protection obligations in Islamic jurisprudence.


## Footnotes


[^1]: Rogers, M., Ali, S. & Naeem, M. (2025). *Child Protection Systems in the Maldives*. Chapter in forthcoming volume. Historical overview of institutional care, referencing Islaahiya establishment 1979. See also UNICEF, *Situation Analysis of Children in the Maldives* (2021), pp. 34–37.

[^2]: The dynamics of concealment and non-disclosure in small island communities are discussed extensively in Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), which notes that "complex politics permeates even child protection activities" and describes the social pressures that suppress reporting.

[^3]: United Nations Treaty Collection, Convention on the Rights of the Child, Status of Ratification: Maldives. Ratified 11 February 1991 with reservations on Articles 14(1) and 21.

[^4]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), describing Law 9/91 as "largely declaratory, with no enforcement mechanisms." See also Hama Jamiyya & Care Society, *NGO Shadow Report on CRC* (2006), which critiqued the gap between legislative statements and practical implementation.

[^5]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), documenting the establishment of the URC in 1992 within the Ministry of Home Affairs and its transfer to the Ministry of Youth, Women and Social Welfare in 1993.

[^6]: Ibid. See also UNICEF, *Situation Analysis of Children in the Maldives* (2021), on the limited operational capacity of early child protection institutions.

[^7]: UNICEF Maldives, *Post-Tsunami Child Protection Programming Review* (2006). See also Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), noting the tsunami as a catalyst for expanded child protection programming.

[^8]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), documenting the 2006 procedure manual as "based on England's 'Working Together' guidance" and noting the "cultural tensions with local practices" that resulted.

[^9]: Ibid. The analysis of cultural tensions draws on Rogers, Ali & Naeem's discussion of the challenges of transposing Western child protection models into the Maldivian context.

[^10]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), documenting the 2006 training of the first social worker cohort through a one-year Advanced Certificate at the University of Kelaniya, Sri Lanka, the 2007 introduction of the Advanced Diploma, and its subsequent development into a four-year BSW.

[^11]: Ibid. See also the discussion of workforce capacity challenges in the UNICEF *Situation Analysis* (2025, updated).

[^12]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the 2008 institutional restructuring that moved child protection to the Ministry of Health under the Department of Gender and Family Protection Services.

[^13]: Child Helpline International, *Maldives Country Profile* (2008). Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the establishment and early operations of the 1412 helpline.

[^14]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), providing a detailed account of the 2009 baby death case, noting the mother was "known to the system for 7 years through drug use" and that the boyfriend "shook the baby." The case review "recommended a formal supervisory system."

[^15]: Cf. Lord Laming, *The Victoria Climbie Inquiry* (2003); E. Munro, *The Munro Review of Child Protection: Final Report* (2011). Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) draw parallels with international child protection system failures.

[^16]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the Decentralisation Act 2010, the establishment of CFPS, and the 19 atoll-level FCSCs. The figure of Ministry presence in only "20 of 189 inhabited islands" is from the same source.

[^17]: Ibid., on the initiation of Community Social Groups in 2010 and their revival through the IBAMA project.

[^18]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the 2015 implementation of child protection focal points in schools and the absence of referral systems and case conferences between schools and CFPS. The absence of police checks and child protection training for school staff is also documented.

[^19]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the launch of the Maldives Child Protection Database (MCPD) in 2016 and its connection of all 19 FCSCs.

[^20]: Child Rights Protection Act 2019 (Act 19/2019), Republic of Maldives. See Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) for legislative analysis.

[^21]: Children's Ombudsperson & UNICEF, *Child Rights Symposium 2022* proceedings. See also CRPA 2019, Articles 80, 90, 93 on judicial orders.

[^22]: Juvenile Justice Act 2019 (Act 18/2019), Republic of Maldives. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the rehabilitation orientation.

[^23]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), noting that flogging was "NOT abolished" under the JJ Act. See also Human Rights Watch, *Maldives: End Flogging of Children* (2013); CRC Committee, *Concluding Observations on the Maldives* (various years).

[^24]: Alternative Care Regulation 2020-R-69; Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30; Education Act 2020 (Act 24/2020); Domestic Violence Prevention Act 2012 (Act 3/2012); Sexual Offences Act 2014 (Act 17/2014); Prevention of Human Trafficking Act 2013 (Act 12/2013); Special Measures for Perpetrators of Child Sexual Abuse (Act 12/2009).

[^25]: Republic of Maldives, *Combined 6th and 7th Periodic Report to the Committee on the Rights of the Child* (October 2021). UNICEF, *Situation Analysis of Children in the Maldives* (2021, updated 2025). *Child-Equity-Census-2022* analysis.

[^26]: Hama Jamiyya & Care Society, *NGO Shadow Report on the Convention on the Rights of the Child* (2006).

[^27]: National Bureau of Statistics, *"Children in Maldives" Census 2014 Analysis*. The figure of 74% of children living with both parents and 22% with one parent is from the 2022 Census, as reported in Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025).

[^28]: CRPA 2019 (Act 19/2019), definitions and interpretation provisions.

[^29]: Adam, A. (2017). On the exploitation of children brought to Malé for education who are "forced into domestic work." See also Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), discussing the cultural complexity of kinship-based caregiving arrangements.

[^30]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), vulnerability mapping research findings, documenting that children are "used as leverage in custody disputes," are "separated from primary caregivers, dispersed across relatives," and "witnessed violence, experienced direct abuse."

[^31]: Ibid., on the four pathways of child protection referral.

[^32]: CRPA 2019 (Act 19/2019), Articles 80, 90, and 93. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the five-day statutory timeline.

[^33]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), noting that "care plans are rare" and "regular review meetings are not common practice."

[^34]: Ibid., on the absence of "established referral systems or case conferences between schools and CFPS despite Education Act 2020."

[^35]: Ibid., noting that "police checks and child protection training for school staff" are absent.

[^36]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on political interference in child protection: "if child belongs to family of political actor, case may be leaked to media before being reported"; "if father is gang leader/drug trafficker, case may not be reported due to fear of reprisal." Human Rights Watch (2023), reporting that "social workers have received death threats."

[^37]: Ministry of Gender, Family and Social Services, *Quarterly Report March–May 2018*. The figure of 349 cases with 48% being sexual abuse or physical violence is from Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025).

[^38]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), referencing Table 8.60 on reported child abuse cases 2017–2022 by type (neglect, physical, emotional, sexual, bullying, harassment, witnessing DV, cyberbullying).

[^39]: Ibid., citing survey data showing 15% of adolescents experienced sexual abuse with rates among girls double those of boys.

[^40]: Ministry of Gender, Family Development and Security & UNFPA, *Women's Health and Life Experiences Study* (2007), finding childhood sexual abuse prevalence of 12.2% overall and 16.3% in Malé.

[^41]: IGMH data 2005–2009 as reported in Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025): 43 rape cases, 22 involving minors, almost half gang-rape.

[^42]: Adam, A. (2017), on children brought to Malé by relatives for education being "forced into domestic work."

[^43]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on housing-related child protection risks in Malé.

[^44]: See Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) on forensic and victim support capacity gaps. See also CRPA 2019; Sexual Offences Act 2014; Act 12/2009.

[^45]: CRPA 2019 (Act 19/2019), child labour provisions. Education Act 2020 (Act 24/2020), compulsory education provisions. US Department of Labor, *2009 Findings on the Worst Forms of Child Labor: Maldives* (2010), noting the absence of compulsory education law.

[^46]: US Department of Labor, *Findings on the Worst Forms of Child Labor*: Maldives country reports for 2009 ("no compulsory education law... children exploited as domestic servants and in forced prostitution"), 2018 ("minimal advancement"), and 2020 ("moderate advancement").

[^47]: Ibid. See also Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) on children in drug trafficking networks.

[^48]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the legal and social status of children born outside marriage. See also CRPA 2019 on non-discrimination provisions.

[^49]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the establishment of the Juvenile Court in 1997 and its operations prior to the JJ Act.

[^50]: Ibid., reporting five juveniles sentenced to death between 2013 and 2015. See also Human Rights Watch, *"I Wanted to Lie Down and Die": Trafficking and Torture of Eritreans in Sudan and Egypt* (2014), containing references to Maldivian juvenile justice; Amnesty International, *Death Sentences and Executions* (annual reports 2013–2015).

[^51]: JJ Act 2019 (Act 18/2019), provisions on age of criminal responsibility, death penalty prohibition, and rehabilitation mandate. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) on the non-abolition of flogging.

[^52]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), reporting 89 juveniles in rehabilitation 2010–2015 with only 11.2% completing programmes.

[^53]: Ibid., on drug offences as the primary driver of juvenile justice contact.

[^54]: Ibid., on the absence of separate juvenile detention facilities.

[^55]: Ibid., on the National Reintegration Centre and the successful reintegration of only 3 children.

[^56]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), reporting 187 children in alternative care as of March 2023 (82 girls, 105 boys), representing 0.02% of all children.

[^57]: Ibid., reporting 91% in residential settings and only 18 registered foster carers caring for 16 children (9%). See also UN General Assembly, *Guidelines for the Alternative Care of Children* (A/RES/64/142, 2010), on the primacy of family-based care.

[^58]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), providing the institutional history: Islaahiya (1979–2013), Kudakudhinge Hiya (2004, ages 0–9), CTCC (2009–2013), Aman Hiya (2006, girls 9–18), Fiyavethi (2016, Qatar Foundation, under 13).

[^59]: Ibid., on the post-COVID restructuring into 9 smaller residential units of 18–24 children each.

[^60]: Ibid., on the underdevelopment of foster care. See also the CRC reservation on Article 21 (adoption) and its implications for permanency planning.

[^61]: Alternative Care Regulation 2020-R-69; Regulation on Reintegration 2020/R-30.

[^62]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on the Child Helpline 1412 (established 2008) and Social Services Hotline 1421.

[^63]: CRPA 2019 (Act 19/2019), mandatory reporting provisions. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on implementation challenges.

[^64]: Global Initiative to End All Corporal Punishment of Children, *Country Report: Maldives* (2015), documenting legality across all settings. Education Act 2020 on school prohibition. Penal Code 2014 draft on parental use of force. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) on JJ Act non-abolition of flogging.

[^65]: CRC Committee, *Concluding Observations on the Maldives* (various cycles), recommending comprehensive prohibition.

[^66]: CRPA 2019, raising marriage age to 18. Family Act, Section 4(b), the continuing loophole. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025) on the inconsistency.

[^67]: Census 2014 data on 106 married children aged 15–17 (58.5% girls). ADB (2025), child marriage rate of 2.2% before age 18. Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), on reports of child marriage increasing. Family Court Chief Judge describing unregistered marriages as a "huge challenge."

[^68]: Ibid., on conservative fatwas endorsing unregistered marriages and the challenges they pose.

[^69]: UNICEF, *Situation Analysis of Children in the Maldives* (2021, updated 2025). *Child-Equity-Census-2022* analysis. See also UNICEF & Children's Ombudsperson, *Child Rights Symposium 2022*.

[^70]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), vulnerability mapping findings on intersecting disadvantage.

[^71]: UNICEF, *Climate Change and Children in the Maldives* (2021). See also Part XIV of this encyclopedia.

[^72]: Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), reporting 61 child protection social workers serving 128,208 children (average caseload potential of 160) with actual caseworker loads of 70–80 cases each. International recommended caseload of 25–30 from the Child Welfare League of America and British Association of Social Workers.

[^73]: Ibid., on the consequences of workforce overload, including death threats to social workers (HRW, 2023).

[^74]: Ibid., on Ministry presence in only 20 of 189 inhabited islands and the geographic distribution of the workforce.

[^75]: Ibid., on the professional development pathway (Advanced Certificate 2006, Advanced Diploma 2007, BSW) and the 2009 recommendation for a formal supervisory system.

[^76]: Ibid., on the absence of multi-agency case conferencing and inter-agency coordination mechanisms.

[^77]: Ibid., on the vulnerability of children with disabilities, including court proceedings conducted "without interpreters."

[^78]: Ibid., vulnerability mapping finding that children were "both the primary reason women stayed in violent relationships AND the primary reason they eventually left."

[^79]: DHS 2016–17, reporting 13% FGM/C prevalence among women 15–49, declining from 38% (ages 45–49) to 1% (ages 15–19). Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025), noting no legal prohibition or criminalisation. See also Part V of this encyclopedia for detailed treatment.

[^80]: Research gaps identified across Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025); UNICEF *Situation Analysis* (2021, 2025); CRC Committee *Concluding Observations*; and the *Child Rights Symposium 2022*.

[^81]: Policy recommendations synthesised from Rogers, Ali & Naeem (2025); UNICEF *Situation Analysis* (2025); CRC Committee recommendations; and the analysis presented in this chapter.

*Part IV compiled for the Maldives Social Policy Encyclopedia, Version 1.0, March 2026. This chapter draws extensively on Rogers, M., Ali, S. & Naeem, M. (2025), the UNICEF Situation Analysis series (2021, 2025), CRC periodic reports and shadow reports, and primary legislation of the Republic of Maldives.*
